   <TITLE>chapter32</TITLE>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 32</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When the particulars of this conversation were

repeated



<P>by Miss Dashwood to her sister, as they very soon were,



<P>the effect on her was not entirely such as the former



<P>had hoped to see.&nbsp; Not that Marianne appeared to distrust



<P>the truth of any part of it, for she listened to it all



<P>with the most steady and submissive attention, made neither



<P>objection nor remark, attempted no vindication of Willoughby,



<P>and seemed to shew by her tears that she felt it to



<P>be impossible.&nbsp; But though this behaviour assured Elinor



<P>that the conviction of this guilt WAS carried home to



<P>her mind, though she saw with satisfaction the effect of it,



<P>in her no longer avoiding Colonel Brandon when he called,



<P>in her speaking to him, even voluntarily speaking,



<P>with a kind of compassionate respect, and though she



<P>saw her spirits less violently irritated than before,



<P>she did not see her less wretched.&nbsp; Her mind did become



<P>settled, but it was settled in a gloomy dejection.



<P>She felt the loss of Willoughby's character yet more heavily



<P>than she had felt the loss of his heart; his seduction and



<P>desertion of Miss Williams, the misery of that poor girl,



<P>and the doubt of what his designs might ONCE have been



<P>on herself, preyed altogether so much on her spirits,



<P>that she could not bring herself to speak of what she felt



<P>even to Elinor; and, brooding over her sorrows in silence,



<P>gave more pain to her sister than could have been communicated



<P>by the most open and most frequent confession of them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; To give the feelings or the language of Mrs.

Dashwood



<P>on receiving and answering Elinor's letter would be only



<P>to give a repetition of what her daughters had already felt



<P>and said; of a disappointment hardly less painful than



<P>Marianne's, and an indignation even greater than Elinor's.



<P>Long letters from her, quickly succeeding each other,



<P>arrived to tell all that she suffered and thought;



<P>to express her anxious solicitude for Marianne, and entreat



<P>she would bear up with fortitude under this misfortune.



<P>Bad indeed must the nature of Marianne's affliction be,



<P>when her mother could talk of fortitude! mortifying



<P>and humiliating must be the origin of those regrets,



<P>which SHE could wish her not to indulge!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Against the interest of her own individual

comfort,



<P>Mrs. Dashwood had determined that it would be better for



<P>Marianne to be any where, at that time, than at Barton,



<P>where every thing within her view would be bringing back



<P>the past in the strongest and most afflicting manner,



<P>by constantly placing Willoughby before her, such as



<P>she had always seen him there.&nbsp; She recommended it to



<P>her daughters, therefore, by all means not to shorten their



<P>visit to Mrs. Jennings; the length of which, though never



<P>exactly fixed, had been expected by all to comprise at least



<P>five or six weeks.&nbsp; A variety of occupations, of objects,



<P>and of company, which could not be procured at Barton,



<P>would be inevitable there, and might yet, she hoped,



<P>cheat Marianne, at times, into some interest beyond herself,



<P>and even into some amusement, much as the ideas of both



<P>might now be spurned by her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; From all danger of seeing Willoughby again,



<P>her mother considered her to be at least equally safe



<P>in town as in the country, since his acquaintance must



<P>now be dropped by all who called themselves her friends.



<P>Design could never bring them in each other's way:



<P>negligence could never leave them exposed to a surprise;



<P>and chance had less in its favour in the crowd of London



<P>than even in the retirement of Barton, where it might



<P>force him before her while paying that visit at Allenham



<P>on his marriage, which Mrs. Dashwood, from foreseeing at



<P>first as a probable event, had brought herself to expect



<P>as a certain one.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She had yet another reason for wishing her

children



<P>to remain where they were; a letter from her son-in-law



<P>had told her that he and his wife were to be in town



<P>before the middle of February, and she judged it right



<P>that they should sometimes see their brother.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne had promised to be guided by her mother's

opinion,



<P>and she submitted to it therefore without opposition,



<P>though it proved perfectly different from what she wished



<P>and expected, though she felt it to be entirely wrong,



<P>formed on mistaken grounds, and that by requiring her



<P>longer continuance in London it deprived her of the only



<P>possible alleviation of her wretchedness, the personal



<P>sympathy of her mother, and doomed her to such society and



<P>such scenes as must prevent her ever knowing a moment's rest.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; But it was a matter of great consolation to

her,



<P>that what brought evil to herself would bring good to



<P>her sister; and Elinor, on the other hand, suspecting that



<P>it would not be in her power to avoid Edward entirely,



<P>comforted herself by thinking, that though their longer



<P>stay would therefore militate against her own happiness,



<P>it would be better for Marianne than an immediate return



<P>into Devonshire.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Her carefulness in guarding her sister from

ever



<P>hearing Willoughby's name mentioned, was not thrown away.



<P>Marianne, though without knowing it herself, reaped all



<P>its advantage; for neither Mrs. Jennings, nor Sir John,



<P>nor even Mrs. Palmer herself, ever spoke of him before her.



<P>Elinor wished that the same forbearance could have extended



<P>towards herself, but that was impossible, and she was



<P>obliged to listen day after day to the indignation of them all.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Sir John, could not have thought it possible.



<P>"A man of whom he had always had such reason to think well!



<P>Such a good-natured fellow!&nbsp; He did not believe there was a



<P>bolder rider in England!&nbsp; It was an unaccountable business.



<P>He wished him at the devil with all his heart.&nbsp; He would



<P>not speak another word to him, meet him where he might,



<P>for all the world!&nbsp; No, not if it were to be by the side



<P>of Barton covert, and they were kept watching for two



<P>hours together.&nbsp; Such a scoundrel of a fellow! such



<P>a deceitful dog!&nbsp; It was only the last time they met



<P>that he had offered him one of Folly's puppies! and this



<P>was the end of it!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Palmer, in her way, was equally angry.



<P>"She was determined to drop his acquaintance immediately,



<P>and she was very thankful that she had never been acquainted



<P>with him at all.&nbsp; She wished with all her heart Combe



<P>Magna was not so near Cleveland; but it did not signify,



<P>for it was a great deal too far off to visit; she hated



<P>him so much that she was resolved never to mention



<P>his name again, and she should tell everybody she saw,



<P>how good-for-nothing he was."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The rest of Mrs. Palmer's sympathy was shewn

in procuring



<P>all the particulars in her power of the approaching marriage,



<P>and communicating them to Elinor.&nbsp; She could soon tell



<P>at what coachmaker's the new carriage was building,



<P>by what painter Mr. Willoughby's portrait was drawn,



<P>and at what warehouse Miss Grey's clothes might be seen.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The calm and polite unconcern of Lady Middleton



<P>on the occasion was a happy relief to Elinor's spirits,



<P>oppressed as they often were by the clamorous kindness



<P>of the others.&nbsp; It was a great comfort to her to be sure



<P>of exciting no interest in ONE person at least among their



<P>circle of friends: a great comfort to know that there



<P>was ONE who would meet her without feeling any curiosity



<P>after particulars, or any anxiety for her sister's health.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Every qualification is raised at times, by

the



<P>circumstances of the moment, to more than its real value;



<P>and she was sometimes worried down by officious condolence



<P>to rate good-breeding as more indispensable to comfort



<P>than good-nature.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lady Middleton expressed her sense of the affair



<P>about once every day, or twice, if the subject occurred



<P>very often, by saying, "It is very shocking, indeed!"



<P>and by the means of this continual though gentle vent,



<P>was able not only to see the Miss Dashwoods from the



<P>first without the smallest emotion, but very soon



<P>to see them without recollecting a word of the matter;



<P>and having thus supported the dignity of her own sex,



<P>and spoken her decided censure of what was wrong



<P>in the other, she thought herself at liberty to attend



<P>to the interest of her own assemblies, and therefore



<P>determined (though rather against the opinion of Sir John)



<P>that as Mrs. Willoughby would at once be a woman of elegance



<P>and fortune, to leave her card with her as soon as she married.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Colonel Brandon's delicate, unobtrusive enquiries



<P>were never unwelcome to Miss Dashwood.&nbsp; He had abundantly



<P>earned the privilege of intimate discussion of her



<P>sister's disappointment, by the friendly zeal with



<P>which he had endeavoured to soften it, and they always



<P>conversed with confidence.&nbsp; His chief reward for the



<P>painful exertion of disclosing past sorrows and present



<P>humiliations, was given in the pitying eye with which



<P>Marianne sometimes observed him, and the gentleness



<P>of her voice whenever (though it did not often happen)



<P>she was obliged, or could oblige herself to speak to him.



<P>THESE assured him that his exertion had produced an



<P>increase of good-will towards himself, and THESE gave



<P>Elinor hopes of its being farther augmented hereafter;



<P>but Mrs. Jennings, who knew nothing of all this, who knew



<P>only that the Colonel continued as grave as ever, and that



<P>she could neither prevail on him to make the offer himself,



<P>nor commission her to make it for him, began, at the



<P>end of two days, to think that, instead of Midsummer,



<P>they would not be married till Michaelmas, and by the



<P>end of a week that it would not be a match at all.



<P>The good understanding between the Colonel and Miss



<P>Dashwood seemed rather to declare that the honours



<P>of the mulberry-tree, the canal, and the yew arbour,



<P>would all be made over to HER; and Mrs. Jennings had,



<P>for some time ceased to think at all of Mrs. Ferrars.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Early in February, within a fortnight from

the



<P>receipt of Willoughby's letter, Elinor had the painful



<P>office of informing her sister that he was married.



<P>She had taken care to have the intelligence conveyed



<P>to herself, as soon as it was known that the ceremony



<P>was over, as she was desirous that Marianne should not



<P>receive the first notice of it from the public papers,



<P>which she saw her eagerly examining every morning.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She received the news with resolute composure;



<P>made no observation on it, and at first shed no tears;



<P>but after a short time they would burst out, and for the



<P>rest of the day, she was in a state hardly less pitiable



<P>than when she first learnt to expect the event.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The Willoughbys left town as soon as they were

married;



<P>and Elinor now hoped, as there could be no danger



<P>of her seeing either of them, to prevail on her sister,



<P>who had never yet left the house since the blow first fell,



<P>to go out again by degrees as she had done before.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; About this time the two Miss Steeles, lately

arrived



<P>at their cousin's house in Bartlett's Buildings,



<P>Holburn, presented themselves again before their more



<P>grand relations in Conduit and Berkeley Streets;



<P>and were welcomed by them all with great cordiality.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor only was sorry to see them.&nbsp; Their

presence



<P>always gave her pain, and she hardly knew how to make



<P>a very gracious return to the overpowering delight of Lucy



<P>in finding her STILL in town.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I should have been quite disappointed if I

had not



<P>found you here STILL," said she repeatedly, with a strong



<P>emphasis on the word.&nbsp; "But I always thought I SHOULD.



<P>I was almost sure you would not leave London yet awhile;



<P>though you TOLD me, you know, at Barton, that you should



<P>not stay above a MONTH.&nbsp; But I thought, at the time,



<P>that you would most likely change your mind when it came



<P>to the point.&nbsp; It would have been such a great pity



<P>to have went away before your brother and sister came.



<P>And now to be sure you will be in no hurry to be gone.



<P>I am amazingly glad you did not keep to YOUR WORD."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor perfectly understood her, and was forced



<P>to use all her self-command to make it appear that she



<P>did NOT.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, my dear," said Mrs. Jennings, "and how

did



<P>you travel?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Not in the stage, I assure you," replied Miss

Steele,



<P>with quick exultation; "we came post all the way, and had



<P>a very smart beau to attend us.&nbsp; Dr. Davies was coming



<P>to town, and so we thought we'd join him in a post-chaise;



<P>and he behaved very genteelly, and paid ten or twelve



<P>shillings more than we did."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, oh!" cried Mrs. Jennings; "very pretty,



<P>indeed! and the Doctor is a single man, I warrant you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "There now," said Miss Steele, affectedly simpering,



<P>"everybody laughs at me so about the Doctor, and I



<P>cannot think why.&nbsp; My cousins say they are sure I have



<P>made a conquest; but for my part I declare I never think



<P>about him from one hour's end to another.&nbsp; 'Lord! here



<P>comes your beau, Nancy,' my cousin said t'other day,



<P>when she saw him crossing the street to the house.



<P>My beau, indeed! said I--I cannot think who you mean.



<P>The Doctor is no beau of mine."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Aye, aye, that is very pretty talking--but

it won't do--



<P>the Doctor is the man, I see."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, indeed!" replied her cousin, with affected

earnestness,



<P>"and I beg you will contradict it, if you ever hear it talked of."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings directly gave her the gratifying



<P>assurance that she certainly would NOT, and Miss Steele



<P>was made completely happy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I suppose you will go and stay with your brother



<P>and sister, Miss Dashwood, when they come to town,"



<P>said Lucy, returning, after a cessation of hostile hints,



<P>to the charge.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, I do not think we shall."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, yes, I dare say you will."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor would not humour her by farther opposition.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What a charming thing it is that Mrs. Dashwood

can



<P>spare you both for so long a time together!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Long a time, indeed!" interposed Mrs. Jennings.



<P>"Why, their visit is but just begun!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lucy was silenced.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am sorry we cannot see your sister, Miss

Dashwood,"



<P>said Miss Steele.&nbsp; "I am sorry she is not well--"



<P>for Marianne had left the room on their arrival.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You are very good.&nbsp; My sister will be

equally



<P>sorry to miss the pleasure of seeing you; but she has



<P>been very much plagued lately with nervous head-aches,



<P>which make her unfit for company or conversation."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, dear, that is a great pity! but such old



<P>friends as Lucy and me!--I think she might see US;



<P>and I am sure we would not speak a word."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor, with great civility, declined the proposal.



<P>Her sister was perhaps laid down upon the bed, or in her



<P>dressing gown, and therefore not able to come to them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, if that's all," cried Miss Steele, "we

can



<P>just as well go and see HER."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor began to find this impertinence too

much for



<P>her temper; but she was saved the trouble of checking it,



<P>by Lucy's sharp reprimand, which now, as on many occasions,



<P>though it did not give much sweetness to the manners



<P>of one sister, was of advantage in governing those of



<P>the other.



<P>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 26</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor could not find herself in the carriage

with Mrs. Jennings,



<P>and beginning a journey to London under her protection,



<P>and as her guest, without wondering at her own situation,



<P>so short had their acquaintance with that lady been,



<P>so wholly unsuited were they in age and disposition,



<P>and so many had been her objections against such a measure



<P>only a few days before! But these objections had all,



<P>with that happy ardour of youth which Marianne and her mother



<P>equally shared, been overcome or overlooked; and Elinor,



<P>in spite of every occasional doubt of Willoughby's constancy,



<P>could not witness the rapture of delightful expectation



<P>which filled the whole soul and beamed in the eyes



<P>of Marianne, without feeling how blank was her own prospect,



<P>how cheerless her own state of mind in the comparison,



<P>and how gladly she would engage in the solicitude of



<P>Marianne's situation to have the same animating object



<P>in view, the same possibility of hope.&nbsp; A short, a very



<P>short time however must now decide what Willoughby's



<P>intentions were; in all probability he was already in town.



<P>Marianne's eagerness to be gone declared her dependence



<P>on finding him there; and Elinor was resolved not only upon



<P>gaining every new light as to his character which her



<P>own observation or the intelligence of others could give her,



<P>but likewise upon watching his behaviour to her sister



<P>with such zealous attention, as to ascertain what he was



<P>and what he meant, before many meetings had taken place.



<P>Should the result of her observations be unfavourable,



<P>she was determined at all events to open the eyes



<P>of her sister; should it be otherwise, her exertions



<P>would be of a different nature--she must then learn



<P>to avoid every selfish comparison, and banish every regret



<P>which might lessen her satisfaction in the happiness of Marianne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They were three days on their journey, and

Marianne's



<P>behaviour as they travelled was a happy specimen of what



<P>future complaisance and companionableness to Mrs. Jennings



<P>might be expected to be.&nbsp; She sat in silence almost all



<P>the way, wrapt in her own meditations, and scarcely ever



<P>voluntarily speaking, except when any object of picturesque



<P>beauty within their view drew from her an exclamation



<P>of delight exclusively addressed to her sister.&nbsp; To atone



<P>for this conduct therefore, Elinor took immediate possession



<P>of the post of civility which she had assigned herself,



<P>behaved with the greatest attention to Mrs. Jennings,



<P>talked with her, laughed with her, and listened to her



<P>whenever she could; and Mrs. Jennings on her side



<P>treated them both with all possible kindness, was solicitous



<P>on every occasion for their ease and enjoyment, and only



<P>disturbed that she could not make them choose their own



<P>dinners at the inn, nor extort a confession of their



<P>preferring salmon to cod, or boiled fowls to veal cutlets.



<P>They reached town by three o'clock the third day, glad to



<P>be released, after such a journey, from the confinement



<P>of a carriage, and ready to enjoy all the luxury of a good fire.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The house was handsome, and handsomely fitted

up,



<P>and the young ladies were immediately put in possession



<P>of a very comfortable apartment.&nbsp; It had formerly



<P>been Charlotte's, and over the mantelpiece still hung



<P>a landscape in coloured silks of her performance,



<P>in proof of her having spent seven years at a great school



<P>in town to some effect.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As dinner was not to be ready in less than

two



<P>hours from their arrival, Elinor determined to employ



<P>the interval in writing to her mother, and sat down for



<P>that purpose.&nbsp; In a few moments Marianne did the same.



<P>"I am writing home, Marianne," said Elinor; "had not you



<P>better defer your letter for a day or two?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am NOT going to write to my mother,"



<P>replied Marianne, hastily, and as if wishing to avoid



<P>any farther inquiry.&nbsp; Elinor said no more; it immediately



<P>struck her that she must then be writing to Willoughby;



<P>and the conclusion which as instantly followed was,



<P>that, however mysteriously they might wish to conduct



<P>the affair, they must be engaged.&nbsp; This conviction,



<P>though not entirely satisfactory, gave her pleasure,



<P>and she continued her letter with greater alacrity.



<P>Marianne's was finished in a very few minutes;



<P>in length it could be no more than a note; it was then



<P>folded up, sealed, and directed with eager rapidity.



<P>Elinor thought she could distinguish a large W in



<P>the direction; and no sooner was it complete than Marianne,



<P>ringing the bell, requested the footman who answered it



<P>to get that letter conveyed for her to the two-penny post.



<P>This decided the matter at once.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Her spirits still continued very high; but

there



<P>was a flutter in them which prevented their giving much



<P>pleasure to her sister, and this agitation increased as



<P>the evening drew on.&nbsp; She could scarcely eat any dinner,



<P>and when they afterwards returned to the drawing room,



<P>seemed anxiously listening to the sound of every carriage.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; It was a great satisfaction to Elinor that

Mrs. Jennings,



<P>by being much engaged in her own room, could see little



<P>of what was passing.&nbsp; The tea things were brought in,



<P>and already had Marianne been disappointed more than once



<P>by a rap at a neighbouring door, when a loud one was suddenly



<P>heard which could not be mistaken for one at any other house,



<P>Elinor felt secure of its announcing Willoughby's approach,



<P>and Marianne, starting up, moved towards the door.



<P>Every thing was silent; this could not be borne many seconds;



<P>she opened the door, advanced a few steps towards the stairs,



<P>and after listening half a minute, returned into the room



<P>in all the agitation which a conviction of having heard



<P>him would naturally produce; in the ecstasy of her



<P>feelings at that instant she could not help exclaiming,



<P>"Oh, Elinor, it is Willoughby, indeed it is!" and seemed



<P>almost ready to throw herself into his arms, when Colonel



<P>Brandon appeared.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; It was too great a shock to be borne with calmness,



<P>and she immediately left the room.&nbsp; Elinor was disappointed too;



<P>but at the same time her regard for Colonel Brandon ensured



<P>his welcome with her; and she felt particularly hurt that



<P>a man so partial to her sister should perceive that she



<P>experienced nothing but grief and disappointment in seeing him.



<P>She instantly saw that it was not unnoticed by him,



<P>that he even observed Marianne as she quitted the room,



<P>with such astonishment and concern, as hardly left him



<P>the recollection of what civility demanded towards herself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Is your sister ill?" said he.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor answered in some distress that she was,



<P>and then talked of head-aches, low spirits, and over fatigues;



<P>and of every thing to which she could decently attribute



<P>her sister's behaviour.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He heard her with the most earnest attention,



<P>but seeming to recollect himself, said no more on the subject,



<P>and began directly to speak of his pleasure at seeing them



<P>in London, making the usual inquiries about their journey,



<P>and the friends they had left behind.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In this calm kind of way, with very little

interest



<P>on either side, they continued to talk, both of them out



<P>of spirits, and the thoughts of both engaged elsewhere.



<P>Elinor wished very much to ask whether Willoughby were



<P>then in town, but she was afraid of giving him pain



<P>by any enquiry after his rival; and at length, by way



<P>of saying something, she asked if he had been in London



<P>ever since she had seen him last.&nbsp; "Yes," he replied,



<P>with some embarrassment, "almost ever since; I have been



<P>once or twice at Delaford for a few days, but it has never



<P>been in my power to return to Barton."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This, and the manner in which it was said,



<P>immediately brought back to her remembrance all the



<P>circumstances of his quitting that place, with the



<P>uneasiness and suspicions they had caused to Mrs. Jennings,



<P>and she was fearful that her question had implied



<P>much more curiosity on the subject than she had ever felt.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings soon came in.&nbsp; "Oh! Colonel,"

said she,



<P>with her usual noisy cheerfulness, "I am monstrous glad



<P>to see you--sorry I could not come before--beg your



<P>pardon, but I have been forced to look about me a little,



<P>and settle my matters; for it is a long while since I



<P>have been at home, and you know one has always a world



<P>of little odd things to do after one has been away for



<P>any time; and then I have had Cartwright to settle with--



<P>Lord, I have been as busy as a bee ever since dinner!



<P>But pray, Colonel, how came you to conjure out that I should



<P>be in town today?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I had the pleasure of hearing it at Mr. Palmer's,



<P>where I have been dining."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, you did; well, and how do they all do

at their



<P>house?&nbsp; How does Charlotte do?&nbsp; I warrant you she is a fine



<P>size by this time."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Mrs. Palmer appeared quite well, and I am

commissioned



<P>to tell you, that you will certainly see her to-morrow."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Ay, to be sure, I thought as much.&nbsp; Well,

Colonel,



<P>I have brought two young ladies with me, you see--that is,



<P>you see but one of them now, but there is another somewhere.



<P>Your friend, Miss Marianne, too--which you will not be



<P>sorry to hear.&nbsp; I do not know what you and Mr. Willoughby



<P>will do between you about her.&nbsp; Ay, it is a fine thing



<P>to be young and handsome.&nbsp; Well! I was young once, but I



<P>never was very handsome--worse luck for me.&nbsp; However, I got



<P>a very good husband, and I don't know what the greatest



<P>beauty can do more.&nbsp; Ah! poor man! he has been dead



<P>these eight years and better.&nbsp; But Colonel, where have



<P>you been to since we parted?&nbsp; And how does your business



<P>go on?&nbsp; Come, come, let's have no secrets among friends."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He replied with his accustomary mildness to

all



<P>her inquiries, but without satisfying her in any.



<P>Elinor now began to make the tea, and Marianne was



<P>obliged to appear again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; After her entrance, Colonel Brandon became



<P>more thoughtful and silent than he had been before,



<P>and Mrs. Jennings could not prevail on him to stay long.



<P>No other visitor appeared that evening, and the ladies



<P>were unanimous in agreeing to go early to bed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne rose the next morning with recovered

spirits



<P>and happy looks.&nbsp; The disappointment of the evening before



<P>seemed forgotten in the expectation of what was to happen



<P>that day.&nbsp; They had not long finished their breakfast before



<P>Mrs. Palmer's barouche stopped at the door, and in a few



<P>minutes she came laughing into the room: so delighted



<P>to see them all, that it was hard to say whether she



<P>received most pleasure from meeting her mother or the Miss



<P>Dashwoods again.&nbsp; So surprised at their coming to town,



<P>though it was what she had rather expected all along;



<P>so angry at their accepting her mother's invitation



<P>after having declined her own, though at the same time



<P>she would never have forgiven them if they had not come!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Mr. Palmer will be so happy to see you,"



<P>said she; "What do you think he said when he heard



<P>of your coming with Mamma?&nbsp; I forget what it was now,



<P>but it was something so droll!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; After an hour or two spent in what her mother

called



<P>comfortable chat, or in other words, in every variety of inquiry



<P>concerning all their acquaintance on Mrs. Jennings's side,



<P>and in laughter without cause on Mrs. Palmer's, it was



<P>proposed by the latter that they should all accompany



<P>her to some shops where she had business that morning,



<P>to which Mrs. Jennings and Elinor readily consented,



<P>as having likewise some purchases to make themselves;



<P>and Marianne, though declining it at first was induced



<P>to go likewise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Wherever they went, she was evidently always

on



<P>the watch.&nbsp; In Bond Street especially, where much of



<P>their business lay, her eyes were in constant inquiry;



<P>and in whatever shop the party were engaged, her mind was



<P>equally abstracted from every thing actually before them,



<P>from all that interested and occupied the others.



<P>Restless and dissatisfied every where, her sister could



<P>never obtain her opinion of any article of purchase,



<P>however it might equally concern them both: she received



<P>no pleasure from anything; was only impatient to be at



<P>home again, and could with difficulty govern her vexation



<P>at the tediousness of Mrs. Palmer, whose eye was caught



<P>by every thing pretty, expensive, or new; who was wild



<P>to buy all, could determine on none, and dawdled away her



<P>time in rapture and indecision.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; It was late in the morning before they returned

home;



<P>and no sooner had they entered the house than Marianne flew



<P>eagerly up stairs, and when Elinor followed, she found



<P>her turning from the table with a sorrowful countenance,



<P>which declared that no Willoughby had been there.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Has no letter been left here for me since

we went out?"



<P>said she to the footman who then entered with the parcels.



<P>She was answered in the negative.&nbsp; "Are you quite sure



<P>of it?" she replied.&nbsp; "Are you certain that no servant,



<P>no porter has left any letter or note?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The man replied that none had.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How very odd!" said she, in a low and disappointed



<P>voice, as she turned away to the window.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How odd, indeed!" repeated Elinor within herself,



<P>regarding her sister with uneasiness.&nbsp; "If she had not



<P>known him to be in town she would not have written to him,



<P>as she did; she would have written to Combe Magna;



<P>and if he is in town, how odd that he should neither



<P>come nor write!&nbsp; Oh! my dear mother, you must be wrong



<P>in permitting an engagement between a daughter so young,



<P>a man so little known, to be carried on in so doubtful,



<P>so mysterious a manner!&nbsp; I long to inquire; and how will MY



<P>interference be borne."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She determined, after some consideration, that

if



<P>appearances continued many days longer as unpleasant as they



<P>now were, she would represent in the strongest manner



<P>to her mother the necessity of some serious enquiry into the affair.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Palmer and two elderly ladies of Mrs.

Jennings's



<P>intimate acquaintance, whom she had met and invited



<P>in the morning, dined with them.&nbsp; The former left them



<P>soon after tea to fulfill her evening engagements;



<P>and Elinor was obliged to assist in making a whist table



<P>for the others.&nbsp; Marianne was of no use on these occasions,



<P>as she would never learn the game; but though her time



<P>was therefore at her own disposal, the evening was by no



<P>means more productive of pleasure to her than to Elinor,



<P>for it was spent in all the anxiety of expectation and the



<P>pain of disappointment.&nbsp; She sometimes endeavoured for a



<P>few minutes to read; but the book was soon thrown aside,



<P>and she returned to the more interesting employment



<P>of walking backwards and forwards across the room,



<P>pausing for a moment whenever she came to the window,



<P>in hopes of distinguishing the long-expected rap.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 27</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "If this open weather holds much longer," said

Mrs. Jennings,



<P>when they met at breakfast the following morning,



<P>"Sir John will not like leaving Barton next week;



<P>'tis a sad thing for sportsmen to lose a day's pleasure.



<P>Poor souls!&nbsp; I always pity them when they do; they seem



<P>to take it so much to heart."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "That is true," cried Marianne, in a cheerful

voice,



<P>and walking to the window as she spoke, to examine the day.



<P>"I had not thought of that.&nbsp; This weather will keep many



<P>sportsmen in the country."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; It was a lucky recollection, all her good spirits

were



<P>restored by it.&nbsp; "It is charming weather for THEM indeed,"



<P>she continued, as she sat down to the breakfast table



<P>with a happy countenance.&nbsp; "How much they must enjoy



<P>it! But" (with a little return of anxiety) "it cannot



<P>be expected to last long.&nbsp; At this time of the year,



<P>and after such a series of rain, we shall certainly



<P>have very little more of it.&nbsp; Frosts will soon set in,



<P>and in all probability with severity.&nbsp; In another day



<P>or two perhaps; this extreme mildness can hardly last



<P>longer--nay, perhaps it may freeze tonight!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "At any rate," said Elinor, wishing to prevent



<P>Mrs. Jennings from seeing her sister's thoughts as clearly



<P>as she did, "I dare say we shall have Sir John and Lady



<P>Middleton in town by the end of next week."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Ay, my dear, I'll warrant you we do.&nbsp;

Mary always



<P>has her own way."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And now," silently conjectured Elinor, "she

will



<P>write to Combe by this day's post."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; But if she DID, the letter was written and

sent away



<P>with a privacy which eluded all her watchfulness to ascertain



<P>the fact.&nbsp; Whatever the truth of it might be, and far



<P>as Elinor was from feeling thorough contentment about it,



<P>yet while she saw Marianne in spirits, she could not be



<P>very uncomfortable herself.&nbsp; And Marianne was in spirits;



<P>happy in the mildness of the weather, and still happier



<P>in her expectation of a frost.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The morning was chiefly spent in leaving cards

at



<P>the houses of Mrs. Jennings's acquaintance to inform



<P>them of her being in town; and Marianne was all the time



<P>busy in observing the direction of the wind, watching the



<P>variations of the sky and imagining an alteration in the air.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Don't you find it colder than it was in the

morning,



<P>Elinor? There seems to me a very decided difference.



<P>I can hardly keep my hands warm even in my muff.&nbsp; It was



<P>not so yesterday, I think.&nbsp; The clouds seem parting too,



<P>the sun will be out in a moment, and we shall have a



<P>clear afternoon."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor was alternately diverted and pained;



<P>but Marianne persevered, and saw every night in the



<P>brightness of the fire, and every morning in the appearance



<P>of the atmosphere, the certain symptoms of approaching frost.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The Miss Dashwoods had no greater reason to

be



<P>dissatisfied with Mrs. Jennings's style of living, and set



<P>of acquaintance, than with her behaviour to themselves,



<P>which was invariably kind.&nbsp; Every thing in her household



<P>arrangements was conducted on the most liberal plan,



<P>and excepting a few old city friends, whom, to Lady



<P>Middleton's regret, she had never dropped, she visited



<P>no one to whom an introduction could at all discompose



<P>the feelings of her young companions.&nbsp; Pleased to find



<P>herself more comfortably situated in that particular than



<P>she had expected, Elinor was very willing to compound



<P>for the want of much real enjoyment from any of their



<P>evening parties, which, whether at home or abroad,



<P>formed only for cards, could have little to amuse her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Colonel Brandon, who had a general invitation



<P>to the house, was with them almost every day; he came



<P>to look at Marianne and talk to Elinor, who often derived



<P>more satisfaction from conversing with him than from any



<P>other daily occurrence, but who saw at the same time



<P>with much concern his continued regard for her sister.



<P>She feared it was a strengthening regard.&nbsp; It grieved her



<P>to see the earnestness with which he often watched Marianne,



<P>and his spirits were certainly worse than when at Barton.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; About a week after their arrival, it became



<P>certain that Willoughby was also arrived.&nbsp; His card



<P>was on the table when they came in from the morning's drive.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Good God!" cried Marianne, "he has been here

while



<P>we were out."&nbsp; Elinor, rejoiced to be assured of his



<P>being in London, now ventured to say, "Depend upon it,



<P>he will call again tomorrow."&nbsp; But Marianne seemed



<P>hardly to hear her, and on Mrs. Jenning's entrance,



<P>escaped with the precious card.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This event, while it raised the spirits of

Elinor,



<P>restored to those of her sister all, and more than all,



<P>their former agitation.&nbsp; From this moment her mind was



<P>never quiet; the expectation of seeing him every hour



<P>of the day, made her unfit for any thing.&nbsp; She insisted



<P>on being left behind, the next morning, when the others



<P>went out.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor's thoughts were full of what might be

passing



<P>in Berkeley Street during their absence; but a moment's



<P>glance at her sister when they returned was enough to



<P>inform her, that Willoughby had paid no second visit there.



<P>A note was just then brought in, and laid on the table,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "For me!" cried Marianne, stepping hastily

forward.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, ma'am, for my mistress."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; But Marianne, not convinced, took it instantly

up.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is indeed for Mrs. Jennings; how provoking!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You are expecting a letter, then?" said Elinor,



<P>unable to be longer silent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes, a little--not much."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; After a short pause.&nbsp; "You have no confidence



<P>in me, Marianne."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Nay, Elinor, this reproach from YOU--you who

have



<P>confidence in no one!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Me!" returned Elinor in some confusion; "indeed,



<P>Marianne, I have nothing to tell."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Nor I," answered Marianne with energy, "our

situations



<P>then are alike.&nbsp; We have neither of us any thing to tell;



<P>you, because you do not communicate, and I, because



<P>I conceal nothing."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor, distressed by this charge of reserve

in herself,



<P>which she was not at liberty to do away, knew not how,



<P>under such circumstances, to press for greater openness



<P>in Marianne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings soon appeared, and the note being



<P>given her, she read it aloud.&nbsp; It was from Lady Middleton,



<P>announcing their arrival in Conduit Street the night before,



<P>and requesting the company of her mother and cousins



<P>the following evening.&nbsp; Business on Sir John's part,



<P>and a violent cold on her own, prevented their calling



<P>in Berkeley Street.&nbsp; The invitation was accepted;



<P>but when the hour of appointment drew near, necessary as



<P>it was in common civility to Mrs. Jennings, that they



<P>should both attend her on such a visit, Elinor had some



<P>difficulty in persuading her sister to go, for still



<P>she had seen nothing of Willoughby; and therefore was



<P>not more indisposed for amusement abroad, than unwilling



<P>to run the risk of his calling again in her absence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor found, when the evening was over,



<P>that disposition is not materially altered by a change



<P>of abode, for although scarcely settled in town,



<P>Sir John had contrived to collect around him, nearly twenty



<P>young people, and to amuse them with a ball.&nbsp; This was



<P>an affair, however, of which Lady Middleton did not approve.



<P>In the country, an unpremeditated dance was very allowable;



<P>but in London, where the reputation of elegance was more



<P>important and less easily attained, it was risking too much



<P>for the gratification of a few girls, to have it known that



<P>Lady Middleton had given a small dance of eight or nine couple,



<P>with two violins, and a mere side-board collation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mr. and Mrs. Palmer were of the party; from

the former,



<P>whom they had not seen before since their arrival in town,



<P>as he was careful to avoid the appearance of any attention



<P>to his mother-in-law, and therefore never came near her,



<P>they received no mark of recognition on their entrance.



<P>He looked at them slightly, without seeming to know



<P>who they were, and merely nodded to Mrs. Jennings from



<P>the other side of the room.&nbsp; Marianne gave one glance



<P>round the apartment as she entered: it was enough--HE



<P>was not there--and she sat down, equally ill-disposed



<P>to receive or communicate pleasure.&nbsp; After they had been



<P>assembled about an hour, Mr. Palmer sauntered towards



<P>the Miss Dashwoods to express his surprise on seeing them



<P>in town, though Colonel Brandon had been first informed



<P>of their arrival at his house, and he had himself said



<P>something very droll on hearing that they were to come.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I thought you were both in Devonshire," said

he.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Did you?" replied Elinor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "When do you go back again?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I do not know." And thus ended their discourse.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Never had Marianne been so unwilling to dance



<P>in her life, as she was that evening, and never so much



<P>fatigued by the exercise.&nbsp; She complained of it



<P>as they returned to Berkeley Street.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Aye, aye," said Mrs. Jennings, "we know the

reason



<P>of all that very well; if a certain person who shall



<P>be nameless, had been there, you would not have been a



<P>bit tired: and to say the truth it was not very pretty



<P>of him not to give you the meeting when he was invited."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Invited!" cried Marianne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "So my daughter Middleton told me, for it seems

Sir



<P>John met him somewhere in the street this morning."



<P>Marianne said no more, but looked exceedingly hurt.



<P>Impatient in this situation to be doing something



<P>that might lead to her sister's relief, Elinor resolved



<P>to write the next morning to her mother, and hoped



<P>by awakening her fears for the health of Marianne,



<P>to procure those inquiries which had been so long delayed;



<P>and she was still more eagerly bent on this measure



<P>by perceiving after breakfast on the morrow, that Marianne



<P>was again writing to Willoughby, for she could not suppose



<P>it to be to any other person.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; About the middle of the day, Mrs. Jennings

went out by



<P>herself on business, and Elinor began her letter directly,



<P>while Marianne, too restless for employment, too anxious



<P>for conversation, walked from one window to the other,



<P>or sat down by the fire in melancholy meditation.



<P>Elinor was very earnest in her application to her mother,



<P>relating all that had passed, her suspicions of



<P>Willoughby's inconstancy, urging her by every plea



<P>of duty and affection to demand from Marianne an account



<P>of her real situation with respect to him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Her letter was scarcely finished, when a rap



<P>foretold a visitor, and Colonel Brandon was announced.



<P>Marianne, who had seen him from the window, and who hated



<P>company of any kind, left the room before he entered it.



<P>He looked more than usually grave, and though expressing



<P>satisfaction at finding Miss Dashwood alone, as if he



<P>had somewhat in particular to tell her, sat for some



<P>time without saying a word.&nbsp; Elinor, persuaded that he



<P>had some communication to make in which her sister



<P>was concerned, impatiently expected its opening.



<P>It was not the first time of her feeling the same kind



<P>of conviction; for, more than once before, beginning with



<P>the observation of "your sister looks unwell to-day,"



<P>or "your sister seems out of spirits," he had appeared



<P>on the point, either of disclosing, or of inquiring,



<P>something particular about her.&nbsp; After a pause of several



<P>minutes, their silence was broken, by his asking her



<P>in a voice of some agitation, when he was to congratulate



<P>her on the acquisition of a brother?&nbsp; Elinor was not



<P>prepared for such a question, and having no answer ready,



<P>was obliged to adopt the simple and common expedient,



<P>of asking what he meant?&nbsp; He tried to smile as he replied,



<P>"your sister's engagement to Mr. Willoughby is very generally known."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It cannot be generally known," returned Elinor,



<P>"for her own family do not know it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He looked surprised and said, "I beg your pardon,



<P>I am afraid my inquiry has been impertinent; but I had not



<P>supposed any secrecy intended, as they openly correspond,



<P>and their marriage is universally talked of."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How can that be? By whom can you have heard



<P>it mentioned?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "By many--by some of whom you know nothing,

by others



<P>with whom you are most intimate, Mrs. Jennings, Mrs. Palmer,



<P>and the Middletons.&nbsp; But still I might not have believed it,



<P>for where the mind is perhaps rather unwilling to



<P>be convinced, it will always find something to support



<P>its doubts, if I had not, when the servant let me in today,



<P>accidentally seen a letter in his hand, directed to



<P>Mr. Willoughby in your sister's writing.&nbsp; I came to inquire,



<P>but I was convinced before I could ask the question.



<P>Is every thing finally settled?&nbsp; Is it impossible to-?



<P>But I have no right, and I could have no chance of succeeding.



<P>Excuse me, Miss Dashwood.&nbsp; I believe I have been wrong



<P>in saying so much, but I hardly know what to do, and on



<P>your prudence I have the strongest dependence.&nbsp; Tell me



<P>that it is all absolutely resolved on, that any attempt,



<P>that in short concealment, if concealment be possible,



<P>is all that remains."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; These words, which conveyed to Elinor a direct

avowal



<P>of his love for her sister, affected her very much.



<P>She was not immediately able to say anything, and even



<P>when her spirits were recovered, she debated for a short



<P>time, on the answer it would be most proper to give.



<P>The real state of things between Willoughby and her sister



<P>was so little known to herself, that in endeavouring



<P>to explain it, she might be as liable to say too much



<P>as too little.&nbsp; Yet as she was convinced that Marianne's



<P>affection for Willoughby, could leave no hope of Colonel



<P>Brandon's success, whatever the event of that affection



<P>might be, and at the same time wished to shield her conduct



<P>from censure, she thought it most prudent and kind,



<P>after some consideration, to say more than she really knew



<P>or believed.&nbsp; She acknowledged, therefore, that though



<P>she had never been informed by themselves of the terms



<P>on which they stood with each other, of their mutual



<P>affection she had no doubt, and of their correspondence



<P>she was not astonished to hear.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He listened to her with silent attention, and

on



<P>her ceasing to speak, rose directly from his seat,



<P>and after saying in a voice of emotion, "to your sister



<P>I wish all imaginable happiness; to Willoughby that he



<P>may endeavour to deserve her,"--took leave, and went away.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor derived no comfortable feelings from

this



<P>conversation, to lessen the uneasiness of her mind on



<P>other points; she was left, on the contrary, with a



<P>melancholy impression of Colonel Brandon's unhappiness,



<P>and was prevented even from wishing it removed,



<P>by her anxiety for the very event that must confirm it.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter33</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 33</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; After some opposition, Marianne yielded to

her



<P>sister's entreaties, and consented to go out with her



<P>and Mrs. Jennings one morning for half an hour. She



<P>expressly conditioned, however, for paying no visits,



<P>and would do no more than accompany them to Gray's in



<P>Sackville Street, where Elinor was carrying on a negotiation



<P>for the exchange of a few old-fashioned jewels of her mother.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When they stopped at the door, Mrs. Jennings

recollected



<P>that there was a lady at the other end of the street



<P>on whom she ought to call; and as she had no business



<P>at Gray's, it was resolved, that while her young friends



<P>transacted their's, she should pay her visit and



<P>return for them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; On ascending the stairs, the Miss Dashwoods

found



<P>so many people before them in the room, that there was



<P>not a person at liberty to tend to their orders; and they



<P>were obliged to wait.&nbsp; All that could be done was, to sit



<P>down at that end of the counter which seemed to promise the



<P>quickest succession; one gentleman only was standing there,



<P>and it is probable that Elinor was not without hope



<P>of exciting his politeness to a quicker despatch.



<P>But the correctness of his eye, and the delicacy



<P>of his taste, proved to be beyond his politeness.



<P>He was giving orders for a toothpick-case for himself,



<P>and till its size, shape, and ornaments were determined,



<P>all of which, after examining and debating for a quarter



<P>of an hour over every toothpick-case in the shop,



<P>were finally arranged by his own inventive fancy, he had



<P>no leisure to bestow any other attention on the two ladies,



<P>than what was comprised in three or four very broad stares;



<P>a kind of notice which served to imprint on Elinor



<P>the remembrance of a person and face, of strong,



<P>natural, sterling insignificance, though adorned in



<P>the first style of fashion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne was spared from the troublesome feelings



<P>of contempt and resentment, on this impertinent examination



<P>of their features, and on the puppyism of his manner



<P>in deciding on all the different horrors of the different



<P>toothpick-cases presented to his inspection, by remaining



<P>unconscious of it all; for she was as well able to collect



<P>her thoughts within herself, and be as ignorant of what was



<P>passing around her, in Mr. Gray's shop, as in her own bedroom.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; At last the affair was decided.&nbsp; The ivory,



<P>the gold, and the pearls, all received their appointment,



<P>and the gentleman having named the last day on which his



<P>existence could be continued without the possession of the



<P>toothpick-case, drew on his gloves with leisurely care,



<P>and bestowing another glance on the Miss Dashwoods, but such



<P>a one as seemed rather to demand than express admiration,



<P>walked off with a happy air of real conceit and affected indifference.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor lost no time in bringing her business

forward,



<P>was on the point of concluding it, when another gentleman



<P>presented himself at her side.&nbsp; She turned her eyes towards



<P>his face, and found him with some surprise to be her brother.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Their affection and pleasure in meeting was

just enough



<P>to make a very creditable appearance in Mr. Gray's shop.



<P>John Dashwood was really far from being sorry to see



<P>his sisters again; it rather gave them satisfaction;



<P>and his inquiries after their mother were respectful



<P>and attentive.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor found that he and Fanny had been in

town



<P>two days.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I wished very much to call upon you yesterday,"



<P>said he, "but it was impossible, for we were obliged



<P>to take Harry to see the wild beasts at Exeter Exchange;



<P>and we spent the rest of the day with Mrs. Ferrars.



<P>Harry was vastly pleased.&nbsp; THIS morning I had fully intended



<P>to call on you, if I could possibly find a spare half hour,



<P>but one has always so much to do on first coming to town.



<P>I am come here to bespeak Fanny a seal.&nbsp; But tomorrow I



<P>think I shall certainly be able to call in Berkeley Street,



<P>and be introduced to your friend Mrs. Jennings.



<P>I understand she is a woman of very good fortune.



<P>And the Middletons too, you must introduce me to THEM.



<P>As my mother-in-law's relations, I shall be happy to show



<P>them every respect.&nbsp; They are excellent neighbours to you in



<P>the country, I understand."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Excellent indeed.&nbsp; Their attention to

our comfort,



<P>their friendliness in every particular, is more than I



<P>can express."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am extremely glad to hear it, upon my word;



<P>extremely glad indeed.&nbsp; But so it ought to be; they are



<P>people of large fortune, they are related to you, and



<P>every civility and accommodation that can serve to make



<P>your situation pleasant might be reasonably expected.



<P>And so you are most comfortably settled in your little cottage



<P>and want for nothing!&nbsp; Edward brought us a most charming



<P>account of the place: the most complete thing of its kind,



<P>he said, that ever was, and you all seemed to enjoy it beyond



<P>any thing.&nbsp; It was a great satisfaction to us to hear it,



<P>I assure you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor did feel a little ashamed of her brother;



<P>and was not sorry to be spared the necessity of answering him,



<P>by the arrival of Mrs. Jennings's servant, who came to tell



<P>her that his mistress waited for them at the door.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mr. Dashwood attended them down stairs, was

introduced



<P>to Mrs. Jennings at the door of her carriage, and repeating



<P>his hope of being able to call on them the next day,



<P>took leave.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; His visit was duly paid.&nbsp; He came with

a pretence at



<P>an apology from their sister-in-law, for not coming too;



<P>"but she was so much engaged with her mother, that really



<P>she had no leisure for going any where."&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings,



<P>however, assured him directly, that she should not stand



<P>upon ceremony, for they were all cousins, or something



<P>like it, and she should certainly wait on Mrs. John



<P>Dashwood very soon, and bring her sisters to see her.



<P>His manners to THEM, though calm, were perfectly kind;



<P>to Mrs. Jennings, most attentively civil; and on Colonel



<P>Brandon's coming in soon after himself, he eyed him with a



<P>curiosity which seemed to say, that he only wanted to know



<P>him to be rich, to be equally civil to HIM.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; After staying with them half an hour, he asked



<P>Elinor to walk with him to Conduit Street, and introduce



<P>him to Sir John and Lady Middleton.&nbsp; The weather was



<P>remarkably fine, and she readily consented.&nbsp; As soon



<P>as they were out of the house, his enquiries began.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Who is Colonel Brandon?&nbsp; Is he a man

of fortune?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes; he has very good property in Dorsetshire."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am glad of it.&nbsp; He seems a most gentlemanlike

man;



<P>and I think, Elinor, I may congratulate you on the prospect



<P>of a very respectable establishment in life."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Me, brother! what do you mean?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "He likes you.&nbsp; I observed him narrowly,

and am



<P>convinced of it.&nbsp; What is the amount of his fortune?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I believe about two thousand a year."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Two thousand a-year;" and then working himself



<P>up to a pitch of enthusiastic generosity, he added,



<P>"Elinor, I wish with all my heart it were TWICE as much,



<P>for your sake."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Indeed I believe you," replied Elinor; "but

I am



<P>very sure that Colonel Brandon has not the smallest wish



<P>of marrying ME."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You are mistaken, Elinor; you are very much

mistaken.



<P>A very little trouble on your side secures him.



<P>Perhaps just at present he may be undecided; the smallness



<P>of your fortune may make him hang back; his friends



<P>may all advise him against it.&nbsp; But some of those little



<P>attentions and encouragements which ladies can so easily



<P>give will fix him, in spite of himself.&nbsp; And there can be



<P>no reason why you should not try for him.&nbsp; It is not to be



<P>supposed that any prior attachment on your side--in short,



<P>you know as to an attachment of that kind, it is quite



<P>out of the question, the objections are insurmountable--



<P>you have too much sense not to see all that.&nbsp; Colonel Brandon



<P>must be the man; and no civility shall be wanting on



<P>my part to make him pleased with you and your family.



<P>It is a match that must give universal satisfaction.



<P>In short, it is a kind of thing that"--lowering his voice



<P>to an important whisper--"will be exceedingly welcome



<P>to ALL PARTIES." Recollecting himself, however, he added,



<P>"That is, I mean to say--your friends are all truly



<P>anxious to see you well settled; Fanny particularly,



<P>for she has your interest very much at heart, I assure you.



<P>And her mother too, Mrs. Ferrars, a very good-natured woman,



<P>I am sure it would give her great pleasure; she said as much



<P>the other day."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor would not vouchsafe any answer.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It would be something remarkable, now," he

continued,



<P>"something droll, if Fanny should have a brother and I



<P>a sister settling at the same time.&nbsp; And yet it is not



<P>very unlikely."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Is Mr. Edward Ferrars," said Elinor, with

resolution,



<P>"going to be married?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is not actually settled, but there is such



<P>a thing in agitation.&nbsp; He has a most excellent mother.



<P>Mrs. Ferrars, with the utmost liberality, will come forward,



<P>and settle on him a thousand a year, if the match



<P>takes place.&nbsp; The lady is the Hon. Miss Morton, only daughter



<P>of the late Lord Morton, with thirty thousand pounds.



<P>A very desirable connection on both sides, and I have not



<P>a doubt of its taking place in time.&nbsp; A thousand a-year



<P>is a great deal for a mother to give away, to make over



<P>for ever; but Mrs. Ferrars has a noble spirit.&nbsp; To give



<P>you another instance of her liberality:--The other day,



<P>as soon as we came to town, aware that money could



<P>not be very plenty with us just now, she put bank-notes



<P>into Fanny's hands to the amount of two hundred pounds.



<P>And extremely acceptable it is, for we must live at a great



<P>expense while we are here."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He paused for her assent and compassion; and

she



<P>forced herself to say,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Your expenses both in town and country must

certainly



<P>be considerable; but your income is a large one."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Not so large, I dare say, as many people suppose.



<P>I do not mean to complain, however; it is undoubtedly



<P>a comfortable one, and I hope will in time be better.



<P>The enclosure of Norland Common, now carrying on,



<P>is a most serious drain.&nbsp; And then I have made a little



<P>purchase within this half year; East Kingham Farm,



<P>you must remember the place, where old Gibson used to live.



<P>The land was so very desirable for me in every respect,



<P>so immediately adjoining my own property, that I felt it



<P>my duty to buy it.&nbsp; I could not have answered it to my



<P>conscience to let it fall into any other hands.&nbsp; A man must



<P>pay for his convenience; and it HAS cost me a vast deal



<P>of money."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "More than you think it really and intrinsically

worth."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Why, I hope not that.&nbsp; I might have sold

it again,



<P>the next day, for more than I gave: but, with regard to the



<P>purchase-money, I might have been very unfortunate indeed;



<P>for the stocks were at that time so low, that if I had not



<P>happened to have the necessary sum in my banker's hands,



<P>I must have sold out to very great loss."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor could only smile.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Other great and inevitable expenses too we

have



<P>had on first coming to Norland.&nbsp; Our respected father,



<P>as you well know, bequeathed all the Stanhill effects



<P>that remained at Norland (and very valuable they were)



<P>to your mother.&nbsp; Far be it from me to repine at his



<P>doing so; he had an undoubted right to dispose of his



<P>own property as he chose, but, in consequence of it,



<P>we have been obliged to make large purchases of linen,



<P>china, &amp;c. to supply the place of what was taken away.



<P>You may guess, after all these expenses, how very far we



<P>must be from being rich, and how acceptable Mrs. Ferrars's



<P>kindness is."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Certainly," said Elinor; "and assisted by

her liberality,



<P>I hope you may yet live to be in easy circumstances."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Another year or two may do much towards it,"



<P>he gravely replied; "but however there is still a great



<P>deal to be done.&nbsp; There is not a stone laid of Fanny's



<P>green-house, and nothing but the plan of the flower-garden



<P>marked out."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Where is the green-house to be?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Upon the knoll behind the house.&nbsp; The

old



<P>walnut trees are all come down to make room for it.



<P>It will be a very fine object from many parts of the park,



<P>and the flower-garden will slope down just before it,



<P>and be exceedingly pretty.&nbsp; We have cleared away all the old



<P>thorns that grew in patches over the brow."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor kept her concern and her censure to

herself;



<P>and was very thankful that Marianne was not present,



<P>to share the provocation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Having now said enough to make his poverty

clear,



<P>and to do away the necessity of buying a pair of ear-rings



<P>for each of his sisters, in his next visit at Gray's



<P>his thoughts took a cheerfuller turn, and he began to



<P>congratulate Elinor on having such a friend as Mrs. Jennings.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "She seems a most valuable woman indeed--Her

house,



<P>her style of living, all bespeak an exceeding good income;



<P>and it is an acquaintance that has not only been



<P>of great use to you hitherto, but in the end may prove



<P>materially advantageous.--Her inviting you to town is



<P>certainly a vast thing in your favour; and indeed, it



<P>speaks altogether so great a regard for you, that in all



<P>probability when she dies you will not be forgotten.--



<P>She must have a great deal to leave."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Nothing at all, I should rather suppose; for

she has



<P>only her jointure, which will descend to her children."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But it is not to be imagined that she lives

up to



<P>her income.&nbsp; Few people of common prudence will do THAT;



<P>and whatever she saves, she will be able to dispose of."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And do you not think it more likely that she



<P>should leave it to her daughters, than to us?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Her daughters are both exceedingly well married,



<P>and therefore I cannot perceive the necessity of her



<P>remembering them farther.&nbsp; Whereas, in my opinion, by her



<P>taking so much notice of you, and treating you in this



<P>kind of way, she has given you a sort of claim on her



<P>future consideration, which a conscientious woman would



<P>not disregard.&nbsp; Nothing can be kinder than her behaviour;



<P>and she can hardly do all this, without being aware



<P>of the expectation it raises."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But she raises none in those most concerned.



<P>Indeed, brother, your anxiety for our welfare and prosperity



<P>carries you too far."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Why, to be sure," said he, seeming to recollect

himself,



<P>"people have little, have very little in their power.



<P>But, my dear Elinor, what is the matter with Marianne?--



<P>she looks very unwell, has lost her colour, and is grown



<P>quite thin.&nbsp; Is she ill?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "She is not well, she has had a nervous complaint



<P>on her for several weeks."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am sorry for that.&nbsp; At her time of

life,



<P>any thing of an illness destroys the bloom for ever!



<P>Her's has been a very short one!&nbsp; She was as handsome a girl



<P>last September, as I ever saw; and as likely to attract



<P>the man.&nbsp; There was something in her style of beauty,



<P>to please them particularly.&nbsp; I remember Fanny used to say



<P>that she would marry sooner and better than you did;



<P>not but what she is exceedingly fond of YOU, but so it



<P>happened to strike her.&nbsp; She will be mistaken, however.



<P>I question whether Marianne NOW, will marry a man worth



<P>more than five or six hundred a-year, at the utmost,



<P>and I am very much deceived if YOU do not do better.



<P>Dorsetshire!&nbsp; I know very little of Dorsetshire; but, my dear



<P>Elinor, I shall be exceedingly glad to know more of it;



<P>and I think I can answer for your having Fanny and myself



<P>among the earliest and best pleased of your visitors."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor tried very seriously to convince him

that



<P>there was no likelihood of her marrying Colonel Brandon;



<P>but it was an expectation of too much pleasure to himself



<P>to be relinquished, and he was really resolved on seeking



<P>an intimacy with that gentleman, and promoting the marriage



<P>by every possible attention.&nbsp; He had just compunction



<P>enough for having done nothing for his sisters himself,



<P>to be exceedingly anxious that everybody else should



<P>do a great deal; and an offer from Colonel Brandon,



<P>or a legacy from Mrs. Jennings, was the easiest means



<P>of atoning for his own neglect.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They were lucky enough to find Lady Middleton



<P>at home, and Sir John came in before their visit ended.



<P>Abundance of civilities passed on all sides.&nbsp; Sir John



<P>was ready to like anybody, and though Mr. Dashwood did



<P>not seem to know much about horses, he soon set him



<P>down as a very good-natured fellow: while Lady Middleton



<P>saw enough of fashion in his appearance to think his



<P>acquaintance worth having; and Mr. Dashwood went away



<P>delighted with both.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I shall have a charming account to carry



<P>to Fanny," said he, as he walked back with his sister.



<P>"Lady Middleton is really a most elegant woman!&nbsp; Such



<P>a woman as I am sure Fanny will be glad to know.



<P>And Mrs. Jennings too, an exceedingly well-behaved woman,



<P>though not so elegant as her daughter.&nbsp; Your sister need



<P>not have any scruple even of visiting HER, which, to say



<P>the truth, has been a little the case, and very naturally;



<P>for we only knew that Mrs. Jennings was the widow of a man



<P>who had got all his money in a low way; and Fanny and



<P>Mrs. Ferrars were both strongly prepossessed, that neither



<P>she nor her daughters were such kind of women as Fanny



<P>would like to associate with.&nbsp; But now I can carry her



<P>a most satisfactory account of both."



<P>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">
