<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 25</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Though Mrs. Jennings was in the habit of spending

a large



<P>portion of the year at the houses of her children and friends,



<P>she was not without a settled habitation of her own.



<P>Since the death of her husband, who had traded with success



<P>in a less elegant part of the town, she had resided every



<P>winter in a house in one of the streets near Portman Square.



<P>Towards this home, she began on the approach of January



<P>to turn her thoughts, and thither she one day abruptly,



<P>and very unexpectedly by them, asked the elder Misses



<P>Dashwood to accompany her.&nbsp; Elinor, without observing



<P>the varying complexion of her sister, and the animated look



<P>which spoke no indifference to the plan, immediately gave



<P>a grateful but absolute denial for both, in which she



<P>believed herself to be speaking their united inclinations.



<P>The reason alleged was their determined resolution



<P>of not leaving their mother at that time of the year.



<P>Mrs. Jennings received the refusal with some surprise,



<P>and repeated her invitation immediately.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, Lord! I am sure your mother can spare

you



<P>very well, and I DO beg you will favour me with



<P>your company, for I've quite set my heart upon it.



<P>Don't fancy that you will be any inconvenience to me,



<P>for I shan't put myself at all out of my way for you.



<P>It will only be sending Betty by the coach, and I



<P>hope I can afford THAT.&nbsp; We three shall be able to go



<P>very well in my chaise; and when we are in town,



<P>if you do not like to go wherever I do, well and good,



<P>you may always go with one of my daughters.&nbsp; I am sure



<P>your mother will not object to it; for I have had such



<P>good luck in getting my own children off my hands that she



<P>will think me a very fit person to have the charge of you;



<P>and if I don't get one of you at least well married



<P>before I have done with you, it shall not be my fault.



<P>I shall speak a good word for you to all the young men,



<P>you may depend upon it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have a notion," said Sir John, "that Miss

Marianne



<P>would not object to such a scheme, if her elder sister



<P>would come into it.&nbsp; It is very hard indeed that she



<P>should not have a little pleasure, because Miss Dashwood



<P>does not wish it.&nbsp; So I would advise you two, to set off



<P>for town, when you are tired of Barton, without saying



<P>a word to Miss Dashwood about it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Nay," cried Mrs. Jennings, "I am sure I shall

be



<P>monstrous glad of Miss Marianne's company, whether Miss



<P>Dashwood will go or not, only the more the merrier say I,



<P>and I thought it would be more comfortable for them to



<P>be together; because, if they got tired of me, they might talk



<P>to one another, and laugh at my old ways behind my back.



<P>But one or the other, if not both of them, I must have.



<P>Lord bless me! how do you think I can live poking by myself,



<P>I who have been always used till this winter to have



<P>Charlotte with me.&nbsp; Come, Miss Marianne, let us strike



<P>hands upon the bargain, and if Miss Dashwood will change



<P>her mind by and bye, why so much the better."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I thank you, ma'am, sincerely thank you,"

said Marianne,



<P>with warmth: "your invitation has insured my gratitude for ever,



<P>and it would give me such happiness, yes, almost the greatest



<P>happiness I am capable of, to be able to accept it.



<P>But my mother, my dearest, kindest mother,--I feel the



<P>justice of what Elinor has urged, and if she were to be



<P>made less happy, less comfortable by our absence--Oh! no,



<P>nothing should tempt me to leave her.&nbsp; It should not,



<P>must not be a struggle."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings repeated her assurance that Mrs.

Dashwood



<P>could spare them perfectly well; and Elinor, who now



<P>understood her sister, and saw to what indifference to



<P>almost every thing else she was carried by her eagerness



<P>to be with Willoughby again, made no farther direct



<P>opposition to the plan, and merely referred it to her



<P>mother's decision, from whom however she scarcely expected



<P>to receive any support in her endeavour to prevent a visit,



<P>which she could not approve of for Marianne, and which



<P>on her own account she had particular reasons to avoid.



<P>Whatever Marianne was desirous of, her mother would be eager



<P>to promote--she could not expect to influence the latter



<P>to cautiousness of conduct in an affair respecting which she



<P>had never been able to inspire her with distrust; and she



<P>dared not explain the motive of her own disinclination



<P>for going to London.&nbsp; That Marianne, fastidious as she was,



<P>thoroughly acquainted with Mrs. Jennings' manners,



<P>and invariably disgusted by them, should overlook every



<P>inconvenience of that kind, should disregard whatever



<P>must be most wounding to her irritable feelings, in her



<P>pursuit of one object, was such a proof, so strong,



<P>so full, of the importance of that object to her, as Elinor,



<P>in spite of all that had passed, was not prepared to witness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; On being informed of the invitation, Mrs. Dashwood,



<P>persuaded that such an excursion would be productive



<P>of much amusement to both her daughters, and perceiving



<P>through all her affectionate attention to herself,



<P>how much the heart of Marianne was in it, would not hear



<P>of their declining the offer upon HER account; insisted on



<P>their both accepting it directly; and then began to foresee,



<P>with her usual cheerfulness, a variety of advantages that



<P>would accrue to them all, from this separation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am delighted with the plan," she cried,



<P>"it is exactly what I could wish.&nbsp; Margaret and I shall



<P>be as much benefited by it as yourselves.&nbsp; When you



<P>and the Middletons are gone, we shall go on so quietly



<P>and happily together with our books and our music! You



<P>will find Margaret so improved when you come back again!



<P>I have a little plan of alteration for your bedrooms too,



<P>which may now be performed without any inconvenience



<P>to any one.&nbsp; It is very right that you SHOULD go to town;



<P>I would have every young woman of your condition in life



<P>acquainted with the manners and amusements of London.



<P>You will be under the care of a motherly good sort



<P>of woman, of whose kindness to you I can have no doubt.



<P>And in all probability you will see your brother,



<P>and whatever may be his faults, or the faults of his wife,



<P>when I consider whose son he is, I cannot bear to have you so



<P>wholly estranged from each other."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Though with your usual anxiety for our happiness,"



<P>said Elinor, "you have been obviating every impediment



<P>to the present scheme which occurred to you, there is



<P>still one objection which, in my opinion, cannot be so



<P>easily removed."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne's countenance sunk.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And what," said Mrs. Dashwood, "is my dear

prudent



<P>Elinor going to suggest?&nbsp; What formidable obstacle is she



<P>now to bring forward?&nbsp; Do let me hear a word about the



<P>expense of it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "My objection is this; though I think very

well of



<P>Mrs. Jennings's heart, she is not a woman whose society



<P>can afford us pleasure, or whose protection will give



<P>us consequence."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "That is very true," replied her mother, "but

of



<P>her society, separately from that of other people,



<P>you will scarcely have any thing at all, and you will



<P>almost always appear in public with Lady Middleton."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "If Elinor is frightened away by her dislike

of



<P>Mrs. Jennings," said Marianne, "at least it need not prevent



<P>MY accepting her invitation.&nbsp; I have no such scruples,



<P>and I am sure I could put up with every unpleasantness



<P>of that kind with very little effort."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor could not help smiling at this display

of



<P>indifference towards the manners of a person, to whom she



<P>had often had difficulty in persuading Marianne to behave



<P>with tolerable politeness; and resolved within herself,



<P>that if her sister persisted in going, she would



<P>go likewise, as she did not think it proper that Marianne



<P>should be left to the sole guidance of her own judgment,



<P>or that Mrs. Jennings should be abandoned to the mercy



<P>of Marianne for all the comfort of her domestic hours.



<P>To this determination she was the more easily reconciled,



<P>by recollecting that Edward Ferrars, by Lucy's account,



<P>was not to be in town before February; and that



<P>their visit, without any unreasonable abridgement,



<P>might be previously finished.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I will have you BOTH go," said Mrs. Dashwood;



<P>"these objections are nonsensical.&nbsp; You will have much



<P>pleasure in being in London, and especially in being together;



<P>and if Elinor would ever condescend to anticipate enjoyment,



<P>she would foresee it there from a variety of sources;



<P>she would, perhaps, expect some from improving her



<P>acquaintance with her sister-in-law's family."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor had often wished for an opportunity

of



<P>attempting to weaken her mother's dependence on the



<P>attachment of Edward and herself, that the shock might



<P>be less when the whole truth were revealed, and now



<P>on this attack, though almost hopeless of success,



<P>she forced herself to begin her design by saying,



<P>as calmly as she could, "I like Edward Ferrars very much,



<P>and shall always be glad to see him; but as to the rest



<P>of the family, it is a matter of perfect indifference



<P>to me, whether I am ever known to them or not."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood smiled, and said nothing.



<P>Marianne lifted up her eyes in astonishment, and Elinor



<P>conjectured that she might as well have held her tongue.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; After very little farther discourse, it was

finally



<P>settled that the invitation should be fully accepted.



<P>Mrs. Jennings received the information with a great



<P>deal of joy, and many assurances of kindness and care;



<P>nor was it a matter of pleasure merely to her.&nbsp; Sir John



<P>was delighted; for to a man, whose prevailing anxiety



<P>was the dread of being alone, the acquisition of two,



<P>to the number of inhabitants in London, was something.



<P>Even Lady Middleton took the trouble of being delighted,



<P>which was putting herself rather out of her way;



<P>and as for the Miss Steeles, especially Lucy, they had



<P>never been so happy in their lives as this intelligence



<P>made them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor submitted to the arrangement which counteracted



<P>her wishes with less reluctance than she had expected



<P>to feel.&nbsp; With regard to herself, it was now a matter



<P>of unconcern whether she went to town or not, and when



<P>she saw her mother so thoroughly pleased with the plan,



<P>and her sister exhilarated by it in look, voice, and manner,



<P>restored to all her usual animation, and elevated to more



<P>than her usual gaiety, she could not be dissatisfied



<P>with the cause, and would hardly allow herself to distrust



<P>the consequence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne's joy was almost a degree beyond happiness,



<P>so great was the perturbation of her spirits and her



<P>impatience to be gone.&nbsp; Her unwillingness to quit her



<P>mother was her only restorative to calmness; and at the



<P>moment of parting her grief on that score was excessive.



<P>Her mother's affliction was hardly less, and Elinor



<P>was the only one of the three, who seemed to consider



<P>the separation as any thing short of eternal.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Their departure took place in the first week

in January.



<P>The Middletons were to follow in about a week.&nbsp; The Miss



<P>Steeles kept their station at the park, and were to quit



<P>it only with the rest of the family.



<P>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter31</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER&nbsp; 31</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; From a night of more sleep than she had expected,



<P>Marianne awoke the next morning to the same consciousness



<P>of misery in which she had closed her eyes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor encouraged her as much as possible to

talk



<P>of what she felt; and before breakfast was ready, they had



<P>gone through the subject again and again; and with the same



<P>steady conviction and affectionate counsel on Elinor's side,



<P>the same impetuous feelings and varying opinions on



<P>Marianne's, as before.&nbsp; Sometimes she could believe



<P>Willoughby to be as unfortunate and as innocent as herself,



<P>and at others, lost every consolation in the impossibility



<P>of acquitting him.&nbsp; At one moment she was absolutely



<P>indifferent to the observation of all the world, at another



<P>she would seclude herself from it for ever, and at a third



<P>could resist it with energy.&nbsp; In one thing, however,



<P>she was uniform, when it came to the point, in avoiding,



<P>where it was possible, the presence of Mrs. Jennings,



<P>and in a determined silence when obliged to endure it.



<P>Her heart was hardened against the belief of Mrs. Jennings's



<P>entering into her sorrows with any compassion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, no, no, it cannot be," she cried;



<P>"she cannot feel.&nbsp; Her kindness is not sympathy;



<P>her good-nature is not tenderness.&nbsp; All that she wants



<P>is gossip, and she only likes me now because I supply it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor had not needed this to be assured of

the injustice



<P>to which her sister was often led in her opinion of others,



<P>by the irritable refinement of her own mind, and the too



<P>great importance placed by her on the delicacies of a



<P>strong sensibility, and the graces of a polished manner.



<P>Like half the rest of the world, if more than half there



<P>be that are clever and good, Marianne, with excellent



<P>abilities and an excellent disposition, was neither



<P>reasonable nor candid.&nbsp; She expected from other people



<P>the same opinions and feelings as her own, and she judged



<P>of their motives by the immediate effect of their actions



<P>on herself.&nbsp; Thus a circumstance occurred, while the



<P>sisters were together in their own room after breakfast,



<P>which sunk the heart of Mrs. Jennings still lower



<P>in her estimation; because, through her own weakness,



<P>it chanced to prove a source of fresh pain to herself,



<P>though Mrs. Jennings was governed in it by an impulse



<P>of the utmost goodwill.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; With a letter in her outstretched hand, and

countenance



<P>gaily smiling, from the persuasion of bringing comfort,



<P>she entered their room, saying,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Now, my dear, I bring you something that I

am sure



<P>will do you good."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne heard enough.&nbsp; In one moment

her imagination



<P>placed before her a letter from Willoughby, full of tenderness



<P>and contrition, explanatory of all that had passed, satisfactory,



<P>convincing; and instantly followed by Willoughby himself,



<P>rushing eagerly into the room to inforce, at her feet,



<P>by the eloquence of his eyes, the assurances of his letter.



<P>The work of one moment was destroyed by the next.



<P>The hand writing of her mother, never till then unwelcome,



<P>was before her; and, in the acuteness of the disappointment



<P>which followed such an ecstasy of more than hope,



<P>she felt as if, till that instant, she had never suffered.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The cruelty of Mrs. Jennings no language, within



<P>her reach in her moments of happiest eloquence,



<P>could have expressed; and now she could reproach her



<P>only by the tears which streamed from her eyes with



<P>passionate violence--a reproach, however, so entirely



<P>lost on its object, that after many expressions of pity,



<P>she withdrew, still referring her to the letter of comfort.



<P>But the letter, when she was calm enough to read it,



<P>brought little comfort.&nbsp; Willoughby filled every page.



<P>Her mother, still confident of their engagement, and relying



<P>as warmly as ever on his constancy, had only been roused



<P>by Elinor's application, to intreat from Marianne greater



<P>openness towards them both; and this, with such tenderness



<P>towards her, such affection for Willoughby, and such



<P>a conviction of their future happiness in each other,



<P>that she wept with agony through the whole of it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; All her impatience to be at home again now

returned;



<P>her mother was dearer to her than ever; dearer through



<P>the very excess of her mistaken confidence in Willoughby,



<P>and she was wildly urgent to be gone.&nbsp; Elinor, unable herself



<P>to determine whether it were better for Marianne to be



<P>in London or at Barton, offered no counsel of her own



<P>except of patience till their mother's wishes could be known;



<P>and at length she obtained her sister's consent to wait



<P>for that knowledge.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings left them earlier than usual;

for she



<P>could not be easy till the Middletons and Palmers were able



<P>to grieve as much as herself; and positively refusing



<P>Elinor's offered attendance, went out alone for the rest



<P>of the morning.&nbsp; Elinor, with a very heavy heart, aware of



<P>the pain she was going to communicate, and perceiving,



<P>by Marianne's letter, how ill she had succeeded in laying



<P>any foundation for it, then sat down to write her mother



<P>an account of what had passed, and entreat her directions



<P>for the future; while Marianne, who came into the drawing-room



<P>on Mrs. Jennings's going away, remained fixed at the table



<P>where Elinor wrote, watching the advancement of her pen,



<P>grieving over her for the hardship of such a task,



<P>and grieving still more fondly over its effect on her mother.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In this manner they had continued about a quarter



<P>of an hour, when Marianne, whose nerves could not then



<P>bear any sudden noise, was startled by a rap at the door.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Who can this be?" cried Elinor.&nbsp; "So

early too! I



<P>thought we HAD been safe."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne moved to the window--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is Colonel Brandon!" said she, with vexation.



<P>"We are never safe from HIM."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "He will not come in, as Mrs. Jennings is from

home."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I will not trust to THAT," retreating to her

own room.



<P>"A man who has nothing to do with his own time has no



<P>conscience in his intrusion on that of others."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The event proved her conjecture right, though

it



<P>was founded on injustice and error; for Colonel Brandon



<P>DID come in; and Elinor, who was convinced that



<P>solicitude for Marianne brought him thither, and who saw



<P>THAT solicitude in his disturbed and melancholy look,



<P>and in his anxious though brief inquiry after her,



<P>could not forgive her sister for esteeming him so lightly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I met Mrs. Jennings in Bond Street," said

he,



<P>after the first salutation, "and she encouraged me



<P>to come on; and I was the more easily encouraged,



<P>because I thought it probable that I might find you alone,



<P>which I was very desirous of doing.&nbsp; My object--my



<P>wish--my sole wish in desiring it--I hope, I believe



<P>it is--is to be a means of giving comfort;--no, I must



<P>not say comfort--not present comfort--but conviction,



<P>lasting conviction to your sister's mind.&nbsp; My regard for her,



<P>for yourself, for your mother--will you allow me to prove it,



<P>by relating some circumstances which nothing but a VERY



<P>sincere regard--nothing but an earnest desire of being



<P>useful--I think I am justified--though where so many hours



<P>have been spent in convincing myself that I am right,



<P>is there not some reason to fear I may be wrong?"



<P>He stopped.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I understand you," said Elinor.&nbsp; "You

have something



<P>to tell me of Mr. Willoughby, that will open his character



<P>farther.&nbsp; Your telling it will be the greatest act of friendship



<P>that can be shewn Marianne.&nbsp; MY gratitude will be insured



<P>immediately by any information tending to that end, and HERS



<P>must be gained by it in time.&nbsp; Pray, pray let me hear it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You shall; and, to be brief, when I quitted

Barton



<P>last October,--but this will give you no idea--I must go



<P>farther back.&nbsp; You will find me a very awkward narrator,



<P>Miss Dashwood; I hardly know where to begin.&nbsp; A short



<P>account of myself, I believe, will be necessary, and it



<P>SHALL be a short one.&nbsp; On such a subject," sighing heavily,



<P>"can I have little temptation to be diffuse."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He stopt a moment for recollection, and then,



<P>with another sigh, went on.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You have probably entirely forgotten a conversation--



<P>(it is not to be supposed that it could make any impression



<P>on you)--a conversation between us one evening at Barton



<P>Park--it was the evening of a dance--in which I alluded



<P>to a lady I had once known, as resembling, in some measure,



<P>your sister Marianne."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Indeed," answered Elinor, "I have NOT forgotten

it."



<P>He looked pleased by this remembrance, and added,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "If I am not deceived by the uncertainty, the

partiality



<P>of tender recollection, there is a very strong resemblance



<P>between them, as well in mind as person.&nbsp; The same warmth



<P>of heart, the same eagerness of fancy and spirits.



<P>This lady was one of my nearest relations, an orphan from



<P>her infancy, and under the guardianship of my father.



<P>Our ages were nearly the same, and from our earliest years



<P>we were playfellows and friends.&nbsp; I cannot remember the



<P>time when I did not love Eliza; and my affection for her,



<P>as we grew up, was such, as perhaps, judging from my



<P>present forlorn and cheerless gravity, you might think me



<P>incapable of having ever felt.&nbsp; Her's, for me, was, I believe,



<P>fervent as the attachment of your sister to Mr. Willoughby



<P>and it was, though from a different cause, no less unfortunate.



<P>At seventeen she was lost to me for ever.&nbsp; She was



<P>married--married against her inclination to my brother.



<P>Her fortune was large, and our family estate much encumbered.



<P>And this, I fear, is all that can be said for the



<P>conduct of one, who was at once her uncle and guardian.



<P>My brother did not deserve her; he did not even love her.



<P>I had hoped that her regard for me would support her



<P>under any difficulty, and for some time it did; but at



<P>last the misery of her situation, for she experienced



<P>great unkindness, overcame all her resolution, and though



<P>she had promised me that nothing--but how blindly I



<P>relate!&nbsp; I have never told you how this was brought on.



<P>We were within a few hours of eloping together for Scotland.



<P>The treachery, or the folly, of my cousin's maid betrayed us.



<P>I was banished to the house of a relation far distant,



<P>and she was allowed no liberty, no society, no amusement,



<P>till my father's point was gained.&nbsp; I had depended on her



<P>fortitude too far, and the blow was a severe one--



<P>but had her marriage been happy, so young as I then was,



<P>a few months must have reconciled me to it, or at least



<P>I should not have now to lament it.&nbsp; This however



<P>was not the case.&nbsp; My brother had no regard for her;



<P>his pleasures were not what they ought to have been,



<P>and from the first he treated her unkindly.&nbsp; The consequence



<P>of this, upon a mind so young, so lively, so inexperienced



<P>as Mrs. Brandon's, was but too natural.&nbsp; She resigned



<P>herself at first to all the misery of her situation;



<P>and happy had it been if she had not lived to overcome those



<P>regrets which the remembrance of me occasioned.&nbsp; But can we



<P>wonder that, with such a husband to provoke inconstancy,



<P>and without a friend to advise or restrain her (for



<P>my father lived only a few months after their marriage,



<P>and I was with my regiment in the East Indies) she



<P>should fall?&nbsp; Had I remained in England, perhaps--but I



<P>meant to promote the happiness of both by removing



<P>from her for years, and for that purpose had procured



<P>my exchange.&nbsp; The shock which her marriage had given me,"



<P>he continued, in a voice of great agitation, "was of



<P>trifling weight--was nothing to what I felt when I heard,



<P>about two years afterwards, of her divorce.&nbsp; It was



<P>THAT which threw this gloom,--even now the recollection



<P>of what I suffered--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He could say no more, and rising hastily walked

for a few



<P>minutes about the room.&nbsp; Elinor, affected by his relation,



<P>and still more by his distress, could not speak.&nbsp; He saw



<P>her concern, and coming to her, took her hand, pressed it,



<P>and kissed it with grateful respect.&nbsp; A few minutes more



<P>of silent exertion enabled him to proceed with composure.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It was nearly three years after this unhappy



<P>period before I returned to England.&nbsp; My first care,



<P>when I DID arrive, was of course to seek for her;



<P>but the search was as fruitless as it was melancholy.



<P>I could not trace her beyond her first seducer, and there



<P>was every reason to fear that she had removed from him



<P>only to sink deeper in a life of sin.&nbsp; Her legal allowance



<P>was not adequate to her fortune, nor sufficient for her



<P>comfortable maintenance, and I learnt from my brother that



<P>the power of receiving it had been made over some months



<P>before to another person.&nbsp; He imagined, and calmly could he



<P>imagine it, that her extravagance, and consequent distress,



<P>had obliged her to dispose of it for some immediate relief.



<P>At last, however, and after I had been six months in England,



<P>I DID find her.&nbsp; Regard for a former servant of my own,



<P>who had since fallen into misfortune, carried me to visit



<P>him in a spunging-house, where he was confined for debt;



<P>and there, the same house, under a similar confinement,



<P>was my unfortunate sister.&nbsp; So altered--so faded--worn



<P>down by acute suffering of every kind! hardly could I



<P>believe the melancholy and sickly figure before me,



<P>to be the remains of the lovely, blooming, healthful girl,



<P>on whom I had once doted.&nbsp; What I endured in so beholding



<P>her--but I have no right to wound your feelings by attempting



<P>to describe it--I have pained you too much already.



<P>That she was, to all appearance, in the last stage



<P>of a consumption, was--yes, in such a situation it was



<P>my greatest comfort.&nbsp; Life could do nothing for her,



<P>beyond giving time for a better preparation for death;



<P>and that was given.&nbsp; I saw her placed in comfortable lodgings,



<P>and under proper attendants; I visited her every day



<P>during the rest of her short life: I was with her in her



<P>last moments."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Again he stopped to recover himself; and Elinor



<P>spoke her feelings in an exclamation of tender concern,



<P>at the fate of his unfortunate friend.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Your sister, I hope, cannot be offended,"

said he,



<P>"by the resemblance I have fancied between her and my



<P>poor disgraced relation.&nbsp; Their fates, their fortunes,



<P>cannot be the same; and had the natural sweet



<P>disposition of the one been guarded by a firmer mind,



<P>or a happier marriage, she might have been all that you



<P>will live to see the other be.&nbsp; But to what does all this



<P>lead?&nbsp; I seem to have been distressing you for nothing.



<P>Ah! Miss Dashwood--a subject such as this--untouched



<P>for fourteen years--it is dangerous to handle it at all!



<P>I WILL be more collected--more concise.&nbsp; She left to my care



<P>her only child, a little girl, the offspring of her first



<P>guilty connection, who was then about three years old.



<P>She loved the child, and had always kept it with her.



<P>It was a valued, a precious trust to me; and gladly



<P>would I have discharged it in the strictest sense,



<P>by watching over her education myself, had the nature



<P>of our situations allowed it; but I had no family, no home;



<P>and my little Eliza was therefore placed at school.



<P>I saw her there whenever I could, and after the death of my



<P>brother, (which happened about five years ago, and which



<P>left to me the possession of the family property,) she



<P>visited me at Delaford.&nbsp; I called her a distant relation;



<P>but I am well aware that I have in general been suspected



<P>of a much nearer connection with her.&nbsp; It is now three



<P>years ago (she had just reached her fourteenth year,)



<P>that I removed her from school, to place her under the care



<P>of a very respectable woman, residing in Dorsetshire,



<P>who had the charge of four or five other girls of about



<P>the same time of life; and for two years I had every reason



<P>to be pleased with her situation.&nbsp; But last February,



<P>almost a twelvemonth back, she suddenly disappeared.



<P>I had allowed her, (imprudently, as it has since turned



<P>out,) at her earnest desire, to go to Bath with one of



<P>her young friends, who was attending her father there



<P>for his health.&nbsp; I knew him to be a very good sort of man,



<P>and I thought well of his daughter--better than she deserved,



<P>for, with a most obstinate and ill-judged secrecy,



<P>she would tell nothing, would give no clue, though she



<P>certainly knew all.&nbsp; He, her father, a well-meaning,



<P>but not a quick-sighted man, could really, I believe,



<P>give no information; for he had been generally confined



<P>to the house, while the girls were ranging over the town



<P>and making what acquaintance they chose; and he tried



<P>to convince me, as thoroughly as he was convinced himself,



<P>of his daughter's being entirely unconcerned in the business.



<P>In short, I could learn nothing but that she was gone;



<P>all the rest, for eight long months, was left to conjecture.



<P>What I thought, what I feared, may be imagined; and what I



<P>suffered too."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Good heavens!" cried Elinor, "could it be--could



<P>Willoughby!"--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "The first news that reached me of her," he

continued,



<P>"came in a letter from herself, last October.



<P>It was forwarded to me from Delaford, and I received it



<P>on the very morning of our intended party to Whitwell;



<P>and this was the reason of my leaving Barton so suddenly,



<P>which I am sure must at the time have appeared strange



<P>to every body, and which I believe gave offence to some.



<P>Little did Mr. Willoughby imagine, I suppose, when his



<P>looks censured me for incivility in breaking up the party,



<P>that I was called away to the relief of one whom he



<P>had made poor and miserable; but HAD he known it,



<P>what would it have availed?&nbsp; Would he have been less



<P>gay or less happy in the smiles of your sister?&nbsp; No,



<P>he had already done that, which no man who CAN feel



<P>for another would do.&nbsp; He had left the girl whose



<P>youth and innocence he had seduced, in a situation of



<P>the utmost distress, with no creditable home, no help,



<P>no friends, ignorant of his address!&nbsp; He had left her,



<P>promising to return; he neither returned, nor wrote,



<P>nor relieved her."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "This is beyond every thing!" exclaimed Elinor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "His character is now before you; expensive,

dissipated,



<P>and worse than both.&nbsp; Knowing all this, as I have now



<P>known it many weeks, guess what I must have felt on seeing



<P>your sister as fond of him as ever, and on being assured



<P>that she was to marry him: guess what I must have felt



<P>for all your sakes.&nbsp; When I came to you last week and



<P>found you alone, I came determined to know the truth;



<P>though irresolute what to do when it WAS known.



<P>My behaviour must have seemed strange to you then;



<P>but now you will comprehend it.&nbsp; To suffer you all to be



<P>so deceived; to see your sister--but what could I do?



<P>I had no hope of interfering with success; and sometimes



<P>I thought your sister's influence might yet reclaim him.



<P>But now, after such dishonorable usage, who can tell what



<P>were his designs on her.&nbsp; Whatever they may have been,



<P>however, she may now, and hereafter doubtless WILL



<P>turn with gratitude towards her own condition, when she



<P>compares it with that of my poor Eliza, when she considers



<P>the wretched and hopeless situation of this poor girl,



<P>and pictures her to herself, with an affection for him so strong,



<P>still as strong as her own, and with a mind tormented



<P>by self-reproach, which must attend her through life.



<P>Surely this comparison must have its use with her.



<P>She will feel her own sufferings to be nothing.&nbsp; They



<P>proceed from no misconduct, and can bring no disgrace.



<P>On the contrary, every friend must be made still more



<P>her friend by them.&nbsp; Concern for her unhappiness,



<P>and respect for her fortitude under it, must strengthen



<P>every attachment.&nbsp; Use your own discretion, however,



<P>in communicating to her what I have told you.&nbsp; You must



<P>know best what will be its effect; but had I not seriously,



<P>and from my heart believed it might be of service,



<P>might lessen her regrets, I would not have suffered



<P>myself to trouble you with this account of my family



<P>afflictions, with a recital which may seem to have been



<P>intended to raise myself at the expense of others."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor's thanks followed this speech with grateful



<P>earnestness; attended too with the assurance of her



<P>expecting material advantage to Marianne, from the



<P>communication of what had passed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have been more pained," said she, "by her



<P>endeavors to acquit him than by all the rest; for it



<P>irritates her mind more than the most perfect conviction



<P>of his unworthiness can do.&nbsp; Now, though at first she



<P>will suffer much, I am sure she will soon become easier.



<P>Have you," she continued, after a short silence,



<P>"ever seen Mr. Willoughby since you left him at Barton?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes," he replied gravely, "once I have.&nbsp;

One meeting



<P>was unavoidable."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor, startled by his manner, looked at him

anxiously,



<P>saying,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What? have you met him to--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I could meet him no other way.&nbsp; Eliza

had confessed



<P>to me, though most reluctantly, the name of her lover;



<P>and when he returned to town, which was within a fortnight



<P>after myself, we met by appointment, he to defend,



<P>I to punish his conduct.&nbsp; We returned unwounded,



<P>and the meeting, therefore, never got abroad."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor sighed over the fancied necessity of

this;



<P>but to a man and a soldier she presumed not to censure it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Such," said Colonel Brandon, after a pause,



<P>"has been the unhappy resemblance between the fate of mother



<P>and daughter! and so imperfectly have I discharged my trust!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Is she still in town?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No; as soon as she recovered from her lying-in,



<P>for I found her near her delivery, I removed her and her



<P>child into the country, and there she remains."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Recollecting, soon afterwards, that he was

probably



<P>dividing Elinor from her sister, he put an end to his visit,



<P>receiving from her again the same grateful acknowledgments,



<P>and leaving her full of compassion and esteem for him.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter19</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 19</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Edward remained a week at the cottage; he was

earnestly



<P>pressed by Mrs. Dashwood to stay longer; but, as if he



<P>were bent only on self-mortification, he seemed resolved



<P>to be gone when his enjoyment among his friends was at



<P>the height.&nbsp; His spirits, during the last two or three days,



<P>though still very unequal, were greatly improved--he grew



<P>more and more partial to the house and environs--never



<P>spoke of going away without a sigh--declared his time



<P>to be wholly disengaged--even doubted to what place he



<P>should go when he left them--but still, go he must.



<P>Never had any week passed so quickly--he could hardly



<P>believe it to be gone.&nbsp; He said so repeatedly; other things



<P>he said too, which marked the turn of his feelings and gave



<P>the lie to his actions.&nbsp; He had no pleasure at Norland;



<P>he detested being in town; but either to Norland or London,



<P>he must go.&nbsp; He valued their kindness beyond any thing,



<P>and his greatest happiness was in being with them.



<P>Yet, he must leave them at the end of a week, in spite



<P>of their wishes and his own, and without any restraint



<P>on his time.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor placed all that was astonishing in this



<P>way of acting to his mother's account; and it was



<P>happy for her that he had a mother whose character



<P>was so imperfectly known to her, as to be the general



<P>excuse for every thing strange on the part of her son.



<P>Disappointed, however, and vexed as she was, and sometimes



<P>displeased with his uncertain behaviour to herself,



<P>she was very well disposed on the whole to regard his actions



<P>with all the candid allowances and generous qualifications,



<P>which had been rather more painfully extorted from her,



<P>for Willoughby's service, by her mother.&nbsp; His want of spirits,



<P>of openness, and of consistency, were most usually



<P>attributed to his want of independence, and his better



<P>knowledge of Mrs. Ferrars's disposition and designs.



<P>The shortness of his visit, the steadiness of his purpose



<P>in leaving them, originated in the same fettered inclination,



<P>the same inevitable necessity of temporizing with his mother.



<P>The old well-established grievance of duty against will,



<P>parent against child, was the cause of all.&nbsp; She would have



<P>been glad to know when these difficulties were to cease,



<P>this opposition was to yield,--when Mrs. Ferrars would



<P>be reformed, and her son be at liberty to be happy.



<P>But from such vain wishes she was forced to turn for comfort



<P>to the renewal of her confidence in Edward's affection,



<P>to the remembrance of every mark of regard in look or word



<P>which fell from him while at Barton, and above all



<P>to that flattering proof of it which he constantly wore



<P>round his finger.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I think, Edward," said Mrs. Dashwood, as they

were



<P>at breakfast the last morning, "you would be a happier man



<P>if you had any profession to engage your time and give



<P>an interest to your plans and actions.&nbsp; Some inconvenience



<P>to your friends, indeed, might result from it--you



<P>would not be able to give them so much of your time.



<P>But (with a smile) you would be materially benefited



<P>in one particular at least--you would know where to go



<P>when you left them."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I do assure you," he replied, "that I have

long



<P>thought on this point, as you think now.&nbsp; It has been,



<P>and is, and probably will always be a heavy misfortune



<P>to me, that I have had no necessary business to engage me,



<P>no profession to give me employment, or afford me any



<P>thing like independence.&nbsp; But unfortunately my own nicety,



<P>and the nicety of my friends, have made me what I am,



<P>an idle, helpless being.&nbsp; We never could agree in our



<P>choice of a profession.&nbsp; I always preferred the church,



<P>as I still do.&nbsp; But that was not smart enough for my family.



<P>They recommended the army.&nbsp; That was a great deal



<P>too smart for me.&nbsp; The law was allowed to be genteel



<P>enough; many young men, who had chambers in the Temple,



<P>made a very good appearance in the first circles,



<P>and drove about town in very knowing gigs.&nbsp; But I had



<P>no inclination for the law, even in this less abstruse



<P>study of it, which my family approved.&nbsp; As for the navy,



<P>it had fashion on its side, but I was too old when the



<P>subject was first started to enter it--and, at length,



<P>as there was no necessity for my having any profession



<P>at all, as I might be as dashing and expensive without



<P>a red coat on my back as with one, idleness was pronounced



<P>on the whole to be most advantageous and honourable,



<P>and a young man of eighteen is not in general so earnestly



<P>bent on being busy as to resist the solicitations of his



<P>friends to do nothing.&nbsp; I was therefore entered at Oxford



<P>and have been properly idle ever since."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "The consequence of which, I suppose, will

be,"



<P>said Mrs. Dashwood, "since leisure has not promoted



<P>your own happiness, that your sons will be brought up



<P>to as many pursuits, employments, professions, and trades



<P>as Columella's."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "They will be brought up," said he, in a serious

accent,



<P>"to be as unlike myself as is possible.&nbsp; In feeling,



<P>in action, in condition, in every thing."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Come, come; this is all an effusion of immediate



<P>want of spirits, Edward.&nbsp; You are in a melancholy humour,



<P>and fancy that any one unlike yourself must be happy.



<P>But remember that the pain of parting from friends



<P>will be felt by every body at times, whatever be their



<P>education or state.&nbsp; Know your own happiness.&nbsp; You want



<P>nothing but patience--or give it a more fascinating name,



<P>call it hope.&nbsp; Your mother will secure to you, in time,



<P>that independence you are so anxious for; it is her duty,



<P>and it will, it must ere long become her happiness to



<P>prevent your whole youth from being wasted in discontent.



<P>How much may not a few months do?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I think," replied Edward, "that I may defy

many



<P>months to produce any good to me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This desponding turn of mind, though it could

not



<P>be communicated to Mrs. Dashwood, gave additional pain



<P>to them all in the parting, which shortly took place,



<P>and left an uncomfortable impression on Elinor's



<P>feelings especially, which required some trouble and time



<P>to subdue.&nbsp; But as it was her determination to subdue it,



<P>and to prevent herself from appearing to suffer more than



<P>what all her family suffered on his going away, she did



<P>not adopt the method so judiciously employed by Marianne,



<P>on a similar occasion, to augment and fix her sorrow,



<P>by seeking silence, solitude and idleness.&nbsp; Their means



<P>were as different as their objects, and equally suited



<P>to the advancement of each.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor sat down to her drawing-table as soon

as he



<P>was out of the house, busily employed herself the whole day,



<P>neither sought nor avoided the mention of his name,



<P>appeared to interest herself almost as much as ever in the



<P>general concerns of the family, and if, by this conduct,



<P>she did not lessen her own grief, it was at least prevented



<P>from unnecessary increase, and her mother and sisters



<P>were spared much solicitude on her account.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Such behaviour as this, so exactly the reverse



<P>of her own, appeared no more meritorious to Marianne,



<P>than her own had seemed faulty to her.&nbsp; The business



<P>of self-command she settled very easily;--with strong



<P>affections it was impossible, with calm ones it could



<P>have no merit.&nbsp; That her sister's affections WERE calm,



<P>she dared not deny, though she blushed to acknowledge it;



<P>and of the strength of her own, she gave a very striking proof,



<P>by still loving and respecting that sister, in spite



<P>of this mortifying conviction.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Without shutting herself up from her family,



<P>or leaving the house in determined solitude to avoid them,



<P>or lying awake the whole night to indulge meditation,



<P>Elinor found every day afforded her leisure enough



<P>to think of Edward, and of Edward's behaviour, in every



<P>possible variety which the different state of her spirits



<P>at different times could produce,--with tenderness,



<P>pity, approbation, censure, and doubt.&nbsp; There were moments



<P>in abundance, when, if not by the absence of her mother



<P>and sisters, at least by the nature of their employments,



<P>conversation was forbidden among them, and every effect



<P>of solitude was produced.&nbsp; Her mind was inevitably



<P>at liberty; her thoughts could not be chained elsewhere;



<P>and the past and the future, on a subject so interesting,



<P>must be before her, must force her attention, and engross



<P>her memory, her reflection, and her fancy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; From a reverie of this kind, as she sat at

her



<P>drawing-table, she was roused one morning, soon after



<P>Edward's leaving them, by the arrival of company.



<P>She happened to be quite alone.&nbsp; The closing of the



<P>little gate, at the entrance of the green court in front



<P>of the house, drew her eyes to the window, and she saw



<P>a large party walking up to the door.&nbsp; Amongst them



<P>were Sir John and Lady Middleton and Mrs. Jennings,



<P>but there were two others, a gentleman and lady, who were



<P>quite unknown to her.&nbsp; She was sitting near the window,



<P>and as soon as Sir John perceived her, he left the rest



<P>of the party to the ceremony of knocking at the door,



<P>and stepping across the turf, obliged her to open the



<P>casement to speak to him, though the space was so short



<P>between the door and the window, as to make it hardly



<P>possible to speak at one without being heard at the other.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well," said he, "we have brought you some

strangers.



<P>How do you like them?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Hush! they will hear you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Never mind if they do.&nbsp; It is only the

Palmers.



<P>Charlotte is very pretty, I can tell you.&nbsp; You may see her



<P>if you look this way."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As Elinor was certain of seeing her in a couple



<P>of minutes, without taking that liberty, she begged



<P>to be excused.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Where is Marianne? Has she run away because

we



<P>are come? I see her instrument is open."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "She is walking, I believe."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They were now joined by Mrs. Jennings, who

had not



<P>patience enough to wait till the door was opened before



<P>she told HER story.&nbsp; She came hallooing to the window,



<P>"How do you do, my dear?&nbsp; How does Mrs. Dashwood do?



<P>And where are your sisters?&nbsp; What! all alone! you



<P>will be glad of a little company to sit with you.



<P>I have brought my other son and daughter to see you.



<P>Only think of their coming so suddenly!&nbsp; I thought I heard



<P>a carriage last night, while we were drinking our tea,



<P>but it never entered my head that it could be them.



<P>I thought of nothing but whether it might not be Colonel



<P>Brandon come back again; so I said to Sir John, I do think



<P>I hear a carriage; perhaps it is Colonel Brandon come



<P>back again"--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor was obliged to turn from her, in the

middle



<P>of her story, to receive the rest of the party; Lady



<P>Middleton introduced the two strangers; Mrs. Dashwood



<P>and Margaret came down stairs at the same time, and they



<P>all sat down to look at one another, while Mrs. Jennings



<P>continued her story as she walked through the passage



<P>into the parlour, attended by Sir John.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Palmer was several years younger than

Lady



<P>Middleton, and totally unlike her in every respect.



<P>She was short and plump, had a very pretty face,



<P>and the finest expression of good humour in it that could



<P>possibly be.&nbsp; Her manners were by no means so elegant



<P>as her sister's, but they were much more prepossessing.



<P>She came in with a smile, smiled all the time of her visit,



<P>except when she laughed, and smiled when she went away.



<P>Her husband was a grave looking young man of five or six



<P>and twenty, with an air of more fashion and sense than



<P>his wife, but of less willingness to please or be pleased.



<P>He entered the room with a look of self-consequence,



<P>slightly bowed to the ladies, without speaking a word,



<P>and, after briefly surveying them and their apartments,



<P>took up a newspaper from the table, and continued to read it



<P>as long as he staid.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Palmer, on the contrary, who was strongly

endowed



<P>by nature with a turn for being uniformly civil and happy,



<P>was hardly seated before her admiration of the parlour



<P>and every thing in it burst forth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well! what a delightful room this is! I never



<P>saw anything so charming!&nbsp; Only think, Mamma, how it



<P>is improved since I was here last! I always thought it



<P>such a sweet place, ma'am! (turning to Mrs. Dashwood)



<P>but you have made it so charming!&nbsp; Only look, sister,



<P>how delightful every thing is! How I should like such



<P>a house for myself!&nbsp; Should not you, Mr. Palmer?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mr. Palmer made her no answer, and did not

even raise



<P>his eyes from the newspaper.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Mr. Palmer does not hear me," said she, laughing;



<P>"he never does sometimes.&nbsp; It is so ridiculous!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This was quite a new idea to Mrs. Dashwood;

she had



<P>never been used to find wit in the inattention of any one,



<P>and could not help looking with surprise at them both.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings, in the meantime, talked on as

loud



<P>as she could, and continued her account of their surprise,



<P>the evening before, on seeing their friends, without



<P>ceasing till every thing was told.&nbsp; Mrs. Palmer laughed



<P>heartily at the recollection of their astonishment,



<P>and every body agreed, two or three times over, that it



<P>had been quite an agreeable surprise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You may believe how glad we all were to see

them,"



<P>added Mrs. Jennings, leaning forward towards Elinor,



<P>and speaking in a low voice as if she meant to be heard



<P>by no one else, though they were seated on different sides



<P>of the room; "but, however, I can't help wishing they had



<P>not travelled quite so fast, nor made such a long journey



<P>of it, for they came all round by London upon account



<P>of some business, for you know (nodding significantly and



<P>pointing to her daughter) it was wrong in her situation.



<P>I wanted her to stay at home and rest this morning,



<P>but she would come with us; she longed so much to see



<P>you all!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Palmer laughed, and said it would not

do her



<P>any harm.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "She expects to be confined in February,"



<P>continued Mrs. Jennings.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lady Middleton could no longer endure such

a conversation,



<P>and therefore exerted herself to ask Mr. Palmer if there



<P>was any news in the paper.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, none at all," he replied, and read on.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Here comes Marianne," cried Sir John.&nbsp;

"Now, Palmer,



<P>you shall see a monstrous pretty girl."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He immediately went into the passage, opened

the front door,



<P>and ushered her in himself.&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings asked her,



<P>as soon as she appeared, if she had not been to Allenham;



<P>and Mrs. Palmer laughed so heartily at the question,



<P>as to show she understood it.&nbsp; Mr. Palmer looked up



<P>on her entering the room, stared at her some minutes,



<P>and then returned to his newspaper.&nbsp; Mrs. Palmer's eye



<P>was now caught by the drawings which hung round the room.



<P>She got up to examine them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh! dear, how beautiful these are!&nbsp; Well!

how delightful!



<P>Do but look, mama, how sweet! I declare they are quite charming;



<P>I could look at them for ever." And then sitting down again,



<P>she very soon forgot that there were any such things in the room.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When Lady Middleton rose to go away, Mr. Palmer



<P>rose also, laid down the newspaper, stretched himself



<P>and looked at them all around.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "My love, have you been asleep?" said his wife,

laughing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He made her no answer; and only observed, after

again



<P>examining the room, that it was very low pitched,



<P>and that the ceiling was crooked.&nbsp; He then made his bow,



<P>and departed with the rest.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Sir John had been very urgent with them all

to



<P>spend the next day at the park.&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood, who did



<P>not chuse to dine with them oftener than they dined



<P>at the cottage, absolutely refused on her own account;



<P>her daughters might do as they pleased.&nbsp; But they had no



<P>curiosity to see how Mr. and Mrs. Palmer ate their dinner,



<P>and no expectation of pleasure from them in any other way.



<P>They attempted, therefore, likewise, to excuse themselves;



<P>the weather was uncertain, and not likely to be good.



<P>But Sir John would not be satisfied--the carriage should



<P>be sent for them and they must come.&nbsp; Lady Middleton too,



<P>though she did not press their mother, pressed them.



<P>Mrs. Jennings and Mrs. Palmer joined their entreaties, all



<P>seemed equally anxious to avoid a family party; and the young



<P>ladies were obliged to yield.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Why should they ask us?" said Marianne, as

soon as they



<P>were gone.&nbsp; "The rent of this cottage is said to be low;



<P>but we have it on very hard terms, if we are to dine



<P>at the park whenever any one is staying either with them,



<P>or with us."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "They mean no less to be civil and kind to

us now,"



<P>said Elinor, "by these frequent invitations, than by



<P>those which we received from them a few weeks ago.



<P>The alteration is not in them, if their parties are grown



<P>tedious and dull.&nbsp; We must look for the change elsewhere."



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter18</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 18</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor saw, with great uneasiness the low spirits



<P>of her friend.&nbsp; His visit afforded her but a very



<P>partial satisfaction, while his own enjoyment in it



<P>appeared so imperfect.&nbsp; It was evident that he was unhappy;



<P>she wished it were equally evident that he still



<P>distinguished her by the same affection which once



<P>she had felt no doubt of inspiring; but hitherto the



<P>continuance of his preference seemed very uncertain;



<P>and the reservedness of his manner towards her contradicted



<P>one moment what a more animated look had intimated the preceding one.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He joined her and Marianne in the breakfast-room



<P>the next morning before the others were down; and Marianne,



<P>who was always eager to promote their happiness as far



<P>as she could, soon left them to themselves.&nbsp; But before she



<P>was half way upstairs she heard the parlour door open, and,



<P>turning round, was astonished to see Edward himself come out.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am going into the village to see my horses,"



<P>said be, "as you are not yet ready for breakfast; I shall



<P>be back again presently."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

***

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Edward returned to them with fresh admiration



<P>of the surrounding country; in his walk to the village,



<P>he had seen many parts of the valley to advantage;



<P>and the village itself, in a much higher situation than



<P>the cottage, afforded a general view of the whole, which had



<P>exceedingly pleased him.&nbsp; This was a subject which ensured



<P>Marianne's attention, and she was beginning to describe



<P>her own admiration of these scenes, and to question him more



<P>minutely on the objects that had particularly struck him,



<P>when Edward interrupted her by saying, "You must not



<P>enquire too far, Marianne--remember I have no knowledge



<P>in the picturesque, and I shall offend you by my ignorance



<P>and want of taste if we come to particulars.&nbsp; I shall call



<P>hills steep, which ought to be bold; surfaces strange



<P>and uncouth, which ought to be irregular and rugged;



<P>and distant objects out of sight, which ought only to be



<P>indistinct through the soft medium of a hazy atmosphere.



<P>You must be satisfied with such admiration as I can



<P>honestly give.&nbsp; I call it a very fine country--the



<P>hills are steep, the woods seem full of fine timber,



<P>and the valley looks comfortable and snug--with rich



<P>meadows and several neat farm houses scattered here



<P>and there.&nbsp; It exactly answers my idea of a fine country,



<P>because it unites beauty with utility--and I dare say it



<P>is a picturesque one too, because you admire it; I can



<P>easily believe it to be full of rocks and promontories,



<P>grey moss and brush wood, but these are all lost on me.



<P>I know nothing of the picturesque."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am afraid it is but too true," said Marianne;



<P>"but why should you boast of it?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I suspect," said Elinor, "that to avoid one

kind



<P>of affectation, Edward here falls into another.&nbsp; Because he



<P>believes many people pretend to more admiration of the beauties



<P>of nature than they really feel, and is disgusted with



<P>such pretensions, he affects greater indifference and less



<P>discrimination in viewing them himself than he possesses.



<P>He is fastidious and will have an affectation of his own."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is very true," said Marianne, "that admiration



<P>of landscape scenery is become a mere jargon.



<P>Every body pretends to feel and tries to describe with



<P>the taste and elegance of him who first defined what



<P>picturesque beauty was.&nbsp; I detest jargon of every kind,



<P>and sometimes I have kept my feelings to myself,



<P>because I could find no language to describe them



<P>in but what was worn and hackneyed out of all sense and meaning."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am convinced," said Edward, "that you really

feel



<P>all the delight in a fine prospect which you profess



<P>to feel.&nbsp; But, in return, your sister must allow me



<P>to feel no more than I profess.&nbsp; I like a fine prospect,



<P>but not on picturesque principles.&nbsp; I do not like crooked,



<P>twisted, blasted trees.&nbsp; I admire them much more if they



<P>are tall, straight, and flourishing.&nbsp; I do not like ruined,



<P>tattered cottages.&nbsp; I am not fond of nettles or thistles,



<P>or heath blossoms.&nbsp; I have more pleasure in a snug



<P>farm-house than a watch-tower--and a troop of tidy,



<P>happy villages please me better than the finest banditti



<P>in the world."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne looked with amazement at Edward,



<P>with compassion at her sister.&nbsp; Elinor only laughed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The subject was continued no farther; and Marianne



<P>remained thoughtfully silent, till a new object suddenly



<P>engaged her attention.&nbsp; She was sitting by Edward, and



<P>in taking his tea from Mrs. Dashwood, his hand passed



<P>so directly before her, as to make a ring, with a plait



<P>of hair in the centre, very conspicuous on one of his fingers.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I never saw you wear a ring before, Edward,"

she cried.



<P>"Is that Fanny's hair? I remember her promising to give



<P>you some.&nbsp; But I should have thought her hair had been darker."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne spoke inconsiderately what she really

felt--



<P>but when she saw how much she had pained Edward, her own



<P>vexation at her want of thought could not be surpassed



<P>by his.&nbsp; He coloured very deeply, and giving a momentary



<P>glance at Elinor, replied, "Yes; it is my sister's hair.



<P>The setting always casts a different shade on it,



<P>you know."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor had met his eye, and looked conscious

likewise.



<P>That the hair was her own, she instantaneously felt as



<P>well satisfied as Marianne; the only difference in their



<P>conclusions was, that what Marianne considered as a free



<P>gift from her sister, Elinor was conscious must have been



<P>procured by some theft or contrivance unknown to herself.



<P>She was not in a humour, however, to regard it as an affront,



<P>and affecting to take no notice of what passed,



<P>by instantly talking of something else, she internally



<P>resolved henceforward to catch every opportunity of eyeing



<P>the hair and of satisfying herself, beyond all doubt,



<P>that it was exactly the shade of her own.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Edward's embarrassment lasted some time, and

it



<P>ended in an absence of mind still more settled.



<P>He was particularly grave the whole morning.



<P>Marianne severely censured herself for what she had said;



<P>but her own forgiveness might have been more speedy,



<P>had she known how little offence it had given her sister.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Before the middle of the day, they were visited

by Sir



<P>John and Mrs. Jennings, who, having heard of the arrival



<P>of a gentleman at the cottage, came to take a survey



<P>of the guest.&nbsp; With the assistance of his mother-in-law,



<P>Sir John was not long in discovering that the name of



<P>Ferrars began with an F. and this prepared a future mine



<P>of raillery against the devoted Elinor, which nothing but



<P>the newness of their acquaintance with Edward could have



<P>prevented from being immediately sprung.&nbsp; But, as it was,



<P>she only learned, from some very significant looks, how far



<P>their penetration, founded on Margaret's instructions, extended.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Sir John never came to the Dashwoods without

either



<P>inviting them to dine at the park the next day, or to drink



<P>tea with them that evening.&nbsp; On the present occasion,



<P>for the better entertainment of their visitor, towards



<P>whose amusement he felt himself bound to contribute,



<P>he wished to engage them for both.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You MUST drink tea with us to night," said

he,



<P>"for we shall be quite alone--and tomorrow you must



<P>absolutely dine with us, for we shall be a large party."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings enforced the necessity.&nbsp;

"And who knows



<P>but you may raise a dance," said she.&nbsp; "And that will



<P>tempt YOU, Miss Marianne."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "A dance!" cried Marianne.&nbsp; "Impossible!

Who is to dance?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Who! why yourselves, and the Careys, and Whitakers



<P>to be sure.--What! you thought nobody could dance



<P>because a certain person that shall be nameless is gone!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I wish with all my soul," cried Sir John,



<P>"that Willoughby were among us again."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This, and Marianne's blushing, gave new suspicions



<P>to Edward.&nbsp; "And who is Willoughby?" said he, in a low voice,



<P>to Miss Dashwood, by whom he was sitting.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She gave him a brief reply.&nbsp; Marianne's

countenance



<P>was more communicative.&nbsp; Edward saw enough to comprehend,



<P>not only the meaning of others, but such of Marianne's



<P>expressions as had puzzled him before; and when their



<P>visitors left them, he went immediately round her, and said,



<P>in a whisper, "I have been guessing.&nbsp; Shall I tell you



<P>my guess?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What do you mean?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Shall I tell you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Certainly."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well then; I guess that Mr. Willoughby hunts."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne was surprised and confused, yet she

could



<P>not help smiling at the quiet archness of his manner,



<P>and after a moment's silence, said,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, Edward! How can you?--But the time will

come



<P>I hope...I am sure you will like him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I do not doubt it," replied he, rather astonished



<P>at her earnestness and warmth; for had he not imagined it



<P>to be a joke for the good of her acquaintance in general,



<P>founded only on a something or a nothing between Mr. Willoughby



<P>and herself, he would not have ventured to mention it.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter30</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 30</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings came immediately to their room

on her return,



<P>and without waiting to have her request of admittance answered,



<P>opened the door and walked in with a look of real concern.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How do you do my dear?"--said she in a voice

of great



<P>compassion to Marianne, who turned away her face without



<P>attempting to answer.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How is she, Miss Dashwood?--Poor thing! she

looks very bad.--



<P>No wonder.&nbsp; Ay, it is but too true.&nbsp; He is to be married



<P>very soon--a good-for-nothing fellow!&nbsp; I have no patience



<P>with him.&nbsp; Mrs. Taylor told me of it half an hour ago,



<P>and she was told it by a particular friend of Miss



<P>Grey herself, else I am sure I should not have believed it;



<P>and I was almost ready to sink as it was.&nbsp; Well, said I,



<P>all I can say is, that if this be true, he has used



<P>a young lady of my acquaintance abominably ill, and I



<P>wish with all my soul his wife may plague his heart out.



<P>And so I shall always say, my dear, you may depend on it.



<P>I have no notion of men's going on in this way; and if ever



<P>I meet him again, I will give him such a dressing as he



<P>has not had this many a day.&nbsp; But there is one comfort,



<P>my dear Miss Marianne; he is not the only young man



<P>in the world worth having; and with your pretty face



<P>you will never want admirers.&nbsp; Well, poor thing!&nbsp; I won't



<P>disturb her any longer, for she had better have her cry



<P>out at once and have done with.&nbsp; The Parrys and Sandersons



<P>luckily are coming tonight you know, and that will amuse her."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She then went away, walking on tiptoe out of

the room,



<P>as if she supposed her young friend's affliction could



<P>be increased by noise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne, to the surprise of her sister,



<P>determined on dining with them.&nbsp; Elinor even advised



<P>her against it.&nbsp; But "no, she would go down; she could



<P>bear it very well, and the bustle about her would



<P>be less."&nbsp; Elinor, pleased to have her governed for a



<P>moment by such a motive, though believing it hardly



<P>possible that she could sit out the dinner, said no more;



<P>and adjusting her dress for her as well as she could,



<P>while Marianne still remained on the bed, was ready



<P>to assist her into the dining room as soon as they were



<P>summoned to it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When there, though looking most wretchedly,



<P>she ate more and was calmer than her sister had expected.



<P>Had she tried to speak, or had she been conscious of half



<P>Mrs. Jennings's well-meant but ill-judged attentions



<P>to her, this calmness could not have been maintained;



<P>but not a syllable escaped her lips; and the abstraction



<P>of her thoughts preserved her in ignorance of every thing



<P>that was passing before her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor, who did justice to Mrs. Jennings's

kindness,



<P>though its effusions were often distressing, and sometimes



<P>almost ridiculous, made her those acknowledgments,



<P>and returned her those civilities, which her sister could



<P>not make or return for herself.&nbsp; Their good friend saw



<P>that Marianne was unhappy, and felt that every thing



<P>was due to her which might make her at all less so.



<P>She treated her therefore, with all the indulgent fondness



<P>of a parent towards a favourite child on the last day of



<P>its holidays.&nbsp; Marianne was to have the best place by the fire,



<P>was to be tempted to eat by every delicacy in the house,



<P>and to be amused by the relation of all the news of the day.



<P>Had not Elinor, in the sad countenance of her sister,



<P>seen a check to all mirth, she could have been entertained



<P>by Mrs. Jennings's endeavours to cure a disappointment in love,



<P>by a variety of sweetmeats and olives, and a good fire.



<P>As soon, however, as the consciousness of all this was



<P>forced by continual repetition on Marianne, she could



<P>stay no longer.&nbsp; With a hasty exclamation of Misery,



<P>and a sign to her sister not to follow her, she directly got



<P>up and hurried out of the room.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Poor soul!" cried Mrs. Jennings, as soon as

she was gone,



<P>"how it grieves me to see her!&nbsp; And I declare if she is



<P>not gone away without finishing her wine!&nbsp; And the dried



<P>cherries too!&nbsp; Lord! nothing seems to do her any good.



<P>I am sure if I knew of any thing she would like, I would



<P>send all over the town for it.&nbsp; Well, it is the oddest



<P>thing to me, that a man should use such a pretty girl



<P>so ill!&nbsp; But when there is plenty of money on one side,



<P>and next to none on the other, Lord bless you! they care



<P>no more about such things!--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "The lady then--Miss Grey I think you called

her--



<P>is very rich?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Fifty thousand pounds, my dear.&nbsp; Did

you ever see



<P>her? a smart, stylish girl they say, but not handsome.



<P>I remember her aunt very well, Biddy Henshawe; she married



<P>a very wealthy man.&nbsp; But the family are all rich together.



<P>Fifty thousand pounds! and by all accounts, it won't come



<P>before it's wanted; for they say he is all to pieces.



<P>No wonder! dashing about with his curricle and hunters!



<P>Well, it don't signify talking; but when a young man,



<P>be who he will, comes and makes love to a pretty girl,



<P>and promises marriage, he has no business to fly off



<P>from his word only because he grows poor, and a richer



<P>girl is ready to have him.&nbsp; Why don't he, in such a case,



<P>sell his horses, let his house, turn off his servants,



<P>and make a thorough reform at once? I warrant you,



<P>Miss Marianne would have been ready to wait till matters



<P>came round.&nbsp; But that won't do now-a-days; nothing in the



<P>way of pleasure can ever be given up by the young men of



<P>this age."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Do you know what kind of a girl Miss Grey

is?



<P>Is she said to be amiable?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I never heard any harm of her; indeed I hardly

ever



<P>heard her mentioned; except that Mrs. Taylor did say



<P>this morning, that one day Miss Walker hinted to her,



<P>that she believed Mr. and Mrs. Ellison would not be sorry



<P>to have Miss Grey married, for she and Mrs. Ellison could



<P>never agree."--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And who are the Ellisons?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Her guardians, my dear.&nbsp; But now she

is of age



<P>and may choose for herself; and a pretty choice she has



<P>made!--What now," after pausing a moment--"your poor sister
