<P>is gone to her own room, I suppose, to moan by herself.



<P>Is there nothing one can get to comfort her?&nbsp; Poor dear,



<P>it seems quite cruel to let her be alone.&nbsp; Well, by-and-by we



<P>shall have a few friends, and that will amuse her a little.



<P>What shall we play at?&nbsp; She hates whist I know; but is there



<P>no round game she cares for?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Dear ma'am, this kindness is quite unnecessary.



<P>Marianne, I dare say, will not leave her room again



<P>this evening.&nbsp; I shall persuade her if I can to go



<P>early to bed, for I am sure she wants rest."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Aye, I believe that will be best for her.&nbsp;

Let her name



<P>her own supper, and go to bed.&nbsp; Lord! no wonder she has



<P>been looking so bad and so cast down this last week or two,



<P>for this matter I suppose has been hanging over her head as



<P>long as that.&nbsp; And so the letter that came today finished it!



<P>Poor soul!&nbsp; I am sure if I had had a notion of it,



<P>I would not have joked her about it for all my money.



<P>But then you know, how should I guess such a thing?&nbsp; I made



<P>sure of its being nothing but a common love letter, and



<P>you know young people like to be laughed at about them.&nbsp; Lord!



<P>how concerned Sir John and my daughters will be when they



<P>hear it!&nbsp; If I had my senses about me I might have called



<P>in Conduit Street in my way home, and told them of it.



<P>But I shall see them tomorrow."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It would be unnecessary I am sure, for you

to caution



<P>Mrs. Palmer and Sir John against ever naming Mr. Willoughby,



<P>or making the slightest allusion to what has passed,



<P>before my sister.&nbsp; Their own good-nature must point out



<P>to them the real cruelty of appearing to know any thing



<P>about it when she is present; and the less that may ever



<P>be said to myself on the subject, the more my feelings



<P>will be spared, as you my dear madam will easily believe."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh! Lord! yes, that I do indeed.&nbsp; It

must be terrible



<P>for you to hear it talked of; and as for your sister,



<P>I am sure I would not mention a word about it to her



<P>for the world.&nbsp; You saw I did not all dinner time.



<P>No more would Sir John, nor my daughters, for they are



<P>all very thoughtful and considerate; especially if I



<P>give them a hint, as I certainly will.&nbsp; For my part,



<P>I think the less that is said about such things, the better,



<P>the sooner 'tis blown over and forgot.&nbsp; And what does



<P>talking ever do you know?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "In this affair it can only do harm; more so



<P>perhaps than in many cases of a similar kind, for it



<P>has been attended by circumstances which, for the sake



<P>of every one concerned in it, make it unfit to become



<P>the public conversation.&nbsp; I must do THIS justice to



<P>Mr. Willoughby--he has broken no positive engagement



<P>with my sister."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Law, my dear!&nbsp; Don't pretend to defend

him.



<P>No positive engagement indeed! after taking her all



<P>over Allenham House, and fixing on the very rooms they



<P>were to live in hereafter!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor, for her sister's sake, could not press

the



<P>subject farther, and she hoped it was not required of her



<P>for Willoughby's; since, though Marianne might lose much,



<P>he could gain very little by the enforcement of the real truth.



<P>After a short silence on both sides, Mrs. Jennings,



<P>with all her natural hilarity, burst forth again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, my dear, 'tis a true saying about an

ill-wind,



<P>for it will be all the better for Colonel Brandon.



<P>He will have her at last; aye, that he will.&nbsp; Mind me,



<P>now, if they an't married by Mid-summer.&nbsp; Lord! how he'll



<P>chuckle over this news!&nbsp; I hope he will come tonight.



<P>It will be all to one a better match for your sister.



<P>Two thousand a year without debt or drawback--except



<P>the little love-child, indeed; aye, I had forgot her;



<P>but she may be 'prenticed out at a small cost, and then



<P>what does it signify?&nbsp; Delaford is a nice place, I can



<P>tell you; exactly what I call a nice old fashioned place,



<P>full of comforts and conveniences; quite shut in with great



<P>garden walls that are covered with the best fruit-trees



<P>in the country; and such a mulberry tree in one corner!



<P>Lord! how Charlotte and I did stuff the only time we



<P>were there!&nbsp; Then, there is a dove-cote, some delightful



<P>stew-ponds, and a very pretty canal; and every thing,



<P>in short, that one could wish for; and, moreover, it is



<P>close to the church, and only a quarter of a mile from



<P>the turnpike-road, so 'tis never dull, for if you only



<P>go and sit up in an old yew arbour behind the house,



<P>you may see all the carriages that pass along.



<P>Oh! 'tis a nice place!&nbsp; A butcher hard by in the village,



<P>and the parsonage-house within a stone's throw.



<P>To my fancy, a thousand times prettier than Barton Park,



<P>where they are forced to send three miles for their meat,



<P>and have not a neighbour nearer than your mother.



<P>Well, I shall spirit up the Colonel as soon as I can.



<P>One shoulder of mutton, you know, drives another down.



<P>If we CAN but put Willoughby out of her head!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Ay, if we can do THAT, Ma'am," said Elinor,



<P>"we shall do very well with or without Colonel Brandon."



<P>And then rising, she went away to join Marianne,



<P>whom she found, as she expected, in her own room, leaning,



<P>in silent misery, over the small remains of a fire,



<P>which, till Elinor's entrance, had been her only light.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You had better leave me," was all the notice



<P>that her sister received from her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I will leave you," said Elinor, "if you will

go



<P>to bed." But this, from the momentary perverseness



<P>of impatient suffering, she at first refused to do.



<P>Her sister's earnest, though gentle persuasion, however,



<P>soon softened her to compliance, and Elinor saw her



<P>lay her aching head on the pillow, and as she hoped,



<P>in a way to get some quiet rest before she left her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In the drawing-room, whither she then repaired,



<P>she was soon joined by Mrs. Jennings, with a wine-glass,



<P>full of something, in her hand.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "My dear," said she, entering, "I have just

recollected



<P>that I have some of the finest old Constantia wine in the



<P>house that ever was tasted, so I have brought a glass of it



<P>for your sister.&nbsp; My poor husband! how fond he was of it!



<P>Whenever he had a touch of his old colicky gout, he said



<P>it did him more good than any thing else in the world.



<P>Do take it to your sister."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Dear Ma'am," replied Elinor, smiling at the

difference



<P>of the complaints for which it was recommended, "how good



<P>you are!&nbsp; But I have just left Marianne in bed, and, I hope,



<P>almost asleep; and as I think nothing will be of so much



<P>service to her as rest, if you will give me leave,



<P>I will drink the wine myself."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings, though regretting that she had

not been



<P>five minutes earlier, was satisfied with the compromise;



<P>and Elinor, as she swallowed the chief of it, reflected,



<P>that though its effects on a colicky gout were, at present,



<P>of little importance to her, its healing powers,



<P>on a disappointed heart might be as reasonably tried



<P>on herself as on her sister.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Colonel Brandon came in while the party were

at tea,



<P>and by his manner of looking round the room for Marianne,



<P>Elinor immediately fancied that he neither expected



<P>nor wished to see her there, and, in short, that he



<P>was already aware of what occasioned her absence.



<P>Mrs. Jennings was not struck by the same thought;



<P>for soon after his entrance, she walked across the room



<P>to the tea-table where Elinor presided, and whispered--



<P>"The Colonel looks as grave as ever you see.&nbsp; He knows



<P>nothing of it; do tell him, my dear."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He shortly afterwards drew a chair close to

her's,



<P>and, with a look which perfectly assured her of his



<P>good information, inquired after her sister.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Marianne is not well," said she.&nbsp; "She

has been



<P>indisposed all day, and we have persuaded her to go to bed."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Perhaps, then," he hesitatingly replied, "what

I



<P>heard this morning may be--there may be more truth in it



<P>than I could believe possible at first."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What did you hear?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "That a gentleman, whom I had reason to think--in

short,



<P>that a man, whom I KNEW to be engaged--but how shall I



<P>tell you?&nbsp; If you know it already, as surely you must,



<P>I may be spared."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You mean," answered Elinor, with forced calmness,



<P>"Mr. Willoughby's marriage with Miss Grey.&nbsp; Yes, we DO



<P>know it all.&nbsp; This seems to have been a day of general



<P>elucidation, for this very morning first unfolded it to us.



<P>Mr. Willoughby is unfathomable!&nbsp; Where did you hear it?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "In a stationer's shop in Pall Mall, where

I



<P>had business.&nbsp; Two ladies were waiting for their carriage,



<P>and one of them was giving the other an account of the



<P>intended match, in a voice so little attempting concealment,



<P>that it was impossible for me not to hear all.&nbsp; The name



<P>of Willoughby, John Willoughby, frequently repeated,



<P>first caught my attention; and what followed was a positive



<P>assertion that every thing was now finally settled



<P>respecting his marriage with Miss Grey--it was no longer



<P>to be a secret--it would take place even within a few weeks,



<P>with many particulars of preparations and other matters.



<P>One thing, especially, I remember, because it served



<P>to identify the man still more:--as soon as the ceremony



<P>was over, they were to go to Combe Magna, his seat



<P>in Somersetshire.&nbsp; My astonishment!--but it would be



<P>impossible to describe what I felt.&nbsp; The communicative



<P>lady I learnt, on inquiry, for I stayed in the shop



<P>till they were gone, was a Mrs. Ellison, and that, as I



<P>have been since informed, is the name of Miss Grey's guardian."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is.&nbsp; But have you likewise heard that

Miss Grey



<P>has fifty thousand pounds?&nbsp; In that, if in any thing,



<P>we may find an explanation."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It may be so; but Willoughby is capable--at

least



<P>I think"--he stopped a moment; then added in a voice



<P>which seemed to distrust itself, "And your sister--



<P>how did she--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Her sufferings have been very severe.&nbsp;

I have



<P>only to hope that they may be proportionately short.



<P>It has been, it is a most cruel affliction.&nbsp; Till yesterday,



<P>I believe, she never doubted his regard; and even now,



<P>perhaps--but I am almost convinced that he never was



<P>really attached to her.&nbsp; He has been very deceitful! and,



<P>in some points, there seems a hardness of heart about him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Ah!" said Colonel Brandon, "there is, indeed!&nbsp;

But



<P>your sister does not--I think you said so--she does



<P>not consider quite as you do?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You know her disposition, and may believe

how eagerly



<P>she would still justify him if she could."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He made no answer; and soon afterwards, by

the removal



<P>of the tea-things, and the arrangement of the card parties,



<P>the subject was necessarily dropped.&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings, who had



<P>watched them with pleasure while they were talking, and who



<P>expected to see the effect of Miss Dashwood's communication,



<P>in such an instantaneous gaiety on Colonel Brandon's side,



<P>as might have become a man in the bloom of youth, of hope



<P>and happiness, saw him, with amazement, remain the whole



<P>evening more serious and thoughtful than usual.



<P>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 24</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In a firm, though cautious tone, Elinor thus

began.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I should be undeserving of the confidence

you have



<P>honoured me with, if I felt no desire for its continuance,



<P>or no farther curiosity on its subject.&nbsp; I will



<P>not apologize therefore for bringing it forward again."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Thank you," cried Lucy warmly, "for breaking



<P>the ice; you have set my heart at ease by it; for I was



<P>somehow or other afraid I had offended you by what I



<P>told you that Monday."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Offended me! How could you suppose so? Believe

me,"



<P>and Elinor spoke it with the truest sincerity,



<P>"nothing could be farther from my intention than to give



<P>you such an idea.&nbsp; Could you have a motive for the trust,



<P>that was not honourable and flattering to me?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And yet I do assure you," replied Lucy, her

little



<P>sharp eyes full of meaning, "there seemed to me to be



<P>a coldness and displeasure in your manner that made me



<P>quite uncomfortable.&nbsp; I felt sure that you was angry with me;



<P>and have been quarrelling with myself ever since, for having



<P>took such a liberty as to trouble you with my affairs.



<P>But I am very glad to find it was only my own fancy,



<P>and that you really do not blame me.&nbsp; If you knew what a



<P>consolation it was to me to relieve my heart speaking to you



<P>of what I am always thinking of every moment of my life,



<P>your compassion would make you overlook every thing else



<P>I am sure."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Indeed, I can easily believe that it was a

very great



<P>relief to you, to acknowledge your situation to me, and be



<P>assured that you shall never have reason to repent it.



<P>Your case is a very unfortunate one; you seem to me to



<P>be surrounded with difficulties, and you will have need



<P>of all your mutual affection to support you under them.



<P>Mr. Ferrars, I believe, is entirely dependent on his mother."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "He has only two thousand pounds of his own;

it would



<P>be madness to marry upon that, though for my own part,



<P>I could give up every prospect of more without a sigh.



<P>I have been always used to a very small income, and could



<P>struggle with any poverty for him; but I love him too well



<P>to be the selfish means of robbing him, perhaps, of all that



<P>his mother might give him if he married to please her.



<P>We must wait, it may be for many years.&nbsp; With almost every



<P>other man in the world, it would be an alarming prospect;



<P>but Edward's affection and constancy nothing can deprive me of



<P>I know."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "That conviction must be every thing to you;



<P>and he is undoubtedly supported by the same trust in your's.



<P>If the strength of your reciprocal attachment had failed,



<P>as between many people, and under many circumstances



<P>it naturally would during a four years' engagement,



<P>your situation would have been pitiable, indeed."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lucy here looked up; but Elinor was careful



<P>in guarding her countenance from every expression



<P>that could give her words a suspicious tendency.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Edward's love for me," said Lucy, "has been

pretty



<P>well put to the test, by our long, very long absence



<P>since we were first engaged, and it has stood the trial



<P>so well, that I should be unpardonable to doubt it now.



<P>I can safely say that he has never gave me one moment's



<P>alarm on that account from the first."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor hardly knew whether to smile or sigh



<P>at this assertion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lucy went on.&nbsp; "I am rather of a jealous

temper too



<P>by nature, and from our different situations in life,



<P>from his being so much more in the world than me, and our



<P>continual separation, I was enough inclined for suspicion,



<P>to have found out the truth in an instant, if there had been



<P>the slightest alteration in his behaviour to me when we met,



<P>or any lowness of spirits that I could not account for,



<P>or if he had talked more of one lady than another,



<P>or seemed in any respect less happy at Longstaple than he



<P>used to be.&nbsp; I do not mean to say that I am particularly



<P>observant or quick-sighted in general, but in such a case



<P>I am sure I could not be deceived."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "All this," thought Elinor, "is very pretty;



<P>but it can impose upon neither of us."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But what," said she after a short silence,



<P>"are your views? or have you none but that of waiting for



<P>Mrs. Ferrars's death, which is a melancholy and shocking



<P>extremity?--Is her son determined to submit to this,



<P>and to all the tediousness of the many years of suspense



<P>in which it may involve you, rather than run the risk



<P>of her displeasure for a while by owning the truth?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "If we could be certain that it would be only



<P>for a while!&nbsp; But Mrs. Ferrars is a very headstrong



<P>proud woman, and in her first fit of anger upon hearing



<P>it, would very likely secure every thing to Robert,



<P>and the idea of that, for Edward's sake, frightens away



<P>all my inclination for hasty measures."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And for your own sake too, or you are carrying



<P>your disinterestedness beyond reason."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lucy looked at Elinor again, and was silent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Do you know Mr. Robert Ferrars?" asked Elinor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Not at all--I never saw him; but I fancy he



<P>is very unlike his brother--silly and a great coxcomb."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "A great coxcomb!" repeated Miss Steele, whose

ear had



<P>caught those words by a sudden pause in Marianne's music.--



<P>"Oh, they are talking of their favourite beaux, I dare say."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No sister," cried Lucy, "you are mistaken

there, our



<P>favourite beaux are NOT great coxcombs."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I can answer for it that Miss Dashwood's is

not,"



<P>said Mrs. Jennings, laughing heartily; "for he is one



<P>of the modestest, prettiest behaved young men I ever saw;



<P>but as for Lucy, she is such a sly little creature,



<P>there is no finding out who SHE likes."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh," cried Miss Steele, looking significantly

round



<P>at them, "I dare say Lucy's beau is quite as modest



<P>and pretty behaved as Miss Dashwood's."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor blushed in spite of herself.&nbsp; Lucy

bit her lip,



<P>and looked angrily at her sister.&nbsp; A mutual silence took



<P>place for some time.&nbsp; Lucy first put an end to it by saying



<P>in a lower tone, though Marianne was then giving them



<P>the powerful protection of a very magnificent concerto--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I will honestly tell you of one scheme which

has



<P>lately come into my head, for bringing matters to bear;



<P>indeed I am bound to let you into the secret, for you



<P>are a party concerned.&nbsp; I dare say you have seen enough



<P>of Edward to know that he would prefer the church to every



<P>other profession; now my plan is that he should take



<P>orders as soon as he can, and then through your interest,



<P>which I am sure you would be kind enough to use out of



<P>friendship for him, and I hope out of some regard to me,



<P>your brother might be persuaded to give him Norland living;



<P>which I understand is a very good one, and the present



<P>incumbent not likely to live a great while.&nbsp; That would



<P>be enough for us to marry upon, and we might trust to time



<P>and chance for the rest."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I should always be happy," replied Elinor,

"to show



<P>any mark of my esteem and friendship for Mr. Ferrars;



<P>but do you not perceive that my interest on such an



<P>occasion would be perfectly unnecessary?&nbsp; He is brother



<P>to Mrs. John Dashwood--THAT must be recommendation enough



<P>to her husband."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But Mrs. John Dashwood would not much approve



<P>of Edward's going into orders."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Then I rather suspect that my interest would



<P>do very little."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They were again silent for many minutes.&nbsp;

At length



<P>Lucy exclaimed with a deep sigh,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I believe it would be the wisest way to put

an end



<P>to the business at once by dissolving the engagement.



<P>We seem so beset with difficulties on every side,



<P>that though it would make us miserable for a time,



<P>we should be happier perhaps in the end.&nbsp; But you will



<P>not give me your advice, Miss Dashwood?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No," answered Elinor, with a smile, which

concealed



<P>very agitated feelings, "on such a subject I certainly



<P>will not.&nbsp; You know very well that my opinion would have



<P>no weight with you, unless it were on the side of your wishes."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Indeed you wrong me," replied Lucy, with great



<P>solemnity; "I know nobody of whose judgment I think



<P>so highly as I do of yours; and I do really believe,



<P>that if you was to say to me, 'I advise you by all means



<P>to put an end to your engagement with Edward Ferrars,



<P>it will be more for the happiness of both of you,'



<P>I should resolve upon doing it immediately."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor blushed for the insincerity of Edward's



<P>future wife, and replied, "This compliment would effectually



<P>frighten me from giving any opinion on the subject



<P>had I formed one.&nbsp; It raises my influence much too high;



<P>the power of dividing two people so tenderly attached



<P>is too much for an indifferent person."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "'Tis because you are an indifferent person,"

said Lucy,



<P>with some pique, and laying a particular stress on those words,



<P>"that your judgment might justly have such weight with me.



<P>If you could be supposed to be biased in any respect



<P>by your own feelings, your opinion would not be worth having."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor thought it wisest to make no answer

to this,



<P>lest they might provoke each other to an unsuitable increase



<P>of ease and unreserve; and was even partly determined



<P>never to mention the subject again.&nbsp; Another pause



<P>therefore of many minutes' duration, succeeded this speech,



<P>and Lucy was still the first to end it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Shall you be in town this winter, Miss Dashwood?"



<P>said she with all her accustomary complacency.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Certainly not."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am sorry for that," returned the other,



<P>while her eyes brightened at the information,



<P>"it would have gave me such pleasure to meet you there!



<P>But I dare say you will go for all that.&nbsp; To be sure,



<P>your brother and sister will ask you to come to them."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It will not be in my power to accept their

invitation



<P>if they do."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How unlucky that is! I had quite depended

upon



<P>meeting you there.&nbsp; Anne and me are to go the latter end



<P>of January to some relations who have been wanting us to



<P>visit them these several years!&nbsp; But I only go for the sake



<P>of seeing Edward.&nbsp; He will be there in February, otherwise



<P>London would have no charms for me; I have not spirits for it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor was soon called to the card-table by

the



<P>conclusion of the first rubber, and the confidential



<P>discourse of the two ladies was therefore at an end,



<P>to which both of them submitted without any reluctance,



<P>for nothing had been said on either side to make them



<P>dislike each other less than they had done before;



<P>and Elinor sat down to the card table with the melancholy



<P>persuasion that Edward was not only without affection



<P>for the person who was to be his wife; but that he had



<P>not even the chance of being tolerably happy in marriage,



<P>which sincere affection on HER side would have given,



<P>for self-interest alone could induce a woman to keep a man



<P>to an engagement, of which she seemed so thoroughly aware



<P>that he was weary.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; From this time the subject was never revived

by Elinor,



<P>and when entered on by Lucy, who seldom missed an opportunity



<P>of introducing it, and was particularly careful to inform



<P>her confidante, of her happiness whenever she received a letter



<P>from Edward, it was treated by the former with calmness



<P>and caution, and dismissed as soon as civility would allow;



<P>for she felt such conversations to be an indulgence which



<P>Lucy did not deserve, and which were dangerous to herself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The visit of the Miss Steeles at Barton Park

was



<P>lengthened far beyond what the first invitation implied.



<P>Their favour increased; they could not be spared;



<P>Sir John would not hear of their going; and in spite



<P>of their numerous and long arranged engagements in Exeter,



<P>in spite of the absolute necessity of returning to fulfill



<P>them immediately, which was in full force at the end



<P>of every week, they were prevailed on to stay nearly two



<P>months at the park, and to assist in the due celebration



<P>of that festival which requires a more than ordinary



<P>share of private balls and large dinners to proclaim



<P>its importance.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter08</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 8</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings was a widow with an ample jointure.



<P>She had only two daughters, both of whom she had lived



<P>to see respectably married, and she had now therefore



<P>nothing to do but to marry all the rest of the world.



<P>In the promotion of this object she was zealously active,



<P>as far as her ability reached; and missed no opportunity



<P>of projecting weddings among all the young people



<P>of her acquaintance.&nbsp; She was remarkably quick in the



<P>discovery of attachments, and had enjoyed the advantage



<P>of raising the blushes and the vanity of many a young



<P>lady by insinuations of her power over such a young man;



<P>and this kind of discernment enabled her soon after her



<P>arrival at Barton decisively to pronounce that Colonel



<P>Brandon was very much in love with Marianne Dashwood.



<P>She rather suspected it to be so, on the very first



<P>evening of their being together, from his listening



<P>so attentively while she sang to them; and when the visit



<P>was returned by the Middletons' dining at the cottage,



<P>the fact was ascertained by his listening to her again.



<P>It must be so.&nbsp; She was perfectly convinced of it.



<P>It would be an excellent match, for HE was rich, and SHE



<P>was handsome.&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings had been anxious to see



<P>Colonel Brandon well married, ever since her connection



<P>with Sir John first brought him to her knowledge;



<P>and she was always anxious to get a good husband for every



<P>pretty girl.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The immediate advantage to herself was by no

means



<P>inconsiderable, for it supplied her with endless jokes



<P>against them both.&nbsp; At the park she laughed at the colonel,



<P>and in the cottage at Marianne.&nbsp; To the former her



<P>raillery was probably, as far as it regarded only himself,



<P>perfectly indifferent; but to the latter it was at



<P>first incomprehensible; and when its object was understood,



<P>she hardly knew whether most to laugh at its absurdity,



<P>or censure its impertinence, for she considered it as an



<P>unfeeling reflection on the colonel's advanced years,



<P>and on his forlorn condition as an old bachelor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood, who could not think a man five

years



<P>younger than herself, so exceedingly ancient as he appeared



<P>to the youthful fancy of her daughter, ventured to clear



<P>Mrs. Jennings from the probability of wishing to throw



<P>ridicule on his age.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But at least, Mamma, you cannot deny the absurdity



<P>of the accusation, though you may not think it intentionally



<P>ill-natured.&nbsp; Colonel Brandon is certainly younger than



<P>Mrs. Jennings, but he is old enough to be MY father;



<P>and if he were ever animated enough to be in love,



<P>must have long outlived every sensation of the kind.



<P>It is too ridiculous!&nbsp; When is a man to be safe from such wit,



<P>if age and infirmity will not protect him?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Infirmity!" said Elinor, "do you call Colonel

Brandon



<P>infirm?&nbsp; I can easily suppose that his age may appear much



<P>greater to you than to my mother; but you can hardly



<P>deceive yourself as to his having the use of his limbs!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Did not you hear him complain of the rheumatism?



<P>and is not that the commonest infirmity of declining life?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "My dearest child," said her mother, laughing,



<P>"at this rate you must be in continual terror of MY decay;



<P>and it must seem to you a miracle that my life has been



<P>extended to the advanced age of forty."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Mamma, you are not doing me justice.&nbsp;

I know very well



<P>that Colonel Brandon is not old enough to make his friends



<P>yet apprehensive of losing him in the course of nature.



<P>He may live twenty years longer.&nbsp; But thirty-five has



<P>nothing to do with matrimony."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Perhaps," said Elinor, "thirty-five and seventeen

had



<P>better not have any thing to do with matrimony together.



<P>But if there should by any chance happen to be a woman



<P>who is single at seven and twenty, I should not think



<P>Colonel Brandon's being thirty-five any objection to his



<P>marrying HER."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "A woman of seven and twenty," said Marianne,



<P>after pausing a moment, "can never hope to feel or inspire



<P>affection again, and if her home be uncomfortable,



<P>or her fortune small, I can suppose that she might



<P>bring herself to submit to the offices of a nurse,



<P>for the sake of the provision and security of a wife.



<P>In his marrying such a woman therefore there would be



<P>nothing unsuitable.&nbsp; It would be a compact of convenience,



<P>and the world would be satisfied.&nbsp; In my eyes it would



<P>be no marriage at all, but that would be nothing.



<P>To me it would seem only a commercial exchange, in which



<P>each wished to be benefited at the expense of the other."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It would be impossible, I know," replied Elinor,



<P>"to convince you that a woman of seven and twenty could



<P>feel for a man of thirty-five anything near enough



<P>to love, to make him a desirable companion to her.



<P>But I must object to your dooming Colonel Brandon and



<P>his wife to the constant confinement of a sick chamber,



<P>merely because he chanced to complain yesterday (a



<P>very cold damp day) of a slight rheumatic feel in one



<P>of his shoulders."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But he talked of flannel waistcoats," said

Marianne;



<P>"and with me a flannel waistcoat is invariably connected



<P>with aches, cramps, rheumatisms, and every species of



<P>ailment that can afflict the old and the feeble."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Had he been only in a violent fever, you would

not



<P>have despised him half so much.&nbsp; Confess, Marianne, is not



<P>there something interesting to you in the flushed cheek,



<P>hollow eye, and quick pulse of a fever?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Soon after this, upon Elinor's leaving the

room,



<P>"Mamma," said Marianne, "I have an alarm on the subject



<P>of illness which I cannot conceal from you.&nbsp; I am sure



<P>Edward Ferrars is not well.&nbsp; We have now been here almost



<P>a fortnight, and yet he does not come.&nbsp; Nothing but real



<P>indisposition could occasion this extraordinary delay.



<P>What else can detain him at Norland?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Had you any idea of his coming so soon?"



<P>said Mrs. Dashwood.&nbsp; "I had none.&nbsp; On the contrary,



<P>if I have felt any anxiety at all on the subject, it has



<P>been in recollecting that he sometimes showed a want



<P>of pleasure and readiness in accepting my invitation,



<P>when I talked of his coming to Barton.&nbsp; Does Elinor



<P>expect him already?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have never mentioned it to her, but of course



<P>she must."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I rather think you are mistaken, for when

I



<P>was talking to her yesterday of getting a new grate



<P>for the spare bedchamber, she observed that there



<P>was no immediate hurry for it, as it was not likely



<P>that the room would be wanted for some time."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How strange this is! what can be the meaning

of it!



<P>But the whole of their behaviour to each other has been



<P>unaccountable! How cold, how composed were their last



<P>adieus! How languid their conversation the last evening



<P>of their being together! In Edward's farewell there was no



<P>distinction between Elinor and me: it was the good wishes



<P>of an affectionate brother to both.&nbsp; Twice did I leave



<P>them purposely together in the course of the last morning,



<P>and each time did he most unaccountably follow me out



<P>of the room.&nbsp; And Elinor, in quitting Norland and Edward,



<P>cried not as I did.&nbsp; Even now her self-command is invariable.



<P>When is she dejected or melancholy? When does she try



<P>to avoid society, or appear restless and dissatisfied



<P>in it?"



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter20</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 20</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As the Miss Dashwoods entered the drawing-room

of the park



<P>the next day, at one door, Mrs. Palmer came running in at



<P>the other, looking as good humoured and merry as before.



<P>She took them all most affectionately by the hand,



<P>and expressed great delight in seeing them again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am so glad to see you!" said she, seating

herself



<P>between Elinor and Marianne, "for it is so bad a day I was



<P>afraid you might not come, which would be a shocking thing,



<P>as we go away again tomorrow. We must go, for the Westons



<P>come to us next week you know.&nbsp; It was quite a sudden thing



<P>our coming at all, and I knew nothing of it till the carriage



<P>was coming to the door, and then Mr. Palmer asked me if I



<P>would go with him to Barton.&nbsp; He is so droll! He never



<P>tells me any thing! I am so sorry we cannot stay longer;



<P>however we shall meet again in town very soon, I hope."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They were obliged to put an end to such an

expectation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Not go to town!" cried Mrs. Palmer, with a

laugh,



<P>"I shall be quite disappointed if you do not.&nbsp; I could



<P>get the nicest house in world for you, next door to ours,



<P>in Hanover-square.&nbsp; You must come, indeed.&nbsp; I am sure



<P>I shall be very happy to chaperon you at any time till



<P>I am confined, if Mrs. Dashwood should not like to go



<P>into public."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They thanked her; but were obliged to resist

all



<P>her entreaties.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, my love," cried Mrs. Palmer to her husband,



<P>who just then entered the room--"you must help me to



<P>persuade the Miss Dashwoods to go to town this winter."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Her love made no answer; and after slightly

bowing



<P>to the ladies, began complaining of the weather.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How horrid all this is!" said he.&nbsp; "Such

weather



<P>makes every thing and every body disgusting.&nbsp; Dullness



<P>is as much produced within doors as without, by rain.



<P>It makes one detest all one's acquaintance.&nbsp; What the



<P>devil does Sir John mean by not having a billiard room



<P>in his house?&nbsp; How few people know what comfort is!&nbsp; Sir



<P>John is as stupid as the weather."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The rest of the company soon dropt in.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am afraid, Miss Marianne," said Sir John,

"you have



<P>not been able to take your usual walk to Allenham today."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne looked very grave and said nothing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, don't be so sly before us," said Mrs.

Palmer;



<P>"for we know all about it, I assure you; and I admire your



<P>taste very much, for I think he is extremely handsome.



<P>We do not live a great way from him in the country, you know.



<P>Not above ten miles, I dare say."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Much nearer thirty," said her husband.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Ah, well! there is not much difference.



<P>I never was at his house; but they say it is a sweet



<P>pretty place."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "As vile a spot as I ever saw in my life,"



<P>said Mr. Palmer.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne remained perfectly silent, though

her



<P>countenance betrayed her interest in what was said.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Is it very ugly?" continued Mrs. Palmer--"then

it



<P>must be some other place that is so pretty I suppose."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When they were seated in the dining room, Sir

John



<P>observed with regret that they were only eight all together.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "My dear," said he to his lady, "it is very

provoking



<P>that we should be so few.&nbsp; Why did not you ask the Gilberts



<P>to come to us today?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Did not I tell you, Sir John, when you spoke

to me



<P>about it before, that it could not be done?&nbsp; They dined



<P>with us last."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You and I, Sir John," said Mrs. Jennings,



<P>"should not stand upon such ceremony."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Then you would be very ill-bred," cried Mr.

Palmer.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "My love you contradict every body," said his

wife



<P>with her usual laugh.&nbsp; "Do you know that you are quite rude?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I did not know I contradicted any body in

calling



<P>your mother ill-bred."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Ay, you may abuse me as you please," said

the good-natured



<P>old lady, "you have taken Charlotte off my hands, and cannot



<P>give her back again.&nbsp; So there I have the whip hand of you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Charlotte laughed heartily to think that her



<P>husband could not get rid of her; and exultingly said,



<P>she did not care how cross he was to her, as they must



<P>live together.&nbsp; It was impossible for any one to be more



<P>thoroughly good-natured, or more determined to be happy



<P>than Mrs. Palmer.&nbsp; The studied indifference, insolence,



<P>and discontent of her husband gave her no pain;



<P>and when he scolded or abused her, she was highly diverted.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Mr. Palmer is so droll!" said she, in a whisper,



<P>to Elinor.&nbsp; "He is always out of humour."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor was not inclined, after a little observation,



<P>to give him credit for being so genuinely and unaffectedly



<P>ill-natured or ill-bred as he wished to appear.



<P>His temper might perhaps be a little soured by finding,



<P>like many others of his sex, that through some unaccountable



<P>bias in favour of beauty, he was the husband of a very silly



<P>woman,--but she knew that this kind of blunder was too



<P>common for any sensible man to be lastingly hurt by it.--



<P>It was rather a wish of distinction, she believed,



<P>which produced his contemptuous treatment of every body,



<P>and his general abuse of every thing before him.



<P>It was the desire of appearing superior to other people.



<P>The motive was too common to be wondered at; but the means,



<P>however they might succeed by establishing his superiority



<P>in ill-breeding, were not likely to attach any one to him



<P>except his wife.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, my dear Miss Dashwood," said Mrs. Palmer

soon afterwards,



<P>"I have got such a favour to ask of you and your sister.



<P>Will you come and spend some time at Cleveland this



<P>Christmas?&nbsp; Now, pray do,--and come while the Westons are



<P>with us.&nbsp; You cannot think how happy I shall be!&nbsp; It will



<P>be quite delightful!--My love," applying to her husband,



<P>"don't you long to have the Miss Dashwoods come to Cleveland?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Certainly," he replied, with a sneer--"I came



<P>into Devonshire with no other view."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "There now,"--said his lady, "you see Mr. Palmer



<P>expects you; so you cannot refuse to come."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They both eagerly and resolutely declined her

invitation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But indeed you must and shall come.&nbsp;

I am sure you



<P>will like it of all things.&nbsp; The Westons will be with us,



<P>and it will be quite delightful.&nbsp; You cannot think



<P>what a sweet place Cleveland is; and we are so gay now,



<P>for Mr. Palmer is always going about the country canvassing



<P>against the election; and so many people came to dine



<P>with us that I never saw before, it is quite charming!&nbsp; But,



<P>poor fellow! it is very fatiguing to him! for he is forced



<P>to make every body like him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor could hardly keep her countenance as

she



<P>assented to the hardship of such an obligation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How charming it will be," said Charlotte,

"when he



<P>is in Parliament!--won't it? How I shall laugh!&nbsp; It will



<P>be so ridiculous to see all his letters directed to him



<P>with an M.P.--But do you know, he says, he will never frank



<P>for me?&nbsp; He declares he won't.&nbsp; Don't you, Mr. Palmer?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mr. Palmer took no notice of her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "He cannot bear writing, you know," she continued--



<P>"he says it is quite shocking."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No," said he, "I never said any thing so irrational.



<P>Don't palm all your abuses of languages upon me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "There now; you see how droll he is.&nbsp;

This is always



<P>the way with him!&nbsp; Sometimes he won't speak to me for half



<P>a day together, and then he comes out with something



<P>so droll--all about any thing in the world."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She surprised Elinor very much as they returned



<P>into the drawing-room, by asking her whether she did



<P>not like Mr. Palmer excessively.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Certainly," said Elinor; "he seems very agreeable."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well--I am so glad you do.&nbsp; I thought

you would,



<P>he is so pleasant; and Mr. Palmer is excessively pleased



<P>with you and your sisters I can tell you, and you can't



<P>think how disappointed he will be if you don't come



<P>to Cleveland.--I can't imagine why you should object



<P>to it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor was again obliged to decline her invitation;



<P>and by changing the subject, put a stop to her entreaties.



<P>She thought it probable that as they lived in the



<P>same county, Mrs. Palmer might be able to give some



<P>more particular account of Willoughby's general



<P>character, than could be gathered from the Middletons'



<P>partial acquaintance with him; and she was eager to gain



<P>from any one, such a confirmation of his merits as might



<P>remove the possibility of fear from Marianne.&nbsp; She began



<P>by inquiring if they saw much of Mr. Willoughby at Cleveland,



<P>and whether they were intimately acquainted with him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh dear, yes; I know him extremely well,"



<P>replied Mrs. Palmer;--"Not that I ever spoke



<P>to him, indeed; but I have seen him for ever in town.



<P>Somehow or other I never happened to be staying at Barton



<P>while he was at Allenham.&nbsp; Mama saw him here once before;--



<P>but I was with my uncle at Weymouth.&nbsp; However, I dare say



<P>we should have seen a great deal of him in Somersetshire,



<P>if it had not happened very unluckily that we should never



<P>have been in the country together.&nbsp; He is very little



<P>at Combe, I believe; but if he were ever so much there,



<P>I do not think Mr. Palmer would visit him, for he is



<P>in the opposition, you know, and besides it is such a



<P>way off.&nbsp; I know why you inquire about him, very well;



<P>your sister is to marry him.&nbsp; I am monstrous glad of it,



<P>for then I shall have her for a neighbour you know."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Upon my word," replied Elinor, "you know much



<P>more of the matter than I do, if you have any reason



<P>to expect such a match."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Don't pretend to deny it, because you know

it is



<P>what every body talks of.&nbsp; I assure you I heard of it



<P>in my way through town."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "My dear Mrs. Palmer!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Upon my honour I did.--I met Colonel Brandon



<P>Monday morning in Bond-street, just before we left town,



<P>and he told me of it directly."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You surprise me very much.&nbsp; Colonel Brandon

tell



<P>you of it!&nbsp; Surely you must be mistaken.&nbsp; To give such



<P>intelligence to a person who could not be interested in it,



<P>even if it were true, is not what I should expect Colonel



<P>Brandon to do."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But I do assure you it was so, for all that,



<P>and I will tell you how it happened.&nbsp; When we met him,



<P>he turned back and walked with us; and so we began talking



<P>of my brother and sister, and one thing and another,



<P>and I said to him, 'So, Colonel, there is a new family



<P>come to Barton cottage, I hear, and mama sends me word



<P>they are very pretty, and that one of them is going to be



<P>married to Mr. Willoughby of Combe Magna.&nbsp; Is it true,



<P>pray? for of course you must know, as you have been in



<P>Devonshire so lately.'"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And what did the Colonel say?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh--he did not say much; but he looked as

if he



<P>knew it to be true, so from that moment I set it down



<P>as certain.&nbsp; It will be quite delightful, I declare!



<P>When is it to take place?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Mr. Brandon was very well I hope?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh! yes, quite well; and so full of your praises,



<P>he did nothing but say fine things of you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am flattered by his commendation.&nbsp;

He seems



<P>an excellent man; and I think him uncommonly pleasing."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "So do I.--He is such a charming man, that

it



<P>is quite a pity he should be so grave and so dull.



<P>Mamma says HE was in love with your sister too.--



<P>I assure you it was a great compliment if he was, for he



<P>hardly ever falls in love with any body."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Is Mr. Willoughby much known in your part



<P>of Somersetshire?" said Elinor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh! yes, extremely well; that is, I do not

believe



<P>many people are acquainted with him, because Combe Magna



<P>is so far off; but they all think him extremely agreeable



<P>I assure you.&nbsp; Nobody is more liked than Mr. Willoughby



<P>wherever he goes, and so you may tell your sister.



<P>She is a monstrous lucky girl to get him, upon my honour;



<P>not but that he is much more lucky in getting her,



<P>because she is so very handsome and agreeable, that nothing



<P>can be good enough for her.&nbsp; However, I don't think



<P>her hardly at all handsomer than you, I assure you;



<P>for I think you both excessively pretty, and so does



<P>Mr. Palmer too I am sure, though we could not get him



<P>to own it last night."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Palmer's information respecting Willoughby



<P>was not very material; but any testimony in his favour,



<P>however small, was pleasing to her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am so glad we are got acquainted at last,"



<P>continued Charlotte.--"And now I hope we shall always be



<P>great friends.&nbsp; You can't think how much I longed to see you!



<P>It is so delightful that you should live at the cottage!



<P>Nothing can be like it, to be sure!&nbsp; And I am so glad



<P>your sister is going to be well married!&nbsp; I hope you will



<P>be a great deal at Combe Magna.&nbsp; It is a sweet place,



<P>by all accounts."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You have been long acquainted with Colonel

Brandon,



<P>have not you?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes, a great while; ever since my sister married.--



<P>He was a particular friend of Sir John's. I believe,"



<P>she added in a low voice, "he would have been very



<P>glad to have had me, if he could.&nbsp; Sir John and Lady



<P>Middleton wished it very much.&nbsp; But mama did not think



<P>the match good enough for me, otherwise Sir John would



<P>have mentioned it to the Colonel, and we should have been



<P>married immediately."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Did not Colonel Brandon know of Sir John's

proposal



<P>to your mother before it was made?&nbsp; Had he never owned



<P>his affection to yourself?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, no; but if mama had not objected to it,



<P>I dare say he would have liked it of all things.



<P>He had not seen me then above twice, for it was before



<P>I left school.&nbsp; However, I am much happier as I am.



<P>Mr. Palmer is the kind of man I like."



<P>
