</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter34</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 34</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. John Dashwood had so much confidence in

her



<P>husband's judgment, that she waited the very next day



<P>both on Mrs. Jennings and her daughter; and her



<P>confidence was rewarded by finding even the former,



<P>even the woman with whom her sisters were staying,



<P>by no means unworthy her notice; and as for Lady Middleton,



<P>she found her one of the most charming women in the world!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lady Middleton was equally pleased with Mrs.

Dashwood.



<P>There was a kind of cold hearted selfishness on both sides,



<P>which mutually attracted them; and they sympathised



<P>with each other in an insipid propriety of demeanor,



<P>and a general want of understanding.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The same manners, however, which recommended

Mrs. John



<P>Dashwood to the good opinion of Lady Middleton did not suit



<P>the fancy of Mrs. Jennings, and to HER she appeared nothing



<P>more than a little proud-looking woman of uncordial address,



<P>who met her husband's sisters without any affection,



<P>and almost without having anything to say to them;



<P>for of the quarter of an hour bestowed on Berkeley Street,



<P>she sat at least seven minutes and a half in silence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor wanted very much to know, though she

did



<P>not chuse to ask, whether Edward was then in town;



<P>but nothing would have induced Fanny voluntarily



<P>to mention his name before her, till able to tell her



<P>that his marriage with Miss Morton was resolved on,



<P>or till her husband's expectations on Colonel Brandon



<P>were answered; because she believed them still so very



<P>much attached to each other, that they could not be too



<P>sedulously divided in word and deed on every occasion.



<P>The intelligence however, which SHE would not give,



<P>soon flowed from another quarter.&nbsp; Lucy came very shortly



<P>to claim Elinor's compassion on being unable to see Edward,



<P>though he had arrived in town with Mr. and Mrs. Dashwood.



<P>He dared not come to Bartlett's Buildings for fear



<P>of detection, and though their mutual impatience to meet,



<P>was not to be told, they could do nothing at present



<P>but write.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Edward assured them himself of his being in

town,



<P>within a very short time, by twice calling in Berkeley Street.



<P>Twice was his card found on the table, when they returned



<P>from their morning's engagements.&nbsp; Elinor was pleased



<P>that he had called; and still more pleased that she had



<P>missed him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The Dashwoods were so prodigiously delighted



<P>with the Middletons, that, though not much in the habit



<P>of giving anything, they determined to give them--



<P>a dinner; and soon after their acquaintance began,



<P>invited them to dine in Harley Street, where they had



<P>taken a very good house for three months.&nbsp; Their sisters



<P>and Mrs. Jennings were invited likewise, and John Dashwood



<P>was careful to secure Colonel Brandon, who, always glad



<P>to be where the Miss Dashwoods were, received his eager



<P>civilities with some surprise, but much more pleasure.



<P>They were to meet Mrs. Ferrars; but Elinor could not learn



<P>whether her sons were to be of the party.&nbsp; The expectation



<P>of seeing HER, however, was enough to make her interested



<P>in the engagement; for though she could now meet Edward's



<P>mother without that strong anxiety which had once promised



<P>to attend such an introduction, though she could now see



<P>her with perfect indifference as to her opinion of herself,



<P>her desire of being in company with Mrs. Ferrars,



<P>her curiosity to know what she was like, was as lively as ever.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The interest with which she thus anticipated

the



<P>party, was soon afterwards increased, more powerfully



<P>than pleasantly, by her hearing that the Miss Steeles



<P>were also to be at it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; So well had they recommended themselves to

Lady Middleton,



<P>so agreeable had their assiduities made them to her,



<P>that though Lucy was certainly not so elegant, and her



<P>sister not even genteel, she was as ready as Sir John



<P>to ask them to spend a week or two in Conduit Street;



<P>and it happened to be particularly convenient to the Miss



<P>Steeles, as soon as the Dashwoods' invitation was known,



<P>that their visit should begin a few days before the party



<P>took place.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Their claims to the notice of Mrs. John Dashwood,



<P>as the nieces of the gentleman who for many years had



<P>had the care of her brother, might not have done much,



<P>however, towards procuring them seats at her table;



<P>but as Lady Middleton's guests they must be welcome; and Lucy,



<P>who had long wanted to be personally known to the family,



<P>to have a nearer view of their characters and her own



<P>difficulties, and to have an opportunity of endeavouring



<P>to please them, had seldom been happier in her life,



<P>than she was on receiving Mrs. John Dashwood's card.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; On Elinor its effect was very different.&nbsp;

She began



<P>immediately to determine, that Edward who lived with



<P>his mother, must be asked as his mother was, to a party



<P>given by his sister; and to see him for the first time,



<P>after all that passed, in the company of Lucy!--she hardly



<P>knew how she could bear it!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; These apprehensions, perhaps, were not founded



<P>entirely on reason, and certainly not at all on truth.



<P>They were relieved however, not by her own recollection,



<P>but by the good will of Lucy, who believed herself to be



<P>inflicting a severe disappointment when she told her



<P>that Edward certainly would not be in Harley Street on Tuesday,



<P>and even hoped to be carrying the pain still farther



<P>by persuading her that he was kept away by the extreme



<P>affection for herself, which he could not conceal when they



<P>were together.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The important Tuesday came that was to introduce



<P>the two young ladies to this formidable mother-in-law.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Pity me, dear Miss Dashwood!" said Lucy, as

they



<P>walked up the stairs together--for the Middletons arrived



<P>so directly after Mrs. Jennings, that they all followed



<P>the servant at the same time--"There is nobody here but



<P>you, that can feel for me.--I declare I can hardly stand.



<P>Good gracious!--In a moment I shall see the person that all



<P>my happiness depends on--that is to be my mother!"--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor could have given her immediate relief



<P>by suggesting the possibility of its being Miss Morton's mother,



<P>rather than her own, whom they were about to behold;



<P>but instead of doing that, she assured her, and with



<P>great sincerity, that she did pity her--to the utter



<P>amazement of Lucy, who, though really uncomfortable herself,



<P>hoped at least to be an object of irrepressible envy to Elinor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Ferrars was a little, thin woman, upright,



<P>even to formality, in her figure, and serious,



<P>even to sourness, in her aspect.&nbsp; Her complexion was sallow;



<P>and her features small, without beauty, and naturally



<P>without expression; but a lucky contraction of the brow



<P>had rescued her countenance from the disgrace of insipidity,



<P>by giving it the strong characters of pride and ill nature.



<P>She was not a woman of many words; for, unlike people



<P>in general, she proportioned them to the number of



<P>her ideas; and of the few syllables that did escape her,



<P>not one fell to the share of Miss Dashwood, whom she eyed



<P>with the spirited determination of disliking her at all events.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor could not NOW be made unhappy by this

behaviour.--



<P>A few months ago it would have hurt her exceedingly; but it



<P>was not in Mrs. Ferrars' power to distress her by it now;--



<P>and the difference of her manners to the Miss Steeles,



<P>a difference which seemed purposely made to humble her more,



<P>only amused her.&nbsp; She could not but smile to see the graciousness



<P>of both mother and daughter towards the very person--



<P>for Lucy was particularly distinguished--whom of all others,



<P>had they known as much as she did, they would have been most



<P>anxious to mortify; while she herself, who had comparatively



<P>no power to wound them, sat pointedly slighted by both.



<P>But while she smiled at a graciousness so misapplied,



<P>she could not reflect on the mean-spirited folly from



<P>which it sprung, nor observe the studied attentions



<P>with which the Miss Steeles courted its continuance,



<P>without thoroughly despising them all four.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lucy was all exultation on being so honorably



<P>distinguished; and Miss Steele wanted only to be teazed



<P>about Dr. Davis to be perfectly happy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The dinner was a grand one, the servants were

numerous,



<P>and every thing bespoke the Mistress's inclination



<P>for show, and the Master's ability to support it.



<P>In spite of the improvements and additions which were



<P>making to the Norland estate, and in spite of its owner



<P>having once been within some thousand pounds of being



<P>obliged to sell out at a loss, nothing gave any symptom



<P>of that indigence which he had tried to infer from it;--



<P>no poverty of any kind, except of conversation, appeared--



<P>but there, the deficiency was considerable.&nbsp; John Dashwood



<P>had not much to say for himself that was worth hearing,



<P>and his wife had still less.&nbsp; But there was no peculiar



<P>disgrace in this; for it was very much the case with



<P>the chief of their visitors, who almost all laboured



<P>under one or other of these disqualifications for being



<P>agreeable--Want of sense, either natural or improved--want



<P>of elegance--want of spirits--or want of temper.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When the ladies withdrew to the drawing-room



<P>after dinner, this poverty was particularly evident,



<P>for the gentlemen HAD supplied the discourse with some



<P>variety--the variety of politics, inclosing land,



<P>and breaking horses--but then it was all over; and one



<P>subject only engaged the ladies till coffee came in,



<P>which was the comparative heights of Harry Dashwood,



<P>and Lady Middleton's second son William, who were nearly



<P>of the same age.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Had both the children been there, the affair

might



<P>have been determined too easily by measuring them at once;



<P>but as Harry only was present, it was all conjectural



<P>assertion on both sides; and every body had a right to



<P>be equally positive in their opinion, and to repeat it



<P>over and over again as often as they liked.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The parties stood thus:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The two mothers, though each really convinced

that



<P>her own son was the tallest, politely decided in favour



<P>of the other.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The two grandmothers, with not less partiality,



<P>but more sincerity, were equally earnest in support



<P>of their own descendant.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lucy, who was hardly less anxious to please

one parent



<P>than the other, thought the boys were both remarkably tall



<P>for their age, and could not conceive that there could



<P>be the smallest difference in the world between them;



<P>and Miss Steele, with yet greater address gave it,



<P>as fast as she could, in favour of each.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor, having once delivered her opinion on



<P>William's side, by which she offended Mrs. Ferrars and



<P>Fanny still more, did not see the necessity of enforcing



<P>it by any farther assertion; and Marianne, when called



<P>on for her's, offended them all, by declaring that she



<P>had no opinion to give, as she had never thought about it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Before her removing from Norland, Elinor had

painted



<P>a very pretty pair of screens for her sister-in-law,



<P>which being now just mounted and brought home,



<P>ornamented her present drawing room; and these screens,



<P>catching the eye of John Dashwood on his following



<P>the other gentlemen into the room, were officiously



<P>handed by him to Colonel Brandon for his admiration.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "These are done by my eldest sister," said

he; "and you,



<P>as a man of taste, will, I dare say, be pleased with them.



<P>I do not know whether you have ever happened to see any



<P>of her performances before, but she is in general reckoned



<P>to draw extremely well."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The Colonel, though disclaiming all pretensions



<P>to connoisseurship, warmly admired the screens, as he



<P>would have done any thing painted by Miss Dashwood;



<P>and on the curiosity of the others being of course excited,



<P>they were handed round for general inspection.



<P>Mrs. Ferrars, not aware of their being Elinor's work,



<P>particularly requested to look at them; and after they had



<P>received gratifying testimony of Lady Middletons's approbation,



<P>Fanny presented them to her mother, considerately informing



<P>her, at the same time, that they were done by Miss Dashwood.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Hum"--said Mrs. Ferrars--"very pretty,"--and

without



<P>regarding them at all, returned them to her daughter.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Perhaps Fanny thought for a moment that her

mother



<P>had been quite rude enough,--for, colouring a little,



<P>she immediately said,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "They are very pretty, ma'am--an't they?" But

then again,



<P>the dread of having been too civil, too encouraging herself,



<P>probably came over her, for she presently added,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Do you not think they are something in Miss



<P>Morton's style of painting, Ma'am?--She DOES paint most



<P>delightfully!--How beautifully her last landscape is done!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Beautifully indeed!&nbsp; But SHE does every

thing well."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne could not bear this.--She was already



<P>greatly displeased with Mrs. Ferrars; and such ill-timed



<P>praise of another, at Elinor's expense, though she



<P>had not any notion of what was principally meant by it,



<P>provoked her immediately to say with warmth,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "This is admiration of a very particular kind!--



<P>what is Miss Morton to us?--who knows, or who cares,



<P>for her?--it is Elinor of whom WE think and speak."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; And so saying, she took the screens out of

her



<P>sister-in-law's hands, to admire them herself as they



<P>ought to be admired.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Ferrars looked exceedingly angry, and

drawing



<P>herself up more stiffly than ever, pronounced in retort



<P>this bitter philippic, "Miss Morton is Lord Morton's daughter."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Fanny looked very angry too, and her husband

was



<P>all in a fright at his sister's audacity.&nbsp; Elinor was



<P>much more hurt by Marianne's warmth than she had been



<P>by what produced it; but Colonel Brandon's eyes, as they



<P>were fixed on Marianne, declared that he noticed only



<P>what was amiable in it, the affectionate heart which could



<P>not bear to see a sister slighted in the smallest point.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne's feelings did not stop here.&nbsp;

The cold



<P>insolence of Mrs. Ferrars's general behaviour to her sister,



<P>seemed, to her, to foretell such difficulties and distresses



<P>to Elinor, as her own wounded heart taught her to think



<P>of with horror; and urged by a strong impulse of



<P>affectionate sensibility, she moved after a moment,



<P>to her sister's chair, and putting one arm round her neck,



<P>and one cheek close to hers, said in a low, but eager,



<P>voice,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Dear, dear Elinor, don't mind them.&nbsp;

Don't let them



<P>make YOU unhappy."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She could say no more; her spirits were quite

overcome,



<P>and hiding her face on Elinor's shoulder, she burst



<P>into tears.&nbsp; Every body's attention was called, and almost



<P>every body was concerned.--Colonel Brandon rose up and went



<P>to them without knowing what he did.--Mrs. Jennings,



<P>with a very intelligent "Ah! poor dear," immediately gave



<P>her her salts; and Sir John felt so desperately enraged



<P>against the author of this nervous distress, that he



<P>instantly changed his seat to one close by Lucy Steele,



<P>and gave her, in a whisper, a brief account of the whole



<P>shocking affair.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In a few minutes, however, Marianne was recovered



<P>enough to put an end to the bustle, and sit down among



<P>the rest; though her spirits retained the impression



<P>of what had passed, the whole evening.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Poor Marianne!" said her brother to Colonel

Brandon,



<P>in a low voice, as soon as he could secure his attention,--



<P>"She has not such good health as her sister,--she is very



<P>nervous,--she has not Elinor's constitution;--and one must



<P>allow that there is something very trying to a young woman



<P>who HAS BEEN a beauty in the loss of her personal attractions.



<P>You would not think it perhaps, but Marianne WAS remarkably



<P>handsome a few months ago; quite as handsome as Elinor.--



<P>Now you see it is all gone."



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter35</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 35</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor's curiosity to see Mrs. Ferrars was

satisfied.--



<P>She had found in her every thing that could tend to make



<P>a farther connection between the families undesirable.--



<P>She had seen enough of her pride, her meanness, and her



<P>determined prejudice against herself, to comprehend all



<P>the difficulties that must have perplexed the engagement,



<P>and retarded the marriage, of Edward and herself, had he been



<P>otherwise free;--and she had seen almost enough to be thankful



<P>for her OWN sake, that one greater obstacle preserved her



<P>from suffering under any other of Mrs. Ferrars's creation,



<P>preserved her from all dependence upon her caprice, or any



<P>solicitude for her good opinion.&nbsp; Or at least, if she did not



<P>bring herself quite to rejoice in Edward's being fettered



<P>to Lucy, she determined, that had Lucy been more amiable,



<P>she OUGHT to have rejoiced.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She wondered that Lucy's spirits could be so

very much



<P>elevated by the civility of Mrs. Ferrars;--that her interest



<P>and her vanity should so very much blind her as to make



<P>the attention which seemed only paid her because she was



<P>NOT ELINOR, appear a compliment to herself--or to allow



<P>her to derive encouragement from a preference only given her,



<P>because her real situation was unknown.&nbsp; But that it was so,



<P>had not only been declared by Lucy's eyes at the time,



<P>but was declared over again the next morning more openly,



<P>for at her particular desire, Lady Middleton set her down



<P>in Berkeley Street on the chance of seeing Elinor alone,



<P>to tell her how happy she was.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The chance proved a lucky one, for a message

from



<P>Mrs. Palmer soon after she arrived, carried Mrs. Jennings away.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "My dear friend," cried Lucy, as soon as they

were



<P>by themselves, "I come to talk to you of my happiness.



<P>Could anything be so flattering as Mrs. Ferrars's way



<P>of treating me yesterday?&nbsp; So exceeding affable as she



<P>was!--You know how I dreaded the thoughts of seeing her;--



<P>but the very moment I was introduced, there was such an



<P>affability in her behaviour as really should seem to say,



<P>she had quite took a fancy to me.&nbsp; Now was not it so?--



<P>You saw it all; and was not you quite struck with it?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "She was certainly very civil to you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Civil!--Did you see nothing but only civility?--



<P>I saw a vast deal more.&nbsp; Such kindness as fell to the share



<P>of nobody but me!--No pride, no hauteur, and your sister



<P>just the same--all sweetness and affability!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor wished to talk of something else, but

Lucy still



<P>pressed her to own that she had reason for her happiness;



<P>and Elinor was obliged to go on.--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Undoubtedly, if they had known your engagement,"



<P>said she, "nothing could be more flattering than their



<P>treatment of you;--but as that was not the case"--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I guessed you would say so"--replied Lucy



<P>quickly--"but there was no reason in the world why



<P>Mrs. Ferrars should seem to like me, if she did not,



<P>and her liking me is every thing.&nbsp; You shan't talk me



<P>out of my satisfaction.&nbsp; I am sure it will all end well,



<P>and there will be no difficulties at all, to what I



<P>used to think.&nbsp; Mrs. Ferrars is a charming woman,



<P>and so is your sister.&nbsp; They are both delightful women,



<P>indeed!--I wonder I should never hear you say how agreeable



<P>Mrs. Dashwood was!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; To this Elinor had no answer to make, and did

not



<P>attempt any.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Are you ill, Miss Dashwood?--you seem low--you



<P>don't speak;--sure you an't well."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I never was in better health."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am glad of it with all my heart; but really

you did



<P>not look it.&nbsp; I should be sorry to have YOU ill; you, that have



<P>been the greatest comfort to me in the world!--Heaven



<P>knows what I should have done without your friendship."--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor tried to make a civil answer, though

doubting



<P>her own success.&nbsp; But it seemed to satisfy Lucy, for she



<P>directly replied,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Indeed I am perfectly convinced of your regard



<P>for me, and next to Edward's love, it is the greatest



<P>comfort I have.--Poor Edward!--But now there is one



<P>good thing, we shall be able to meet, and meet pretty often,



<P>for Lady Middleton's delighted with Mrs. Dashwood,



<P>so we shall be a good deal in Harley Street, I dare say,



<P>and Edward spends half his time with his sister--besides,



<P>Lady Middleton and Mrs. Ferrars will visit now;--



<P>and Mrs. Ferrars and your sister were both so good to say



<P>more than once, they should always be glad to see me.--



<P>They are such charming women!--I am sure if ever you



<P>tell your sister what I think of her, you cannot speak



<P>too high."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; But Elinor would not give her any encouragement



<P>to hope that she SHOULD tell her sister.&nbsp; Lucy continued.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am sure I should have seen it in a moment,



<P>if Mrs. Ferrars had took a dislike to me.&nbsp; If she had only



<P>made me a formal courtesy, for instance, without saying



<P>a word, and never after had took any notice of me,



<P>and never looked at me in a pleasant way--you know



<P>what I mean--if I had been treated in that forbidding



<P>sort of way, I should have gave it all up in despair.



<P>I could not have stood it.&nbsp; For where she DOES dislike,



<P>I know it is most violent."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor was prevented from making any reply

to this



<P>civil triumph, by the door's being thrown open, the servant's



<P>announcing Mr. Ferrars, and Edward's immediately walking in.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; It was a very awkward moment; and the countenance

of each



<P>shewed that it was so.&nbsp; They all looked exceedingly foolish;



<P>and Edward seemed to have as great an inclination to walk



<P>out of the room again, as to advance farther into it.



<P>The very circumstance, in its unpleasantest form,



<P>which they would each have been most anxious to avoid,



<P>had fallen on them.--They were not only all three together,



<P>but were together without the relief of any other person.



<P>The ladies recovered themselves first.&nbsp; It was not Lucy's



<P>business to put herself forward, and the appearance of



<P>secrecy must still be kept up.&nbsp; She could therefore only



<P>LOOK her tenderness, and after slightly addressing him,



<P>said no more.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; But Elinor had more to do; and so anxious was

she,



<P>for his sake and her own, to do it well, that she



<P>forced herself, after a moment's recollection,



<P>to welcome him, with a look and manner that were almost easy,



<P>and almost open; and another struggle, another effort still



<P>improved them.&nbsp; She would not allow the presence of Lucy,



<P>nor the consciousness of some injustice towards herself,



<P>to deter her from saying that she was happy to see him,



<P>and that she had very much regretted being from home,



<P>when he called before in Berkeley Street.&nbsp; She would



<P>not be frightened from paying him those attentions which,



<P>as a friend and almost a relation, were his due, by the



<P>observant eyes of Lucy, though she soon perceived them



<P>to be narrowly watching her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Her manners gave some re-assurance to Edward,

and he



<P>had courage enough to sit down; but his embarrassment still



<P>exceeded that of the ladies in a proportion, which the case



<P>rendered reasonable, though his sex might make it rare;



<P>for his heart had not the indifference of Lucy's, nor



<P>could his conscience have quite the ease of Elinor's.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lucy, with a demure and settled air, seemed

determined



<P>to make no contribution to the comfort of the others,



<P>and would not say a word; and almost every thing that WAS



<P>said, proceeded from Elinor, who was obliged to volunteer



<P>all the information about her mother's health, their coming



<P>to town, &amp;c. which Edward ought to have inquired about,



<P>but never did.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Her exertions did not stop here; for she soon



<P>afterwards felt herself so heroically disposed as



<P>to determine, under pretence of fetching Marianne,



<P>to leave the others by themselves; and she really did it,



<P>and THAT in the handsomest manner, for she loitered away



<P>several minutes on the landing-place, with the most



<P>high-minded fortitude, before she went to her sister.



<P>When that was once done, however, it was time for the raptures



<P>of Edward to cease; for Marianne's joy hurried her into



<P>the drawing-room immediately.&nbsp; Her pleasure in seeing him



<P>was like every other of her feelings, strong in itself,



<P>and strongly spoken.&nbsp; She met him with a hand that would



<P>be taken, and a voice that expressed the affection of a sister.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Dear Edward!" she cried, "this is a moment

of great



<P>happiness!--This would almost make amends for every thing?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Edward tried to return her kindness as it deserved,



<P>but before such witnesses he dared not say half what he



<P>really felt.&nbsp; Again they all sat down, and for a moment



<P>or two all were silent; while Marianne was looking with the



<P>most speaking tenderness, sometimes at Edward and sometimes



<P>at Elinor, regretting only that their delight in each



<P>other should be checked by Lucy's unwelcome presence.



<P>Edward was the first to speak, and it was to notice



<P>Marianne's altered looks, and express his fear of her



<P>not finding London agree with her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, don't think of me!" she replied with spirited



<P>earnestness, though her eyes were filled with tears



<P>as she spoke, "don't think of MY health.&nbsp; Elinor is well,



<P>you see.&nbsp; That must be enough for us both."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This remark was not calculated to make Edward

or



<P>Elinor more easy, nor to conciliate the good will of Lucy,



<P>who looked up at Marianne with no very benignant expression.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Do you like London?" said Edward, willing

to say



<P>any thing that might introduce another subject.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Not at all.&nbsp; I expected much pleasure

in it,



<P>but I have found none.&nbsp; The sight of you, Edward, is the



<P>only comfort it has afforded; and thank Heaven! you



<P>are what you always were!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She paused--no one spoke.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I think, Elinor," she presently added, "we

must



<P>employ Edward to take care of us in our return to Barton.



<P>In a week or two, I suppose, we shall be going; and, I trust,



<P>Edward will not be very unwilling to accept the charge."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Poor Edward muttered something, but what it

was,



<P>nobody knew, not even himself.&nbsp; But Marianne, who saw



<P>his agitation, and could easily trace it to whatever



<P>cause best pleased herself, was perfectly satisfied,



<P>and soon talked of something else.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "We spent such a day, Edward, in Harley Street



<P>yesterday!&nbsp; So dull, so wretchedly dull!--But I have much



<P>to say to you on that head, which cannot be said now."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; And with this admirable discretion did she

defer



<P>the assurance of her finding their mutual relatives more



<P>disagreeable than ever, and of her being particularly



<P>disgusted with his mother, till they were more in private.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But why were you not there, Edward?--Why did

you



<P>not come?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I was engaged elsewhere."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Engaged!&nbsp; But what was that, when such

friends



<P>were to be met?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Perhaps, Miss Marianne," cried Lucy, eager

to take



<P>some revenge on her, "you think young men never stand



<P>upon engagements, if they have no mind to keep them,



<P>little as well as great."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor was very angry, but Marianne seemed

entirely



<P>insensible of the sting; for she calmly replied,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Not so, indeed; for, seriously speaking, I

am very



<P>sure that conscience only kept Edward from Harley Street.



<P>And I really believe he HAS the most delicate conscience



<P>in the world; the most scrupulous in performing



<P>every engagement, however minute, and however it



<P>may make against his interest or pleasure.&nbsp; He is the



<P>most fearful of giving pain, of wounding expectation,



<P>and the most incapable of being selfish, of any body



<P>I ever saw.&nbsp; Edward, it is so, and I will say it.



<P>What! are you never to hear yourself praised!--Then you



<P>must be no friend of mine; for those who will accept



<P>of my love and esteem, must submit to my open commendation."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The nature of her commendation, in the present

case,



<P>however, happened to be particularly ill-suited to the



<P>feelings of two thirds of her auditors, and was so very



<P>unexhilarating to Edward, that he very soon got up to go away.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Going so soon!" said Marianne; "my dear Edward,



<P>this must not be."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; And drawing him a little aside, she whispered



<P>her persuasion that Lucy could not stay much longer.



<P>But even this encouragement failed, for he would go;



<P>and Lucy, who would have outstaid him, had his visit lasted



<P>two hours, soon afterwards went away.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What can bring her here so often?" said Marianne,



<P>on her leaving them.&nbsp; "Could not she see that we wanted



<P>her gone!--how teazing to Edward!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Why so?--we were all his friends, and Lucy

has been



<P>the longest known to him of any.&nbsp; It is but natural



<P>that he should like to see her as well as ourselves."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne looked at her steadily, and said,

"You know,



<P>Elinor, that this is a kind of talking which I cannot bear.



<P>If you only hope to have your assertion contradicted,



<P>as I must suppose to be the case, you ought to recollect



<P>that I am the last person in the world to do it.



<P>I cannot descend to be tricked out of assurances, that are



<P>not really wanted."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She then left the room; and Elinor dared not

follow



<P>her to say more, for bound as she was by her promise



<P>of secrecy to Lucy, she could give no information that



<P>would convince Marianne; and painful as the consequences



<P>of her still continuing in an error might be, she was



<P>obliged to submit to it.&nbsp; All that she could hope, was



<P>that Edward would not often expose her or himself to the



<P>distress of hearing Marianne's mistaken warmth, nor to the



<P>repetition of any other part of the pain that had attended



<P>their recent meeting--and this she had every reason to expect.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter21</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 21</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The Palmers returned to Cleveland the next

day,



<P>and the two families at Barton were again left to entertain



<P>each other.&nbsp; But this did not last long; Elinor had hardly



<P>got their last visitors out of her head, had hardly done



<P>wondering at Charlotte's being so happy without a cause,



<P>at Mr. Palmer's acting so simply, with good abilities,



<P>and at the strange unsuitableness which often existed between



<P>husband and wife, before Sir John's and Mrs. Jennings's



<P>active zeal in the cause of society, procured her some



<P>other new acquaintance to see and observe.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In a morning's excursion to Exeter, they had

met with



<P>two young ladies, whom Mrs. Jennings had the satisfaction



<P>of discovering to be her relations, and this was enough



<P>for Sir John to invite them directly to the park,



<P>as soon as their present engagements at Exeter were over.



<P>Their engagements at Exeter instantly gave way before



<P>such an invitation, and Lady Middleton was thrown into



<P>no little alarm on the return of Sir John, by hearing



<P>that she was very soon to receive a visit from two girls



<P>whom she had never seen in her life, and of whose elegance,--



<P>whose tolerable gentility even, she could have no proof;



<P>for the assurances of her husband and mother on that subject



<P>went for nothing at all.&nbsp; Their being her relations too



<P>made it so much the worse; and Mrs. Jennings's attempts



<P>at consolation were therefore unfortunately founded,



<P>when she advised her daughter not to care about their being



<P>so fashionable; because they were all cousins and must put



<P>up with one another.&nbsp; As it was impossible, however, now to



<P>prevent their coming, Lady Middleton resigned herself to the



<P>idea of it, with all the philosophy of a well-bred woman,



<P>contenting herself with merely giving her husband a gentle



<P>reprimand on the subject five or six times every day.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The young ladies arrived: their appearance

was by



<P>no means ungenteel or unfashionable.&nbsp; Their dress was



<P>very smart, their manners very civil, they were delighted



<P>with the house, and in raptures with the furniture,



<P>and they happened to be so doatingly fond of children



<P>that Lady Middleton's good opinion was engaged in their



<P>favour before they had been an hour at the Park.



<P>She declared them to be very agreeable girls indeed,



<P>which for her ladyship was enthusiastic admiration.



<P>Sir John's confidence in his own judgment rose with this



<P>animated praise, and he set off directly for the cottage



<P>to tell the Miss Dashwoods of the Miss Steeles' arrival,



<P>and to assure them of their being the sweetest girls



<P>in the world.&nbsp; From such commendation as this, however,



<P>there was not much to be learned; Elinor well knew



<P>that the sweetest girls in the world were to be met



<P>with in every part of England, under every possible



<P>variation of form, face, temper and understanding.



<P>Sir John wanted the whole family to walk to the Park directly



<P>and look at his guests.&nbsp; Benevolent, philanthropic man!&nbsp; It



<P>was painful to him even to keep a third cousin to himself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Do come now," said he--"pray come--you must

come--I



<P>declare you shall come--You can't think how you will



<P>like them.&nbsp; Lucy is monstrous pretty, and so good humoured



<P>and agreeable!&nbsp; The children are all hanging about her already,



<P>as if she was an old acquaintance.&nbsp; And they both long



<P>to see you of all things, for they have heard at Exeter



<P>that you are the most beautiful creatures in the world;



<P>and I have told them it is all very true, and a great



<P>deal more.&nbsp; You will be delighted with them I am sure.



<P>They have brought the whole coach full of playthings



<P>for the children.&nbsp; How can you be so cross as not to come?



<P>Why they are your cousins, you know, after a fashion.



<P>YOU are my cousins, and they are my wife's, so you must



<P>be related."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; But Sir John could not prevail.&nbsp; He could

only obtain



<P>a promise of their calling at the Park within a day or two,



<P>and then left them in amazement at their indifference,



<P>to walk home and boast anew of their attractions to the



<P>Miss Steeles, as he had been already boasting of the Miss



<P>Steeles to them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When their promised visit to the Park and consequent



<P>introduction to these young ladies took place, they found



<P>in the appearance of the eldest, who was nearly thirty,



<P>with a very plain and not a sensible face, nothing to admire;



<P>but in the other, who was not more than two or three



<P>and twenty, they acknowledged considerable beauty; her



<P>features were pretty, and she had a sharp quick eye,



<P>and a smartness of air, which though it did not give



<P>actual elegance or grace, gave distinction to her person.--



<P>Their manners were particularly civil, and Elinor soon



<P>allowed them credit for some kind of sense, when she



<P>saw with what constant and judicious attention they



<P>were making themselves agreeable to Lady Middleton.



<P>With her children they were in continual raptures,



<P>extolling their beauty, courting their notice, and humouring



<P>their whims; and such of their time as could be spared from



<P>the importunate demands which this politeness made on it,



<P>was spent in admiration of whatever her ladyship was doing,



<P>if she happened to be doing any thing, or in taking patterns



<P>of some elegant new dress, in which her appearance



<P>the day before had thrown them into unceasing delight.



<P>Fortunately for those who pay their court through



<P>such foibles, a fond mother, though, in pursuit of praise



<P>for her children, the most rapacious of human beings,



<P>is likewise the most credulous; her demands are exorbitant;



<P>but she will swallow any thing; and the excessive



<P>affection and endurance of the Miss Steeles towards



<P>her offspring were viewed therefore by Lady Middleton



<P>without the smallest surprise or distrust.&nbsp; She saw with



<P>maternal complacency all the impertinent encroachments



<P>and mischievous tricks to which her cousins submitted.



<P>She saw their sashes untied, their hair pulled about



<P>their ears, their work-bags searched, and their knives



<P>and scissors stolen away, and felt no doubt of its being



<P>a reciprocal enjoyment.&nbsp; It suggested no other surprise



<P>than that Elinor and Marianne should sit so composedly by,



<P>without claiming a share in what was passing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "John is in such spirits today!" said she,

on his



<P>taking Miss Steeles's pocket handkerchief, and throwing



<P>it out of window--"He is full of monkey tricks."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; And soon afterwards, on the second boy's violently



<P>pinching one of the same lady's fingers, she fondly observed,



<P>"How playful William is!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And here is my sweet little Annamaria," she

added,



<P>tenderly caressing a little girl of three years old,



<P>who had not made a noise for the last two minutes;



<P>"And she is always so gentle and quiet--Never was there



<P>such a quiet little thing!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; But unfortunately in bestowing these embraces,



<P>a pin in her ladyship's head dress slightly scratching



<P>the child's neck, produced from this pattern of gentleness



<P>such violent screams, as could hardly be outdone by any



<P>creature professedly noisy.&nbsp; The mother's consternation



<P>was excessive; but it could not surpass the alarm of the



<P>Miss Steeles, and every thing was done by all three,



<P>in so critical an emergency, which affection could suggest



<P>as likely to assuage the agonies of the little sufferer.



<P>She was seated in her mother's lap, covered with kisses,



<P>her wound bathed with lavender-water, by one of the



<P>Miss Steeles, who was on her knees to attend her,



<P>and her mouth stuffed with sugar plums by the other.



<P>With such a reward for her tears, the child was too wise



<P>to cease crying.&nbsp; She still screamed and sobbed lustily,



<P>kicked her two brothers for offering to touch her, and all



<P>their united soothings were ineffectual till Lady Middleton



<P>luckily remembering that in a scene of similar distress



<P>last week, some apricot marmalade had been successfully



<P>applied for a bruised temple, the same remedy was eagerly



<P>proposed for this unfortunate scratch, and a slight



<P>intermission of screams in the young lady on hearing it,



<P>gave them reason to hope that it would not be rejected.--



<P>She was carried out of the room therefore in her



<P>mother's arms, in quest of this medicine, and as the



<P>two boys chose to follow, though earnestly entreated



<P>by their mother to stay behind, the four young ladies



<P>were left in a quietness which the room had not known for



<P>many hours.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Poor little creatures!" said Miss Steele,

as soon



<P>as they were gone.&nbsp; "It might have been a very sad accident."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yet I hardly know how," cried Marianne, "unless

it



<P>had been under totally different circumstances.



<P>But this is the usual way of heightening alarm, where there



<P>is nothing to be alarmed at in reality."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What a sweet woman Lady Middleton is!" said

Lucy Steele.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne was silent; it was impossible for

her to say



<P>what she did not feel, however trivial the occasion;



<P>and upon Elinor therefore the whole task of telling lies



<P>when politeness required it, always fell.&nbsp; She did her



<P>best when thus called on, by speaking of Lady Middleton



<P>with more warmth than she felt, though with far less than



<P>Miss Lucy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And Sir John too," cried the elder sister,



<P>"what a charming man he is!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Here too, Miss Dashwood's commendation, being

only



<P>simple and just, came in without any eclat.&nbsp; She merely



<P>observed that he was perfectly good humoured and friendly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And what a charming little family they have!&nbsp;

I



<P>never saw such fine children in my life.--I declare I



<P>quite doat upon them already, and indeed I am always



<P>distractedly fond of children."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I should guess so," said Elinor, with a smile,



<P>"from what I have witnessed this morning."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have a notion," said Lucy, "you think the

little



<P>Middletons rather too much indulged; perhaps they may be the



<P>outside of enough; but it is so natural in Lady Middleton;



<P>and for my part, I love to see children full of life



<P>and spirits; I cannot bear them if they are tame and quiet."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I confess," replied Elinor, "that while I

am at



<P>Barton Park, I never think of tame and quiet children



<P>with any abhorrence."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; A short pause succeeded this speech, which

was first



<P>broken by Miss Steele, who seemed very much disposed



<P>for conversation, and who now said rather abruptly,



<P>"And how do you like Devonshire, Miss Dashwood?&nbsp; I suppose



<P>you were very sorry to leave Sussex."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In some surprise at the familiarity of this

question,



<P>or at least of the manner in which it was spoken,



<P>Elinor replied that she was.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Norland is a prodigious beautiful place, is

not it?"



<P>added Miss Steele.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "We have heard Sir John admire it excessively,"



<P>said Lucy, who seemed to think some apology necessary



<P>for the freedom of her sister.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I think every one MUST admire it," replied

Elinor,



<P>"who ever saw the place; though it is not to be supposed



<P>that any one can estimate its beauties as we do."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And had you a great many smart beaux there?&nbsp;

I



<P>suppose you have not so many in this part of the world;



<P>for my part, I think they are a vast addition always."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But why should you think," said Lucy, looking

ashamed



<P>of her sister, "that there are not as many genteel young



<P>men in Devonshire as Sussex?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Nay, my dear, I'm sure I don't pretend to

say that there



<P>an't.&nbsp; I'm sure there's a vast many smart beaux in Exeter;



<P>but you know, how could I tell what smart beaux there



<P>might be about Norland; and I was only afraid the Miss



<P>Dashwoods might find it dull at Barton, if they had not



<P>so many as they used to have.&nbsp; But perhaps you young ladies



<P>may not care about the beaux, and had as lief be without



<P>them as with them.&nbsp; For my part, I think they are vastly



<P>agreeable, provided they dress smart and behave civil.



<P>But I can't bear to see them dirty and nasty.&nbsp; Now there's



<P>Mr. Rose at Exeter, a prodigious smart young man,



<P>quite a beau, clerk to Mr. Simpson, you know, and yet if you



<P>do but meet him of a morning, he is not fit to be seen.--



<P>I suppose your brother was quite a beau, Miss Dashwood,



<P>before he married, as he was so rich?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Upon my word," replied Elinor, "I cannot tell

you,



<P>for I do not perfectly comprehend the meaning of the word.



<P>But this I can say, that if he ever was a beau before



<P>he married, he is one still for there is not the smallest



<P>alteration in him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh! dear! one never thinks of married men's

being



<P>beaux--they have something else to do."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Lord! Anne," cried her sister, "you can talk

of



<P>nothing but beaux;--you will make Miss Dashwood believe you



<P>think of nothing else." And then to turn the discourse,



<P>she began admiring the house and the furniture.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This specimen of the Miss Steeles was enough.



<P>The vulgar freedom and folly of the eldest left



<P>her no recommendation, and as Elinor was not blinded



<P>by the beauty, or the shrewd look of the youngest,



<P>to her want of real elegance and artlessness, she left



<P>the house without any wish of knowing them better.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Not so the Miss Steeles.--They came from Exeter,

well



<P>provided with admiration for the use of Sir John Middleton,



<P>his family, and all his relations, and no niggardly



<P>proportion was now dealt out to his fair cousins, whom they



<P>declared to be the most beautiful, elegant, accomplished,



<P>and agreeable girls they had ever beheld, and with whom



<P>they were particularly anxious to be better acquainted.--



<P>And to be better acquainted therefore, Elinor soon found



<P>was their inevitable lot, for as Sir John was entirely



<P>on the side of the Miss Steeles, their party would be



<P>too strong for opposition, and that kind of intimacy



<P>must be submitted to, which consists of sitting an hour



<P>or two together in the same room almost every day.



<P>Sir John could do no more; but he did not know that any



<P>more was required: to be together was, in his opinion,



<P>to be intimate, and while his continual schemes for their



<P>meeting were effectual, he had not a doubt of their being



<P>established friends.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; To do him justice, he did every thing in his

power



<P>to promote their unreserve, by making the Miss Steeles



<P>acquainted with whatever he knew or supposed of his cousins'



<P>situations in the most delicate particulars,--and Elinor



<P>had not seen them more than twice, before the eldest of



<P>them wished her joy on her sister's having been so lucky



<P>as to make a conquest of a very smart beau since she



<P>came to Barton.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "'Twill be a fine thing to have her married

so young



<P>to be sure," said she, "and I hear he is quite a beau,



<P>and prodigious handsome.&nbsp; And I hope you may have as good



<P>luck yourself soon,--but perhaps you may have a friend



<P>in the corner already."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor could not suppose that Sir John would

be more



<P>nice in proclaiming his suspicions of her regard for Edward,



<P>than he had been with respect to Marianne; indeed it was



<P>rather his favourite joke of the two, as being somewhat



<P>newer and more conjectural; and since Edward's visit,



<P>they had never dined together without his drinking to her



<P>best affections with so much significancy and so many nods



<P>and winks, as to excite general attention.&nbsp; The letter F--



<P>had been likewise invariably brought forward, and found



<P>productive of such countless jokes, that its character



<P>as the wittiest letter in the alphabet had been long



<P>established with Elinor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The Miss Steeles, as she expected, had now

all the



<P>benefit of these jokes, and in the eldest of them they



<P>raised a curiosity to know the name of the gentleman



<P>alluded to, which, though often impertinently expressed,



<P>was perfectly of a piece with her general inquisitiveness



<P>into the concerns of their family.&nbsp; But Sir John did not



<P>sport long with the curiosity which he delighted to raise,



<P>for he had at least as much pleasure in telling the name,



<P>as Miss Steele had in hearing it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "His name is Ferrars," said he, in a very audible

whisper;



<P>"but pray do not tell it, for it's a great secret."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Ferrars!" repeated Miss Steele; "Mr. Ferrars

is



<P>the happy man, is he? What! your sister-in-law's brother,



<P>Miss Dashwood? a very agreeable young man to be sure;



<P>I know him very well."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How can you say so, Anne?" cried Lucy, who

generally



<P>made an amendment to all her sister's assertions.



<P>"Though we have seen him once or twice at my uncle's, it



<P>is rather too much to pretend to know him very well."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor heard all this with attention and surprise.



<P>"And who was this uncle?&nbsp; Where did he live?&nbsp; How came



<P>they acquainted?"&nbsp; She wished very much to have the subject



<P>continued, though she did not chuse to join in it herself;



<P>but nothing more of it was said, and for the first time



<P>in her life, she thought Mrs. Jennings deficient either



<P>in curiosity after petty information, or in a disposition



<P>to communicate it.&nbsp; The manner in which Miss Steele had



<P>spoken of Edward, increased her curiosity; for it struck



<P>her as being rather ill-natured, and suggested the suspicion



<P>of that lady's knowing, or fancying herself to know something



<P>to his disadvantage.--But her curiosity was unavailing,



<P>for no farther notice was taken of Mr. Ferrars's name by



<P>Miss Steele when alluded to, or even openly mentioned by Sir John.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 9</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The Dashwoods were now settled at Barton with

tolerable



<P>comfort to themselves.&nbsp; The house and the garden, with all



<P>the objects surrounding them, were now become familiar,



<P>and the ordinary pursuits which had given to Norland



<P>half its charms were engaged in again with far greater



<P>enjoyment than Norland had been able to afford, since the



<P>loss of their father.&nbsp; Sir John Middleton, who called



<P>on them every day for the first fortnight, and who was



<P>not in the habit of seeing much occupation at home,



<P>could not conceal his amazement on finding them always employed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Their visitors, except those from Barton Park,



<P>were not many; for, in spite of Sir John's urgent entreaties



<P>that they would mix more in the neighbourhood, and repeated



<P>assurances of his carriage being always at their service,



<P>the independence of Mrs. Dashwood's spirit overcame the



<P>wish of society for her children; and she was resolute



<P>in declining to visit any family beyond the distance



<P>of a walk.&nbsp; There were but few who could be so classed;



<P>and it was not all of them that were attainable.



<P>About a mile and a half from the cottage, along the narrow



<P>winding valley of Allenham, which issued from that of Barton,



<P>as formerly described, the girls had, in one of their



<P>earliest walks, discovered an ancient respectable looking



<P>mansion which, by reminding them a little of Norland,



<P>interested their imagination and made them wish to be



<P>better acquainted with it.&nbsp; But they learnt, on enquiry,



<P>that its possessor, an elderly lady of very good character,



<P>was unfortunately too infirm to mix with the world,



<P>and never stirred from home.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The whole country about them abounded in beautiful

walks.



<P>The high downs which invited them from almost every window



<P>of the cottage to seek the exquisite enjoyment of air



<P>on their summits, were a happy alternative when the dirt



<P>of the valleys beneath shut up their superior beauties;



<P>and towards one of these hills did Marianne and Margaret



<P>one memorable morning direct their steps, attracted by the



<P>partial sunshine of a showery sky, and unable longer to bear



<P>the confinement which the settled rain of the two preceding



<P>days had occasioned.&nbsp; The weather was not tempting enough



<P>to draw the two others from their pencil and their book,



<P>in spite of Marianne's declaration that the day would



<P>be lastingly fair, and that every threatening cloud would



<P>be drawn off from their hills; and the two girls set off together.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They gaily ascended the downs, rejoicing in

their own



<P>penetration at every glimpse of blue sky; and when they



<P>caught in their faces the animating gales of a high



<P>south-westerly wind, they pitied the fears which had prevented



<P>their mother and Elinor from sharing such delightful sensations.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Is there a felicity in the world," said Marianne,



<P>"superior to this?--Margaret, we will walk here at least



<P>two hours."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Margaret agreed, and they pursued their way

against



<P>the wind, resisting it with laughing delight for about



<P>twenty minutes longer, when suddenly the clouds united over



<P>their heads, and a driving rain set full in their face.--



<P>Chagrined and surprised, they were obliged, though unwillingly,



<P>to turn back, for no shelter was nearer than their own house.



<P>One consolation however remained for them, to which the



<P>exigence of the moment gave more than usual propriety;



<P>it was that of running with all possible speed down the steep



<P>side of the hill which led immediately to their garden gate.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They set off.&nbsp; Marianne had at first the

advantage,



<P>but a false step brought her suddenly to the ground;



<P>and Margaret, unable to stop herself to assist her,



<P>was involuntarily hurried along, and reached the bottom



<P>in safety.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; A gentleman carrying a gun, with two pointers



<P>playing round him, was passing up the hill and within



<P>a few yards of Marianne, when her accident happened.



<P>He put down his gun and ran to her assistance.&nbsp; She had



<P>raised herself from the ground, but her foot had been



<P>twisted in her fall, and she was scarcely able to stand.



<P>The gentleman offered his services; and perceiving that her



<P>modesty declined what her situation rendered necessary,



<P>took her up in his arms without farther delay, and carried



<P>her down the hill.&nbsp; Then passing through the garden,



<P>the gate of which had been left open by Margaret, he bore her



<P>directly into the house, whither Margaret was just arrived,



<P>and quitted not his hold till he had seated her in a chair



<P>in the parlour.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor and her mother rose up in amazement

at



<P>their entrance, and while the eyes of both were fixed



<P>on him with an evident wonder and a secret admiration



<P>which equally sprung from his appearance, he apologized



<P>for his intrusion by relating its cause, in a manner



<P>so frank and so graceful that his person, which was



<P>uncommonly handsome, received additional charms from his voice



<P>and expression.&nbsp; Had he been even old, ugly, and vulgar,



<P>the gratitude and kindness of Mrs. Dashwood would



<P>have been secured by any act of attention to her child;



<P>but the influence of youth, beauty, and elegance,



<P>gave an interest to the action which came home to her feelings.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She thanked him again and again; and, with

a sweetness



<P>of address which always attended her, invited him to



<P>be seated.&nbsp; But this he declined, as he was dirty and wet.



<P>Mrs. Dashwood then begged to know to whom she was obliged.



<P>His name, he replied, was Willoughby, and his present



<P>home was at Allenham, from whence he hoped she would



<P>allow him the honour of calling tomorrow to enquire



<P>after Miss Dashwood.&nbsp; The honour was readily granted,



<P>and he then departed, to make himself still more interesting,



<P>in the midst of an heavy rain.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; His manly beauty and more than common gracefulness



<P>were instantly the theme of general admiration,



<P>and the laugh which his gallantry raised against Marianne



<P>received particular spirit from his exterior attractions.--



<P>Marianne herself had seen less of his person that the rest,



<P>for the confusion which crimsoned over her face, on his



<P>lifting her up, had robbed her of the power of regarding



<P>him after their entering the house.&nbsp; But she had seen



<P>enough of him to join in all the admiration of the others,



<P>and with an energy which always adorned her praise.



<P>His person and air were equal to what her fancy had ever



<P>drawn for the hero of a favourite story; and in his carrying



<P>her into the house with so little previous formality, there



<P>was a rapidity of thought which particularly recommended



<P>the action to her.&nbsp; Every circumstance belonging to him



<P>was interesting.&nbsp; His name was good, his residence was in



<P>their favourite village, and she soon found out that of all



<P>manly dresses a shooting-jacket was the most becoming.



<P>Her imagination was busy, her reflections were pleasant,



<P>and the pain of a sprained ankle was disregarded.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Sir John called on them as soon as the next

interval



<P>of fair weather that morning allowed him to get out



<P>of doors; and Marianne's accident being related to him,



<P>he was eagerly asked whether he knew any gentleman



<P>of the name of Willoughby at Allenham.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Willoughby!" cried Sir John; "what, is HE



<P>in the country? That is good news however; I will



<P>ride over tomorrow, and ask him to dinner on Thursday."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You know him then," said Mrs. Dashwood.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Know him! to be sure I do.&nbsp; Why, he is

down here



<P>every year."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And what sort of a young man is he?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "As good a kind of fellow as ever lived, I

assure you.



<P>A very decent shot, and there is not a bolder rider



<P>in England."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And is that all you can say for him?" cried

Marianne,



<P>indignantly.&nbsp; "But what are his manners on more intimate



<P>acquaintance? What his pursuits, his talents, and genius?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Sir John was rather puzzled.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Upon my soul," said he, "I do not know much

about him



<P>as to all THAT.&nbsp; But he is a pleasant, good humoured fellow,



<P>and has got the nicest little black bitch of a pointer



<P>I ever saw.&nbsp; Was she out with him today?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; But Marianne could no more satisfy him as to

the



<P>colour of Mr. Willoughby's pointer, than he could



<P>describe to her the shades of his mind.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But who is he?" said Elinor.&nbsp; "Where

does he come



<P>from?&nbsp; Has he a house at Allenham?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; On this point Sir John could give more certain

intelligence;



<P>and he told them that Mr. Willoughby had no property



<P>of his own in the country; that he resided there only



<P>while he was visiting the old lady at Allenham Court,



<P>to whom he was related, and whose possessions he was



<P>to inherit; adding, "Yes, yes, he is very well worth



<P>catching I can tell you, Miss Dashwood; he has a pretty



<P>little estate of his own in Somersetshire besides;



<P>and if I were you, I would not give him up to my



<P>younger sister, in spite of all this tumbling down hills.



<P>Miss Marianne must not expect to have all the men to herself.



<P>Brandon will be jealous, if she does not take care."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I do not believe," said Mrs. Dashwood, with

a



<P>good humoured smile, "that Mr. Willoughby will be incommoded



<P>by the attempts of either of MY daughters towards what



<P>you call CATCHING him.&nbsp; It is not an employment to which



<P>they have been brought up.&nbsp; Men are very safe with us,



<P>let them be ever so rich.&nbsp; I am glad to find, however,



<P>from what you say, that he is a respectable young man,



<P>and one whose acquaintance will not be ineligible."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "He is as good a sort of fellow, I believe,



<P>as ever lived," repeated Sir John.&nbsp; "I remember



<P>last Christmas at a little hop at the park, he danced



<P>from eight o'clock till four, without once sitting down."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Did he indeed?" cried Marianne with sparkling

eyes,



<P>"and with elegance, with spirit?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes; and he was up again at eight to ride

to covert."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "That is what I like; that is what a young

man ought



<P>to be.&nbsp; Whatever be his pursuits, his eagerness in them



<P>should know no moderation, and leave him no sense of fatigue."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Aye, aye, I see how it will be," said Sir

John, "I see



<P>how it will be.&nbsp; You will be setting your cap at him now,



<P>and never think of poor Brandon."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "That is an expression, Sir John," said Marianne,



<P>warmly, "which I particularly dislike.&nbsp; I abhor every



<P>common-place phrase by which wit is intended; and 'setting



<P>one's cap at a man,' or 'making a conquest,' are the most



<P>odious of all.&nbsp; Their tendency is gross and illiberal;



<P>and if their construction could ever be deemed clever,



<P>time has long ago destroyed all its ingenuity."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Sir John did not much understand this reproof;



<P>but he laughed as heartily as if he did, and then replied,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Ay, you will make conquests enough, I dare

say,



<P>one way or other.&nbsp; Poor Brandon! he is quite smitten already,



<P>and he is very well worth setting your cap at, I can



<P>tell you, in spite of all this tumbling about and spraining



<P>of ankles."



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter37</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 37</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Palmer was so well at the end of a fortnight,



<P>that her mother felt it no longer necessary to give up



<P>the whole of her time to her; and, contenting herself with



<P>visiting her once or twice a day, returned from that period



<P>to her own home, and her own habits, in which she found



<P>the Miss Dashwoods very ready to ressume their former share.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; About the third or fourth morning after their



<P>being thus resettled in Berkeley Street, Mrs. Jennings,



<P>on returning from her ordinary visit to Mrs. Palmer,



<P>entered the drawing-room, where Elinor was sitting



<P>by herself, with an air of such hurrying importance



<P>as prepared her to hear something wonderful; and giving her



<P>time only to form that idea, began directly to justify it,



<P>by saying,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Lord! my dear Miss Dashwood! have you heard

the news?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, ma'am.&nbsp; What is it?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Something so strange!&nbsp; But you shall

hear it all.--



<P>When I got to Mr. Palmer's, I found Charlotte quite



<P>in a fuss about the child.&nbsp; She was sure it was very



<P>ill--it cried, and fretted, and was all over pimples.



<P>So I looked at it directly, and, 'Lord! my dear,'



<P>says I, 'it is nothing in the world, but the red gum--'



<P>and nurse said just the same.&nbsp; But Charlotte, she would



<P>not be satisfied, so Mr. Donavan was sent for; and luckily



<P>he happened to just come in from Harley Street, so he



<P>stepped over directly, and as soon as ever he saw the child,



<P>be said just as we did, that it was nothing in the world



<P>but the red gum, and then Charlotte was easy.&nbsp; And so,



<P>just as he was going away again, it came into my head,



<P>I am sure I do not know how I happened to think of it,



<P>but it came into my head to ask him if there was any news.



<P>So upon that, he smirked, and simpered, and looked grave,



<P>and seemed to know something or other, and at last he



<P>said in a whisper, 'For fear any unpleasant report



<P>should reach the young ladies under your care as to their



<P>sister's indisposition, I think it advisable to say,



<P>that I believe there is no great reason for alarm; I hope



<P>Mrs. Dashwood will do very well.'"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What! is Fanny ill?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "That is exactly what I said, my dear.&nbsp;

'Lord!' says I,



<P>'is Mrs. Dashwood ill?' So then it all came out; and the



<P>long and the short of the matter, by all I can learn,



<P>seems to be this.&nbsp; Mr. Edward Ferrars, the very young



<P>man I used to joke with you about (but however, as it



<P>turns out, I am monstrous glad there was never any thing



<P>in it), Mr. Edward Ferrars, it seems, has been engaged



<P>above this twelvemonth to my cousin Lucy!--There's for you,



<P>my dear!--And not a creature knowing a syllable of the matter,



<P>except Nancy!--Could you have believed such a thing possible?--



<P>There is no great wonder in their liking one another;



<P>but that matters should be brought so forward between them,



<P>and nobody suspect it!--THAT is strange!--I never happened



<P>to see them together, or I am sure I should have found it



<P>out directly.&nbsp; Well, and so this was kept a great secret,



<P>for fear of Mrs. Ferrars, and neither she nor your



<P>brother or sister suspected a word of the matter;--



<P>till this very morning, poor Nancy, who, you know, is a



<P>well-meaning creature, but no conjurer, popt it all out.



<P>'Lord!' thinks she to herself, 'they are all so fond



<P>of Lucy, to be sure they will make no difficulty about it;'



<P>and so, away she went to your sister, who was sitting all



<P>alone at her carpet-work, little suspecting what was to



<P>come--for she had just been saying to your brother, only five



<P>minutes before, that she thought to make a match between



<P>Edward and some Lord's daughter or other, I forget who.



<P>So you may think what a blow it was to all her vanity



<P>and pride.&nbsp; She fell into violent hysterics immediately,



<P>with such screams as reached your brother's ears,



<P>as he was sitting in his own dressing-room down stairs,



<P>thinking about writing a letter to his steward in the country.



<P>So up he flew directly, and a terrible scene took place,



<P>for Lucy was come to them by that time, little dreaming



<P>what was going on.&nbsp; Poor soul!&nbsp; I pity HER.&nbsp; And I must

say,



<P>I think she was used very hardly; for your sister scolded



<P>like any fury, and soon drove her into a fainting fit.



<P>Nancy, she fell upon her knees, and cried bitterly;



<P>and your brother, he walked about the room, and said



<P>he did not know what to do.&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood declared



<P>they should not stay a minute longer in the house,



<P>and your brother was forced to go down upon HIS knees too,



<P>to persuade her to let them stay till they had packed



<P>up their clothes.&nbsp; THEN she fell into hysterics again,



<P>and he was so frightened that he would send for Mr. Donavan,



<P>and Mr. Donavan found the house in all this uproar.



<P>The carriage was at the door ready to take my poor



<P>cousins away, and they were just stepping in as he



<P>came off; poor Lucy in such a condition, he says,



<P>she could hardly walk; and Nancy, she was almost as bad.



<P>I declare, I have no patience with your sister; and I hope,



<P>with all my heart, it will be a match in spite of her.



<P>Lord! what a taking poor Mr. Edward will be in when he



<P>hears of it!&nbsp; To have his love used so scornfully! for



<P>they say he is monstrous fond of her, as well he may.



<P>I should not wonder, if he was to be in the greatest



<P>passion!--and Mr. Donavan thinks just the same.&nbsp; He and I



<P>had a great deal of talk about it; and the best of all is,



<P>that he is gone back again to Harley Street, that he may



<P>be within call when Mrs. Ferrars is told of it, for she



<P>was sent for as soon as ever my cousins left the house,



<P>for your sister was sure SHE would be in hysterics too;



<P>and so she may, for what I care.&nbsp; I have no pity for



<P>either of them.&nbsp; I have no notion of people's making



<P>such a to-do about money and greatness.&nbsp; There is no



<P>reason on earth why Mr. Edward and Lucy should not marry;



<P>for I am sure Mrs. Ferrars may afford to do very well



<P>by her son, and though Lucy has next to nothing herself,



<P>she knows better than any body how to make the most



<P>of every thing; I dare say, if Mrs. Ferrars would only



<P>allow him five hundred a-year, she would make as good



<P>an appearance with it as any body else would with eight.



<P>Lord! how snug they might live in such another cottage



<P>as yours--or a little bigger--with two maids, and two men;



<P>and I believe I could help them to a housemaid, for my



<P>Betty has a sister out of place, that would fit them



<P>exactly."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Here Mrs. Jennings ceased, and as Elinor had

had



<P>time enough to collect her thoughts, she was able



<P>to give such an answer, and make such observations,



<P>as the subject might naturally be supposed to produce.



<P>Happy to find that she was not suspected of any extraordinary



<P>interest in it; that Mrs. Jennings (as she had of late



<P>often hoped might be the case) had ceased to imagine her



<P>at all attached to Edward; and happy above all the rest,



<P>in the absence of Marianne, she felt very well able



<P>to speak of the affair without embarrassment, and to



<P>give her judgment, as she believed, with impartiality
