<P>on the conduct of every one concerned in it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She could hardly determine what her own expectation



<P>of its event really was; though she earnestly tried



<P>to drive away the notion of its being possible to end



<P>otherwise at last, than in the marriage of Edward and Lucy.



<P>What Mrs. Ferrars would say and do, though there could



<P>not be a doubt of its nature, she was anxious to hear;



<P>and still more anxious to know how Edward would



<P>conduct himself.&nbsp; For HIM she felt much compassion;--



<P>for Lucy very little--and it cost her some pains to procure



<P>that little;--for the rest of the party none at all.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As Mrs. Jennings could talk on no other subject,



<P>Elinor soon saw the necessity of preparing Marianne for



<P>its discussion.&nbsp; No time was to be lost in undeceiving her,



<P>in making her acquainted with the real truth, and in



<P>endeavouring to bring her to hear it talked of by others,



<P>without betraying that she felt any uneasiness for her sister,



<P>or any resentment against Edward.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor's office was a painful one.--She was

going



<P>to remove what she really believed to be her sister's



<P>chief consolation,--to give such particulars of Edward as she



<P>feared would ruin him for ever in her good opinion,-and



<P>to make Marianne, by a resemblance in their situations,



<P>which to HER fancy would seem strong, feel all her own



<P>disappointment over again.&nbsp; But unwelcome as such a task



<P>must be, it was necessary to be done, and Elinor therefore



<P>hastened to perform it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She was very far from wishing to dwell on her

own



<P>feelings, or to represent herself as suffering much,



<P>any otherwise than as the self-command she had practised



<P>since her first knowledge of Edward's engagement, might



<P>suggest a hint of what was practicable to Marianne.



<P>Her narration was clear and simple; and though it could



<P>not be given without emotion, it was not accompanied



<P>by violent agitation, nor impetuous grief.--THAT belonged



<P>rather to the hearer, for Marianne listened with horror,



<P>and cried excessively.&nbsp; Elinor was to be the comforter



<P>of others in her own distresses, no less than in theirs;



<P>and all the comfort that could be given by assurances



<P>of her own composure of mind, and a very earnest



<P>vindication of Edward from every charge but of imprudence,



<P>was readily offered.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; But Marianne for some time would give credit

to neither.



<P>Edward seemed a second Willoughby; and acknowledging



<P>as Elinor did, that she HAD loved him most sincerely,



<P>could she feel less than herself!&nbsp; As for Lucy Steele,



<P>she considered her so totally unamiable, so absolutely



<P>incapable of attaching a sensible man, that she could



<P>not be persuaded at first to believe, and afterwards



<P>to pardon, any former affection of Edward for her.



<P>She would not even admit it to have been natural;



<P>and Elinor left her to be convinced that it was so,



<P>by that which only could convince her, a better knowledge



<P>of mankind.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Her first communication had reached no farther

than



<P>to state the fact of the engagement, and the length of time



<P>it had existed.--Marianne's feelings had then broken in,



<P>and put an end to all regularity of detail; and for some



<P>time all that could be done was to soothe her distress,



<P>lessen her alarms, and combat her resentment.&nbsp; The first



<P>question on her side, which led to farther particulars,



<P>was,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How long has this been known to you, Elinor?

has



<P>he written to you?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have known it these four months.&nbsp; When

Lucy



<P>first came to Barton Park last November, she told me



<P>in confidence of her engagement."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; At these words, Marianne's eyes expressed the

astonishment



<P>which her lips could not utter.&nbsp; After a pause of wonder,



<P>she exclaimed--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Four months!--Have you known of this four

months?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor confirmed it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What!--while attending me in all my misery,

has this



<P>been on your heart?--And I have reproached you for being happy!"--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It was not fit that you should then know how

much



<P>I was the reverse!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Four months!"--cried Marianne again.--"So

calm!--



<P>so cheerful!--how have you been supported?"--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "By feeling that I was doing my duty.--My promise

to



<P>Lucy, obliged me to be secret.&nbsp; I owed it to her, therefore,



<P>to avoid giving any hint of the truth; and I owed it to my



<P>family and friends, not to create in them a solicitude about me,



<P>which it could not be in my power to satisfy."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne seemed much struck.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have very often wished to undeceive yourself

and my



<P>mother," added Elinor; "and once or twice I have attempted it;--



<P>but without betraying my trust, I never could have convinced you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Four months!--and yet you loved him!"--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes. But I did not love only him;--and while

the comfort



<P>of others was dear to me, I was glad to spare them from knowing



<P>how much I felt.&nbsp; Now, I can think and speak of it with



<P>little emotion.&nbsp; I would not have you suffer on my account;



<P>for I assure you I no longer suffer materially myself.



<P>I have many things to support me.&nbsp; I am not conscious of having



<P>provoked the disappointment by any imprudence of my own,



<P>I have borne it as much as possible without spreading



<P>it farther.&nbsp; I acquit Edward of essential misconduct.



<P>I wish him very happy; and I am so sure of his always



<P>doing his duty, that though now he may harbour some regret,



<P>in the end he must become so.&nbsp; Lucy does not want sense,



<P>and that is the foundation on which every thing good may



<P>be built.--And after all, Marianne, after all that is



<P>bewitching in the idea of a single and constant attachment,



<P>and all that can be said of one's happiness depending



<P>entirely on any particular person, it is not meant--it



<P>is not fit--it is not possible that it should be so.--



<P>Edward will marry Lucy; he will marry a woman superior



<P>in person and understanding to half her sex; and time



<P>and habit will teach him to forget that he ever thought



<P>another superior to HER."--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "If such is your way of thinking," said Marianne,



<P>"if the loss of what is most valued is so easily



<P>to be made up by something else, your resolution,



<P>your self-command, are, perhaps, a little less to be



<P>wondered at.--They are brought more within my comprehension."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I understand you.--You do not suppose that

I have ever



<P>felt much.--For four months, Marianne, I have had all this



<P>hanging on my mind, without being at liberty to speak



<P>of it to a single creature; knowing that it would make



<P>you and my mother most unhappy whenever it were explained



<P>to you, yet unable to prepare you for it in the least.--



<P>It was told me,--it was in a manner forced on me by the



<P>very person herself, whose prior engagement ruined all



<P>my prospects; and told me, as I thought, with triumph.--



<P>This person's suspicions, therefore, I have had to oppose,



<P>by endeavouring to appear indifferent where I have been most



<P>deeply interested;--and it has not been only once;--I have



<P>had her hopes and exultation to listen to again and again.--



<P>I have known myself to be divided from Edward for ever,



<P>without hearing one circumstance that could make me less



<P>desire the connection.--Nothing has proved him unworthy;



<P>nor has anything declared him indifferent to me.--



<P>I have had to contend against the unkindness of his sister,



<P>and the insolence of his mother; and have suffered the



<P>punishment of an attachment, without enjoying its advantages.--



<P>And all this has been going on at a time, when, as you



<P>know too well, it has not been my only unhappiness.--



<P>If you can think me capable of ever feeling--surely you



<P>may suppose that I have suffered NOW.&nbsp; The composure



<P>of mind with which I have brought myself at present



<P>to consider the matter, the consolation that I have been



<P>willing to admit, have been the effect of constant and



<P>painful exertion;--they did not spring up of themselves;--



<P>they did not occur to relieve my spirits at first.--



<P>No, Marianne.--THEN, if I had not been bound to silence,



<P>perhaps nothing could have kept me entirely--not even what I



<P>owed to my dearest friends--from openly shewing that I was



<P>VERY unhappy."--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne was quite subdued.--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh! Elinor," she cried, "you have made me

hate



<P>myself for ever.--How barbarous have I been to you!--



<P>you, who have been my only comfort, who have borne with me



<P>in all my misery, who have seemed to be only suffering



<P>for me!--Is this my gratitude?--Is this the only return I



<P>can make you?--Because your merit cries out upon myself,



<P>I have been trying to do it away."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The tenderest caresses followed this confession.



<P>In such a frame of mind as she was now in, Elinor had



<P>no difficulty in obtaining from her whatever promise



<P>she required; and at her request, Marianne engaged



<P>never to speak of the affair to any one with the least



<P>appearance of bitterness;--to meet Lucy without betraying



<P>the smallest increase of dislike to her;--and even to see



<P>Edward himself, if chance should bring them together,



<P>without any diminution of her usual cordiality.--



<P>These were great concessions;--but where Marianne felt



<P>that she had injured, no reparation could be too much



<P>for her to make.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She performed her promise of being discreet,



<P>to admiration.--She attended to all that Mrs. Jennings



<P>had to say upon the subject, with an unchanging complexion,



<P>dissented from her in nothing, and was heard three



<P>times to say, "Yes, ma'am."--She listened to her praise



<P>of Lucy with only moving from one chair to another,



<P>and when Mrs. Jennings talked of Edward's affection,



<P>it cost her only a spasm in her throat.--Such advances



<P>towards heroism in her sister, made Elinor feel equal



<P>to any thing herself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The next morning brought a farther trial of

it,



<P>in a visit from their brother, who came with a most serious



<P>aspect to talk over the dreadful affair, and bring them



<P>news of his wife.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You have heard, I suppose," said he with great

solemnity,



<P>as soon as he was seated, "of the very shocking discovery



<P>that took place under our roof yesterday."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They all looked their assent; it seemed too

awful



<P>a moment for speech.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Your sister," he continued, "has suffered

dreadfully.



<P>Mrs. Ferrars too--in short it has been a scene of such



<P>complicated distress--but I will hope that the storm may



<P>be weathered without our being any of us quite overcome.



<P>Poor Fanny! she was in hysterics all yesterday.



<P>But I would not alarm you too much.&nbsp; Donavan says there



<P>is nothing materially to be apprehended; her constitution



<P>is a good one, and her resolution equal to any thing.



<P>She has borne it all, with the fortitude of an angel!



<P>She says she never shall think well of anybody again;



<P>and one cannot wonder at it, after being so deceived!--



<P>meeting with such ingratitude, where so much kindness



<P>had been shewn, so much confidence had been placed!&nbsp; It



<P>was quite out of the benevolence of her heart, that she



<P>had asked these young women to her house; merely because



<P>she thought they deserved some attention, were harmless,



<P>well-behaved girls, and would be pleasant companions;



<P>for otherwise we both wished very much to have invited you



<P>and Marianne to be with us, while your kind friend there,



<P>was attending her daughter.&nbsp; And now to be so rewarded!



<P>'I wish, with all my heart,' says poor Fanny in her



<P>affectionate way, 'that we had asked your sisters instead



<P>of them.'"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Here he stopped to be thanked; which being

done,



<P>he went on.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What poor Mrs. Ferrars suffered, when first

Fanny



<P>broke it to her, is not to be described.&nbsp; While she with



<P>the truest affection had been planning a most eligible



<P>connection for him, was it to be supposed that he could



<P>be all the time secretly engaged to another person!--such



<P>a suspicion could never have entered her head!&nbsp; If she



<P>suspected ANY prepossession elsewhere, it could not be



<P>in THAT quarter.&nbsp; 'THERE, to be sure,' said she, 'I might



<P>have thought myself safe.' She was quite in an agony.



<P>We consulted together, however, as to what should be done,



<P>and at last she determined to send for Edward.



<P>He came.&nbsp; But I am sorry to relate what ensued.



<P>All that Mrs. Ferrars could say to make him put an end



<P>to the engagement, assisted too as you may well suppose



<P>by my arguments, and Fanny's entreaties, was of



<P>no avail.&nbsp; Duty, affection, every thing was disregarded.



<P>I never thought Edward so stubborn, so unfeeling before.



<P>His mother explained to him her liberal designs, in case



<P>of his marrying Miss Morton; told him she would settle on



<P>him the Norfolk estate, which, clear of land-tax, brings



<P>in a good thousand a-year; offered even, when matters



<P>grew desperate, to make it twelve hundred; and in opposition



<P>to this, if he still persisted in this low connection,



<P>represented to him the certain penury that must attend



<P>the match.&nbsp; His own two thousand pounds she protested



<P>should be his all; she would never see him again; and so far



<P>would she be from affording him the smallest assistance,



<P>that if he were to enter into any profession with a view



<P>of better support, she would do all in her power to prevent



<P>him advancing in it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Here Marianne, in an ecstasy of indignation,



<P>clapped her hands together, and cried, "Gracious God!



<P>can this be possible!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well may you wonder, Marianne," replied her

brother,



<P>"at the obstinacy which could resist such arguments as these.



<P>Your exclamation is very natural."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne was going to retort, but she remembered



<P>her promises, and forbore.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "All this, however," he continued, "was urged

in vain.



<P>Edward said very little; but what he did say, was in



<P>the most determined manner.&nbsp; Nothing should prevail on



<P>him to give up his engagement.&nbsp; He would stand to it,



<P>cost him what it might."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Then," cried Mrs. Jennings with blunt sincerity,



<P>no longer able to be silent, "he has acted like an honest



<P>man! I beg your pardon, Mr. Dashwood, but if he had



<P>done otherwise, I should have thought him a rascal.



<P>I have some little concern in the business, as well



<P>as yourself, for Lucy Steele is my cousin, and I believe



<P>there is not a better kind of girl in the world, nor one



<P>who more deserves a good husband."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; John Dashwood was greatly astonished; but his

nature



<P>was calm, not open to provocation, and he never wished



<P>to offend anybody, especially anybody of good fortune.



<P>He therefore replied, without any resentment,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I would by no means speak disrespectfully

of any



<P>relation of yours, madam.&nbsp; Miss Lucy Steele is, I dare say,



<P>a very deserving young woman, but in the present case



<P>you know, the connection must be impossible.



<P>And to have entered into a secret engagement with a



<P>young man under her uncle's care, the son of a woman



<P>especially of such very large fortune as Mrs. Ferrars,



<P>is perhaps, altogether a little extraordinary. In short,



<P>I do not mean to reflect upon the behaviour of any person



<P>whom you have a regard for, Mrs. Jennings.&nbsp; We all wish



<P>her extremely happy; and Mrs. Ferrars's conduct throughout



<P>the whole, has been such as every conscientious, good mother,



<P>in like circumstances, would adopt.&nbsp; It has been dignified



<P>and liberal.&nbsp; Edward has drawn his own lot, and I fear



<P>it will be a bad one."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne sighed out her similar apprehension;



<P>and Elinor's heart wrung for the feelings of Edward,



<P>while braving his mother's threats, for a woman who could



<P>not reward him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, sir," said Mrs. Jennings, "and how did

it end?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am sorry to say, ma'am, in a most unhappy

rupture:--



<P>Edward is dismissed for ever from his mother's notice.



<P>He left her house yesterday, but where he is gone, or whether



<P>he is still in town, I do not know; for WE of course can



<P>make no inquiry."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Poor young man!--and what is to become of

him?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What, indeed, ma'am!&nbsp; It is a melancholy

consideration.



<P>Born to the prospect of such affluence!&nbsp; I cannot conceive



<P>a situation more deplorable.&nbsp; The interest of two thousand



<P>pounds--how can a man live on it?--and when to that is added



<P>the recollection, that he might, but for his own folly,



<P>within three months have been in the receipt of two



<P>thousand, five hundred a-year (for Miss Morton has



<P>thirty thousand pounds,) I cannot picture to myself



<P>a more wretched condition.&nbsp; We must all feel for him;



<P>and the more so, because it is totally out of our power



<P>to assist him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Poor young man!" cried Mrs. Jennings, "I am

sure



<P>he should be very welcome to bed and board at my house;



<P>and so I would tell him if I could see him.&nbsp; It is not fit



<P>that he should be living about at his own charge now,



<P>at lodgings and taverns."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor's heart thanked her for such kindness

towards Edward,



<P>though she could not forbear smiling at the form of it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "If he would only have done as well by himself,"



<P>said John Dashwood, "as all his friends were disposed to do



<P>by him, he might now have been in his proper situation,



<P>and would have wanted for nothing.&nbsp; But as it is, it must



<P>be out of anybody's power to assist him.&nbsp; And there is one



<P>thing more preparing against him, which must be worse than



<P>all--his mother has determined, with a very natural kind



<P>of spirit, to settle THAT estate upon Robert immediately,



<P>which might have been Edward's, on proper conditions.



<P>I left her this morning with her lawyer, talking over



<P>the business."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well!" said Mrs. Jennings, "that is HER revenge.



<P>Everybody has a way of their own.&nbsp; But I don't think mine



<P>would be, to make one son independent, because another had



<P>plagued me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne got up and walked about the room.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Can anything be more galling to the spirit

of a man,"



<P>continued John, "than to see his younger brother in



<P>possession of an estate which might have been his own?



<P>Poor Edward!&nbsp; I feel for him sincerely."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; A few minutes more spent in the same kind of

effusion,



<P>concluded his visit; and with repeated assurances to his



<P>sisters that he really believed there was no material



<P>danger in Fanny's indisposition, and that they need



<P>not therefore be very uneasy about it, he went away;



<P>leaving the three ladies unanimous in their sentiments



<P>on the present occasion, as far at least as it regarded



<P>Mrs. Ferrars's conduct, the Dashwoods', and Edward's.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne's indignation burst forth as soon

as he



<P>quitted the room; and as her vehemence made reserve



<P>impossible in Elinor, and unnecessary in Mrs. Jennings,



<P>they all joined in a very spirited critique upon the party.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter23</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 23</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; However small Elinor's general dependence on



<P>Lucy's veracity might be, it was impossible for her



<P>on serious reflection to suspect it in the present case,



<P>where no temptation could be answerable to the folly



<P>of inventing a falsehood of such a description.&nbsp; What Lucy



<P>had asserted to be true, therefore, Elinor could not,



<P>dared not longer doubt; supported as it was too on every



<P>side by such probabilities and proofs, and contradicted



<P>by nothing but her own wishes.&nbsp; Their opportunity of



<P>acquaintance in the house of Mr. Pratt was a foundation



<P>for the rest, at once indisputable and alarming; and Edward's



<P>visit near Plymouth, his melancholy state of mind,



<P>his dissatisfaction at his own prospects, his uncertain



<P>behaviour towards herself, the intimate knowledge of the



<P>Miss Steeles as to Norland and their family connections,



<P>which had often surprised her, the picture, the letter,



<P>the ring, formed altogether such a body of evidence,



<P>as overcame every fear of condemning him unfairly,



<P>and established as a fact, which no partiality could



<P>set aside, his ill-treatment of herself.--Her resentment



<P>of such behaviour, her indignation at having been its dupe,



<P>for a short time made her feel only for herself;



<P>but other ideas, other considerations, soon arose.



<P>Had Edward been intentionally deceiving her?&nbsp; Had he feigned



<P>a regard for her which he did not feel?&nbsp; Was his engagement



<P>to Lucy an engagement of the heart?&nbsp; No; whatever it might



<P>once have been, she could not believe it such at present.



<P>His affection was all her own.&nbsp; She could not be deceived



<P>in that.&nbsp; Her mother, sisters, Fanny, all had been



<P>conscious of his regard for her at Norland; it was not



<P>an illusion of her own vanity.&nbsp; He certainly loved her.



<P>What a softener of the heart was this persuasion!&nbsp; How much



<P>could it not tempt her to forgive!&nbsp; He had been blamable,



<P>highly blamable, in remaining at Norland after he first



<P>felt her influence over him to be more than it ought



<P>to be.&nbsp; In that, he could not be defended; but if he



<P>had injured her, how much more had he injured himself;



<P>if her case were pitiable, his was hopeless.



<P>His imprudence had made her miserable for a while; but it



<P>seemed to have deprived himself of all chance of ever



<P>being otherwise.&nbsp; She might in time regain tranquillity;



<P>but HE, what had he to look forward to?&nbsp; Could he



<P>ever be tolerably happy with Lucy Steele; could he,



<P>were his affection for herself out of the question,



<P>with his integrity, his delicacy, and well-informed mind,



<P>be satisfied with a wife like her--illiterate, artful,



<P>and selfish?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The youthful infatuation of nineteen would

naturally



<P>blind him to every thing but her beauty and good nature;



<P>but the four succeeding years--years, which if rationally



<P>spent, give such improvement to the understanding, must



<P>have opened his eyes to her defects of education,



<P>while the same period of time, spent on her side



<P>in inferior society and more frivolous pursuits,



<P>had perhaps robbed her of that simplicity which might



<P>once have given an interesting character to her beauty.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; If in the supposition of his seeking to marry

herself,



<P>his difficulties from his mother had seemed great,



<P>how much greater were they now likely to be, when



<P>the object of his engagement was undoubtedly inferior



<P>in connections, and probably inferior in fortune to herself.



<P>These difficulties, indeed, with a heart so alienated



<P>from Lucy, might not press very hard upon his patience;



<P>but melancholy was the state of the person by whom the



<P>expectation of family opposition and unkindness, could



<P>be felt as a relief!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As these considerations occurred to her in

painful



<P>succession, she wept for him, more than for herself.



<P>Supported by the conviction of having done nothing to



<P>merit her present unhappiness, and consoled by the belief



<P>that Edward had done nothing to forfeit her esteem,



<P>she thought she could even now, under the first smart



<P>of the heavy blow, command herself enough to guard every



<P>suspicion of the truth from her mother and sisters.



<P>And so well was she able to answer her own expectations,



<P>that when she joined them at dinner only two hours



<P>after she had first suffered the extinction of all her



<P>dearest hopes, no one would have supposed from the



<P>appearance of the sisters, that Elinor was mourning



<P>in secret over obstacles which must divide her for ever



<P>from the object of her love, and that Marianne was



<P>internally dwelling on the perfections of a man, of whose



<P>whole heart she felt thoroughly possessed, and whom she



<P>expected to see in every carriage which drove near their house.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The necessity of concealing from her mother

and



<P>Marianne, what had been entrusted in confidence to herself,



<P>though it obliged her to unceasing exertion, was no



<P>aggravation of Elinor's distress.&nbsp; On the contrary



<P>it was a relief to her, to be spared the communication



<P>of what would give such affliction to them, and to be



<P>saved likewise from hearing that condemnation of Edward,



<P>which would probably flow from the excess of their partial



<P>affection for herself, and which was more than she felt



<P>equal to support.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; From their counsel, or their conversation,

she knew



<P>she could receive no assistance, their tenderness and



<P>sorrow must add to her distress, while her self-command



<P>would neither receive encouragement from their example



<P>nor from their praise.&nbsp; She was stronger alone,



<P>and her own good sense so well supported her, that her



<P>firmness was as unshaken, her appearance of cheerfulness



<P>as invariable, as with regrets so poignant and so fresh,



<P>it was possible for them to be.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Much as she had suffered from her first conversation



<P>with Lucy on the subject, she soon felt an earnest wish



<P>of renewing it; and this for more reasons than one.



<P>She wanted to hear many particulars of their engagement



<P>repeated again, she wanted more clearly to understand



<P>what Lucy really felt for Edward, whether there were any



<P>sincerity in her declaration of tender regard for him,



<P>and she particularly wanted to convince Lucy, by her



<P>readiness to enter on the matter again, and her calmness



<P>in conversing on it, that she was no otherwise interested



<P>in it than as a friend, which she very much feared



<P>her involuntary agitation, in their morning discourse,



<P>must have left at least doubtful.&nbsp; That Lucy was disposed



<P>to be jealous of her appeared very probable: it was plain



<P>that Edward had always spoken highly in her praise,



<P>not merely from Lucy's assertion, but from her venturing



<P>to trust her on so short a personal acquaintance,



<P>with a secret so confessedly and evidently important.



<P>And even Sir John's joking intelligence must have had



<P>some weight.&nbsp; But indeed, while Elinor remained so well



<P>assured within herself of being really beloved by Edward,



<P>it required no other consideration of probabilities



<P>to make it natural that Lucy should be jealous;



<P>and that she was so, her very confidence was a proof.



<P>What other reason for the disclosure of the affair could



<P>there be, but that Elinor might be informed by it of Lucy's



<P>superior claims on Edward, and be taught to avoid him



<P>in future?&nbsp; She had little difficulty in understanding thus



<P>much of her rival's intentions, and while she was firmly



<P>resolved to act by her as every principle of honour and



<P>honesty directed, to combat her own affection for Edward



<P>and to see him as little as possible; she could not deny



<P>herself the comfort of endeavouring to convince Lucy



<P>that her heart was unwounded.&nbsp; And as she could now have



<P>nothing more painful to hear on the subject than had already



<P>been told, she did not mistrust her own ability of going



<P>through a repetition of particulars with composure.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; But it was not immediately that an opportunity



<P>of doing so could be commanded, though Lucy was as well



<P>disposed as herself to take advantage of any that occurred;



<P>for the weather was not often fine enough to allow



<P>of their joining in a walk, where they might most easily



<P>separate themselves from the others; and though they



<P>met at least every other evening either at the park



<P>or cottage, and chiefly at the former, they could



<P>not be supposed to meet for the sake of conversation.



<P>Such a thought would never enter either Sir John or Lady



<P>Middleton's head; and therefore very little leisure



<P>was ever given for a general chat, and none at all for



<P>particular discourse.&nbsp; They met for the sake of eating,



<P>drinking, and laughing together, playing at cards,



<P>or consequences, or any other game that was sufficiently noisy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; One or two meetings of this kind had taken

place,



<P>without affording Elinor any chance of engaging Lucy



<P>in private, when Sir John called at the cottage one morning,



<P>to beg, in the name of charity, that they would all



<P>dine with Lady Middleton that day, as he was obliged



<P>to attend the club at Exeter, and she would otherwise be



<P>quite alone, except her mother and the two Miss Steeles.



<P>Elinor, who foresaw a fairer opening for the point she



<P>had in view, in such a party as this was likely to be,



<P>more at liberty among themselves under the tranquil



<P>and well-bred direction of Lady Middleton than when



<P>her husband united them together in one noisy purpose,



<P>immediately accepted the invitation; Margaret, with her



<P>mother's permission, was equally compliant, and Marianne,



<P>though always unwilling to join any of their parties,



<P>was persuaded by her mother, who could not bear to have her



<P>seclude herself from any chance of amusement, to go likewise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The young ladies went, and Lady Middleton was

happily



<P>preserved from the frightful solitude which had threatened her.



<P>The insipidity of the meeting was exactly such as Elinor



<P>had expected; it produced not one novelty of thought



<P>or expression, and nothing could be less interesting



<P>than the whole of their discourse both in the dining



<P>parlour and drawing room: to the latter, the children



<P>accompanied them, and while they remained there, she was



<P>too well convinced of the impossibility of engaging Lucy's



<P>attention to attempt it.&nbsp; They quitted it only with the



<P>removal of the tea-things.&nbsp; The card-table was then placed,



<P>and Elinor began to wonder at herself for having ever



<P>entertained a hope of finding time for conversation



<P>at the park.&nbsp; They all rose up in preparation for a round game.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am glad," said Lady Middleton to Lucy,



<P>"you are not going to finish poor little Annamaria's



<P>basket this evening; for I am sure it must hurt your



<P>eyes to work filigree by candlelight.&nbsp; And we will make



<P>the dear little love some amends for her disappointment



<P>to-morrow, and then I hope she will not much mind it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This hint was enough, Lucy recollected herself

instantly



<P>and replied, "Indeed you are very much mistaken,



<P>Lady Middleton; I am only waiting to know whether you can



<P>make your party without me, or I should have been at my



<P>filigree already.&nbsp; I would not disappoint the little angel



<P>for all the world: and if you want me at the card-table now,



<P>I am resolved to finish the basket after supper."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You are very good, I hope it won't hurt your

eyes--



<P>will you ring the bell for some working candles?



<P>My poor little girl would be sadly disappointed, I know,



<P>if the basket was not finished tomorrow, for though I



<P>told her it certainly would not, I am sure she depends



<P>upon having it done."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lucy directly drew her work table near her



<P>and reseated herself with an alacrity and cheerfulness



<P>which seemed to infer that she could taste no greater



<P>delight than in making a filigree basket for a spoilt child.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lady Middleton proposed a rubber of Casino

to the others.



<P>No one made any objection but Marianne, who with her usual



<P>inattention to the forms of general civility, exclaimed,



<P>"Your Ladyship will have the goodness to excuse ME--you



<P>know I detest cards.&nbsp; I shall go to the piano-forte;



<P>I have not touched it since it was tuned."&nbsp; And without



<P>farther ceremony, she turned away and walked to the instrument.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lady Middleton looked as if she thanked heaven



<P>that SHE had never made so rude a speech.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Marianne can never keep long from that instrument



<P>you know, ma'am," said Elinor, endeavouring to smooth



<P>away the offence; "and I do not much wonder at it; for it



<P>is the very best toned piano-forte I ever heard."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The remaining five were now to draw their cards.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Perhaps," continued Elinor, "if I should happen



<P>to cut out, I may be of some use to Miss Lucy Steele,



<P>in rolling her papers for her; and there is so much still



<P>to be done to the basket, that it must be impossible



<P>I think for her labour singly, to finish it this evening.



<P>I should like the work exceedingly, if she would allow



<P>me a share in it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Indeed I shall be very much obliged to you



<P>for your help," cried Lucy, "for I find there is more



<P>to be done to it than I thought there was; and it would



<P>be a shocking thing to disappoint dear Annamaria after all."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh! that would be terrible, indeed," said

Miss Steele--



<P>"Dear little soul, how I do love her!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You are very kind," said Lady Middleton to

Elinor;



<P>"and as you really like the work, perhaps you will be



<P>as well pleased not to cut in till another rubber,



<P>or will you take your chance now?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor joyfully profited by the first of these

proposals,



<P>and thus by a little of that address which Marianne



<P>could never condescend to practise, gained her own end,



<P>and pleased Lady Middleton at the same time.&nbsp; Lucy made room



<P>for her with ready attention, and the two fair rivals were



<P>thus seated side by side at the same table, and, with the



<P>utmost harmony, engaged in forwarding the same work.



<P>The pianoforte at which Marianne, wrapped up in her own



<P>music and her own thoughts, had by this time forgotten



<P>that any body was in the room besides herself, was luckily



<P>so near them that Miss Dashwood now judged she might safely,



<P>under the shelter of its noise, introduce the interesting



<P>subject, without any risk of being heard at the card-table.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter22</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 22</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne, who had never much toleration for

any



<P>thing like impertinence, vulgarity, inferiority of parts,



<P>or even difference of taste from herself, was at



<P>this time particularly ill-disposed, from the state



<P>of her spirits, to be pleased with the Miss Steeles,



<P>or to encourage their advances; and to the invariable



<P>coldness of her behaviour towards them, which checked every



<P>endeavour at intimacy on their side, Elinor principally



<P>attributed that preference of herself which soon became



<P>evident in the manners of both, but especially of Lucy,



<P>who missed no opportunity of engaging her in conversation,



<P>or of striving to improve their acquaintance by an easy



<P>and frank communication of her sentiments.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lucy was naturally clever; her remarks were

often



<P>just and amusing; and as a companion for half an hour



<P>Elinor frequently found her agreeable; but her powers



<P>had received no aid from education: she was ignorant



<P>and illiterate; and her deficiency of all mental improvement,



<P>her want of information in the most common particulars,



<P>could not be concealed from Miss Dashwood, in spite of her



<P>constant endeavour to appear to advantage.&nbsp; Elinor saw,



<P>and pitied her for, the neglect of abilities which education



<P>might have rendered so respectable; but she saw, with less



<P>tenderness of feeling, the thorough want of delicacy,



<P>of rectitude, and integrity of mind, which her attentions,



<P>her assiduities, her flatteries at the Park betrayed;



<P>and she could have no lasting satisfaction in the company



<P>of a person who joined insincerity with ignorance;



<P>whose want of instruction prevented their meeting



<P>in conversation on terms of equality, and whose conduct



<P>toward others made every shew of attention and deference



<P>towards herself perfectly valueless.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You will think my question an odd one, I dare

say,"



<P>said Lucy to her one day, as they were walking together



<P>from the park to the cottage--"but pray, are you



<P>personally acquainted with your sister-in-law's mother,



<P>Mrs. Ferrars?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor DID think the question a very odd one,



<P>and her countenance expressed it, as she answered that she



<P>had never seen Mrs. Ferrars.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Indeed!" replied Lucy; "I wonder at that,

for I



<P>thought you must have seen her at Norland sometimes.



<P>Then, perhaps, you cannot tell me what sort of a woman



<P>she is?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No," returned Elinor, cautious of giving her

real



<P>opinion of Edward's mother, and not very desirous



<P>of satisfying what seemed impertinent curiosity--



<P>"I know nothing of her."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am sure you think me very strange, for enquiring



<P>about her in such a way," said Lucy, eyeing Elinor attentively



<P>as she spoke; "but perhaps there may be reasons--I wish



<P>I might venture; but however I hope you will do me the justice



<P>of believing that I do not mean to be impertinent."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor made her a civil reply, and they walked

on



<P>for a few minutes in silence.&nbsp; It was broken by Lucy,



<P>who renewed the subject again by saying, with some



<P>hesitation,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I cannot bear to have you think me impertinently

curious.



<P>I am sure I would rather do any thing in the world than be



<P>thought so by a person whose good opinion is so well worth



<P>having as yours.&nbsp; And I am sure I should not have the smallest



<P>fear of trusting YOU; indeed, I should be very glad of your



<P>advice how to manage in such and uncomfortable situation



<P>as I am; but, however, there is no occasion to trouble YOU.



<P>I am sorry you do not happen to know Mrs. Ferrars."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am sorry I do NOT," said Elinor, in great

astonishment,



<P>"if it could be of any use to YOU to know my opinion of her.



<P>But really I never understood that you were at all connected



<P>with that family, and therefore I am a little surprised,



<P>I confess, at so serious an inquiry into her character."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I dare say you are, and I am sure I do not

at all



<P>wonder at it.&nbsp; But if I dared tell you all, you would not be



<P>so much surprised.&nbsp; Mrs. Ferrars is certainly nothing to me



<P>at present--but the time MAY come--how soon it will come



<P>must depend upon herself--when we may be very intimately connected."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She looked down as she said this, amiably bashful,



<P>with only one side glance at her companion to observe its



<P>effect on her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Good heavens!" cried Elinor, "what do you

mean?



<P>Are you acquainted with Mr. Robert Ferrars?&nbsp; Can you be?"



<P>And she did not feel much delighted with the idea of such



<P>a sister-in-law.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No," replied Lucy, "not to Mr. ROBERT Ferrars--I



<P>never saw him in my life; but," fixing her eyes upon Elinor,



<P>"to his eldest brother."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; What felt Elinor at that moment? Astonishment,



<P>that would have been as painful as it was strong, had not



<P>an immediate disbelief of the assertion attended it.



<P>She turned towards Lucy in silent amazement, unable to divine



<P>the reason or object of such a declaration; and though



<P>her complexion varied, she stood firm in incredulity,



<P>and felt in no danger of an hysterical fit, or a swoon.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You may well be surprised," continued Lucy;



<P>"for to be sure you could have had no idea of it before;



<P>for I dare say he never dropped the smallest hint of it



<P>to you or any of your family; because it was always meant



<P>to be a great secret, and I am sure has been faithfully



<P>kept so by me to this hour.&nbsp; Not a soul of all my relations



<P>know of it but Anne, and I never should have mentioned



<P>it to you, if I had not felt the greatest dependence



<P>in the world upon your secrecy; and I really thought my



<P>behaviour in asking so many questions about Mrs. Ferrars



<P>must seem so odd, that it ought to be explained.



<P>And I do not think Mr. Ferrars can be displeased,



<P>when he knows I have trusted you, because I know he has



<P>the highest opinion in the world of all your family,



<P>and looks upon yourself and the other Miss Dashwoods quite



<P>as his own sisters."--She paused.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor for a few moments remained silent.



<P>Her astonishment at what she heard was at first too



<P>great for words; but at length forcing herself to speak,



<P>and to speak cautiously, she said, with calmness of manner,



<P>which tolerably well concealed her surprise and solicitude--



<P>"May I ask if your engagement is of long standing?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "We have been engaged these four years."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Four years!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor, though greatly shocked, still felt

unable



<P>to believe it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I did not know," said she, "that you were

even



<P>acquainted till the other day."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Our acquaintance, however, is of many years

date.



<P>He was under my uncle's care, you know, a considerable while."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Your uncle!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes; Mr. Pratt.&nbsp; Did you never hear him

talk



<P>of Mr. Pratt?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I think I have," replied Elinor, with an exertion



<P>of spirits, which increased with her increase of emotion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "He was four years with my uncle, who lives

at Longstaple,



<P>near Plymouth.&nbsp; It was there our acquaintance begun,



<P>for my sister and me was often staying with my uncle,



<P>and it was there our engagement was formed, though not till



<P>a year after he had quitted as a pupil; but he was almost



<P>always with us afterwards.&nbsp; I was very unwilling to enter



<P>into it, as you may imagine, without the knowledge and



<P>approbation of his mother; but I was too young, and loved



<P>him too well, to be so prudent as I ought to have been.--



<P>Though you do not know him so well as me, Miss Dashwood,



<P>you must have seen enough of him to be sensible he is



<P>very capable of making a woman sincerely attached to him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Certainly," answered Elinor, without knowing

what



<P>she said; but after a moment's reflection, she added,



<P>with revived security of Edward's honour and love,



<P>and her companion's falsehood--"Engaged to Mr. Edward



<P>Ferrars!--I confess myself so totally surprised at



<P>what you tell me, that really--I beg your pardon;



<P>but surely there must be some mistake of person or name.



<P>We cannot mean the same Mr. Ferrars."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "We can mean no other," cried Lucy, smiling.&nbsp;

"Mr. Edward



<P>Ferrars, the eldest son of Mrs. Ferrars, of Park Street,



<P>and brother of your sister-in-law, Mrs. John Dashwood,



<P>is the person I mean; you must allow that I am not likely



<P>to be deceived as to the name of the man on who all my happiness depends."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is strange," replied Elinor, in a most

painful perplexity,



<P>"that I should never have heard him even mention your name."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No; considering our situation, it was not

strange.



<P>Our first care has been to keep the matter secret.--



<P>You knew nothing of me, or my family, and, therefore,



<P>there could be no OCCASION for ever mentioning my name



<P>to you; and, as he was always particularly afraid of his



<P>sister's suspecting any thing, THAT was reason enough



<P>for his not mentioning it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She was silent.--Elinor's security sunk; but

her



<P>self-command did not sink with it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Four years you have been engaged," said she



<P>with a firm voice.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes; and heaven knows how much longer we may

have



<P>to wait.&nbsp; Poor Edward!&nbsp; It puts him quite out of heart."



<P>Then taking a small miniature from her pocket, she added,



<P>"To prevent the possibility of mistake, be so good as to look



<P>at this face.&nbsp; It does not do him justice, to be sure,



<P>but yet I think you cannot be deceived as to the person



<P>it was drew for.--I have had it above these three years."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She put it into her hands as she spoke; and

when Elinor



<P>saw the painting, whatever other doubts her fear of a



<P>too hasty decision, or her wish of detecting falsehood



<P>might suffer to linger in her mind, she could have none of



<P>its being Edward's face.&nbsp; She returned it almost instantly,



<P>acknowledging the likeness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have never been able," continued Lucy, "to

give



<P>him my picture in return, which I am very much vexed at,



<P>for he has been always so anxious to get it!&nbsp; But I am



<P>determined to set for it the very first opportunity."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You are quite in the right," replied Elinor

calmly.



<P>They then proceeded a few paces in silence.&nbsp; Lucy spoke first.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am sure," said she, "I have no doubt in

the world



<P>of your faithfully keeping this secret, because you must



<P>know of what importance it is to us, not to have it reach



<P>his mother; for she would never approve of it, I dare say.



<P>I shall have no fortune, and I fancy she is an exceeding



<P>proud woman."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I certainly did not seek your confidence,"

said Elinor;



<P>"but you do me no more than justice in imagining that I



<P>may be depended on.&nbsp; Your secret is safe with me;



<P>but pardon me if I express some surprise at so unnecessary



<P>a communication.&nbsp; You must at least have felt that my



<P>being acquainted with it could not add to its safety."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As she said this, she looked earnestly at Lucy,



<P>hoping to discover something in her countenance; perhaps the



<P>falsehood of the greatest part of what she had been saying;



<P>but Lucy's countenance suffered no change.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I was afraid you would think I was taking

a great



<P>liberty with you," said she, "in telling you all this.



<P>I have not known you long to be sure, personally at least,



<P>but I have known you and all your family by description



<P>a great while; and as soon as I saw you, I felt almost as if



<P>you was an old acquaintance.&nbsp; Besides in the present case,



<P>I really thought some explanation was due to you after my



<P>making such particular inquiries about Edward's mother;



<P>and I am so unfortunate, that I have not a creature whose



<P>advice I can ask.&nbsp; Anne is the only person that knows of it,



<P>and she has no judgment at all; indeed, she does me a great



<P>deal more harm than good, for I am in constant fear of her



<P>betraying me.&nbsp; She does not know how to hold her tongue,



<P>as you must perceive, and I am sure I was in the greatest



<P>fright in the world t'other day, when Edward's name was



<P>mentioned by Sir John, lest she should out with it all.



<P>You can't think how much I go through in my mind from



<P>it altogether.&nbsp; I only wonder that I am alive after what



<P>I have suffered for Edward's sake these last four years.



<P>Every thing in such suspense and uncertainty; and seeing



<P>him so seldom--we can hardly meet above twice a-year.



<P>I am sure I wonder my heart is not quite broke."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Here she took out her handkerchief; but Elinor

did



<P>not feel very compassionate.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Sometimes." continued Lucy, after wiping her

eyes,



<P>"I think whether it would not be better for us both



<P>to break off the matter entirely."&nbsp; As she said this,



<P>she looked directly at her companion.&nbsp; "But then



<P>at other times I have not resolution enough for it.--



<P>I cannot bear the thoughts of making him so miserable,



<P>as I know the very mention of such a thing would do.



<P>And on my own account too--so dear as he is to me--I don't



<P>think I could be equal to it.&nbsp; What would you advise



<P>me to do in such a case, Miss Dashwood?&nbsp; What would you



<P>do yourself?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Pardon me," replied Elinor, startled by the

question;



<P>"but I can give you no advice under such circumstances.



<P>Your own judgment must direct you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "To be sure," continued Lucy, after a few minutes



<P>silence on both sides, "his mother must provide for him



<P>sometime or other; but poor Edward is so cast down by it!



<P>Did you not think him dreadful low-spirited when he was at



<P>Barton?&nbsp; He was so miserable when he left us at Longstaple,



<P>to go to you, that I was afraid you would think him quite ill."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Did he come from your uncle's, then, when

he visited us?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, yes; he had been staying a fortnight with

us.



<P>Did you think he came directly from town?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No," replied Elinor, most feelingly sensible

of



<P>every fresh circumstance in favour of Lucy's veracity;



<P>"I remember he told us, that he had been staying



<P>a fortnight with some friends near Plymouth."



<P>She remembered too, her own surprise at the time,



<P>at his mentioning nothing farther of those friends,



<P>at his total silence with respect even to their names.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Did not you think him sadly out of spirits?"



<P>repeated Lucy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "We did, indeed, particularly so when he first

arrived."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I begged him to exert himself for fear you



<P>should suspect what was the matter; but it made him



<P>so melancholy, not being able to stay more than a



<P>fortnight with us, and seeing me so much affected.--



<P>Poor fellow!--I am afraid it is just the same with him now;



<P>for he writes in wretched spirits.&nbsp; I heard from him just



<P>before I left Exeter;" taking a letter from her pocket



<P>and carelessly showing the direction to Elinor.



<P>"You know his hand, I dare say, a charming one it is;



<P>but that is not written so well as usual.--He was tired,



<P>I dare say, for he had just filled the sheet to me as full



<P>as possible."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor saw that it WAS his hand, and she could

doubt



<P>no longer.&nbsp; This picture, she had allowed herself to believe,



<P>might have been accidentally obtained; it might not have



<P>been Edward's gift; but a correspondence between them



<P>by letter, could subsist only under a positive engagement,



<P>could be authorised by nothing else; for a few moments, she



<P>was almost overcome--her heart sunk within her, and she could



<P>hardly stand; but exertion was indispensably necessary;



<P>and she struggled so resolutely against the oppression



<P>of her feelings, that her success was speedy, and for



<P>the time complete.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Writing to each other," said Lucy, returning

the



<P>letter into her pocket, "is the only comfort we have



<P>in such long separations.&nbsp; Yes, I have one other comfort



<P>in his picture, but poor Edward has not even THAT.



<P>If he had but my picture, he says he should be easy.



<P>I gave him a lock of my hair set in a ring when he was at



<P>Longstaple last, and that was some comfort to him, he said,



<P>but not equal to a picture.&nbsp; Perhaps you might notice



<P>the ring when you saw him?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I did," said Elinor, with a composure of voice,



<P>under which was concealed an emotion and distress beyond



<P>any thing she had ever felt before.&nbsp; She was mortified,



<P>shocked, confounded.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Fortunately for her, they had now reached the

cottage,



<P>and the conversation could be continued no farther.



<P>After sitting with them a few minutes, the Miss Steeles



<P>returned to the Park, and Elinor was then at liberty



<P>to think and be wretched.



<P>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">
