<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 36</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Within a few days after this meeting, the newspapers



<P>announced to the world, that the lady of Thomas Palmer,



<P>Esq. was safely delivered of a son and heir; a very



<P>interesting and satisfactory paragraph, at least to all



<P>those intimate connections who knew it before.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This event, highly important to Mrs. Jennings's

happiness,



<P>produced a temporary alteration in the disposal of her time,



<P>and influenced, in a like degree, the engagements



<P>of her young friends; for as she wished to be as much



<P>as possible with Charlotte, she went thither every morning



<P>as soon as she was dressed, and did not return till late



<P>in the evening; and the Miss Dashwoods, at the particular



<P>request of the Middletons, spent the whole of every day,



<P>in every day in Conduit Street.&nbsp; For their own comfort



<P>they would much rather have remained, at least all



<P>the morning, in Mrs. Jennings's house; but it was not



<P>a thing to be urged against the wishes of everybody.



<P>Their hours were therefore made over to Lady Middleton



<P>and the two Miss Steeles, by whom their company, in fact



<P>was as little valued, as it was professedly sought.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They had too much sense to be desirable companions



<P>to the former; and by the latter they were considered with



<P>a jealous eye, as intruding on THEIR ground, and sharing



<P>the kindness which they wanted to monopolize.&nbsp; Though nothing



<P>could be more polite than Lady Middleton's behaviour to



<P>Elinor and Marianne, she did not really like them at all.



<P>Because they neither flattered herself nor her children,



<P>she could not believe them good-natured; and because they



<P>were fond of reading, she fancied them satirical: perhaps



<P>without exactly knowing what it was to be satirical;



<P>but THAT did not signify.&nbsp; It was censure in common use,



<P>and easily given.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Their presence was a restraint both on her

and on Lucy.



<P>It checked the idleness of one, and the business of the other.



<P>Lady Middleton was ashamed of doing nothing before them,



<P>and the flattery which Lucy was proud to think of



<P>and administer at other times, she feared they would despise



<P>her for offering.&nbsp; Miss Steele was the least discomposed



<P>of the three, by their presence; and it was in their power



<P>to reconcile her to it entirely.&nbsp; Would either of them



<P>only have given her a full and minute account of the whole



<P>affair between Marianne and Mr. Willoughby, she would



<P>have thought herself amply rewarded for the sacrifice



<P>of the best place by the fire after dinner, which their



<P>arrival occasioned.&nbsp; But this conciliation was not granted;



<P>for though she often threw out expressions of pity for her



<P>sister to Elinor, and more than once dropt a reflection



<P>on the inconstancy of beaux before Marianne, no effect



<P>was produced, but a look of indifference from the former,



<P>or of disgust in the latter.&nbsp; An effort even yet lighter



<P>might have made her their friend.&nbsp; Would they only have



<P>laughed at her about the Doctor!&nbsp; But so little were they,



<P>anymore than the others, inclined to oblige her,



<P>that if Sir John dined from home, she might spend a whole



<P>day without hearing any other raillery on the subject,



<P>than what she was kind enough to bestow on herself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; All these jealousies and discontents, however,

were so



<P>totally unsuspected by Mrs. Jennings, that she thought



<P>it a delightful thing for the girls to be together;



<P>and generally congratulated her young friends every night,



<P>on having escaped the company of a stupid old woman so long.



<P>She joined them sometimes at Sir John's, sometimes



<P>at her own house; but wherever it was, she always came



<P>in excellent spirits, full of delight and importance,



<P>attributing Charlotte's well doing to her own care, and ready



<P>to give so exact, so minute a detail of her situation,



<P>as only Miss Steele had curiosity enough to desire.



<P>One thing DID disturb her; and of that she made her



<P>daily complaint.&nbsp; Mr. Palmer maintained the common,



<P>but unfatherly opinion among his sex, of all infants being alike;



<P>and though she could plainly perceive, at different times,



<P>the most striking resemblance between this baby and every



<P>one of his relations on both sides, there was no convincing



<P>his father of it; no persuading him to believe that it



<P>was not exactly like every other baby of the same age;



<P>nor could he even be brought to acknowledge the simple



<P>proposition of its being the finest child in the world.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; I come now to the relation of a misfortune,



<P>which about this time befell Mrs. John Dashwood.



<P>It so happened that while her two sisters with



<P>Mrs. Jennings were first calling on her in Harley Street,



<P>another of her acquaintance had dropt in--a circumstance



<P>in itself not apparently likely to produce evil to her.



<P>But while the imaginations of other people will carry



<P>them away to form wrong judgments of our conduct,



<P>and to decide on it by slight appearances, one's happiness



<P>must in some measure be always at the mercy of chance.



<P>In the present instance, this last-arrived lady allowed



<P>her fancy to so far outrun truth and probability,



<P>that on merely hearing the name of the Miss Dashwoods,



<P>and understanding them to be Mr. Dashwood's sisters,



<P>she immediately concluded them to be staying in Harley Street;



<P>and this misconstruction produced within a day



<P>or two afterwards, cards of invitation for them



<P>as well as for their brother and sister, to a small



<P>musical party at her house.&nbsp; The consequence of which was,



<P>that Mrs. John Dashwood was obliged to submit not only



<P>to the exceedingly great inconvenience of sending her



<P>carriage for the Miss Dashwoods, but, what was still worse,



<P>must be subject to all the unpleasantness of appearing



<P>to treat them with attention: and who could tell that they



<P>might not expect to go out with her a second time?&nbsp; The power



<P>of disappointing them, it was true, must always be her's.



<P>But that was not enough; for when people are determined



<P>on a mode of conduct which they know to be wrong, they feel



<P>injured by the expectation of any thing better from them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne had now been brought by degrees, so

much



<P>into the habit of going out every day, that it was become



<P>a matter of indifference to her, whether she went or not:



<P>and she prepared quietly and mechanically for every



<P>evening's engagement, though without expecting the smallest



<P>amusement from any, and very often without knowing,



<P>till the last moment, where it was to take her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; To her dress and appearance she was grown so

perfectly



<P>indifferent, as not to bestow half the consideration on it,



<P>during the whole of her toilet, which it received from



<P>Miss Steele in the first five minutes of their being



<P>together, when it was finished.&nbsp; Nothing escaped HER minute



<P>observation and general curiosity; she saw every thing,



<P>and asked every thing; was never easy till she knew the price



<P>of every part of Marianne's dress; could have guessed the



<P>number of her gowns altogether with better judgment than



<P>Marianne herself, and was not without hopes of finding out



<P>before they parted, how much her washing cost per week,



<P>and how much she had every year to spend upon herself.



<P>The impertinence of these kind of scrutinies, moreover,



<P>was generally concluded with a compliment, which



<P>though meant as its douceur, was considered by Marianne



<P>as the greatest impertinence of all; for after undergoing



<P>an examination into the value and make of her gown,



<P>the colour of her shoes, and the arrangement of her hair,



<P>she was almost sure of being told that upon "her word



<P>she looked vastly smart, and she dared to say she would



<P>make a great many conquests."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; With such encouragement as this, was she dismissed



<P>on the present occasion, to her brother's carriage;



<P>which they were ready to enter five minutes after it



<P>stopped at the door, a punctuality not very agreeable



<P>to their sister-in-law, who had preceded them to the house



<P>of her acquaintance, and was there hoping for some delay



<P>on their part that might inconvenience either herself



<P>or her coachman.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The events of this evening were not very remarkable.



<P>The party, like other musical parties, comprehended a



<P>great many people who had real taste for the performance,



<P>and a great many more who had none at all; and the performers



<P>themselves were, as usual, in their own estimation,



<P>and that of their immediate friends, the first private



<P>performers in England.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As Elinor was neither musical, nor affecting

to be so,



<P>she made no scruple of turning her eyes from the grand



<P>pianoforte, whenever it suited her, and unrestrained even



<P>by the presence of a harp, and violoncello, would fix



<P>them at pleasure on any other object in the room.&nbsp; In one



<P>of these excursive glances she perceived among a group



<P>of young men, the very he, who had given them a lecture



<P>on toothpick-cases at Gray's.&nbsp; She perceived him soon



<P>afterwards looking at herself, and speaking familiarly



<P>to her brother; and had just determined to find out his



<P>name from the latter, when they both came towards her,



<P>and Mr. Dashwood introduced him to her as Mr. Robert Ferrars.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He addressed her with easy civility, and twisted



<P>his head into a bow which assured her as plainly as



<P>words could have done, that he was exactly the coxcomb



<P>she had heard him described to be by Lucy.&nbsp; Happy had



<P>it been for her, if her regard for Edward had depended



<P>less on his own merit, than on the merit of his nearest



<P>relations!&nbsp; For then his brother's bow must have given



<P>the finishing stroke to what the ill-humour of his mother



<P>and sister would have begun.&nbsp; But while she wondered



<P>at the difference of the two young men, she did not find



<P>that the emptiness of conceit of the one, put her out



<P>of all charity with the modesty and worth of the other.



<P>Why they WERE different, Robert exclaimed to her himself



<P>in the course of a quarter of an hour's conversation;



<P>for, talking of his brother, and lamenting the extreme



<P>GAUCHERIE which he really believed kept him from mixing



<P>in proper society, he candidly and generously attributed it



<P>much less to any natural deficiency, than to the misfortune



<P>of a private education; while he himself, though probably



<P>without any particular, any material superiority



<P>by nature, merely from the advantage of a public school,



<P>was as well fitted to mix in the world as any other man.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Upon my soul," he added, "I believe it is

nothing more;



<P>and so I often tell my mother, when she is grieving



<P>about it.&nbsp; 'My dear Madam,' I always say to her, 'you must



<P>make yourself easy.&nbsp; The evil is now irremediable,



<P>and it has been entirely your own doing.&nbsp; Why would



<P>you be persuaded by my uncle, Sir Robert, against your



<P>own judgment, to place Edward under private tuition,



<P>at the most critical time of his life?&nbsp; If you had only sent



<P>him to Westminster as well as myself, instead of sending



<P>him to Mr. Pratt's, all this would have been prevented.'



<P>This is the way in which I always consider the matter,



<P>and my mother is perfectly convinced of her error."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor would not oppose his opinion, because,



<P>whatever might be her general estimation of the advantage



<P>of a public school, she could not think of Edward's



<P>abode in Mr. Pratt's family, with any satisfaction.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You reside in Devonshire, I think,"--was his



<P>next observation, "in a cottage near Dawlish."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor set him right as to its situation;



<P>and it seemed rather surprising to him that anybody



<P>could live in Devonshire, without living near Dawlish.



<P>He bestowed his hearty approbation however on their



<P>species of house.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "For my own part," said he, "I am excessively

fond



<P>of a cottage; there is always so much comfort, so much



<P>elegance about them.&nbsp; And I protest, if I had any money



<P>to spare, I should buy a little land and build one myself,



<P>within a short distance of London, where I might drive



<P>myself down at any time, and collect a few friends



<P>about me, and be happy.&nbsp; I advise every body who is going



<P>to build, to build a cottage.&nbsp; My friend Lord Courtland



<P>came to me the other day on purpose to ask my advice,



<P>and laid before me three different plans of Bonomi's.



<P>I was to decide on the best of them.&nbsp; 'My dear Courtland,'



<P>said I, immediately throwing them all into the fire, 'do not



<P>adopt either of them, but by all means build a cottage.'



<P>And that I fancy, will be the end of it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Some people imagine that there can be no accommodations,



<P>no space in a cottage; but this is all a mistake.



<P>I was last month at my friend Elliott's, near Dartford.



<P>Lady Elliott wished to give a dance.&nbsp; 'But how can it



<P>be done?' said she; 'my dear Ferrars, do tell me how it



<P>is to be managed.&nbsp; There is not a room in this cottage



<P>that will hold ten couple, and where can the supper be?'



<P>I immediately saw that there could be no difficulty in it,



<P>so I said, 'My dear Lady Elliott, do not be uneasy.



<P>The dining parlour will admit eighteen couple with ease;



<P>card-tables may be placed in the drawing-room; the library



<P>may be open for tea and other refreshments; and let the



<P>supper be set out in the saloon.'&nbsp; Lady Elliott was delighted



<P>with the thought.&nbsp; We measured the dining-room, and found



<P>it would hold exactly eighteen couple, and the affair



<P>was arranged precisely after my plan.&nbsp; So that, in fact,



<P>you see, if people do but know how to set about it,



<P>every comfort may be as well enjoyed in a cottage



<P>as in the most spacious dwelling."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor agreed to it all, for she did not think



<P>he deserved the compliment of rational opposition.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As John Dashwood had no more pleasure in music

than his



<P>eldest sister, his mind was equally at liberty to fix on



<P>any thing else; and a thought struck him during the evening,



<P>which he communicated to his wife, for her approbation,



<P>when they got home.&nbsp; The consideration of Mrs. Dennison's mistake,



<P>in supposing his sisters their guests, had suggested the



<P>propriety of their being really invited to become such,



<P>while Mrs. Jenning's engagements kept her from home.



<P>The expense would be nothing, the inconvenience not more;



<P>and it was altogether an attention which the delicacy



<P>of his conscience pointed out to be requisite to its



<P>complete enfranchisement from his promise to his father.



<P>Fanny was startled at the proposal.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I do not see how it can be done," said she,



<P>"without affronting Lady Middleton, for they spend every day



<P>with her; otherwise I should be exceedingly glad to do it.



<P>You know I am always ready to pay them any attention



<P>in my power, as my taking them out this evening shews.



<P>But they are Lady Middleton's visitors.&nbsp; How can I ask them



<P>away from her?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Her husband, but with great humility, did not

see



<P>the force of her objection.&nbsp; "They had already spent a week



<P>in this manner in Conduit Street, and Lady Middleton



<P>could not be displeased at their giving the same number



<P>of days to such near relations."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Fanny paused a moment, and then, with fresh

vigor, said,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "My love I would ask them with all my heart,

if it



<P>was in my power.&nbsp; But I had just settled within myself



<P>to ask the Miss Steeles to spend a few days with us.



<P>They are very well behaved, good kind of girls; and I think



<P>the attention is due to them, as their uncle did so very



<P>well by Edward.&nbsp; We can ask your sisters some other year,



<P>you know; but the Miss Steeles may not be in town any more.



<P>I am sure you will like them; indeed, you DO like them,



<P>you know, very much already, and so does my mother; and they



<P>are such favourites with Harry!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mr. Dashwood was convinced.&nbsp; He saw the

necessity



<P>of inviting the Miss Steeles immediately, and his conscience



<P>was pacified by the resolution of inviting his sisters



<P>another year; at the same time, however, slyly suspecting



<P>that another year would make the invitation needless,



<P>by bringing Elinor to town as Colonel Brandon's wife,



<P>and Marianne as THEIR visitor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Fanny, rejoicing in her escape, and proud of

the ready



<P>wit that had procured it, wrote the next morning to Lucy,



<P>to request her company and her sister's, for some days,



<P>in Harley Street, as soon as Lady Middleton could spare them.



<P>This was enough to make Lucy really and reasonably happy.



<P>Mrs. Dashwood seemed actually working for her, herself;



<P>cherishing all her hopes, and promoting all her views!



<P>Such an opportunity of being with Edward and his family was,



<P>above all things, the most material to her interest,



<P>and such an invitation the most gratifying to her



<P>feelings!&nbsp; It was an advantage that could not be too



<P>gratefully acknowledged, nor too speedily made use of;



<P>and the visit to Lady Middleton, which had not before had



<P>any precise limits, was instantly discovered to have been



<P>always meant to end in two days' time.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When the note was shown to Elinor, as it was

within ten



<P>minutes after its arrival, it gave her, for the first time,



<P>some share in the expectations of Lucy; for such a mark



<P>of uncommon kindness, vouchsafed on so short an acquaintance,



<P>seemed to declare that the good-will towards her arose



<P>from something more than merely malice against herself;



<P>and might be brought, by time and address, to do



<P>every thing that Lucy wished.&nbsp; Her flattery had already



<P>subdued the pride of Lady Middleton, and made an entry



<P>into the close heart of Mrs. John Dashwood; and these



<P>were effects that laid open the probability of greater.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The Miss Steeles removed to Harley Street,

and all



<P>that reached Elinor of their influence there, strengthened



<P>her expectation of the event.&nbsp; Sir John, who called on



<P>them more than once, brought home such accounts of the



<P>favour they were in, as must be universally striking.



<P>Mrs. Dashwood had never been so much pleased with any



<P>young women in her life, as she was with them; had given



<P>each of them a needle book made by some emigrant;



<P>called Lucy by her Christian name; and did not know



<P>whether she should ever be able to part with them.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter45</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 45</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor, for some time after he left her, for

some time



<P>even after the sound of his carriage had died away, remained



<P>too much oppressed by a crowd of ideas, widely differing in



<P>themselves, but of which sadness was the general result,



<P>to think even of her sister.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Willoughby, he, whom only half an hour ago

she had



<P>abhorred as the most worthless of men, Willoughby, in spite



<P>of all his faults, excited a degree of commiseration



<P>for the sufferings produced by them, which made her



<P>think of him as now separated for ever from her family,



<P>with a tenderness, a regret, rather in proportion, as she



<P>soon acknowledged within herself--to his wishes than to



<P>his merits.&nbsp; She felt that his influence over her mind



<P>was heightened by circumstances which ought not in reason



<P>to have weight; by that person of uncommon attraction,



<P>that open, affectionate, and lively manner which it



<P>was no merit to possess; and by that still ardent love



<P>for Marianne, which it was not even innocent to indulge.



<P>But she felt that it was so, long, long before she could



<P>feel his influence less.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When at last she returned to the unconscious

Marianne,



<P>she found her just awaking, refreshed by so long and sweet



<P>a sleep to the extent of her hopes.&nbsp; Elinor's heart was full.



<P>The past, the present, the future, Willoughby's visit,



<P>Marianne's safety, and her mother's expected arrival,



<P>threw her altogether into an agitation of spirits



<P>which kept off every indication of fatigue, and made



<P>her only fearful of betraying herself to her sister.



<P>Short was the time, however, in which that fear could



<P>affect her, for within half an hour after Willoughby's



<P>leaving the house, she was again called down stairs



<P>by the sound of another carriage.--Eager to save her



<P>mother from every unnecessary moment's horrible suspense,



<P>she ran immediately into the hall, and reached the outward



<P>door just in time to receive and support her as she



<P>entered it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood, whose terror as they drew near

the



<P>house had produced almost the conviction of Marianne's



<P>being no more, had no voice to inquire after her,



<P>no voice even for Elinor; but SHE, waiting neither for



<P>salutation nor inquiry, instantly gave the joyful relief;--



<P>and her mother, catching it with all her usual warmth,



<P>was in a moment as much overcome by her happiness, as she



<P>had been before by her fears.&nbsp; She was supported into



<P>the drawing-room between her daughter and her friend;--



<P>and there, shedding tears of joy, though still unable



<P>to speak, embraced Elinor again and again, turning from her



<P>at intervals to press Colonel Brandon's hand, with a look



<P>which spoke at once her gratitude, and her conviction



<P>of his sharing with herself in the bliss of the moment.



<P>He shared it, however, in a silence even greater than



<P>her own.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As soon as Mrs. Dashwood had recovered herself,



<P>to see Marianne was her first desire; and in two minutes she



<P>was with her beloved child, rendered dearer to her than ever



<P>by absence, unhappiness, and danger.&nbsp; Elinor's delight,



<P>as she saw what each felt in the meeting, was only checked



<P>by an apprehension of its robbing Marianne of farther sleep;--



<P>but Mrs. Dashwood could be calm, could be even prudent,



<P>when the life of a child was at stake, and Marianne,



<P>satisfied in knowing her mother was near her, and conscious



<P>of being too weak for conversation, submitted readily to the



<P>silence and quiet prescribed by every nurse around her.



<P>Mrs. Dashwood WOULD sit up with her all night; and Elinor,



<P>in compliance with her mother's entreaty, went to bed.



<P>But the rest, which one night entirely sleepless,



<P>and many hours of the most wearing anxiety seemed to



<P>make requisite, was kept off by irritation of spirits.



<P>Willoughby, "poor Willoughby," as she now allowed



<P>herself to call him, was constantly in her thoughts; she



<P>would not but have heard his vindication for the world,



<P>and now blamed, now acquitted herself for having judged him



<P>so harshly before.&nbsp; But her promise of relating it to her



<P>sister was invariably painful.&nbsp; She dreaded the performance



<P>of it, dreaded what its effect on Marianne might be;



<P>doubted whether after such an explanation she could ever



<P>be happy with another; and for a moment wished Willoughby



<P>a widower.&nbsp; Then, remembering Colonel Brandon, reproved herself,



<P>felt that to HIS sufferings and his constancy far more



<P>than to his rival's, the reward of her sister was due,



<P>and wished any thing rather than Mrs. Willoughby's death.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The shock of Colonel Brandon's errand at Barton

had been



<P>much softened to Mrs. Dashwood by her own previous alarm;



<P>for so great was her uneasiness about Marianne, that she



<P>had already determined to set out for Cleveland on that



<P>very day, without waiting for any further intelligence,



<P>and had so far settled her journey before his arrival,



<P>that the Careys were then expected every moment to fetch



<P>Margaret away, as her mother was unwilling to take her



<P>where there might be infection.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne continued to mend every day, and the

brilliant



<P>cheerfulness of Mrs. Dashwood's looks and spirits proved



<P>her to be, as she repeatedly declared herself, one of



<P>the happiest women in the world.&nbsp; Elinor could not hear



<P>the declaration, nor witness its proofs without sometimes



<P>wondering whether her mother ever recollected Edward.



<P>But Mrs. Dashwood, trusting to the temperate account



<P>of her own disappointment which Elinor had sent her,



<P>was led away by the exuberance of her joy to think only



<P>of what would increase it.&nbsp; Marianne was restored to her



<P>from a danger in which, as she now began to feel,



<P>her own mistaken judgment in encouraging the unfortunate



<P>attachment to Willoughby, had contributed to place her;--



<P>and in her recovery she had yet another source of joy



<P>unthought of by Elinor.&nbsp; It was thus imparted to her,



<P>as soon as any opportunity of private conference between



<P>them occurred.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "At last we are alone.&nbsp; My Elinor, you

do not yet



<P>know all my happiness.&nbsp; Colonel Brandon loves Marianne.



<P>He has told me so himself."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Her daughter, feeling by turns both pleased

and pained,



<P>surprised and not surprised, was all silent attention.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You are never like me, dear Elinor, or I should



<P>wonder at your composure now.&nbsp; Had I sat down to wish



<P>for any possible good to my family, I should have fixed



<P>on Colonel Brandon's marrying one of you as the object



<P>most desirable.&nbsp; And I believe Marianne will be the most



<P>happy with him of the two."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor was half inclined to ask her reason

for thinking so,



<P>because satisfied that none founded on an impartial



<P>consideration of their age, characters, or feelings,



<P>could be given;--but her mother must always be carried



<P>away by her imagination on any interesting subject,



<P>and therefore instead of an inquiry, she passed it off with a smile.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "He opened his whole heart to me yesterday

as we travelled.



<P>It came out quite unawares, quite undesignedly.&nbsp; I, you may



<P>well believe, could talk of nothing but my child;--he could



<P>not conceal his distress; I saw that it equalled my own,



<P>and he perhaps, thinking that mere friendship, as the world



<P>now goes, would not justify so warm a sympathy--or rather,



<P>not thinking at all, I suppose--giving way to irresistible



<P>feelings, made me acquainted with his earnest, tender, constant,



<P>affection for Marianne.&nbsp; He has loved her, my Elinor, ever since



<P>the first moment of seeing her."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Here, however, Elinor perceived,--not the language,



<P>not the professions of Colonel Brandon, but the natural



<P>embellishments of her mother's active fancy, which fashioned



<P>every thing delightful to her as it chose.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "His regard for her, infinitely surpassing

anything



<P>that Willoughby ever felt or feigned, as much more warm,



<P>as more sincere or constant--which ever we are to call it--



<P>has subsisted through all the knowledge of dear Marianne's



<P>unhappy prepossession for that worthless young man!--and



<P>without selfishness--without encouraging a hope!--could



<P>he have seen her happy with another--Such a noble mind!--



<P>such openness, such sincerity!--no one can be deceived



<P>in HIM."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Colonel Brandon's character," said Elinor,



<P>"as an excellent man, is well established."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I know it is"--replied her mother seriously,

"or



<P>after such a warning, I should be the last to encourage



<P>such affection, or even to be pleased by it.&nbsp; But his coming



<P>for me as he did, with such active, such ready friendship,



<P>is enough to prove him one of the worthiest of men."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "His character, however," answered Elinor,

"does not rest



<P>on ONE act of kindness, to which his affection for Marianne,



<P>were humanity out of the case, would have prompted him.



<P>To Mrs. Jennings, to the Middletons, he has been long



<P>and intimately known; they equally love and respect him;



<P>and even my own knowledge of him, though lately acquired,



<P>is very considerable; and so highly do I value and esteem him,



<P>that if Marianne can be happy with him, I shall be as ready



<P>as yourself to think our connection the greatest blessing



<P>to us in the world.&nbsp; What answer did you give him?--Did you



<P>allow him to hope?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh! my love, I could not then talk of hope

to him



<P>or to myself.&nbsp; Marianne might at that moment be dying.



<P>But he did not ask for hope or encouragement.&nbsp; His was



<P>an involuntary confidence, an irrepressible effusion



<P>to a soothing friend--not an application to a parent.



<P>Yet after a time I DID say, for at first I was quite



<P>overcome--that if she lived, as I trusted she might,



<P>my greatest happiness would lie in promoting their marriage;



<P>and since our arrival, since our delightful security,



<P>I have repeated it to him more fully, have given him every



<P>encouragement in my power.&nbsp; Time, a very little time,



<P>I tell him, will do everything;--Marianne's heart is



<P>not to be wasted for ever on such a man as Willoughby.--



<P>His own merits must soon secure it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "To judge from the Colonel's spirits, however,



<P>you have not yet made him equally sanguine."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No.--He thinks Marianne's affection too deeply



<P>rooted for any change in it under a great length of time,



<P>and even supposing her heart again free, is too diffident



<P>of himself to believe, that with such a difference of age



<P>and disposition he could ever attach her.&nbsp; There, however,



<P>he is quite mistaken.&nbsp; His age is only so much beyond



<P>hers as to be an advantage, as to make his character and



<P>principles fixed;--and his disposition, I am well convinced,



<P>is exactly the very one to make your sister happy.



<P>And his person, his manners too, are all in his favour.



<P>My partiality does not blind me; he certainly is not



<P>so handsome as Willoughby--but at the same time,



<P>there is something much more pleasing in his countenance.--



<P>There was always a something,--if you remember,--in Willoughby's



<P>eyes at times, which I did not like."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor could NOT remember it;--but her mother,



<P>without waiting for her assent, continued,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And his manners, the Colonel's manners are

not only



<P>more pleasing to me than Willoughby's ever were, but they



<P>are of a kind I well know to be more solidly attaching



<P>to Marianne.&nbsp; Their gentleness, their genuine attention



<P>to other people, and their manly unstudied simplicity



<P>is much more accordant with her real disposition, than



<P>the liveliness--often artificial, and often ill-timed



<P>of the other.&nbsp; I am very sure myself, that had Willoughby



<P>turned out as really amiable, as he has proved himself



<P>the contrary, Marianne would yet never have been so happy



<P>with HIM, as she will be with Colonel Brandon."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She paused.--Her daughter could not quite agree



<P>with her, but her dissent was not heard, and therefore



<P>gave no offence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "At Delaford, she will be within an easy distance

of me,"



<P>added Mrs. Dashwood, "even if I remain at Barton; and in all



<P>probability,--for I hear it is a large village,--indeed there



<P>certainly MUST be some small house or cottage close by,



<P>that would suit us quite as well as our present situation."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Poor Elinor!--here was a new scheme for getting



<P>her to Delaford!--but her spirit was stubborn.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "His fortune too!--for at my time of life you

know,



<P>everybody cares about THAT;--and though I neither know



<P>nor desire to know, what it really is, I am sure it must be



<P>a good one."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Here they were interrupted by the entrance

of a



<P>third person, and Elinor withdrew to think it all over



<P>in private, to wish success to her friend, and yet



<P>in wishing it, to feel a pang for Willoughby.



<P>
