</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 44</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor, starting back with a look of horror

at



<P>the sight of him, obeyed the first impulse of her heart



<P>in turning instantly to quit the room, and her hand



<P>was already on the lock, when its action was suspended



<P>by his hastily advancing, and saying, in a voice rather



<P>of command than supplication,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Miss Dashwood, for half an hour--for ten minutes--



<P>I entreat you to stay."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, sir," she replied with firmness, "I shall

NOT stay.



<P>Your business cannot be with ME.&nbsp; The servants, I suppose,



<P>forgot to tell you that Mr. Palmer was not in the house."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Had they told me," he cried with vehemence,



<P>"that Mr. Palmer and all his relations were at the devil,



<P>it would not have turned me from the door.&nbsp; My business



<P>is with you, and only you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "With me!"--in the utmost amazement--"well,

sir,--



<P>be quick--and if you can--less violent."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Sit down, and I will be both."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She hesitated; she knew not what to do.&nbsp;

The possibility



<P>of Colonel Brandon's arriving and finding her there,



<P>came across her.&nbsp; But she had promised to hear him,



<P>and her curiosity no less than her honor was engaged.



<P>After a moment's recollection, therefore, concluding that



<P>prudence required dispatch, and that her acquiescence would



<P>best promote it, she walked silently towards the table,



<P>and sat down.&nbsp; He took the opposite chair, and for half



<P>a minute not a word was said by either.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Pray be quick, sir,"--said Elinor, impatiently;--



<P>"I have no time to spare."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He was sitting in an attitude of deep meditation,



<P>and seemed not to hear her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Your sister," said he, with abruptness, a

moment



<P>afterwards--"is out of danger.&nbsp; I heard it from the servant.



<P>God be praised!--But is it true? is it really true?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor would not speak.&nbsp; He repeated the

inquiry



<P>with yet greater eagerness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "For God's sake tell me, is she out of danger,



<P>or is she not?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "We hope she is."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He rose up, and walked across the room.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Had I known as much half an hour ago--But



<P>since I AM here,"--speaking with a forced vivacity as he



<P>returned to his seat--"what does it signify?--For once,



<P>Miss Dashwood--it will be the last time, perhaps--let us



<P>be cheerful together.--I am in a fine mood for gaiety.--



<P>Tell me honestly"--a deeper glow overspreading his cheeks--



<P>"do you think me most a knave or a fool?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor looked at him with greater astonishment

than ever.



<P>She began to think that be must be in liquor;--the



<P>strangeness of such a visit, and of such manners,



<P>seemed no otherwise intelligible; and with this impression



<P>she immediately rose, saying,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Mr. Willoughby, I advise you at present to

return



<P>to Combe--I am not at leisure to remain with you longer.--



<P>Whatever your business may be with me, will it be better



<P>recollected and explained to-morrow."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I understand you," he replied, with an expressive

smile,



<P>and a voice perfectly calm; "yes, I am very drunk.--



<P>A pint of porter with my cold beef at Marlborough was



<P>enough to over-set me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "At Marlborough!"--cried Elinor, more and more

at



<P>a loss to understand what he would be at.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes,--I left London this morning at eight

o'clock,



<P>and the only ten minutes I have spent out of my chaise



<P>since that time procured me a nuncheon at Marlborough."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The steadiness of his manner, and the intelligence



<P>of his eye as he spoke, convincing Elinor, that whatever



<P>other unpardonable folly might bring him to Cleveland,



<P>he was not brought there by intoxication, she said,



<P>after a moment's recollection,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Mr. Willoughby, you OUGHT to feel, and I certainly



<P>DO--that after what has passed--your coming here in



<P>this manner, and forcing yourself upon my notice,



<P>requires a very particular excuse.--What is it,



<P>that you mean by it?"--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I mean,"--said he, with serious energy--"if

I can,



<P>to make you hate me one degree less than you do NOW.



<P>I mean to offer some kind of explanation, some kind



<P>of apology, for the past; to open my whole heart to you,



<P>and by convincing you, that though I have been always



<P>a blockhead, I have not been always a rascal, to obtain



<P>something like forgiveness from Ma--from your sister."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Is this the real reason of your coming?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Upon my soul it is,"--was his answer, with

a warmth



<P>which brought all the former Willoughby to her remembrance,



<P>and in spite of herself made her think him sincere.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "If that is all, you may be satisfied already,--



<P>for Marianne DOES--she has LONG forgiven you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Has she?"--he cried, in the same eager tone.--



<P>"Then she has forgiven me before she ought to have done it.



<P>But she shall forgive me again, and on more reasonable



<P>grounds.--NOW will you listen to me?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor bowed her assent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I do not know," said he, after a pause of

expectation



<P>on her side, and thoughtfulness on his own,--"how YOU



<P>may have accounted for my behaviour to your sister,



<P>or what diabolical motive you may have imputed to me.--



<P>Perhaps you will hardly think the better of me,--it is



<P>worth the trial however, and you shall hear every thing.



<P>When I first became intimate in your family, I had no



<P>other intention, no other view in the acquaintance



<P>than to pass my time pleasantly while I was obliged to remain



<P>in Devonshire, more pleasantly than I had ever done before.



<P>Your sister's lovely person and interesting manners



<P>could not but please me; and her behaviour to me almost



<P>from the first, was of a kind--It is astonishing,



<P>when I reflect on what it was, and what SHE was, that my



<P>heart should have been so insensible!&nbsp; But at first



<P>I must confess, my vanity only was elevated by it.



<P>Careless of her happiness, thinking only of my own amusement,



<P>giving way to feelings which I had always been too much



<P>in the habit of indulging, I endeavoured, by every means



<P>in my power, to make myself pleasing to her, without any



<P>design of returning her affection."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Miss Dashwood, at this point, turning her eyes

on him



<P>with the most angry contempt, stopped him, by saying,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is hardly worth while, Mr. Willoughby,



<P>for you to relate, or for me to listen any longer.



<P>Such a beginning as this cannot be followed by any thing.--



<P>Do not let me be pained by hearing any thing more on



<P>the subject."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I insist on you hearing the whole of it,"

he replied,



<P>"My fortune was never large, and I had always been expensive,



<P>always in the habit of associating with people of better



<P>income than myself.&nbsp; Every year since my coming of age,



<P>or even before, I believe, had added to my debts; and though



<P>the death of my old cousin, Mrs. Smith, was to set me free;



<P>yet that event being uncertain, and possibly far distant,



<P>it had been for some time my intention to re-establish my



<P>circumstances by marrying a woman of fortune.&nbsp; To attach



<P>myself to your sister, therefore, was not a thing to be



<P>thought of;--and with a meanness, selfishness, cruelty--



<P>which no indignant, no contemptuous look, even of yours,



<P>Miss Dashwood, can ever reprobate too much--I was acting



<P>in this manner, trying to engage her regard, without a



<P>thought of returning it.--But one thing may be said



<P>for me: even in that horrid state of selfish vanity,



<P>I did not know the extent of the injury I meditated,



<P>because I did not THEN know what it was to love.



<P>But have I ever known it?--Well may it be doubted; for, had I



<P>really loved, could I have sacrificed my feelings to vanity,



<P>to avarice?--or, what is more, could I have sacrificed hers?--



<P>But I have done it.&nbsp; To avoid a comparative poverty,



<P>which her affection and her society would have deprived



<P>of all its horrors, I have, by raising myself to affluence,



<P>lost every thing that could make it a blessing."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You did then," said Elinor, a little softened,



<P>"believe yourself at one time attached to her?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "To have resisted such attractions, to have

withstood



<P>such tenderness!--Is there a man on earth who could have



<P>done it?--Yes, I found myself, by insensible degrees,



<P>sincerely fond of her; and the happiest hours of my life



<P>were what I spent with her when I felt my intentions



<P>were strictly honourable, and my feelings blameless.



<P>Even THEN, however, when fully determined on paying



<P>my addresses to her, I allowed myself most improperly



<P>to put off, from day to day, the moment of doing it,



<P>from an unwillingness to enter into an engagement



<P>while my circumstances were so greatly embarrassed.



<P>I will not reason here--nor will I stop for YOU to expatiate



<P>on the absurdity, and the worse than absurdity, of scrupling



<P>to engage my faith where my honour was already bound.



<P>The event has proved, that I was a cunning fool,



<P>providing with great circumspection for a possible



<P>opportunity of making myself contemptible and wretched



<P>for ever.&nbsp; At last, however, my resolution was taken,



<P>and I had determined, as soon as I could engage her alone,



<P>to justify the attentions I had so invariably paid her,



<P>and openly assure her of an affection which I had already



<P>taken such pains to display.&nbsp; But in the interim--in the



<P>interim of the very few hours that were to pass, before I



<P>could have an opportunity of speaking with her in private--



<P>a circumstance occurred--an unlucky circumstance, to ruin



<P>all my resolution, and with it all my comfort.&nbsp; A discovery



<P>took place,"--here he hesitated and looked down.--"Mrs. Smith



<P>had somehow or other been informed, I imagine by some



<P>distant relation, whose interest it was to deprive me of



<P>her favour, of an affair, a connection--but I need not



<P>explain myself farther," he added, looking at her with an



<P>heightened colour and an enquiring eye--"your particular



<P>intimacy--you have probably heard the whole story long ago."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have," returned Elinor, colouring likewise,



<P>and hardening her heart anew against any compassion for him,



<P>"I have heard it all.&nbsp; And how you will explain away any



<P>part of your guilt in that dreadful business, I confess



<P>is beyond my comprehension."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Remember," cried Willoughby, "from whom you

received



<P>the account.&nbsp; Could it be an impartial one?&nbsp; I acknowledge



<P>that her situation and her character ought to have been



<P>respected by me.&nbsp; I do not mean to justify myself, but at



<P>the same time cannot leave you to suppose that I have nothing



<P>to urge--that because she was injured she was irreproachable,



<P>and because I was a libertine, SHE must be a saint.



<P>If the violence of her passions, the weakness of her



<P>understanding--I do not mean, however, to defend myself.



<P>Her affection for me deserved better treatment, and I often,



<P>with great self-reproach, recall the tenderness which,



<P>for a very short time, had the power of creating any return.



<P>I wish--I heartily wish it had never been.&nbsp; But I have injured



<P>more than herself; and I have injured one, whose affection



<P>for me--(may I say it?) was scarcely less warm than hers;



<P>and whose mind--Oh! how infinitely superior!"--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Your indifference, however, towards that unfortunate



<P>girl--I must say it, unpleasant to me as the discussion



<P>of such a subject may well be--your indifference is no



<P>apology for your cruel neglect of her.&nbsp; Do not think yourself



<P>excused by any weakness, any natural defect of understanding



<P>on her side, in the wanton cruelty so evident on yours.



<P>You must have known, that while you were enjoying yourself



<P>in Devonshire pursuing fresh schemes, always gay,



<P>always happy, she was reduced to the extremest indigence."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But, upon my soul, I did NOT know it," he

warmly



<P>replied; "I did not recollect that I had omitted to give



<P>her my direction; and common sense might have told her



<P>how to find it out."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, sir, and what said Mrs. Smith?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "She taxed me with the offence at once, and

my confusion



<P>may be guessed.&nbsp; The purity of her life, the formality



<P>of her notions, her ignorance of the world--every thing



<P>was against me.&nbsp; The matter itself I could not deny,



<P>and vain was every endeavour to soften it.&nbsp; She was



<P>previously disposed, I believe, to doubt the morality of my



<P>conduct in general, and was moreover discontented with



<P>the very little attention, the very little portion of my



<P>time that I had bestowed on her, in my present visit.



<P>In short, it ended in a total breach.&nbsp; By one measure I



<P>might have saved myself.&nbsp; In the height of her morality,



<P>good woman! she offered to forgive the past, if I would



<P>marry Eliza.&nbsp; That could not be--and I was formally



<P>dismissed from her favour and her house.&nbsp; The night



<P>following this affair--I was to go the next morning--



<P>was spent by me in deliberating on what my future conduct



<P>should be.&nbsp; The struggle was great--but it ended too soon.



<P>My affection for Marianne, my thorough conviction of her



<P>attachment to me--it was all insufficient to outweigh



<P>that dread of poverty, or get the better of those false



<P>ideas of the necessity of riches, which I was naturally



<P>inclined to feel, and expensive society had increased.



<P>I had reason to believe myself secure of my present wife,



<P>if I chose to address her, and I persuaded myself to think



<P>that nothing else in common prudence remained for me to do.



<P>A heavy scene however awaited me, before I could leave



<P>Devonshire;--I was engaged to dine with you on that very day;



<P>some apology was therefore necessary for my breaking



<P>this engagement.&nbsp; But whether I should write this apology,



<P>or deliver it in person, was a point of long debate.



<P>To see Marianne, I felt, would be dreadful, and I even doubted



<P>whether I could see her again, and keep to my resolution.



<P>In that point, however, I undervalued my own magnanimity,



<P>as the event declared; for I went, I saw her, and saw



<P>her miserable, and left her miserable--and left her hoping



<P>never to see her again."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Why did you call, Mr. Willoughby?" said Elinor,



<P>reproachfully; "a note would have answered every purpose.--



<P>Why was it necessary to call?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It was necessary to my own pride.&nbsp; I

could not bear



<P>to leave the country in a manner that might lead you,



<P>or the rest of the neighbourhood, to suspect any part



<P>of what had really passed between Mrs. Smith and myself--



<P>and I resolved therefore on calling at the cottage,



<P>in my way to Honiton.&nbsp; The sight of your dear sister,



<P>however, was really dreadful; and, to heighten the matter,



<P>I found her alone.&nbsp; You were all gone I do not know where.



<P>I had left her only the evening before, so fully,



<P>so firmly resolved within my self on doing right!



<P>A few hours were to have engaged her to me for ever;



<P>and I remember how happy, how gay were my spirits, as I



<P>walked from the cottage to Allenham, satisfied with myself,



<P>delighted with every body!&nbsp; But in this, our last interview



<P>of friendship, I approached her with a sense of guilt



<P>that almost took from me the power of dissembling.



<P>Her sorrow, her disappointment, her deep regret, when I told



<P>her that I was obliged to leave Devonshire so immediately--I



<P>never shall forget it--united too with such reliance,



<P>such confidence in me!--Oh, God!--what a hard-hearted rascal



<P>I was!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They were both silent for a few moments.



<P>Elinor first spoke.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Did you tell her that you should soon return?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I do not know what I told her," he replied,

impatiently;



<P>"less than was due to the past, beyond a doubt, and in all



<P>likelihood much more than was justified by the future.



<P>I cannot think of it.--It won't do.--Then came your dear mother



<P>to torture me farther, with all her kindness and confidence.



<P>Thank Heaven! it DID torture me.&nbsp; I was miserable.



<P>Miss Dashwood, you cannot have an idea of the comfort it



<P>gives me to look back on my own misery.&nbsp; I owe such a grudge



<P>to myself for the stupid, rascally folly of my own heart,



<P>that all my past sufferings under it are only triumph and



<P>exultation to me now.&nbsp; Well, I went, left all that I loved,



<P>and went to those to whom, at best, I was only indifferent.



<P>My journey to town--travelling with my own horses,



<P>and therefore so tediously--no creature to speak to--my



<P>own reflections so cheerful--when I looked forward



<P>every thing so inviting!--when I looked back at Barton,



<P>the picture so soothing!--oh, it was a blessed journey!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He stopped.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, sir," said Elinor, who, though pitying

him,



<P>grew impatient for his departure, "and this is all?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Ah!--no,--have you forgot what passed in town?--



<P>That infamous letter--Did she shew it you?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes, I saw every note that passed."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "When the first of hers reached me (as it immediately

did,



<P>for I was in town the whole time,) what I felt is--



<P>in the common phrase, not to be expressed; in a more



<P>simple one--perhaps too simple to raise any emotion--



<P>my feelings were very, very painful.--Every line, every word



<P>was--in the hackneyed metaphor which their dear writer,



<P>were she here, would forbid--a dagger to my heart.



<P>To know that Marianne was in town was--in the same language--



<P>a thunderbolt.--Thunderbolts and daggers!--what a reproof



<P>would she have given me!--her taste, her opinions--I believe



<P>they are better known to me than my own,--and I am sure



<P>they are dearer."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor's heart, which had undergone many changes



<P>in the course of this extraordinary conversation,



<P>was now softened again;--yet she felt it her duty to check



<P>such ideas in her companion as the last.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "This is not right, Mr. Willoughby.--Remember

that



<P>you are married.&nbsp; Relate only what in your conscience



<P>you think necessary for me to hear."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Marianne's note, by assuring me that I was

still



<P>as dear to her as in former days, that in spite of the many,



<P>many weeks we had been separated, she was as constant



<P>in her own feelings, and as full of faith in the constancy



<P>of mine as ever, awakened all my remorse.&nbsp; I say awakened,



<P>because time and London, business and dissipation,



<P>had in some measure quieted it, and I had been growing



<P>a fine hardened villain, fancying myself indifferent to her,



<P>and chusing to fancy that she too must have become



<P>indifferent to me; talking to myself of our past attachment



<P>as a mere idle, trifling business, shrugging up my shoulders



<P>in proof of its being so, and silencing every reproach,



<P>overcoming every scruple, by secretly saying now and then,



<P>'I shall be heartily glad to hear she is well married.'--



<P>But this note made me know myself better.&nbsp; I felt that



<P>she was infinitely dearer to me than any other woman



<P>in the world, and that I was using her infamously.



<P>But every thing was then just settled between Miss Grey



<P>and me.&nbsp; To retreat was impossible.&nbsp; All that I had to do,



<P>was to avoid you both.&nbsp; I sent no answer to Marianne,



<P>intending by that to preserve myself from her farther notice;



<P>and for some time I was even determined not to call in



<P>Berkeley Street;--but at last, judging it wiser to affect



<P>the air of a cool, common acquaintance than anything else,



<P>I watched you all safely out of the house one morning,



<P>and left my name."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Watched us out of the house!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Even so.&nbsp; You would be surprised to hear

how often



<P>I watched you, how often I was on the point of falling



<P>in with you.&nbsp; I have entered many a shop to avoid your sight,



<P>as the carriage drove by.&nbsp; Lodging as I did in Bond Street,



<P>there was hardly a day in which I did not catch a glimpse



<P>of one or other of you; and nothing but the most constant



<P>watchfulness on my side, a most invariably prevailing



<P>desire to keep out of your sight, could have separated us



<P>so long.&nbsp; I avoided the Middletons as much as possible,



<P>as well as everybody else who was likely to prove



<P>an acquaintance in common.&nbsp; Not aware of their being



<P>in town, however, I blundered on Sir John, I believe,



<P>the first day of his coming, and the day after I had called



<P>at Mrs. Jennings's.&nbsp; He asked me to a party, a dance at his



<P>house in the evening.--Had he NOT told me as an inducement



<P>that you and your sister were to be there, I should have



<P>felt it too certain a thing, to trust myself near him.



<P>The next morning brought another short note from Marianne--



<P>still affectionate, open, artless, confiding--everything



<P>that could make MY conduct most hateful.&nbsp; I could not



<P>answer it.&nbsp; I tried--but could not frame a sentence.



<P>But I thought of her, I believe, every moment of the day.



<P>If you CAN pity me, Miss Dashwood, pity my situation as it



<P>was THEN.&nbsp; With my head and heart full of your sister,



<P>I was forced to play the happy lover to another woman!--Those



<P>three or four weeks were worse than all.&nbsp; Well, at last,



<P>as I need not tell you, you were forced on me; and what a



<P>sweet figure I cut!--what an evening of agony it was!--



<P>Marianne, beautiful as an angel on one side, calling me



<P>Willoughby in such a tone!--Oh, God!--holding out her hand



<P>to me, asking me for an explanation, with those bewitching



<P>eyes fixed in such speaking solicitude on my face!--and



<P>Sophia, jealous as the devil on the other hand, looking



<P>all that was--Well, it does not signify; it is over now.--



<P>Such an evening!--I ran away from you all as soon as I could;



<P>but not before I had seen Marianne's sweet face as white



<P>as death.--THAT was the last, last look I ever had of her;--



<P>the last manner in which she appeared to me.&nbsp; It was a horrid



<P>sight!--yet when I thought of her to-day as really dying,



<P>it was a kind of comfort to me to imagine that I knew



<P>exactly how she would appear to those, who saw her last



<P>in this world.&nbsp; She was before me, constantly before me,



<P>as I travelled, in the same look and hue."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; A short pause of mutual thoughtfulness succeeded.



<P>Willoughby first rousing himself, broke it thus:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, let me make haste and be gone.&nbsp;

Your sister



<P>is certainly better, certainly out of danger?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "We are assured of it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Your poor mother, too!--doting on Marianne."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But the letter, Mr. Willoughby, your own letter;



<P>have you any thing to say about that?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes, yes, THAT in particular.&nbsp; Your sister



<P>wrote to me again, you know, the very next morning.



<P>You saw what she said.&nbsp; I was breakfasting at the



<P>Ellisons,--and her letter, with some others, was brought



<P>to me there from my lodgings.&nbsp; It happened to catch



<P>Sophia's eye before it caught mine--and its size,



<P>the elegance of the paper, the hand-writing altogether,



<P>immediately gave her a suspicion.&nbsp; Some vague report had



<P>reached her before of my attachment to some young lady



<P>in Devonshire, and what had passed within her observation



<P>the preceding evening had marked who the young lady was,



<P>and made her more jealous than ever.&nbsp; Affecting that air



<P>of playfulness, therefore, which is delightful in a woman



<P>one loves, she opened the letter directly, and read



<P>its contents.&nbsp; She was well paid for her impudence.



<P>She read what made her wretched.&nbsp; Her wretchedness I could



<P>have borne, but her passion--her malice--At all events it



<P>must be appeased.&nbsp; And, in short--what do you think of my



<P>wife's style of letter-writing?--delicate--tender--



<P>truly feminine--was it not?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Your wife!--The letter was in your own hand-writing."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes, but I had only the credit of servilely

copying



<P>such sentences as I was ashamed to put my name to.



<P>The original was all her own--her own happy thoughts



<P>and gentle diction.&nbsp; But what could I do!--we were engaged,



<P>every thing in preparation, the day almost fixed--But I am



<P>talking like a fool.&nbsp; Preparation!--day!--In honest words,



<P>her money was necessary to me, and in a situation like



<P>mine, any thing was to be done to prevent a rupture.



<P>And after all, what did it signify to my character



<P>in the opinion of Marianne and her friends, in what language



<P>my answer was couched?--It must have been only to one end.



<P>My business was to declare myself a scoundrel, and whether



<P>I did it with a bow or a bluster was of little importance.--



<P>'I am ruined for ever in their opinion--' said I to



<P>myself--'I am shut out for ever from their society,



<P>they already think me an unprincipled fellow, this letter



<P>will only make them think me a blackguard one.' Such were



<P>my reasonings, as, in a sort of desperate carelessness,



<P>I copied my wife's words, and parted with the last relics



<P>of Marianne.&nbsp; Her three notes--unluckily they were all



<P>in my pocketbook, or I should have denied their existence,



<P>and hoarded them for ever--I was forced to put them up,



<P>and could not even kiss them.&nbsp; And the lock of hair--that too



<P>I had always carried about me in the same pocket-book,



<P>which was now searched by Madam with the most ingratiating



<P>virulence,--the dear lock--all, every memento was torn



<P>from me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You are very wrong, Mr. Willoughby, very blamable,"



<P>said Elinor, while her voice, in spite of herself,



<P>betrayed her compassionate emotion; "you ought not to speak



<P>in this way, either of Mrs. Willoughby or my sister.



<P>You had made your own choice.&nbsp; It was not forced on you.



<P>Your wife has a claim to your politeness, to your respect,



<P>at least.&nbsp; She must be attached to you, or she would not



<P>have married you.&nbsp; To treat her with unkindness, to speak



<P>of her slightingly is no atonement to Marianne--nor can I



<P>suppose it a relief to your own conscience."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Do not talk to me of my wife," said he with

a heavy sigh.--



<P>"She does not deserve your compassion.--She knew I had no



<P>regard for her when we married.--Well, married we were,



<P>and came down to Combe Magna to be happy, and afterwards



<P>returned to town to be gay.--And now do you pity me,



<P>Miss Dashwood?--or have I said all this to no purpose?--



<P>Am I--be it only one degree--am I less guilty in your opinion



<P>than I was before?--My intentions were not always wrong.



<P>Have I explained away any part of my guilt?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes, you have certainly removed something--a

little.--



<P>You have proved yourself, on the whole, less faulty than I



<P>had believed you.&nbsp; You have proved your heart less wicked,



<P>much less wicked.&nbsp; But I hardly know--the misery that



<P>you have inflicted--I hardly know what could have made



<P>it worse."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Will you repeat to your sister when she is

recovered,



<P>what I have been telling you?--Let me be a little lightened



<P>too in her opinion as well as in yours.&nbsp; You tell me that



<P>she has forgiven me already.&nbsp; Let me be able to fancy that



<P>a better knowledge of my heart, and of my present feelings,



<P>will draw from her a more spontaneous, more natural, more



<P>gentle, less dignified, forgiveness.&nbsp; Tell her of my misery



<P>and my penitence--tell her that my heart was never inconstant



<P>to her, and if you will, that at this moment she is dearer



<P>to me than ever."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I will tell her all that is necessary to what

may



<P>comparatively be called, your justification.&nbsp; But you have



<P>not explained to me the particular reason of your coming now,



<P>nor how you heard of her illness."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Last night, in Drury Lane lobby, I ran against

Sir



<P>John Middleton, and when he saw who I was--for the first



<P>time these two months--he spoke to me.--That he had cut



<P>me ever since my marriage, I had seen without surprise



<P>or resentment.&nbsp; Now, however, his good-natured, honest,



<P>stupid soul, full of indignation against me, and concern



<P>for your sister, could not resist the temptation of telling



<P>me what he knew ought to--though probably he did not



<P>think it WOULD--vex me horridly.&nbsp; As bluntly as he could



<P>speak it, therefore, he told me that Marianne Dashwood



<P>was dying of a putrid fever at Cleveland--a letter that



<P>morning received from Mrs. Jennings declared her danger



<P>most imminent--the Palmers are all gone off in a fright,



<P>&amp;c.--I was too much shocked to be able to pass myself



<P>off as insensible even to the undiscerning Sir John.



<P>His heart was softened in seeing mine suffer; and so much



<P>of his ill-will was done away, that when we parted,



<P>he almost shook me by the hand while he reminded me of an



<P>old promise about a pointer puppy.&nbsp; What I felt on hearing



<P>that your sister was dying--and dying too, believing me



<P>the greatest villain upon earth, scorning, hating me



<P>in her latest moments--for how could I tell what horrid



<P>projects might not have been imputed?&nbsp; ONE person I



<P>was sure would represent me as capable of any thing--



<P>What I felt was dreadful!--My resolution was soon made,



<P>and at eight o'clock this morning I was in my carriage.



<P>Now you know all."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor made no answer.&nbsp; Her thoughts were

silently fixed



<P>on the irreparable injury which too early an independence



<P>and its consequent habits of idleness, dissipation, and luxury,



<P>had made in the mind, the character, the happiness,



<P>of a man who, to every advantage of person and talents,



<P>united a disposition naturally open and honest, and a



<P>feeling, affectionate temper.&nbsp; The world had made him



<P>extravagant and vain--Extravagance and vanity had made him



<P>cold-hearted and selfish.&nbsp; Vanity, while seeking its own



<P>guilty triumph at the expense of another, had involved



<P>him in a real attachment, which extravagance, or at least



<P>its offspring, necessity, had required to be sacrificed.



<P>Each faulty propensity in leading him to evil, had led



<P>him likewise to punishment.&nbsp; The attachment, from which



<P>against honour, against feeling, against every better interest



<P>he had outwardly torn himself, now, when no longer allowable,



<P>governed every thought; and the connection, for the sake



<P>of which he had, with little scruple, left her sister



<P>to misery, was likely to prove a source of unhappiness



<P>to himself of a far more incurable nature.&nbsp; From a reverie



<P>of this kind she was recalled at the end of some minutes



<P>by Willoughby, who, rousing himself from a reverie at least



<P>equally painful, started up in preparation for going, and said--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "There is no use in staying here; I must be

off."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Are you going back to town?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No--to Combe Magna.&nbsp; I have business

there;



<P>from thence to town in a day or two.&nbsp; Good bye."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He held out his hand.&nbsp; She could not refuse

to give



<P>him hers's;--he pressed it with affection.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And you DO think something better of me than



<P>you did?"--said he, letting it fall, and leaning against



<P>the mantel-piece as if forgetting he was to go.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor assured him that she did;--that she

forgave,



<P>pitied, wished him well--was even interested in his



<P>happiness--and added some gentle counsel as to the behaviour



<P>most likely to promote it.&nbsp; His answer was not very encouraging.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "As to that," said he, "I must rub through

the world



<P>as well as I can.&nbsp; Domestic happiness is out of the question.



<P>If, however, I am allowed to think that you and yours feel



<P>an interest in my fate and actions, it may be the means--it



<P>may put me on my guard--at least, it may be something to



<P>live for.&nbsp; Marianne to be sure is lost to me for ever.



<P>Were I even by any blessed chance at liberty again--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor stopped him with a reproof.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well,"--he replied--"once more good bye. I

shall



<P>now go away and live in dread of one event."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What do you mean?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Your sister's marriage."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You are very wrong.&nbsp; She can never be

more lost



<P>to you than she is now."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But she will be gained by some one else.&nbsp;

And if



<P>that some one should be the very he whom, of all others,



<P>I could least bear--but I will not stay to rob myself



<P>of all your compassionate goodwill, by shewing



<P>that where I have most injured I can least forgive.



<P>Good bye,--God bless you!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; And with these words, he almost ran out of

the room.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter50</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 50</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; After a proper resistance on the part of Mrs.

Ferrars,



<P>just so violent and so steady as to preserve her from that



<P>reproach which she always seemed fearful of incurring,



<P>the reproach of being too amiable, Edward was admitted



<P>to her presence, and pronounced to be again her son.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Her family had of late been exceedingly fluctuating.



<P>For many years of her life she had had two sons;



<P>but the crime and annihilation of Edward a few weeks ago,



<P>had robbed her of one; the similar annihilation of Robert



<P>had left her for a fortnight without any; and now,



<P>by the resuscitation of Edward, she had one again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In spite of his being allowed once more to

live,



<P>however, he did not feel the continuance of his existence



<P>secure, till he had revealed his present engagement;



<P>for the publication of that circumstance, he feared,



<P>might give a sudden turn to his constitution, and carry



<P>him off as rapidly as before.&nbsp; With apprehensive caution



<P>therefore it was revealed, and he was listened to with



<P>unexpected calmness.&nbsp; Mrs. Ferrars at first reasonably



<P>endeavoured to dissuade him from marrying Miss Dashwood,



<P>by every argument in her power;--told him, that in Miss Morton



<P>he would have a woman of higher rank and larger fortune;--



<P>and enforced the assertion, by observing that Miss Morton



<P>was the daughter of a nobleman with thirty thousand pounds,



<P>while Miss Dashwood was only the daughter of a private



<P>gentleman with no more than THREE; but when she found that,



<P>though perfectly admitting the truth of her representation,



<P>he was by no means inclined to be guided by it,



<P>she judged it wisest, from the experience of the past,



<P>to submit--and therefore, after such an ungracious delay



<P>as she owed to her own dignity, and as served to prevent



<P>every suspicion of good-will, she issued her decree



<P>of consent to the marriage of Edward and Elinor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; What she would engage to do towards augmenting



<P>their income was next to be considered; and here it



<P>plainly appeared, that though Edward was now her only son,



<P>he was by no means her eldest; for while Robert was



<P>inevitably endowed with a thousand pounds a-year,



<P>not the smallest objection was made against Edward's taking



<P>orders for the sake of two hundred and fifty at the utmost;



<P>nor was anything promised either for the present or in future,



<P>beyond the ten thousand pounds, which had been given with Fanny.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; It was as much, however, as was desired,



<P>and more than was expected, by Edward and Elinor;



<P>and Mrs. Ferrars herself, by her shuffling excuses,



<P>seemed the only person surprised at her not giving more.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; With an income quite sufficient to their wants



<P>thus secured to them, they had nothing to wait for



<P>after Edward was in possession of the living, but the



<P>readiness of the house, to which Colonel Brandon,



<P>with an eager desire for the accommodation of Elinor,



<P>was making considerable improvements; and after waiting



<P>some time for their completion, after experiencing,



<P>as usual, a thousand disappointments and delays



<P>from the unaccountable dilatoriness of the workmen, Elinor,



<P>as usual, broke through the first positive resolution



<P>of not marrying till every thing was ready, and the



<P>ceremony took place in Barton church early in the autumn.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The first month after their marriage was spent



<P>with their friend at the Mansion-house; from whence



<P>they could superintend the progress of the Parsonage,



<P>and direct every thing as they liked on the spot;--



<P>could chuse papers, project shrubberies, and invent a sweep.



<P>Mrs. Jennings's prophecies, though rather jumbled together,



<P>were chiefly fulfilled; for she was able to visit Edward



<P>and his wife in their Parsonage by Michaelmas, and she



<P>found in Elinor and her husband, as she really believed,



<P>one of the happiest couples in the world.&nbsp; They had



<P>in fact nothing to wish for, but the marriage of Colonel



<P>Brandon and Marianne, and rather better pasturage for



<P>their cows.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They were visited on their first settling by

almost



<P>all their relations and friends.&nbsp; Mrs. Ferrars came



<P>to inspect the happiness which she was almost ashamed



<P>of having authorised; and even the Dashwoods were at



<P>the expense of a journey from Sussex to do them honour.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I will not say that I am disappointed, my

dear sister,"



<P>said John, as they were walking together one morning before



<P>the gates of Delaford House, "THAT would be saying too much,



<P>for certainly you have been one of the most fortunate young



<P>women in the world, as it is.&nbsp; But, I confess, it would



<P>give me great pleasure to call Colonel Brandon brother.



<P>His property here, his place, his house, every thing is in



<P>such respectable and excellent condition!--and his woods!--I



<P>have not seen such timber any where in Dorsetshire, as there



<P>is now standing in Delaford Hanger!--And though, perhaps,



<P>Marianne may not seem exactly the person to attract him--



<P>yet I think it would altogether be advisable for you to



<P>have them now frequently staying with you, for as Colonel



<P>Brandon seems a great deal at home, nobody can tell what



<P>may happen--for, when people are much thrown together,



<P>and see little of anybody else--and it will always be



<P>in your power to set her off to advantage, and so forth;--



<P>in short, you may as well give her a chance--You understand me."--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; But though Mrs. Ferrars DID come to see them,

and always



<P>treated them with the make-believe of decent affection,



<P>they were never insulted by her real favour and preference.



<P>THAT was due to the folly of Robert, and the cunning



<P>of his wife; and it was earned by them before many months



<P>had passed away.&nbsp; The selfish sagacity of the latter,



<P>which had at first drawn Robert into the scrape,



<P>was the principal instrument of his deliverance from it;



<P>for her respectful humility, assiduous attentions,



<P>and endless flatteries, as soon as the smallest opening



<P>was given for their exercise, reconciled Mrs. Ferrars



<P>to his choice, and re-established him completely in



<P>her favour.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The whole of Lucy's behaviour in the affair,



<P>and the prosperity which crowned it, therefore, may be held



<P>forth as a most encouraging instance of what an earnest,



<P>an unceasing attention to self-interest, however its progress



<P>may be apparently obstructed, will do in securing every



<P>advantage of fortune, with no other sacrifice than that of time



<P>and conscience.&nbsp; When Robert first sought her acquaintance,



<P>and privately visited her in Bartlett's Buildings,



<P>it was only with the view imputed to him by his brother.



<P>He merely meant to persuade her to give up the engagement;



<P>and as there could be nothing to overcome but the affection



<P>of both, he naturally expected that one or two interviews



<P>would settle the matter.&nbsp; In that point, however,



<P>and that only, he erred;--for though Lucy soon gave him



<P>hopes that his eloquence would convince her in TIME,



<P>another visit, another conversation, was always wanted



<P>to produce this conviction.&nbsp; Some doubts always lingered



<P>in her mind when they parted, which could only be



<P>removed by another half hour's discourse with himself.



<P>His attendance was by this means secured, and the rest



<P>followed in course.&nbsp; Instead of talking of Edward,



<P>they came gradually to talk only of Robert,--a subject



<P>on which he had always more to say than on any other,



<P>and in which she soon betrayed an interest even equal



<P>to his own; and in short, it became speedily evident



<P>to both, that he had entirely supplanted his brother.



<P>He was proud of his conquest, proud of tricking Edward,



<P>and very proud of marrying privately without his



<P>mother's consent.&nbsp; What immediately followed is known.



<P>They passed some months in great happiness at Dawlish;



<P>for she had many relations and old acquaintances to



<P>cut--and he drew several plans for magnificent cottages;--



<P>and from thence returning to town, procured the forgiveness



<P>of Mrs. Ferrars, by the simple expedient of asking it,



<P>which, at Lucy's instigation, was adopted.&nbsp; The forgiveness,



<P>at first, indeed, as was reasonable, comprehended only Robert;



<P>and Lucy, who had owed his mother no duty and therefore



<P>could have transgressed none, still remained some weeks



<P>longer unpardoned.&nbsp; But perseverance in humility of conduct



<P>and messages, in self-condemnation for Robert's offence,



<P>and gratitude for the unkindness she was treated with,



<P>procured her in time the haughty notice which overcame



<P>her by its graciousness, and led soon afterwards, by rapid



<P>degrees, to the highest state of affection and influence.



<P>Lucy became as necessary to Mrs. Ferrars, as either Robert



<P>or Fanny; and while Edward was never cordially forgiven



<P>for having once intended to marry her, and Elinor,



<P>though superior to her in fortune and birth, was spoken



<P>of as an intruder, SHE was in every thing considered,



<P>and always openly acknowledged, to be a favourite child.



<P>They settled in town, received very liberal assistance



<P>from Mrs. Ferrars, were on the best terms imaginable



<P>with the Dashwoods; and setting aside the jealousies



<P>and ill-will continually subsisting between Fanny and Lucy,



<P>in which their husbands of course took a part, as well



<P>as the frequent domestic disagreements between Robert and



<P>Lucy themselves, nothing could exceed the harmony in which



<P>they all lived together.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; What Edward had done to forfeit the right of

eldest



<P>son, might have puzzled many people to find out; and what



<P>Robert had done to succeed to it, might have puzzled them



<P>still more.&nbsp; It was an arrangement, however, justified in



<P>its effects, if not in its cause; for nothing ever



<P>appeared in Robert's style of living or of talking to give



<P>a suspicion of his regretting the extent of his income,



<P>as either leaving his brother too little, or bringing



<P>himself too much;--and if Edward might be judged from



<P>the ready discharge of his duties in every particular,



<P>from an increasing attachment to his wife and his home,



<P>and from the regular cheerfulness of his spirits,



<P>he might be supposed no less contented with his lot,



<P>no less free from every wish of an exchange.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor's marriage divided her as little from

her



<P>family as could well be contrived, without rendering



<P>the cottage at Barton entirely useless, for her mother



<P>and sisters spent much more than half their time with her.



<P>Mrs. Dashwood was acting on motives of policy as well



<P>as pleasure in the frequency of her visits at Delaford;



<P>for her wish of bringing Marianne and Colonel Brandon together



<P>was hardly less earnest, though rather more liberal than



<P>what John had expressed.&nbsp; It was now her darling object.



<P>Precious as was the company of her daughter to her,



<P>she desired nothing so much as to give up its constant



<P>enjoyment to her valued friend; and to see Marianne settled at



<P>the mansion-house was equally the wish of Edward and Elinor.



<P>They each felt his sorrows, and their own obligations,



<P>and Marianne, by general consent, was to be the reward



<P>of all.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; With such a confederacy against her--with a

knowledge



<P>so intimate of his goodness--with a conviction of his fond



<P>attachment to herself, which at last, though long after it



<P>was observable to everybody else--burst on her--what could she do?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne Dashwood was born to an extraordinary

fate.



<P>She was born to discover the falsehood of her own opinions,



<P>and to counteract, by her conduct, her most favourite maxims.



<P>She was born to overcome an affection formed so late



<P>in life as at seventeen, and with no sentiment



<P>superior to strong esteem and lively friendship,



<P>voluntarily to give her hand to another!--and THAT other,



<P>a man who had suffered no less than herself under the



<P>event of a former attachment, whom, two years before,



<P>she had considered too old to be married,--and who still



<P>sought the constitutional safeguard of a flannel waistcoat!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; But so it was.&nbsp; Instead of falling a sacrifice



<P>to an irresistible passion, as once she had fondly



<P>flattered herself with expecting,--instead of remaining



<P>even for ever with her mother, and finding her only



<P>pleasures in retirement and study, as afterwards in her



<P>more calm and sober judgment she had determined on,--



<P>she found herself at nineteen, submitting to new attachments,



<P>entering on new duties, placed in a new home, a wife,



<P>the mistress of a family, and the patroness of a village.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Colonel Brandon was now as happy, as all those

who best



<P>loved him, believed he deserved to be;--in Marianne he



<P>was consoled for every past affliction;--her regard and her



<P>society restored his mind to animation, and his spirits



<P>to cheerfulness; and that Marianne found her own happiness



<P>in forming his, was equally the persuasion and delight



<P>of each observing friend.&nbsp; Marianne could never love



<P>by halves; and her whole heart became, in time, as much



<P>devoted to her husband, as it had once been to Willoughby.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Willoughby could not hear of her marriage without



<P>a pang; and his punishment was soon afterwards complete



<P>in the voluntary forgiveness of Mrs. Smith, who, by stating



<P>his marriage with a woman of character, as the source



<P>of her clemency, gave him reason for believing that had he



<P>behaved with honour towards Marianne, he might at once have



<P>been happy and rich.&nbsp; That his repentance of misconduct,



<P>which thus brought its own punishment, was sincere,



<P>need not be doubted;--nor that he long thought of Colonel



<P>Brandon with envy, and of Marianne with regret.&nbsp; But that



<P>he was for ever inconsolable, that he fled from society,



<P>or contracted an habitual gloom of temper, or died of a



<P>broken heart, must not be depended on--for he did neither.



<P>He lived to exert, and frequently to enjoy himself.



<P>His wife was not always out of humour, nor his home



<P>always uncomfortable; and in his breed of horses and dogs,



<P>and in sporting of every kind, he found no inconsiderable



<P>degree of domestic felicity.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; For Marianne, however--in spite of his incivility



<P>in surviving her loss--he always retained that decided



<P>regard which interested him in every thing that befell her,



<P>and made her his secret standard of perfection in woman;--



<P>and many a rising beauty would be slighted by him in



<P>after-days as bearing no comparison with Mrs. Brandon.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood was prudent enough to remain

at the cottage,



<P>without attempting a removal to Delaford; and fortunately for



<P>Sir John and Mrs. Jennings, when Marianne was taken from them,



<P>Margaret had reached an age highly suitable for dancing,



<P>and not very ineligible for being supposed to have a lover.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Between Barton and Delaford, there was that

constant



<P>communication which strong family affection would



<P>naturally dictate;--and among the merits and the happiness



<P>of Elinor and Marianne, let it not be ranked as the least



<P>considerable, that though sisters, and living almost within



<P>sight of each other, they could live without disagreement



<P>between themselves, or producing coolness between their husbands.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<UL>

<UL>

<UL><B><FONT SIZE=+1>THE END</FONT></B></UL>

</UL>

</UL>
