<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter46</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 46</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne's illness, though weakening in its

kind,



<P>had not been long enough to make her recovery slow;



<P>and with youth, natural strength, and her mother's presence



<P>in aid, it proceeded so smoothly as to enable her to remove,



<P>within four days after the arrival of the latter,



<P>into Mrs. Palmer's dressing-room.&nbsp; When there, at her own



<P>particular request, for she was impatient to pour forth



<P>her thanks to him for fetching her mother, Colonel Brandon



<P>was invited to visit her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; His emotion on entering the room, in seeing

her altered



<P>looks, and in receiving the pale hand which she immediately



<P>held out to him, was such, as, in Elinor's conjecture,



<P>must arise from something more than his affection for Marianne,



<P>or the consciousness of its being known to others;



<P>and she soon discovered in his melancholy eye and varying



<P>complexion as he looked at her sister, the probable



<P>recurrence of many past scenes of misery to his mind,



<P>brought back by that resemblance between Marianne and Eliza



<P>already acknowledged, and now strengthened by the hollow eye,



<P>the sickly skin, the posture of reclining weakness,



<P>and the warm acknowledgment of peculiar obligation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood, not less watchful of what passed

than



<P>her daughter, but with a mind very differently influenced,



<P>and therefore watching to very different effect,



<P>saw nothing in the Colonel's behaviour but what arose



<P>from the most simple and self-evident sensations, while in



<P>the actions and words of Marianne she persuaded herself



<P>to think that something more than gratitude already dawned.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; At the end of another day or two, Marianne

growing



<P>visibly stronger every twelve hours, Mrs. Dashwood,



<P>urged equally by her own and her daughter's wishes,



<P>began to talk of removing to Barton.&nbsp; On HER measures



<P>depended those of her two friends; Mrs. Jennings could



<P>not quit Cleveland during the Dashwoods' stay; and Colonel



<P>Brandon was soon brought, by their united request,



<P>to consider his own abode there as equally determinate,



<P>if not equally indispensable.&nbsp; At his and Mrs. Jennings's



<P>united request in return, Mrs. Dashwood was prevailed



<P>on to accept the use of his carriage on her journey back,



<P>for the better accommodation of her sick child; and the Colonel,



<P>at the joint invitation of Mrs. Dashwood and Mrs. Jennings,



<P>whose active good-nature made her friendly and hospitable



<P>for other people as well as herself, engaged with pleasure



<P>to redeem it by a visit at the cottage, in the course



<P>of a few weeks.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The day of separation and departure arrived;



<P>and Marianne, after taking so particular and lengthened



<P>a leave of Mrs. Jennings, one so earnestly grateful, so full



<P>of respect and kind wishes as seemed due to her own heart



<P>from a secret acknowledgment of past inattention, and bidding



<P>Colonel Brandon farewell with a cordiality of a friend,



<P>was carefully assisted by him into the carriage, of which he



<P>seemed anxious that she should engross at least half.



<P>Mrs. Dashwood and Elinor then followed, and the others



<P>were left by themselves, to talk of the travellers,



<P>and feel their own dullness, till Mrs. Jennings was summoned



<P>to her chaise to take comfort in the gossip of her maid



<P>for the loss of her two young companions; and Colonel Brandon



<P>immediately afterwards took his solitary way to Delaford.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The Dashwoods were two days on the road, and

Marianne



<P>bore her journey on both, without essential fatigue.



<P>Every thing that the most zealous affection, the most



<P>solicitous care could do to render her comfortable,



<P>was the office of each watchful companion, and each



<P>found their reward in her bodily ease, and her calmness



<P>of spirits.&nbsp; To Elinor, the observation of the latter



<P>was particularly grateful.&nbsp; She, who had seen her week



<P>after week so constantly suffering, oppressed by anguish



<P>of heart which she had neither courage to speak of,



<P>nor fortitude to conceal, now saw with a joy, which no other



<P>could equally share, an apparent composure of mind, which,



<P>in being the result as she trusted of serious reflection,



<P>must eventually lead her to contentment and cheerfulness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As they approached Barton, indeed, and entered



<P>on scenes of which every field and every tree brought



<P>some peculiar, some painful recollection, she grew silent



<P>and thoughtful, and turning away her face from their notice,



<P>sat earnestly gazing through the window.&nbsp; But here,



<P>Elinor could neither wonder nor blame; and when she saw,



<P>as she assisted Marianne from the carriage, that she



<P>had been crying, she saw only an emotion too natural



<P>in itself to raise any thing less tender than pity,



<P>and in its unobtrusiveness entitled to praise.&nbsp; In the



<P>whole of her subsequent manner, she traced the direction



<P>of a mind awakened to reasonable exertion; for no sooner



<P>had they entered their common sitting-room, than Marianne



<P>turned her eyes around it with a look of resolute firmness,



<P>as if determined at once to accustom herself to the sight



<P>of every object with which the remembrance of Willoughby could



<P>be connected.--She said little, but every sentence aimed



<P>at cheerfulness, and though a sigh sometimes escaped her,



<P>it never passed away without the atonement of a smile.



<P>After dinner she would try her piano-forte. She went to it;



<P>but the music on which her eye first rested was an opera,



<P>procured for her by Willoughby, containing some of their



<P>favourite duets, and bearing on its outward leaf her own name



<P>in his hand-writing.--That would not do.--She shook her head,



<P>put the music aside, and after running over the keys



<P>for a minute, complained of feebleness in her fingers,



<P>and closed the instrument again; declaring however



<P>with firmness as she did so, that she should in future



<P>practice much.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The next morning produced no abatement in these



<P>happy symptoms.&nbsp; On the contrary, with a mind and body



<P>alike strengthened by rest, she looked and spoke with



<P>more genuine spirit, anticipating the pleasure of



<P>Margaret's return, and talking of the dear family party



<P>which would then be restored, of their mutual pursuits



<P>and cheerful society, as the only happiness worth a wish.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "When the weather is settled, and I have recovered



<P>my strength," said she, "we will take long walks together



<P>every day.&nbsp; We will walk to the farm at the edge of the down,



<P>and see how the children go on; we will walk to Sir John's



<P>new plantations at Barton Cross, and the Abbeyland;



<P>and we will often go the old ruins of the Priory,



<P>and try to trace its foundations as far as we are told



<P>they once reached.&nbsp; I know we shall be happy.&nbsp; I know



<P>the summer will pass happily away.&nbsp; I mean never to be



<P>later in rising than six, and from that time till dinner



<P>I shall divide every moment between music and reading.



<P>I have formed my plan, and am determined to enter on a course



<P>of serious study.&nbsp; Our own library is too well known to me,



<P>to be resorted to for any thing beyond mere amusement.



<P>But there are many works well worth reading at the Park;



<P>and there are others of more modern production which I



<P>know I can borrow of Colonel Brandon.&nbsp; By reading only six



<P>hours a-day, I shall gain in the course of a twelve-month



<P>a great deal of instruction which I now feel myself



<P>to want."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor honoured her for a plan which originated



<P>so nobly as this; though smiling to see the same eager



<P>fancy which had been leading her to the extreme of languid



<P>indolence and selfish repining, now at work in introducing



<P>excess into a scheme of such rational employment and virtuous



<P>self-control. Her smile however changed to a sigh when she



<P>remembered that promise to Willoughby was yet unfulfilled,



<P>and feared she had that to communicate which might again



<P>unsettle the mind of Marianne, and ruin at least for a time



<P>this fair prospect of busy tranquillity.&nbsp; Willing therefore



<P>to delay the evil hour, she resolved to wait till her



<P>sister's health were more secure, before she appointed it.



<P>But the resolution was made only to be broken.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne had been two or three days at home,

before



<P>the weather was fine enough for an invalid like herself



<P>to venture out.&nbsp; But at last a soft, genial morning appeared;



<P>such as might tempt the daughter's wishes and the



<P>mother's confidence; and Marianne, leaning on Elinor's arm,



<P>was authorised to walk as long as she could without fatigue,



<P>in the lane before the house.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The sisters set out at a pace, slow as the

feebleness



<P>of Marianne in an exercise hitherto untried since her



<P>illness required;--and they had advanced only so far



<P>beyond the house as to admit a full view of the hill,



<P>the important hill behind, when pausing with her eyes



<P>turned towards it, Marianne calmly said,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "There, exactly there,"--pointing with one

hand,



<P>"on that projecting mound,--there I fell; and there I



<P>first saw Willoughby."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Her voice sunk with the word, but presently

reviving



<P>she added,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am thankful to find that I can look with

so little pain



<P>on the spot!--shall we ever talk on that subject, Elinor?"--



<P>hesitatingly it was said.--"Or will it be wrong?--I can talk



<P>of it now, I hope, as I ought to do."--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor tenderly invited her to be open.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "As for regret," said Marianne, "I have done

with that,



<P>as far as HE is concerned.&nbsp; I do not mean to talk to you



<P>of what my feelings have been for him, but what they



<P>are NOW.--At present, if I could be satisfied on one point,



<P>if I could be allowed to think that he was not ALWAYS



<P>acting a part, not ALWAYS deceiving me;--but above all,



<P>if I could be assured that he never was so VERY wicked



<P>as my fears have sometimes fancied him, since the story



<P>of that unfortunate girl"--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She stopt.&nbsp; Elinor joyfully treasured

her words



<P>as she answered,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "If you could be assured of that, you think

you



<P>should be easy."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes. My peace of mind is doubly involved in

it;--



<P>for not only is it horrible to suspect a person, who has



<P>been what HE has been to ME, of such designs,--but what must



<P>it make me appear to myself?--What in a situation like mine,



<P>but a most shamefully unguarded affection could expose



<P>me to"--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How then," asked her sister, "would you account



<P>for his behaviour?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I would suppose him,--Oh, how gladly would

I suppose



<P>him, only fickle, very, very fickle."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor said no more.&nbsp; She was debating

within herself



<P>on the eligibility of beginning her story directly,



<P>or postponing it till Marianne were in stronger health;--



<P>and they crept on for a few minutes in silence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am not wishing him too much good," said

Marianne



<P>at last with a sigh, "when I wish his secret reflections



<P>may be no more unpleasant than my own.&nbsp; He will suffer



<P>enough in them."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Do you compare your conduct with his?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No. I compare it with what it ought to have

been;



<P>I compare it with yours."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Our situations have borne little resemblance."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "They have borne more than our conduct.--Do

not,



<P>my dearest Elinor, let your kindness defend what I know



<P>your judgment must censure.&nbsp; My illness has made me think--



<P>It has given me leisure and calmness for serious recollection.



<P>Long before I was enough recovered to talk, I was perfectly



<P>able to reflect.&nbsp; I considered the past: I saw in my



<P>own behaviour, since the beginning of our acquaintance



<P>with him last autumn, nothing but a series of imprudence



<P>towards myself, and want of kindness to others.



<P>I saw that my own feelings had prepared my sufferings,



<P>and that my want of fortitude under them had almost led



<P>me to the grave.&nbsp; My illness, I well knew, had been



<P>entirely brought on by myself by such negligence of my



<P>own health, as I had felt even at the time to be wrong.



<P>Had I died,--it would have been self-destruction. I



<P>did not know my danger till the danger was removed;



<P>but with such feelings as these reflections gave me,



<P>I wonder at my recovery,--wonder that the very eagerness



<P>of my desire to live, to have time for atonement to my God,



<P>and to you all, did not kill me at once.&nbsp; Had I died,--



<P>in what peculiar misery should I have left you, my nurse,



<P>my friend, my sister!--You, who had seen all the fretful



<P>selfishness of my latter days; who had known all the



<P>murmurings of my heart!--How should I have lived in YOUR



<P>remembrance!--My mother too! How could you have consoled



<P>her!--I cannot express my own abhorrence of myself.



<P>Whenever I looked towards the past, I saw some duty neglected,



<P>or some failing indulged.&nbsp; Every body seemed injured by me.



<P>The kindness, the unceasing kindness of Mrs. Jennings,



<P>I had repaid with ungrateful contempt.&nbsp; To the Middletons,



<P>to the Palmers, the Steeles, to every common acquaintance even,



<P>I had been insolent and unjust; with a heart hardened



<P>against their merits, and a temper irritated by their



<P>very attention.--To John, to Fanny,--yes, even to them,



<P>little as they deserve, I had given less than their due.



<P>But you,--you above all, above my mother, had been wronged



<P>by me.&nbsp; I, and only I, knew your heart and its sorrows;



<P>yet to what did it influence me?--not to any compassion



<P>that could benefit you or myself.--Your example was



<P>before me; but to what avail?--Was I more considerate



<P>of you and your comfort?&nbsp; Did I imitate your forbearance,



<P>or lessen your restraints, by taking any part in those



<P>offices of general complaisance or particular gratitude



<P>which you had hitherto been left to discharge alone?--No;--



<P>not less when I knew you to be unhappy, than when I



<P>had believed you at ease, did I turn away from every



<P>exertion of duty or friendship; scarcely allowing sorrow



<P>to exist but with me, regretting only THAT heart which



<P>had deserted and wronged me, and leaving you, for or I



<P>professed an unbounded affection, to be miserable for my



<P>sake."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Here ceased the rapid flow of her self-reproving

spirit;



<P>and Elinor, impatient to soothe, though too honest



<P>to flatter, gave her instantly that praise and support



<P>which her frankness and her contrition so well deserved.



<P>Marianne pressed her hand and replied,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You are very good.--The future must be my

proof.



<P>I have laid down my plan, and if I am capable of adhering



<P>to it--my feelings shall be governed and my temper improved.



<P>They shall no longer worry others, nor torture myself.



<P>I shall now live solely for my family.&nbsp; You, my mother,



<P>and Margaret, must henceforth be all the world to me;



<P>you will share my affections entirely between you.



<P>>From you, from my home, I shall never again have the smallest



<P>incitement to move; and if I do mix in other society,



<P>it will be only to shew that my spirit is humbled,



<P>my heart amended, and that I can practise the civilities,



<P>the lesser duties of life, with gentleness and forbearance.



<P>As for Willoughby--to say that I shall soon or that I shall



<P>ever forget him, would be idle.&nbsp; His remembrance can be overcome



<P>by no change of circumstances or opinions.&nbsp; But it shall



<P>be regulated, it shall be checked by religion, by reason,



<P>by constant employment."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She paused--and added in a low voice, "If I

could



<P>but know HIS heart, everything would become easy."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor, who had now been for some time reflecting



<P>on the propriety or impropriety of speedily hazarding



<P>her narration, without feeling at all nearer decision than



<P>at first, heard this; and perceiving that as reflection



<P>did nothing, resolution must do all, soon found herself



<P>leading to the fact.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She managed the recital, as she hoped, with

address;



<P>prepared her anxious listener with caution; related simply



<P>and honestly the chief points on which Willoughby



<P>grounded his apology; did justice to his repentance,



<P>and softened only his protestations of present regard.



<P>Marianne said not a word.--She trembled, her eyes



<P>were fixed on the ground, and her lips became whiter



<P>than even sickness had left them.&nbsp; A thousand inquiries



<P>sprung up from her heart, but she dared not urge one.



<P>She caught every syllable with panting eagerness; her hand,



<P>unknowingly to herself, closely pressed her sister's, and



<P>tears covered her cheeks.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor, dreading her being tired, led her towards

home;



<P>and till they reached the door of the cottage,



<P>easily conjecturing what her curiosity must be



<P>though no question was suffered to speak it, talked of



<P>nothing but Willoughby, and their conversation together;



<P>and was carefully minute in every particular of speech



<P>and look, where minuteness could be safely indulged.



<P>As soon as they entered the house, Marianne with a kiss



<P>of gratitude and these two words just articulate through



<P>her tears, "Tell mama," withdrew from her sister and



<P>walked slowly up stairs.&nbsp; Elinor would not attempt



<P>to disturb a solitude so reasonable as what she now sought;



<P>and with a mind anxiously pre-arranging its result,



<P>and a resolution of reviving the subject again,



<P>should Marianne fail to do it, she turned into the parlour



<P>to fulfill her parting injunction.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter47</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 47</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood did not hear unmoved the vindication



<P>of her former favourite.&nbsp; She rejoiced in his being



<P>cleared from some part of his imputed guilt;--she was



<P>sorry for him;--she wished him happy.&nbsp; But the feelings



<P>of the past could not be recalled.--Nothing could restore



<P>him with a faith unbroken--a character unblemished,



<P>to Marianne.&nbsp; Nothing could do away the knowledge



<P>of what the latter had suffered through his means,



<P>nor remove the guilt of his conduct towards Eliza.



<P>Nothing could replace him, therefore, in her former esteem,



<P>nor injure the interests of Colonel Brandon.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Had Mrs. Dashwood, like her daughter, heard

Willoughby's



<P>story from himself--had she witnessed his distress,



<P>and been under the influence of his countenance and his



<P>manner, it is probable that her compassion would have



<P>been greater.&nbsp; But it was neither in Elinor's power,



<P>nor in her wish, to rouse such feelings in another, by her



<P>retailed explanation, as had at first been called forth



<P>in herself.&nbsp; Reflection had given calmness to her judgment,



<P>and sobered her own opinion of Willoughby's deserts;--



<P>she wished, therefore, to declare only the simple truth,



<P>and lay open such facts as were really due to his character,



<P>without any embellishment of tenderness to lead the



<P>fancy astray.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In the evening, when they were all three together,



<P>Marianne began voluntarily to speak of him again;--



<P>but that it was not without an effort, the restless,



<P>unquiet thoughtfulness in which she had been for some time



<P>previously sitting--her rising colour, as she spoke,--



<P>and her unsteady voice, plainly shewed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I wish to assure you both," said she, "that

I see



<P>every thing--as you can desire me to do."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood would have interrupted her instantly



<P>with soothing tenderness, had not Elinor, who really wished



<P>to hear her sister's unbiased opinion, by an eager sign,



<P>engaged her silence.&nbsp; Marianne slowly continued--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is a great relief to me--what Elinor told



<P>me this morning--I have now heard exactly what I



<P>wished to hear."--For some moments her voice was lost;



<P>but recovering herself, she added, and with greater



<P>calmness than before--"I am now perfectly satisfied,



<P>I wish for no change.&nbsp; I never could have been happy



<P>with him, after knowing, as sooner or later I must



<P>have known, all this.--I should have had no confidence,



<P>no esteem.&nbsp; Nothing could have done it away to my feelings."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I know it--I know it," cried her mother.



<P>"Happy with a man of libertine practices!--With one



<P>who so injured the peace of the dearest of our friends,



<P>and the best of men!--No--my Marianne has not a heart



<P>to be made happy with such a man!--Her conscience, her



<P>sensitive counscience, would have felt all that the



<P>conscience of her husband ought to have felt."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne sighed, and repeated, "I wish for

no change."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You consider the matter," said Elinor, "exactly

as



<P>a good mind and a sound understanding must consider it;



<P>and I dare say you perceive, as well as myself, not only



<P>in this, but in many other circumstances, reason enough



<P>to be convinced that your marriage must have involved you



<P>in many certain troubles and disappointments, in which



<P>you would have been poorly supported by an affection,



<P>on his side, much less certain.&nbsp; Had you married,



<P>you must have been always poor.&nbsp; His expensiveness is



<P>acknowledged even by himself, and his whole conduct declares



<P>that self-denial is a word hardly understood by him.



<P>His demands and your inexperience together, on a small,



<P>very small income, must have brought on distresses which



<P>would not be the LESS grievous to you, from having been



<P>entirely unknown and unthought of before.&nbsp; YOUR sense



<P>of honour and honesty would have led you, I know,



<P>when aware of your situation, to attempt all the economy



<P>that would appear to you possible: and, perhaps, as long



<P>as your frugality retrenched only on your own comfort,



<P>you might have been suffered to practice it, but beyond that--



<P>and how little could the utmost of your single management



<P>do to stop the ruin which had begun before your marriage?--



<P>Beyond THAT, had you endeavoured, however reasonably,



<P>to abridge HIS enjoyments, is it not to be feared, that instead



<P>of prevailing on feelings so selfish to consent to it,



<P>you would have lessened your own influence on his heart,



<P>and made him regret the connection which had involved him



<P>in such difficulties?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne's lips quivered, and she repeated

the word



<P>"Selfish?" in a tone that implied--"do you really think



<P>him selfish?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "The whole of his behaviour," replied Elinor,



<P>"from the beginning to the end of the affair, has been



<P>grounded on selfishness.&nbsp; It was selfishness which first



<P>made him sport with your affections; which afterwards,



<P>when his own were engaged, made him delay the confession



<P>of it, and which finally carried him from Barton.



<P>His own enjoyment, or his own ease, was, in every particular,



<P>his ruling principle."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is very true.&nbsp; MY happiness never

was his object."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "At present," continued Elinor, "he regrets

what he



<P>has done.&nbsp; And why does he regret it?--Because he finds



<P>it has not answered towards himself.&nbsp; It has not made



<P>him happy.&nbsp; His circumstances are now unembarrassed--he



<P>suffers from no evil of that kind; and he thinks only



<P>that he has married a woman of a less amiable temper



<P>than yourself.&nbsp; But does it follow that had he married you,



<P>he would have been happy?--The inconveniences would have



<P>been different.&nbsp; He would then have suffered under the



<P>pecuniary distresses which, because they are removed,



<P>he now reckons as nothing.&nbsp; He would have had a wife



<P>of whose temper he could make no complaint, but he would



<P>have been always necessitous--always poor; and probably



<P>would soon have learned to rank the innumerable comforts



<P>of a clear estate and good income as of far more importance,



<P>even to domestic happiness, than the mere temper of a wife."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have not a doubt of it," said Marianne;

"and I



<P>have nothing to regret--nothing but my own folly."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Rather say your mother's imprudence, my child,"



<P>said Mrs. Dashwood; "SHE must be answerable."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne would not let her proceed;--and Elinor,



<P>satisfied that each felt their own error, wished to avoid



<P>any survey of the past that might weaken her sister's



<P>spirits; she, therefore, pursuing the first subject,



<P>immediately continued,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "One observation may, I think, be fairly drawn

from



<P>the whole of the story--that all Willoughby's difficulties



<P>have arisen from the first offence against virtue, in his



<P>behaviour to Eliza Williams.&nbsp; That crime has been the origin



<P>of every lesser one, and of all his present discontents."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne assented most feelingly to the remark;



<P>and her mother was led by it to an enumeration of Colonel



<P>Brandon's injuries and merits, warm as friendship



<P>and design could unitedly dictate.&nbsp; Her daughter did



<P>not look, however, as if much of it were heard by her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor, according to her expectation, saw on

the two



<P>or three following days, that Marianne did not continue



<P>to gain strength as she had done; but while her resolution



<P>was unsubdued, and she still tried to appear cheerful



<P>and easy, her sister could safely trust to the effect



<P>of time upon her health.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Margaret returned, and the family were again

all



<P>restored to each other, again quietly settled at the cottage;



<P>and if not pursuing their usual studies with quite



<P>so much vigour as when they first came to Barton,



<P>at least planning a vigorous prosecution of them in future.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor grew impatient for some tidings of Edward.



<P>She had heard nothing of him since her leaving London,



<P>nothing new of his plans, nothing certain even of his



<P>present abode.&nbsp; Some letters had passed between her



<P>and her brother, in consequence of Marianne's illness;



<P>and in the first of John's, there had been this sentence:--



<P>"We know nothing of our unfortunate Edward, and can make no



<P>enquiries on so prohibited a subject, but conclude him



<P>to be still at Oxford;" which was all the intelligence



<P>of Edward afforded her by the correspondence, for his name



<P>was not even mentioned in any of the succeeding letters.



<P>She was not doomed, however, to be long in ignorance of



<P>his measures.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Their man-servant had been sent one morning

to Exeter



<P>on business; and when, as he waited at table, he had



<P>satisfied the inquiries of his mistress as to the event



<P>of his errand, this was his voluntary communication--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I suppose you know, ma'am, that Mr. Ferrars

is married."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne gave a violent start, fixed her eyes



<P>upon Elinor, saw her turning pale, and fell back in her



<P>chair in hysterics.&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood, whose eyes, as she



<P>answered the servant's inquiry, had intuitively taken



<P>the same direction, was shocked to perceive by Elinor's



<P>countenance how much she really suffered, and a moment



<P>afterwards, alike distressed by Marianne's situation,



<P>knew not on which child to bestow her principal attention.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The servant, who saw only that Miss Marianne

was



<P>taken ill, had sense enough to call one of the maids,



<P>who, with Mrs. Dashwood's assistance, supported her into



<P>the other room.&nbsp; By that time, Marianne was rather better,



<P>and her mother leaving her to the care of Margaret



<P>and the maid, returned to Elinor, who, though still



<P>much disordered, had so far recovered the use of her reason



<P>and voice as to be just beginning an inquiry of Thomas,



<P>as to the source of his intelligence.&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood



<P>immediately took all that trouble on herself; and Elinor



<P>had the benefit of the information without the exertion of seeking it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Who told you that Mr. Ferrars was married,

Thomas?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I see Mr. Ferrars myself, ma'am, this morning



<P>in Exeter, and his lady too, Miss Steele as was.&nbsp; They was



<P>stopping in a chaise at the door of the New London Inn,



<P>as I went there with a message from Sally at the Park



<P>to her brother, who is one of the post-boys. I happened



<P>to look up as I went by the chaise, and so I see directly



<P>it was the youngest Miss Steele; so I took off my hat,



<P>and she knew me and called to me, and inquired after you,



<P>ma'am, and the young ladies, especially Miss Marianne,



<P>and bid me I should give her compliments and Mr. Ferrars's,



<P>their best compliments and service, and how sorry they



<P>was they had not time to come on and see you, but they was



<P>in a great hurry to go forwards, for they was going further



<P>down for a little while, but howsever, when they come back,



<P>they'd make sure to come and see you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But did she tell you she was married, Thomas?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes, ma'am. She smiled, and said how she



<P>had changed her name since she was in these parts.



<P>She was always a very affable and free-spoken young lady,



<P>and very civil behaved.&nbsp; So, I made free to wish her joy."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Was Mr. Ferrars in the carriage with her?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes, ma'am, I just see him leaning back in

it,



<P>but he did not look up;--he never was a gentleman much



<P>for talking."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor's heart could easily account for his

not



<P>putting himself forward; and Mrs. Dashwood probably



<P>found the same explanation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Was there no one else in the carriage?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, ma'am, only they two."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Do you know where they came from?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "They come straight from town, as Miss Lucy--



<P>Mrs. Ferrars told me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And are they going farther westward?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes, ma'am--but not to bide long.&nbsp; They

will soon



<P>be back again, and then they'd be sure and call here."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood now looked at her daughter;



<P>but Elinor knew better than to expect them.



<P>She recognised the whole of Lucy in the message, and was



<P>very confident that Edward would never come near them.



<P>She observed in a low voice, to her mother, that they



<P>were probably going down to Mr. Pratt's, near Plymouth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Thomas's intelligence seemed over.&nbsp; Elinor

looked



<P>as if she wished to hear more.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Did you see them off, before you came away?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, ma'am--the horses were just coming out,

but I



<P>could not bide any longer; I was afraid of being late."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Did Mrs. Ferrars look well?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes, ma'am, she said how she was very well;



<P>and to my mind she was always a very handsome young



<P>lady--and she seemed vastly contented."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood could think of no other question,



<P>and Thomas and the tablecloth, now alike needless,



<P>were soon afterwards dismissed.&nbsp; Marianne had already sent



<P>to say, that she should eat nothing more.&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood's



<P>and Elinor's appetites were equally lost, and Margaret



<P>might think herself very well off, that with so much



<P>uneasiness as both her sisters had lately experienced,



<P>so much reason as they had often had to be careless



<P>of their meals, she had never been obliged to go without



<P>her dinner before.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When the dessert and the wine were arranged,



<P>and Mrs. Dashwood and Elinor were left by themselves,



<P>they remained long together in a similarity of thoughtfulness



<P>and silence.&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood feared to hazard any remark,



<P>and ventured not to offer consolation.&nbsp; She now found



<P>that she had erred in relying on Elinor's representation



<P>of herself; and justly concluded that every thing



<P>had been expressly softened at the time, to spare her



<P>from an increase of unhappiness, suffering as she then



<P>had suffered for Marianne.&nbsp; She found that she had been



<P>misled by the careful, the considerate attention of



<P>her daughter, to think the attachment, which once she



<P>had so well understood, much slighter in reality, than



<P>she had been wont to believe, or than it was now proved



<P>to be.&nbsp; She feared that under this persuasion she had



<P>been unjust, inattentive, nay, almost unkind, to her Elinor;--



<P>that Marianne's affliction, because more acknowledged,



<P>more immediately before her, had too much engrossed



<P>her tenderness, and led her away to forget that in Elinor



<P>she might have a daughter suffering almost as much,



<P>certainly with less self-provocation, and greater fortitude.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter43</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 43</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne got up the next morning at her usual

time;



<P>to every inquiry replied that she was better, and tried to



<P>prove herself so, by engaging in her accustomary employments.



<P>But a day spent in sitting shivering over the fire



<P>with a book in her hand, which she was unable to read,



<P>or in lying, weary and languid, on a sofa, did not speak



<P>much in favour of her amendment; and when, at last,



<P>she went early to bed, more and more indisposed, Colonel



<P>Brandon was only astonished at her sister's composure,



<P>who, though attending and nursing her the whole day,



<P>against Marianne inclination, and forcing proper medicines



<P>on her at night, trusted, like Marianne, to the certainty



<P>and efficacy of sleep, and felt no real alarm.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; A very restless and feverish night, however,



<P>disappointed the expectation of both; and when Marianne,



<P>after persisting in rising, confessed herself unable



<P>to sit up, and returned voluntarily to her bed,



<P>Elinor was very ready to adopt Mrs. Jennings's advice,



<P>of sending for the Palmers' apothecary.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He came, examined his patient, and though encouraging



<P>Miss Dashwood to expect that a very few days would restore



<P>her sister to health, yet, by pronouncing her disorder



<P>to have a putrid tendency, and allowing the word "infection"



<P>to pass his lips, gave instant alarm to Mrs. Palmer,



<P>on her baby's account.&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings, who had been inclined



<P>from the first to think Marianne's complaint more serious



<P>than Elinor, now looked very grave on Mr. Harris's report,



<P>and confirming Charlotte's fears and caution, urged the



<P>necessity of her immediate removal with her infant;



<P>and Mr. Palmer, though treating their apprehensions as idle,



<P>found the anxiety and importunity of his wife too great



<P>to be withstood.&nbsp; Her departure, therefore, was fixed on;



<P>and within an hour after Mr. Harris's arrival, she set off,



<P>with her little boy and his nurse, for the house of a



<P>near relation of Mr. Palmer's, who lived a few miles



<P>on the other side of Bath; whither her husband promised,



<P>at her earnest entreaty, to join her in a day or two;



<P>and whither she was almost equally urgent with her



<P>mother to accompany her.&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings, however, with a



<P>kindness of heart which made Elinor really love her,



<P>declared her resolution of not stirring from Cleveland



<P>as long as Marianne remained ill, and of endeavouring,



<P>by her own attentive care, to supply to her the place



<P>of the mother she had taken her from; and Elinor found her



<P>on every occasion a most willing and active helpmate,



<P>desirous to share in all her fatigues, and often by her



<P>better experience in nursing, of material use.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Poor Marianne, languid and low from the nature



<P>of her malady, and feeling herself universally ill,



<P>could no longer hope that tomorrow would find her recovered;



<P>and the idea of what tomorrow would have produced,



<P>but for this unlucky illness, made every ailment severe;



<P>for on that day they were to have begun their journey home;



<P>and, attended the whole way by a servant of Mrs. Jennings,



<P>were to have taken their mother by surprise on the



<P>following forenoon.&nbsp; The little she said was all in



<P>lamentation of this inevitable delay; though Elinor tried



<P>to raise her spirits, and make her believe, as she THEN



<P>really believed herself, that it would be a very short one.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The next day produced little or no alteration

in the



<P>state of the patient; she certainly was not better, and,



<P>except that there was no amendment, did not appear worse.



<P>Their party was now farther reduced; for Mr. Palmer,



<P>though very unwilling to go as well from real humanity



<P>and good-nature, as from a dislike of appearing to be



<P>frightened away by his wife, was persuaded at last



<P>by Colonel Brandon to perform his promise of following her;



<P>and while he was preparing to go, Colonel Brandon himself,



<P>with a much greater exertion, began to talk of going



<P>likewise.--Here, however, the kindness of Mrs. Jennings



<P>interposed most acceptably; for to send the Colonel away



<P>while his love was in so much uneasiness on her sister's



<P>account, would be to deprive them both, she thought,



<P>of every comfort; and therefore telling him at once



<P>that his stay at Cleveland was necessary to herself,



<P>that she should want him to play at piquet of an evening,



<P>while Miss Dashwood was above with her sister, &amp;c. she



<P>urged him so strongly to remain, that he, who was gratifying



<P>the first wish of his own heart by a compliance, could not



<P>long even affect to demur; especially as Mrs. Jennings's



<P>entreaty was warmly seconded by Mr. Palmer, who seemed



<P>to feel a relief to himself, in leaving behind him a person



<P>so well able to assist or advise Miss Dashwood in any emergence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne was, of course, kept in ignorance

of all



<P>these arrangements.&nbsp; She knew not that she had been



<P>the means of sending the owners of Cleveland away,



<P>in about seven days from the time of their arrival.



<P>It gave her no surprise that she saw nothing of Mrs. Palmer;



<P>and as it gave her likewise no concern, she never mentioned



<P>her name.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Two days passed away from the time of Mr. Palmer's

departure,



<P>and her situation continued, with little variation,



<P>the same.&nbsp; Mr. Harris, who attended her every day,



<P>still talked boldly of a speedy recovery, and Miss Dashwood



<P>was equally sanguine; but the expectation of the others



<P>was by no means so cheerful.&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings had determined



<P>very early in the seizure that Marianne would never



<P>get over it, and Colonel Brandon, who was chiefly



<P>of use in listening to Mrs. Jennings's forebodings,



<P>was not in a state of mind to resist their influence.



<P>He tried to reason himself out of fears, which the different



<P>judgment of the apothecary seemed to render absurd;



<P>but the many hours of each day in which he was left



<P>entirely alone, were but too favourable for the admission



<P>of every melancholy idea, and he could not expel from



<P>his mind the persuasion that he should see Marianne no more.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; On the morning of the third day however, the

gloomy



<P>anticipations of both were almost done away; for when



<P>Mr. Harris arrived, he declared his patient materially better.



<P>Her pulse was much stronger, and every symptom more favourable



<P>than on the preceding visit.&nbsp; Elinor, confirmed in every



<P>pleasant hope, was all cheerfulness; rejoicing that



<P>in her letters to her mother, she had pursued her own



<P>judgment rather than her friend's, in making very light



<P>of the indisposition which delayed them at Cleveland;



<P>and almost fixing on the time when Marianne would be able



<P>to travel.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; But the day did not close so auspiciously as

it began.--



<P>Towards the evening Marianne became ill again, growing



<P>more heavy, restless, and uncomfortable than before.



<P>Her sister, however, still sanguine, was willing to



<P>attribute the change to nothing more than the fatigue



<P>of having sat up to have her bed made; and carefully



<P>administering the cordials prescribed, saw her, with



<P>satisfaction, sink at last into a slumber, from which



<P>she expected the most beneficial effects.&nbsp; Her sleep,



<P>though not so quiet as Elinor wished to see it,



<P>lasted a considerable time; and anxious to observe



<P>the result of it herself, she resolved to sit with her



<P>during the whole of it.&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings, knowing nothing



<P>of any change in the patient, went unusually early to bed;



<P>her maid, who was one of the principal nurses, was recreating



<P>herself in the housekeeper's room, and Elinor remained



<P>alone with Marianne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The repose of the latter became more and more

disturbed;



<P>and her sister, who watched, with unremitting attention



<P>her continual change of posture, and heard the frequent



<P>but inarticulate sounds of complaint which passed her lips,



<P>was almost wishing to rouse her from so painful a slumber,



<P>when Marianne, suddenly awakened by some accidental noise



<P>in the house, started hastily up, and, with feverish wildness,



<P>cried out,--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Is mama coming?--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Not yet," cried the other, concealing her

terror,



<P>and assisting Marianne to lie down again, "but she will



<P>be here, I hope, before it is long.&nbsp; It is a great way,



<P>you know, from hence to Barton."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But she must not go round by London," cried

Marianne,



<P>in the same hurried manner.&nbsp; "I shall never see her,



<P>if she goes by London."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor perceived with alarm that she was not



<P>quite herself, and, while attempting to soothe her,



<P>eagerly felt her pulse.&nbsp; It was lower and quicker than ever!



<P>and Marianne, still talking wildly of mama, her alarm



<P>increased so rapidly, as to determine her on sending



<P>instantly for Mr. Harris, and despatching a messenger



<P>to Barton for her mother.&nbsp; To consult with Colonel Brandon



<P>on the best means of effecting the latter, was a thought



<P>which immediately followed the resolution of its performance;



<P>and as soon she had rung up the maid to take her place



<P>by her sister, she hastened down to the drawing-room,



<P>where she knew he was generally to be found at a much



<P>later hour than the present.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; It was no time for hesitation.&nbsp; Her fears

and her



<P>difficulties were immediately before him.&nbsp; Her fears,



<P>he had no courage, no confidence to attempt the removal of:--



<P>he listened to them in silent despondence;--but her



<P>difficulties were instantly obviated, for with a readiness



<P>that seemed to speak the occasion, and the service



<P>pre-arranged in his mind, he offered himself as the



<P>messenger who should fetch Mrs. Dashwood.&nbsp; Elinor made no



<P>resistance that was not easily overcome.&nbsp; She thanked him



<P>with brief, though fervent gratitude, and while he went



<P>to hurry off his servant with a message to Mr. Harris, and



<P>an order for post-horses directly, she wrote a few lines



<P>to her mother.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The comfort of such a friend at that moment

as Colonel



<P>Brandon--or such a companion for her mother,--how gratefully



<P>was it felt!--a companion whose judgment would guide,



<P>whose attendance must relieve, and whose friendship might



<P>soothe her!--as far as the shock of such a summons COULD



<P>be lessened to her, his presence, his manners, his assistance,



<P>would lessen it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; HE, meanwhile, whatever he might feel, acted

with all



<P>the firmness of a collected mind, made every necessary



<P>arrangement with the utmost despatch, and calculated



<P>with exactness the time in which she might look for



<P>his return.&nbsp; Not a moment was lost in delay of any kind.



<P>The horses arrived, even before they were expected,



<P>and Colonel Brandon only pressing her hand with a look



<P>of solemnity, and a few words spoken too low to reach her ear,



<P>hurried into the carriage.&nbsp; It was then about twelve



<P>o'clock, and she returned to her sister's apartment to wait



<P>for the arrival of the apothecary, and to watch by her



<P>the rest of the night.&nbsp; It was a night of almost equal



<P>suffering to both.&nbsp; Hour after hour passed away in sleepless



<P>pain and delirium on Marianne's side, and in the most



<P>cruel anxiety on Elinor's, before Mr. Harris appeared.



<P>Her apprehensions once raised, paid by their excess for all



<P>her former security; and the servant who sat up with her,



<P>for she would not allow Mrs. Jennings to be called,



<P>only tortured her more, by hints of what her mistress



<P>had always thought.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne's ideas were still, at intervals,



<P>fixed incoherently on her mother, and whenever she



<P>mentioned her name, it gave a pang to the heart of



<P>poor Elinor, who, reproaching herself for having trifled



<P>with so many days of illness, and wretched for some



<P>immediate relief, fancied that all relief might soon



<P>be in vain, that every thing had been delayed too long,



<P>and pictured to herself her suffering mother arriving



<P>too late to see this darling child, or to see her rational.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She was on the point of sending again for Mr.

Harris,



<P>or if HE could not come, for some other advice,



<P>when the former--but not till after five o'clock--arrived.



<P>His opinion, however, made some little amends for his delay,



<P>for though acknowledging a very unexpected and unpleasant



<P>alteration in his patient, he would not allow the danger



<P>to be material, and talked of the relief which a fresh



<P>mode of treatment must procure, with a confidence which,



<P>in a lesser degree, was communicated to Elinor.&nbsp; He promised



<P>to call again in the course of three or four hours,



<P>and left both the patient and her anxious attendant more



<P>composed than he had found them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; With strong concern, and with many reproaches

for not



<P>being called to their aid, did Mrs. Jennings hear in the



<P>morning of what had passed.&nbsp; Her former apprehensions,



<P>now with greater reason restored, left her no doubt of



<P>the event; and though trying to speak comfort to Elinor,



<P>her conviction of her sister's danger would not allow her



<P>to offer the comfort of hope.&nbsp; Her heart was really grieved.



<P>The rapid decay, the early death of a girl so young,



<P>so lovely as Marianne, must have struck a less interested



<P>person with concern.&nbsp; On Mrs. Jennings's compassion



<P>she had other claims.&nbsp; She had been for three months



<P>her companion, was still under her care, and she was



<P>known to have been greatly injured, and long unhappy.



<P>The distress of her sister too, particularly a favourite,



<P>was before her;--and as for their mother, when Mrs. Jennings



<P>considered that Marianne might probably be to HER what



<P>Charlotte was to herself, her sympathy in HER sufferings



<P>was very sincere.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mr. Harris was punctual in his second visit;--



<P>but he came to be disappointed in his hopes of what the



<P>last would produce.&nbsp; His medicines had failed;--the fever



<P>was unabated; and Marianne only more quiet--not more



<P>herself--remained in a heavy stupor.&nbsp; Elinor, catching all,



<P>and more than all, his fears in a moment, proposed to call



<P>in further advice.&nbsp; But he judged it unnecessary: he had



<P>still something more to try, some more fresh application,



<P>of whose success he was as confident as the last, and his



<P>visit concluded with encouraging assurances which reached



<P>the ear, but could not enter the heart of Miss Dashwood.



<P>She was calm, except when she thought of her mother;



<P>but she was almost hopeless; and in this state she continued



<P>till noon, scarcely stirring from her sister's bed,



<P>her thoughts wandering from one image of grief,



<P>one suffering friend to another, and her spirits oppressed



<P>to the utmost by the conversation of Mrs. Jennings,



<P>who scrupled not to attribute the severity and danger



<P>of this attack to the many weeks of previous indisposition



<P>which Marianne's disappointment had brought on.



<P>Elinor felt all the reasonableness of the idea, and it



<P>gave fresh misery to her reflections.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; About noon, however, she began--but with a

caution--a



<P>dread of disappointment which for some time kept her silent,



<P>even to her friend--to fancy, to hope she could perceive



<P>a slight amendment in her sister's pulse;--she waited,



<P>watched, and examined it again and again;--and at last,



<P>with an agitation more difficult to bury under exterior calmness,



<P>than all her foregoing distress, ventured to communicate



<P>her hopes.&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings, though forced, on examination,



<P>to acknowledge a temporary revival, tried to keep her



<P>young friend from indulging a thought of its continuance;--



<P>and Elinor, conning over every injunction of distrust,



<P>told herself likewise not to hope.&nbsp; But it was too late.



<P>Hope had already entered; and feeling all its anxious flutter,



<P>she bent over her sister to watch--she hardly knew for what.



<P>Half an hour passed away, and the favourable symptom



<P>yet blessed her.&nbsp; Others even arose to confirm it.



<P>Her breath, her skin, her lips, all flattered Elinor



<P>with signs of amendment; and Marianne fixed her eyes on



<P>her with a rational, though languid, gaze.&nbsp; Anxiety and



<P>hope now oppressed her in equal degrees, and left her no



<P>moment of tranquillity till the arrival of Mr. Harris at



<P>four o'clock;--when his assurances, his felicitations on



<P>a recovery in her sister even surpassing his expectation,



<P>gave her confidence, comfort, and tears of joy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne was in every respect materially better,



<P>and he declared her entirely out of danger.&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings,



<P>perhaps satisfied with the partial justification of her



<P>forebodings which had been found in their late alarm,



<P>allowed herself to trust in his judgment, and admitted,



<P>with unfeigned joy, and soon with unequivocal cheerfulness,



<P>the probability of an entire recovery.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor could not be cheerful.&nbsp; Her joy

was of a



<P>different kind, and led to any thing rather than to gaiety.



<P>Marianne restored to life, health, friends, and to her



<P>doting mother, was an idea to fill her heart with sensations



<P>of exquisite comfort, and expand it in fervent gratitude;--



<P>but it lead to no outward demonstrations of joy, no words,



<P>no smiles.&nbsp; All within Elinor's breast was satisfaction,



<P>silent and strong.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She continued by the side of her sister, with

little



<P>intermission the whole afternoon, calming every fear,



<P>satisfying every inquiry of her enfeebled spirits,



<P>supplying every succour, and watching almost every look and



<P>every breath.&nbsp; The possibility of a relapse would of course,



<P>in some moments, occur to remind her of what anxiety was--



<P>but when she saw, on her frequent and minute examination,



<P>that every symptom of recovery continued, and saw Marianne



<P>at six o'clock sink into a quiet, steady, and to all



<P>appearance comfortable, sleep, she silenced every doubt.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The time was now drawing on, when Colonel Brandon



<P>might be expected back.&nbsp; At ten o'clock, she trusted,



<P>or at least not much later her mother would be relieved



<P>from the dreadful suspense in which she must now be



<P>travelling towards them.&nbsp; The Colonel, too!--perhaps scarcely



<P>less an object of pity!--Oh!--how slow was the progress



<P>of time which yet kept them in ignorance!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; At seven o'clock, leaving Marianne still sweetly

asleep,



<P>she joined Mrs. Jennings in the drawing-room to tea.



<P>Of breakfast she had been kept by her fears, and of



<P>dinner by their sudden reverse, from eating much;--



<P>and the present refreshment, therefore, with such feelings



<P>of content as she brought to it, was particularly welcome.



<P>Mrs. Jennings would have persuaded her, at its conclusion,



<P>to take some rest before her mother's arrival, and allow HER to



<P>take her place by Marianne; but Elinor had no sense of fatigue,



<P>no capability of sleep at that moment about her, and she was



<P>not to be kept away from her sister an unnecessary instant.



<P>Mrs. Jennings therefore attending her up stairs into the



<P>sick chamber, to satisfy herself that all continued right,



<P>left her there again to her charge and her thoughts,



<P>and retired to her own room to write letters and sleep.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The night was cold and stormy.&nbsp; The wind

roared



<P>round the house, and the rain beat against the windows;



<P>but Elinor, all happiness within, regarded it not.



<P>Marianne slept through every blast; and the travellers--



<P>they had a rich reward in store, for every present inconvenience.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The clock struck eight.&nbsp; Had it been ten,



<P>Elinor would have been convinced that at that moment



<P>she heard a carriage driving up to the house;



<P>and so strong was the persuasion that she DID, in spite



<P>of the ALMOST impossibility of their being already come,



<P>that she moved into the adjoining dressing-closet and



<P>opened a window shutter, to be satisfied of the truth.



<P>She instantly saw that her ears had not deceived her.



<P>The flaring lamps of a carriage were immediately in view.



<P>By their uncertain light she thought she could discern it



<P>to be drawn by four horses; and this, while it told the



<P>excess of her poor mother's alarm, gave some explanation



<P>to such unexpected rapidity.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Never in her life had Elinor found it so difficult



<P>to be calm, as at that moment.&nbsp; The knowledge of what her



<P>mother must be feeling as the carriage stopt at the door--



<P>of her doubt--her dread--perhaps her despair!--and



<P>of what SHE had to tell!--with such knowledge it was



<P>impossible to be calm.&nbsp; All that remained to be done



<P>was to be speedy; and, therefore staying only till



<P>she could leave Mrs. Jennings's maid with her sister,



<P>she hurried down stairs.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The bustle in the vestibule, as she passed

along



<P>an inner lobby, assured her that they were already



<P>in the house.&nbsp; She rushed to the drawing-room,--she



<P>entered it,--and saw only Willoughby.



<P>
