<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 42</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; One other short call in Harley Street, in which

Elinor



<P>received her brother's congratulations on their travelling



<P>so far towards Barton without any expense, and on Colonel



<P>Brandon's being to follow them to Cleveland in a day or two,



<P>completed the intercourse of the brother and sisters



<P>in town;--and a faint invitation from Fanny, to come



<P>to Norland whenever it should happen to be in their way,



<P>which of all things was the most unlikely to occur,



<P>with a more warm, though less public, assurance, from John



<P>to Elinor, of the promptitude with which he should come



<P>to see her at Delaford, was all that foretold any meeting



<P>in the country.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; It amused her to observe that all her friends

seemed



<P>determined to send her to Delaford;--a place, in which,



<P>of all others, she would now least chuse to visit, or wish



<P>to reside; for not only was it considered as her future



<P>home by her brother and Mrs. Jennings, but even Lucy,



<P>when they parted, gave her a pressing invitation to visit



<P>her there.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Very early in April, and tolerably early in

the day,



<P>the two parties from Hanover Square and Berkeley Street set



<P>out from their respective homes, to meet, by appointment,



<P>on the road.&nbsp; For the convenience of Charlotte and her child,



<P>they were to be more than two days on their journey,



<P>and Mr. Palmer, travelling more expeditiously with



<P>Colonel Brandon, was to join them at Cleveland soon after



<P>their arrival.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne, few as had been her hours of comfort



<P>in London, and eager as she had long been to quit it,



<P>could not, when it came to the point, bid adieu to



<P>the house in which she had for the last time enjoyed



<P>those hopes, and that confidence, in Willoughby,



<P>which were now extinguished for ever, without great pain.



<P>Nor could she leave the place in which Willoughby remained,



<P>busy in new engagements, and new schemes, in which SHE



<P>could have no share, without shedding many tears.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor's satisfaction, at the moment of removal,



<P>was more positive.&nbsp; She had no such object for her lingering



<P>thoughts to fix on, she left no creature behind, from whom



<P>it would give her a moment's regret to be divided for ever,



<P>she was pleased to be free herself from the persecution



<P>of Lucy's friendship, she was grateful for bringing



<P>her sister away unseen by Willoughby since his marriage,



<P>and she looked forward with hope to what a few months



<P>of tranquility at Barton might do towards restoring



<P>Marianne's peace of mind, and confirming her own.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Their journey was safely performed.&nbsp; The

second



<P>day brought them into the cherished, or the prohibited,



<P>county of Somerset, for as such was it dwelt on by turns



<P>in Marianne's imagination; and in the forenoon of the third



<P>they drove up to Cleveland.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Cleveland was a spacious, modern-built house,



<P>situated on a sloping lawn.&nbsp; It had no park, but the



<P>pleasure-grounds were tolerably extensive; and like



<P>every other place of the same degree of importance,



<P>it had its open shrubbery, and closer wood walk,



<P>a road of smooth gravel winding round a plantation,



<P>led to the front, the lawn was dotted over with timber,



<P>the house itself was under the guardianship of the fir,



<P>the mountain-ash, and the acacia, and a thick screen of



<P>them altogether, interspersed with tall Lombardy poplars,



<P>shut out the offices.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne entered the house with a heart swelling



<P>with emotion from the consciousness of being only eighty



<P>miles from Barton, and not thirty from Combe Magna;



<P>and before she had been five minutes within its walls,



<P>while the others were busily helping Charlotte to show



<P>her child to the housekeeper, she quitted it again,



<P>stealing away through the winding shrubberies, now just



<P>beginning to be in beauty, to gain a distant eminence;



<P>where, from its Grecian temple, her eye, wandering over



<P>a wide tract of country to the south-east, could fondly



<P>rest on the farthest ridge of hills in the horizon,



<P>and fancy that from their summits Combe Magna might



<P>be seen.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In such moments of precious, invaluable misery,



<P>she rejoiced in tears of agony to be at Cleveland;



<P>and as she returned by a different circuit to the house,



<P>feeling all the happy privilege of country liberty,



<P>of wandering from place to place in free and luxurious solitude,



<P>she resolved to spend almost every hour of every day



<P>while she remained with the Palmers, in the indulgence of



<P>such solitary rambles.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She returned just in time to join the others



<P>as they quitted the house, on an excursion through its



<P>more immediate premises; and the rest of the morning was



<P>easily whiled away, in lounging round the kitchen garden,



<P>examining the bloom upon its walls, and listening to the



<P>gardener's lamentations upon blights, in dawdling through



<P>the green-house, where the loss of her favourite plants,



<P>unwarily exposed, and nipped by the lingering frost,



<P>raised the laughter of Charlotte,--and in visiting her



<P>poultry-yard, where, in the disappointed hopes of her



<P>dairy-maid, by hens forsaking their nests, or being



<P>stolen by a fox, or in the rapid decrease of a promising



<P>young brood, she found fresh sources of merriment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The morning was fine and dry, and Marianne,



<P>in her plan of employment abroad, had not calculated



<P>for any change of weather during their stay at Cleveland.



<P>With great surprise therefore, did she find herself prevented



<P>by a settled rain from going out again after dinner.



<P>She had depended on a twilight walk to the Grecian temple,



<P>and perhaps all over the grounds, and an evening merely



<P>cold or damp would not have deterred her from it;



<P>but a heavy and settled rain even SHE could not fancy dry



<P>or pleasant weather for walking.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Their party was small, and the hours passed

quietly away.



<P>Mrs. Palmer had her child, and Mrs. Jennings her carpet-work;



<P>they talked of the friends they had left behind,



<P>arranged Lady Middleton's engagements, and wondered



<P>whether Mr. Palmer and Colonel Brandon would get farther



<P>than Reading that night.&nbsp; Elinor, however little concerned



<P>in it, joined in their discourse; and Marianne, who had



<P>the knack of finding her way in every house to the library,



<P>however it might be avoided by the family in general,



<P>soon procured herself a book.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Nothing was wanting on Mrs. Palmer's side that

constant



<P>and friendly good humour could do, to make them feel



<P>themselves welcome.&nbsp; The openness and heartiness of her



<P>manner more than atoned for that want of recollection



<P>and elegance which made her often deficient in the forms



<P>of politeness; her kindness, recommended by so pretty



<P>a face, was engaging; her folly, though evident



<P>was not disgusting, because it was not conceited;



<P>and Elinor could have forgiven every thing but her laugh.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The two gentlemen arrived the next day to a

very



<P>late dinner, affording a pleasant enlargement of the party,



<P>and a very welcome variety to their conversation, which a



<P>long morning of the same continued rain had reduced very low.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor had seen so little of Mr. Palmer, and

in that



<P>little had seen so much variety in his address to her



<P>sister and herself, that she knew not what to expect



<P>to find him in his own family.&nbsp; She found him, however,



<P>perfectly the gentleman in his behaviour to all his visitors,



<P>and only occasionally rude to his wife and her mother;



<P>she found him very capable of being a pleasant companion,



<P>and only prevented from being so always, by too great



<P>an aptitude to fancy himself as much superior to people



<P>in general, as he must feel himself to be to Mrs. Jennings



<P>and Charlotte.&nbsp; For the rest of his character and habits,



<P>they were marked, as far as Elinor could perceive,



<P>with no traits at all unusual in his sex and time of life.



<P>He was nice in his eating, uncertain in his hours;



<P>fond of his child, though affecting to slight it;



<P>and idled away the mornings at billiards, which ought



<P>to have been devoted to business.&nbsp; She liked him, however,



<P>upon the whole, much better than she had expected, and in



<P>her heart was not sorry that she could like him no more;--



<P>not sorry to be driven by the observation of his Epicurism,



<P>his selfishness, and his conceit, to rest with complacency



<P>on the remembrance of Edward's generous temper, simple taste,



<P>and diffident feelings.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Of Edward, or at least of some of his concerns,



<P>she now received intelligence from Colonel Brandon,



<P>who had been into Dorsetshire lately; and who,



<P>treating her at once as the disinterested friend



<P>of Mr. Ferrars, and the kind of confidant of himself,



<P>talked to her a great deal of the parsonage at Delaford,



<P>described its deficiencies, and told her what he meant



<P>to do himself towards removing them.--His behaviour



<P>to her in this, as well as in every other particular,



<P>his open pleasure in meeting her after an absence



<P>of only ten days, his readiness to converse with her,



<P>and his deference for her opinion, might very well



<P>justify Mrs. Jennings's persuasion of his attachment,



<P>and would have been enough, perhaps, had not Elinor still,



<P>as from the first, believed Marianne his real favourite,



<P>to make her suspect it herself.&nbsp; But as it was,



<P>such a notion had scarcely ever entered her head,



<P>except by Mrs. Jennings's suggestion; and she could



<P>not help believing herself the nicest observer of the



<P>two;--she watched his eyes, while Mrs. Jennings thought



<P>only of his behaviour;--and while his looks of anxious



<P>solicitude on Marianne's feeling, in her head and throat,



<P>the beginning of a heavy cold, because unexpressed by words,



<P>entirely escaped the latter lady's observation;--SHE could



<P>discover in them the quick feelings, and needless alarm



<P>of a lover.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Two delighful twilight walks on the third and

fourth



<P>evenings of her being there, not merely on the dry gravel



<P>of the shrubbery, but all over the grounds, and especially



<P>in the most distant parts of them, where there was something



<P>more of wildness than in the rest, where the trees were



<P>the oldest, and the grass was the longest and wettest,



<P>had--assisted by the still greater imprudence of sitting



<P>in her wet shoes and stockings--given Marianne a cold



<P>so violent as, though for a day or two trifled with



<P>or denied, would force itself by increasing ailments on



<P>the concern of every body, and the notice of herself.



<P>Prescriptions poured in from all quarters, and as usual,



<P>were all declined.&nbsp; Though heavy and feverish, with a pain



<P>in her limbs, and a cough, and a sore throat, a good night's



<P>rest was to cure her entirely; and it was with difficulty



<P>that Elinor prevailed on her, when she went to bed,



<P>to try one or two of the simplest of the remedies.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter40</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 40</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, Miss Dashwood," said Mrs. Jennings,



<P>sagaciously smiling, as soon as the gentleman had withdrawn,



<P>"I do not ask you what the Colonel has been saying to you;



<P>for though, upon my honour, I TRIED to keep out of hearing,



<P>I could not help catching enough to understand his business.



<P>And I assure you I never was better pleased in my life,



<P>and I wish you joy of it with all my heart."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Thank you, ma'am," said Elinor.&nbsp; "It

is a matter



<P>of great joy to me; and I feel the goodness of Colonel



<P>Brandon most sensibly.&nbsp; There are not many men who would



<P>act as he has done.&nbsp; Few people who have so compassionate



<P>a heart!&nbsp; I never was more astonished in my life."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Lord! my dear, you are very modest.&nbsp;

I an't the least



<P>astonished at it in the world, for I have often thought



<P>of late, there was nothing more likely to happen."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You judged from your knowledge of the Colonel's



<P>general benevolence; but at least you could not foresee



<P>that the opportunity would so very soon occur."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Opportunity!" repeated Mrs. Jennings--"Oh!

as to that,



<P>when a man has once made up his mind to such a thing,



<P>somehow or other he will soon find an opportunity.



<P>Well, my dear, I wish you joy of it again and again;



<P>and if ever there was a happy couple in the world, I think



<P>I shall soon know where to look for them."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You mean to go to Delaford after them I suppose,"



<P>said Elinor, with a faint smile.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Aye, my dear, that I do, indeed.&nbsp; And

as to the house



<P>being a bad one, I do not know what the Colonel would be at,



<P>for it is as good a one as ever I saw."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "He spoke of its being out of repair."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, and whose fault is that? why don't he

repair it?--



<P>who should do it but himself?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They were interrupted by the servant's coming

in to



<P>announce the carriage being at the door; and Mrs. Jennings



<P>immediately preparing to go, said,--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, my dear, I must be gone before I have

had half



<P>my talk out.&nbsp; But, however, we may have it all over in



<P>the evening; for we shall be quite alone.&nbsp; I do not ask



<P>you to go with me, for I dare say your mind is too full



<P>of the matter to care for company; and besides, you must



<P>long to tell your sister all about it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne had left the room before the conversation

began.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Certainly, ma'am, I shall tell Marianne of

it;



<P>but I shall not mention it at present to any body else."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh! very well," said Mrs. Jennings rather

disappointed.



<P>"Then you would not have me tell it to Lucy, for I think



<P>of going as far as Holborn to-day."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, ma'am, not even Lucy if you please.



<P>One day's delay will not be very material; and till I



<P>have written to Mr. Ferrars, I think it ought not to be



<P>mentioned to any body else.&nbsp; I shall do THAT directly.



<P>It is of importance that no time should be lost with him,



<P>for he will of course have much to do relative to



<P>his ordination."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This speech at first puzzled Mrs. Jennings

exceedingly.



<P>Why Mr. Ferrars was to have been written to about it



<P>in such a hurry, she could not immediately comprehend.



<P>A few moments' reflection, however, produced a very happy idea,



<P>and she exclaimed;--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, ho!--I understand you.&nbsp; Mr. Ferrars

is to be



<P>the man.&nbsp; Well, so much the better for him.&nbsp; Ay, to be sure,



<P>he must be ordained in readiness; and I am very glad



<P>to find things are so forward between you.&nbsp; But, my dear,



<P>is not this rather out of character?&nbsp; Should not the Colonel



<P>write himself?--sure, he is the proper person."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor did not quite understand the beginning

of



<P>Mrs. Jennings's speech, neither did she think it worth



<P>inquiring into; and therefore only replied to its conclusion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Colonel Brandon is so delicate a man, that

he rather



<P>wished any one to announce his intentions to Mr. Ferrars



<P>than himself."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And so YOU are forced to do it.&nbsp; Well

THAT is an odd



<P>kind of delicacy!&nbsp; However, I will not disturb you (seeing



<P>her preparing to write.)&nbsp; You know your own concerns best.



<P>So goodby, my dear.&nbsp; I have not heard of any thing to



<P>please me so well since Charlotte was brought to bed."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; And away she went; but returning again in a

moment,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have just been thinking of Betty's sister,

my dear.



<P>I should be very glad to get her so good a mistress.



<P>But whether she would do for a lady's maid, I am sure I



<P>can't tell.&nbsp; She is an excellent housemaid, and works



<P>very well at her needle.&nbsp; However, you will think of all



<P>that at your leisure."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Certainly, ma'am," replied Elinor, not hearing



<P>much of what she said, and more anxious to be alone,



<P>than to be mistress of the subject.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; How she should begin--how she should express



<P>herself in her note to Edward, was now all her concern.



<P>The particular circumstances between them made



<P>a difficulty of that which to any other person would



<P>have been the easiest thing in the world; but she



<P>equally feared to say too much or too little, and sat



<P>deliberating over her paper, with the pen in her band,



<P>till broken in on by the entrance of Edward himself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He had met Mrs. Jennings at the door in her

way to



<P>the carriage, as he came to leave his farewell card; and she,



<P>after apologising for not returning herself, had obliged



<P>him to enter, by saying that Miss Dashwood was above,



<P>and wanted to speak with him on very particular business.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor had just been congratulating herself,



<P>in the midst of her perplexity, that however difficult it



<P>might be to express herself properly by letter, it was



<P>at least preferable to giving the information by word



<P>of mouth, when her visitor entered, to force her upon this



<P>greatest exertion of all.&nbsp; Her astonishment and confusion



<P>were very great on his so sudden appearance.&nbsp; She had



<P>not seen him before since his engagement became public,



<P>and therefore not since his knowing her to be acquainted



<P>with it; which, with the consciousness of what she



<P>had been thinking of, and what she had to tell him,



<P>made her feel particularly uncomfortable for some minutes.



<P>He too was much distressed; and they sat down together



<P>in a most promising state of embarrassment.--Whether he



<P>had asked her pardon for his intrusion on first coming



<P>into the room, he could not recollect; but determining



<P>to be on the safe side, he made his apology in form



<P>as soon as he could say any thing, after taking a chair.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Mrs. Jennings told me," said he, "that you

wished



<P>to speak with me, at least I understood her so--or I



<P>certainly should not have intruded on you in such a manner;



<P>though at the same time, I should have been extremely



<P>sorry to leave London without seeing you and your sister;



<P>especially as it will most likely be some time--it



<P>is not probable that I should soon have the pleasure



<P>of meeting you again.&nbsp; I go to Oxford tomorrow."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You would not have gone, however," said Elinor,



<P>recovering herself, and determined to get over what she



<P>so much dreaded as soon as possible, "without receiving



<P>our good wishes, even if we had not been able to give them



<P>in person.&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings was quite right in what she said.



<P>I have something of consequence to inform you of,



<P>which I was on the point of communicating by paper.



<P>I am charged with a most agreeable office (breathing



<P>rather faster than usual as she spoke.)&nbsp; Colonel Brandon,



<P>who was here only ten minutes ago, has desired me to say,



<P>that understanding you mean to take orders, he has



<P>great pleasure in offering you the living of Delaford



<P>now just vacant, and only wishes it were more valuable.



<P>Allow me to congratulate you on having so respectable



<P>and well-judging a friend, and to join in his wish that



<P>the living--it is about two hundred a-year--were much



<P>more considerable, and such as might better enable you



<P>to--as might be more than a temporary accommodation to



<P>yourself--such, in short, as might establish all your views



<P>of happiness."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; What Edward felt, as he could not say it himself,



<P>it cannot be expected that any one else should say for him.



<P>He LOOKED all the astonishment which such unexpected,



<P>such unthought-of information could not fail of exciting;



<P>but he said only these two words,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Colonel Brandon!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes," continued Elinor, gathering more resolution,



<P>as some of the worst was over, "Colonel Brandon means



<P>it as a testimony of his concern for what has lately



<P>passed--for the cruel situation in which the unjustifiable



<P>conduct of your family has placed you--a concern



<P>which I am sure Marianne, myself, and all your friends,



<P>must share; and likewise as a proof of his high esteem



<P>for your general character, and his particular approbation



<P>of your behaviour on the present occasion."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Colonel Brandon give ME a living!--Can it

be possible?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "The unkindness of your own relations has made

you



<P>astonished to find friendship any where."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No," replied be, with sudden consciousness,

"not to



<P>find it in YOU; for I cannot be ignorant that to you,



<P>to your goodness, I owe it all.--I feel it--I would express



<P>it if I could--but, as you well know, I am no orator."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You are very much mistaken.&nbsp; I do assure

you



<P>that you owe it entirely, at least almost entirely,



<P>to your own merit, and Colonel Brandon's discernment



<P>of it.&nbsp; I have had no hand in it.&nbsp; I did not even know,



<P>till I understood his design, that the living was vacant;



<P>nor had it ever occurred to me that he might have



<P>had such a living in his gift.&nbsp; As a friend of mine,



<P>of my family, he may, perhaps--indeed I know he HAS,



<P>still greater pleasure in bestowing it; but, upon my word,



<P>you owe nothing to my solicitation."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Truth obliged her to acknowledge some small

share



<P>in the action, but she was at the same time so unwilling



<P>to appear as the benefactress of Edward, that she acknowledged



<P>it with hesitation; which probably contributed to fix



<P>that suspicion in his mind which had recently entered it.



<P>For a short time he sat deep in thought, after Elinor had ceased



<P>to speak;--at last, and as if it were rather an effort, he said,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Colonel Brandon seems a man of great worth



<P>and respectability.&nbsp; I have always heard him spoken



<P>of as such, and your brother I know esteems him highly.



<P>He is undoubtedly a sensible man, and in his manners



<P>perfectly the gentleman."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Indeed," replied Elinor, "I believe that you

will find him,



<P>on farther acquaintance, all that you have heard him to be,



<P>and as you will be such very near neighbours (for I understand



<P>the parsonage is almost close to the mansion-house,)



<P>it is particularly important that he SHOULD be all this."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Edward made no answer; but when she had turned



<P>away her head, gave her a look so serious, so earnest,



<P>so uncheerful, as seemed to say, that he might hereafter wish



<P>the distance between the parsonage and the mansion-house



<P>much greater.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Colonel Brandon, I think, lodges in St. James

Street,"



<P>said he, soon afterwards, rising from his chair.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor told him the number of the house.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I must hurry away then, to give him those

thanks



<P>which you will not allow me to give YOU; to assure him



<P>that he has made me a very--an exceedingly happy man."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor did not offer to detain him; and they

parted,



<P>with a very earnest assurance on HER side of her unceasing



<P>good wishes for his happiness in every change of situation



<P>that might befall him; on HIS, with rather an attempt to



<P>return the same good will, than the power of expressing it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "When I see him again," said Elinor to herself,



<P>as the door shut him out, "I shall see him the husband



<P>of Lucy."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; And with this pleasing anticipation, she sat

down



<P>to reconsider the past, recall the words and endeavour



<P>to comprehend all the feelings of Edward; and, of course,



<P>to reflect on her own with discontent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When Mrs. Jennings came home, though she returned



<P>from seeing people whom she had never seen before,



<P>and of whom therefore she must have a great deal to say,



<P>her mind was so much more occupied by the important secret



<P>in her possession, than by anything else, that she



<P>reverted to it again as soon as Elinor appeared.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, my dear," she cried, "I sent you up

to the



<P>young man.&nbsp; Did not I do right?--And I suppose you had



<P>no great difficulty--You did not find him very unwilling



<P>to accept your proposal?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, ma'am; THAT was not very likely."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, and how soon will he be ready?--For

it seems



<P>all to depend upon that."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Really," said Elinor, "I know so little of

these kind



<P>of forms, that I can hardly even conjecture as to the time,



<P>or the preparation necessary; but I suppose two or three



<P>months will complete his ordination."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Two or three months!" cried Mrs. Jennings;

"Lord! my dear,



<P>how calmly you talk of it; and can the Colonel wait two



<P>or three months! Lord bless me!--I am sure it would put ME



<P>quite out of patience!--And though one would be very glad



<P>to do a kindness by poor Mr. Ferrars, I do think it is



<P>not worth while to wait two or three months for him.



<P>Sure somebody else might be found that would do as well;



<P>somebody that is in orders already."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "My dear ma'am," said Elinor, "what can you

be thinking of?--



<P>Why, Colonel Brandon's only object is to be of use to Mr. Ferrars."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Lord bless you, my dear!--Sure you do not

mean to persuade



<P>me that the Colonel only marries you for the sake of giving



<P>ten guineas to Mr. Ferrars!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The deception could not continue after this;



<P>and an explanation immediately took place, by which both



<P>gained considerable amusement for the moment, without any



<P>material loss of happiness to either, for Mrs. Jennings



<P>only exchanged one form of delight for another, and still



<P>without forfeiting her expectation of the first.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Aye, aye, the parsonage is but a small one,"

said she,



<P>after the first ebullition of surprise and satisfaction



<P>was over, "and very likely MAY be out of repair; but to hear



<P>a man apologising, as I thought, for a house that to my



<P>knowledge has five sitting rooms on the ground-floor, and I



<P>think the housekeeper told me could make up fifteen beds!--



<P>and to you too, that had been used to live in Barton



<P>cottage!--It seems quite ridiculous.&nbsp; But, my dear, we must



<P>touch up the Colonel to do some thing to the parsonage,



<P>and make it comfortable for them, before Lucy goes to it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But Colonel Brandon does not seem to have

any idea



<P>of the living's being enough to allow them to marry."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "The Colonel is a ninny, my dear; because he

has two



<P>thousand a-year himself, he thinks that nobody else can marry



<P>on less.&nbsp; Take my word for it, that, if I am alive, I shall



<P>be paying a visit at Delaford Parsonage before Michaelmas;



<P>and I am sure I sha'nt go if Lucy an't there."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor was quite of her opinion, as to the

probability



<P>of their not waiting for any thing more.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

   <TITLE>chapter41</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 41</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Edward, having carried his thanks to Colonel

Brandon,



<P>proceeded with his happiness to Lucy; and such was the



<P>excess of it by the time he reached Bartlett's Buildings,



<P>that she was able to assure Mrs. Jennings, who called



<P>on her again the next day with her congratulations,



<P>that she had never seen him in such spirits before in



<P>her life.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Her own happiness, and her own spirits, were

at



<P>least very certain; and she joined Mrs. Jennings most



<P>heartily in her expectation of their being all comfortably



<P>together in Delaford Parsonage before Michaelmas.



<P>So far was she, at the same time, from any backwardness



<P>to give Elinor that credit which Edward WOULD give her,



<P>that she spoke of her friendship for them both with the most



<P>grateful warmth, was ready to own all their obligation



<P>to her, and openly declared that no exertion for their



<P>good on Miss Dashwood's part, either present or future,



<P>would ever surprise her, for she believed her capable of



<P>doing any thing in the world for those she really valued.



<P>As for Colonel Brandon, she was not only ready to worship



<P>him as a saint, but was moreover truly anxious that



<P>he should be treated as one in all worldly concerns;



<P>anxious that his tithes should be raised to the utmost;



<P>and scarcely resolved to avail herself, at Delaford,



<P>as far as she possibly could, of his servants, his carriage,



<P>his cows, and his poultry.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; It was now above a week since John Dashwood

had



<P>called in Berkeley Street, and as since that time no notice



<P>had been taken by them of his wife's indisposition,



<P>beyond one verbal enquiry, Elinor began to feel it



<P>necessary to pay her a visit.--This was an obligation,



<P>however, which not only opposed her own inclination,



<P>but which had not the assistance of any encouragement



<P>from her companions.&nbsp; Marianne, not contented with



<P>absolutely refusing to go herself, was very urgent



<P>to prevent her sister's going at all; and Mrs. Jennings,



<P>though her carriage was always at Elinor's service,



<P>so very much disliked Mrs. John Dashwood, that not even her



<P>curiosity to see how she looked after the late discovery,



<P>nor her strong desire to affront her by taking Edward's part,



<P>could overcome her unwillingness to be in her company again.



<P>The consequence was, that Elinor set out by herself



<P>to pay a visit, for which no one could really have



<P>less inclination, and to run the risk of a tete-a-tete



<P>with a woman, whom neither of the others had so much



<P>reason to dislike.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood was denied; but before the carriage

could



<P>turn from the house, her husband accidentally came out.



<P>He expressed great pleasure in meeting Elinor, told her



<P>that he had been just going to call in Berkeley Street, and,



<P>assuring her that Fanny would be very glad to see her,



<P>invited her to come in.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They walked up stairs in to the drawing-room.--Nobody



<P>was there.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Fanny is in her own room, I suppose," said

he:--"I



<P>will go to her presently, for I am sure she will not



<P>have the least objection in the world to seeing YOU.--



<P>Very far from it, indeed.&nbsp; NOW especially there



<P>cannot be--but however, you and Marianne were always



<P>great favourites.--Why would not Marianne come?"--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor made what excuse she could for her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am not sorry to see you alone," he replied,



<P>"for I have a good deal to say to you.&nbsp; This living



<P>of Colonel Brandon's--can it be true?--has he really given



<P>it to Edward?--I heard it yesterday by chance, and was



<P>coming to you on purpose to enquire farther about it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is perfectly true.--Colonel Brandon has

given



<P>the living of Delaford to Edward."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Really!--Well, this is very astonishing!--no



<P>relationship!--no connection between them!--and now



<P>that livings fetch such a price!--what was the value of this?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "About two hundred a year."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Very well--and for the next presentation to

a living



<P>of that value--supposing the late incumbent to have



<P>been old and sickly, and likely to vacate it soon--he



<P>might have got I dare say--fourteen hundred pounds.



<P>And how came he not to have settled that matter before this



<P>person's death?--NOW indeed it would be too late to sell it,



<P>but a man of Colonel Brandon's sense!--I wonder he should



<P>be so improvident in a point of such common, such natural,



<P>concern!--Well, I am convinced that there is a vast deal



<P>of inconsistency in almost every human character.&nbsp; I suppose,



<P>however--on recollection--that the case may probably be THIS.



<P>Edward is only to hold the living till the person to whom



<P>the Colonel has really sold the presentation, is old enough



<P>to take it.--Aye, aye, that is the fact, depend upon it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor contradicted it, however, very positively;



<P>and by relating that she had herself been employed



<P>in conveying the offer from Colonel Brandon to Edward,



<P>and, therefore, must understand the terms on which it



<P>was given, obliged him to submit to her authority.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is truly astonishing!"--he cried, after

hearing



<P>what she said--"what could be the Colonel's motive?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "A very simple one--to be of use to Mr. Ferrars."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, well; whatever Colonel Brandon may be,



<P>Edward is a very lucky man.--You will not mention the matter



<P>to Fanny, however, for though I have broke it to her,



<P>and she bears it vastly well,--she will not like to hear



<P>it much talked of."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor had some difficulty here to refrain

from observing,



<P>that she thought Fanny might have borne with composure,



<P>an acquisition of wealth to her brother, by which neither



<P>she nor her child could be possibly impoverished.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Mrs. Ferrars," added he, lowering his voice

to the



<P>tone becoming so important a subject, "knows nothing



<P>about it at present, and I believe it will be best to



<P>keep it entirely concealed from her as long as may be.--



<P>When the marriage takes place, I fear she must hear



<P>of it all."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But why should such precaution be used?--Though



<P>it is not to be supposed that Mrs. Ferrars can have



<P>the smallest satisfaction in knowing that her son has



<P>money enough to live upon,--for THAT must be quite



<P>out of the question; yet why, upon her late behaviour,



<P>is she supposed to feel at all?--She has done with her



<P>son, she cast him off for ever, and has made all those



<P>over whom she had any influence, cast him off likewise.



<P>Surely, after doing so, she cannot be imagined liable



<P>to any impression of sorrow or of joy on his account--



<P>she cannot be interested in any thing that befalls him.--



<P>She would not be so weak as to throw away the comfort



<P>of a child, and yet retain the anxiety of a parent!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Ah! Elinor," said John, "your reasoning is

very good,



<P>but it is founded on ignorance of human nature.



<P>When Edward's unhappy match takes place, depend upon it



<P>his mother will feel as much as if she had never discarded him;



<P>and, therefore every circumstance that may accelerate that



<P>dreadful event, must be concealed from her as much as possible.



<P>Mrs. Ferrars can never forget that Edward is her son."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You surprise me; I should think it must nearly



<P>have escaped her memory by THIS time."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You wrong her exceedingly.&nbsp; Mrs. Ferrars

is one



<P>of the most affectionate mothers in the world."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor was silent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "We think NOW,"--said Mr. Dashwood, after a

short pause,



<P>"of ROBERT'S marrying Miss Morton."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor, smiling at the grave and decisive importance



<P>of her brother's tone, calmly replied,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "The lady, I suppose, has no choice in the

affair."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Choice!--how do you mean?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I only mean that I suppose, from your manner



<P>of speaking, it must be the same to Miss Morton whether



<P>she marry Edward or Robert."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Certainly, there can be no difference; for

Robert



<P>will now to all intents and purposes be considered



<P>as the eldest son;--and as to any thing else, they are



<P>both very agreeable young men: I do not know that one



<P>is superior to the other."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor said no more, and John was also for

a short



<P>time silent.--His reflections ended thus.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Of ONE thing, my dear sister," kindly taking

her hand,



<P>and speaking in an awful whisper,--"I may assure you;--



<P>and I WILL do it, because I know it must gratify you.



<P>I have good reason to think--indeed I have it from the



<P>best authority, or I should not repeat it, for otherwise



<P>it would be very wrong to say any thing about it--but



<P>I have it from the very best authority--not that I ever



<P>precisely heard Mrs. Ferrars say it herself--but her



<P>daughter DID, and I have it from her--That in short,



<P>whatever objections there might be against a certain--a



<P>certain connection--you understand me--it would have been



<P>far preferable to her, it would not have given her half



<P>the vexation that THIS does.&nbsp; I was exceedingly pleased



<P>to hear that Mrs. Ferrars considered it in that light--



<P>a very gratifying circumstance you know to us all.



<P>'It would have been beyond comparison,' she said, 'the least



<P>evil of the two, and she would be glad to compound NOW



<P>for nothing worse.' But however, all that is quite out



<P>of the question--not to be thought of or mentioned--



<P>as to any attachment you know--it never could be--all



<P>that is gone by.&nbsp; But I thought I would just tell you



<P>of this, because I knew how much it must please you.



<P>Not that you have any reason to regret, my dear Elinor.&nbsp; There



<P>is no doubt of your doing exceedingly well--quite as well,



<P>or better, perhaps, all things considered.&nbsp; Has Colonel



<P>Brandon been with you lately?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor had heard enough, if not to gratify

her vanity,



<P>and raise her self-importance, to agitate her nerves



<P>and fill her mind;--and she was therefore glad to be



<P>spared from the necessity of saying much in reply herself,



<P>and from the danger of hearing any thing more from



<P>her brother, by the entrance of Mr. Robert Ferrars.



<P>After a few moments' chat, John Dashwood, recollecting that



<P>Fanny was yet uninformed of her sister's being there,



<P>quitted the room in quest of her; and Elinor was left



<P>to improve her acquaintance with Robert, who, by the



<P>gay unconcern, the happy self-complacency of his manner



<P>while enjoying so unfair a division of his mother's love



<P>and liberality, to the prejudice of his banished brother,



<P>earned only by his own dissipated course of life, and that



<P>brother's integrity, was confirming her most unfavourable



<P>opinion of his head and heart.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They had scarcely been two minutes by themselves,



<P>before he began to speak of Edward; for he, too, had heard



<P>of the living, and was very inquisitive on the subject.



<P>Elinor repeated the particulars of it, as she had given them



<P>to John; and their effect on Robert, though very different,



<P>was not less striking than it had been on HIM.&nbsp; He laughed



<P>most immoderately.&nbsp; The idea of Edward's being a clergyman,



<P>and living in a small parsonage-house, diverted him



<P>beyond measure;--and when to that was added the fanciful



<P>imagery of Edward reading prayers in a white surplice,



<P>and publishing the banns of marriage between John Smith and



<P>Mary Brown, he could conceive nothing more ridiculous.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor, while she waited in silence and immovable



<P>gravity, the conclusion of such folly, could not restrain



<P>her eyes from being fixed on him with a look that spoke



<P>all the contempt it excited.&nbsp; It was a look, however,



<P>very well bestowed, for it relieved her own feelings, and gave



<P>no intelligence to him.&nbsp; He was recalled from wit to wisdom,



<P>not by any reproof of her's, but by his own sensibility.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "We may treat it as a joke," said he, at last,



<P>recovering from the affected laugh which had considerably



<P>lengthened out the genuine gaiety of the moment--"but, upon



<P>my soul, it is a most serious business.&nbsp; Poor Edward!



<P>he is ruined for ever.&nbsp; I am extremely sorry for it--



<P>for I know him to be a very good-hearted creature; as



<P>well-meaning a fellow perhaps, as any in the world.



<P>You must not judge of him, Miss Dashwood, from YOUR



<P>slight acquaintance.--Poor Edward!--His manners are certainly



<P>not the happiest in nature.--But we are not all born,



<P>you know, with the same powers,--the same address.--



<P>Poor fellow!--to see him in a circle of strangers!--



<P>to be sure it was pitiable enough!--but upon my soul,



<P>I believe he has as good a heart as any in the kingdom;



<P>and I declare and protest to you I never was so shocked in my



<P>life, as when it all burst forth.&nbsp; I could not believe it.--



<P>My mother was the first person who told me of it;



<P>and I, feeling myself called on to act with resolution,



<P>immediately said to her, 'My dear madam, I do not know



<P>what you may intend to do on the occasion, but as for myself,



<P>I must say, that if Edward does marry this young woman,



<P>I never will see him again.' That was what I said immediately.--



<P>I was most uncommonly shocked, indeed!--Poor Edward!--he has



<P>done for himself completely--shut himself out for ever from



<P>all decent society!--but, as I directly said to my mother,



<P>I am not in the least surprised at it; from his style



<P>of education, it was always to be expected.&nbsp; My poor mother



<P>was half frantic."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Have you ever seen the lady?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes; once, while she was staying in this house,



<P>I happened to drop in for ten minutes; and I saw



<P>quite enough of her.&nbsp; The merest awkward country girl,



<P>without style, or elegance, and almost without beauty.--



<P>I remember her perfectly.&nbsp; Just the kind of girl I



<P>should suppose likely to captivate poor Edward.



<P>I offered immediately, as soon as my mother related



<P>the affair to me, to talk to him myself, and dissuade



<P>him from the match; but it was too late THEN, I found,



<P>to do any thing, for unluckily, I was not in the way



<P>at first, and knew nothing of it till after the breach



<P>had taken place, when it was not for me, you know,



<P>to interfere.&nbsp; But had I been informed of it a few



<P>hours earlier--I think it is most probable--that something



<P>might have been hit on.&nbsp; I certainly should have represented



<P>it to Edward in a very strong light.&nbsp; 'My dear fellow,'



<P>I should have said, 'consider what you are doing.



<P>You are making a most disgraceful connection, and such a one



<P>as your family are unanimous in disapproving.' I cannot



<P>help thinking, in short, that means might have been found.



<P>But now it is all too late.&nbsp; He must be starved, you know;--



<P>that is certain; absolutely starved."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He had just settled this point with great composure,



<P>when the entrance of Mrs. John Dashwood put an end to the subject.



<P>But though SHE never spoke of it out of her own family,



<P>Elinor could see its influence on her mind, in the something



<P>like confusion of countenance with which she entered,



<P>and an attempt at cordiality in her behaviour to herself.



<P>She even proceeded so far as to be concerned to find



<P>that Elinor and her sister were so soon to leave town,



<P>as she had hoped to see more of them;--an exertion



<P>in which her husband, who attended her into the room,



<P>and hung enamoured over her accents, seemed to distinguish



<P>every thing that was most affectionate and graceful.



<P>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 49</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Unaccountable, however, as the circumstances

of his



<P>release might appear to the whole family, it was certain



<P>that Edward was free; and to what purpose that freedom would



<P>be employed was easily pre-determined by all;--for after



<P>experiencing the blessings of ONE imprudent engagement,



<P>contracted without his mother's consent, as he had already



<P>done for more than four years, nothing less could be expected



<P>of him in the failure of THAT, than the immediate contraction



<P>of another.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; His errand at Barton, in fact, was a simple

one.



<P>It was only to ask Elinor to marry him;--and considering



<P>that he was not altogether inexperienced in such a question,



<P>it might be strange that he should feel so uncomfortable



<P>in the present case as he really did, so much in need of



<P>encouragement and fresh air.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; How soon he had walked himself into the proper



<P>resolution, however, how soon an opportunity of exercising



<P>it occurred, in what manner he expressed himself,



<P>and how he was received, need not be particularly told.



<P>This only need be said;--that when they all sat down to



<P>table at four o'clock, about three hours after his arrival,



<P>he had secured his lady, engaged her mother's consent,



<P>and was not only in the rapturous profession of



<P>the lover, but, in the reality of reason and truth,



<P>one of the happiest of men.&nbsp; His situation indeed was



<P>more than commonly joyful.&nbsp; He had more than the ordinary



<P>triumph of accepted love to swell his heart, and raise



<P>his spirits.&nbsp; He was released without any reproach



<P>to himself, from an entanglement which had long formed



<P>his misery, from a woman whom he had long ceased to love;--



<P>and elevated at once to that security with another,



<P>which he must have thought of almost with despair,



<P>as soon as he had learnt to consider it with desire.



<P>He was brought, not from doubt or suspense, but from



<P>misery to happiness;--and the change was openly spoken



<P>in such a genuine, flowing, grateful cheerfulness,



<P>as his friends had never witnessed in him before.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; His heart was now open to Elinor, all its weaknesses,



<P>all its errors confessed, and his first boyish attachment



<P>to Lucy treated with all the philosophic dignity of twenty-four.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It was a foolish, idle inclination on my side,"



<P>said he, "the consequence of ignorance of the world--



<P>and want of employment.&nbsp; Had my brother given me



<P>some active profession when I was removed at eighteen



<P>from the care of Mr. Pratt, I think--nay, I am sure,



<P>it would never have happened; for though I left Longstaple



<P>with what I thought, at the time, a most unconquerable



<P>preference for his niece, yet had I then had any pursuit,



<P>any object to engage my time and keep me at a distance



<P>from her for a few months, I should very soon have



<P>outgrown the fancied attachment, especially by mixing



<P>more with the world, as in such case I must have done.



<P>But instead of having any thing to do, instead of having any



<P>profession chosen for me, or being allowed to chuse any myself,



<P>I returned home to be completely idle; and for the first



<P>twelvemonth afterwards I had not even the nominal employment,



<P>which belonging to the university would have given me;



<P>for I was not entered at Oxford till I was nineteen.



<P>I had therefore nothing in the world to do, but to fancy



<P>myself in love; and as my mother did not make my home



<P>in every respect comfortable, as I had no friend,



<P>no companion in my brother, and disliked new acquaintance,



<P>it was not unnatural for me to be very often at Longstaple,



<P>where I always felt myself at home, and was always sure



<P>of a welcome; and accordingly I spent the greatest part



<P>of my time there from eighteen to nineteen: Lucy appeared



<P>everything that was amiable and obliging.&nbsp; She was pretty



<P>too--at least I thought so THEN; and I had seen so little



<P>of other women, that I could make no comparisons, and see



<P>no defects.&nbsp; Considering everything, therefore, I hope,



<P>foolish as our engagement was, foolish as it has since



<P>in every way been proved, it was not at the time an unnatural



<P>or an inexcusable piece of folly."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The change which a few hours had wrought in

the minds



<P>and the happiness of the Dashwoods, was such--so great--as



<P>promised them all, the satisfaction of a sleepless night.



<P>Mrs. Dashwood, too happy to be comfortable, knew not how



<P>to love Edward, nor praise Elinor enough, how to be enough



<P>thankful for his release without wounding his delicacy,



<P>nor how at once to give them leisure for unrestrained



<P>conversation together, and yet enjoy, as she wished,



<P>the sight and society of both.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne could speak HER happiness only by

tears.



<P>Comparisons would occur--regrets would arise;--and her joy,



<P>though sincere as her love for her sister, was of a kind to



<P>give her neither spirits nor language.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; But Elinor--how are HER feelings to be described?--From



<P>the moment of learning that Lucy was married to another,



<P>that Edward was free, to the moment of his justifying



<P>the hopes which had so instantly followed, she was every



<P>thing by turns but tranquil.&nbsp; But when the second moment



<P>had passed, when she found every doubt, every solicitude



<P>removed, compared her situation with what so lately it



<P>had been,--saw him honourably released from his former



<P>engagement, saw him instantly profiting by the release,



<P>to address herself and declare an affection as tender,



<P>as constant as she had ever supposed it to be,--she



<P>was oppressed, she was overcome by her own felicity;--



<P>and happily disposed as is the human mind to be easily



<P>familiarized with any change for the better, it required



<P>several hours to give sedateness to her spirits, or any



<P>degree of tranquillity to her heart.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Edward was now fixed at the cottage at least

for



<P>a week;--for whatever other claims might be made on him,



<P>it was impossible that less than a week should be given



<P>up to the enjoyment of Elinor's company, or suffice



<P>to say half that was to be said of the past, the present,



<P>and the future;--for though a very few hours spent in



<P>the hard labor of incessant talking will despatch more



<P>subjects than can really be in common between any two



<P>rational creatures, yet with lovers it is different.



<P>Between THEM no subject is finished, no communication



<P>is even made, till it has been made at least twenty



<P>times over.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lucy's marriage, the unceasing and reasonable

wonder



<P>among them all, formed of course one of the earliest



<P>discussions of the lovers;--and Elinor's particular knowledge



<P>of each party made it appear to her in every view, as one



<P>of the most extraordinary and unaccountable circumstances



<P>she had ever heard.&nbsp; How they could be thrown together,



<P>and by what attraction Robert could be drawn on to marry



<P>a girl, of whose beauty she had herself heard him speak



<P>without any admiration,--a girl too already engaged



<P>to his brother, and on whose account that brother had been



<P>thrown off by his family--it was beyond her comprehension



<P>to make out.&nbsp; To her own heart it was a delightful affair,



<P>to her imagination it was even a ridiculous one, but



<P>to her reason, her judgment, it was completely a puzzle.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Edward could only attempt an explanation by

supposing,



<P>that, perhaps, at first accidentally meeting, the vanity



<P>of the one had been so worked on by the flattery



<P>of the other, as to lead by degrees to all the rest.



<P>Elinor remembered what Robert had told her in Harley Street,



<P>of his opinion of what his own mediation in his brother's



<P>affairs might have done, if applied to in time.



<P>She repeated it to Edward.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "THAT was exactly like Robert,"--was his immediate



<P>observation.--"And THAT," he presently added, "might



<P>perhaps be in HIS head when the acquaintance between



<P>them first began.&nbsp; And Lucy perhaps at first might



<P>think only of procuring his good offices in my favour.



<P>Other designs might afterward arise."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; How long it had been carrying on between them,



<P>however, he was equally at a loss with herself to make out;



<P>for at Oxford, where he had remained for choice ever since



<P>his quitting London, he had had no means of hearing of her



<P>but from herself, and her letters to the very last were



<P>neither less frequent, nor less affectionate than usual.



<P>Not the smallest suspicion, therefore, had ever occurred



<P>to prepare him for what followed;--and when at last it



<P>burst on him in a letter from Lucy herself, he had been



<P>for some time, he believed, half stupified between



<P>the wonder, the horror, and the joy of such a deliverance.



<P>He put the letter into Elinor's hands.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "DEAR SIR,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Being very sure

I have long lost your affections,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; I have thought myself at liberty to bestow

my own



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; on another, and have no doubt of being as happy

with



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; him as I once used to think I might be with

you;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; but I scorn to accept a hand while the heart

was



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; another's.&nbsp; Sincerely wish you happy in

your choice,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; and it shall not be my fault if we are not

always



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; good friends, as our near relationship now

makes



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; proper.&nbsp; I can safely say I owe you no

ill-will,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; and am sure you will be too generous to do

us any



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; ill offices.&nbsp; Your brother has gained

my affections



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; entirely, and as we could not live without

one



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; another, we are just returned from the altar,

and



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; are now on our way to Dawlish for a few weeks,

which



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; place your dear brother has great curiosity

to see,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; but thought I would first trouble you with

these



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; few lines, and shall always remain,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Your sincere

well-wisher, friend, and sister,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

"LUCY FERRARS.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have burnt

all your letters, and will return



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; your picture the first opportunity.&nbsp; Please

to destroy



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; my scrawls--but the ring with my hair you are

very



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; welcome to keep."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor read and returned it without any comment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I will not ask your opinion of it as a composition,"



<P>said Edward.--"For worlds would not I have had a letter



<P>of hers seen by YOU in former days.--In a sister it



<P>is bad enough, but in a wife!--how I have blushed over



<P>the pages of her writing!--and I believe I may say that



<P>since the first half year of our foolish--business--this



<P>is the only letter I ever received from her, of which



<P>the substance made me any amends for the defect of the style."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "However it may have come about," said Elinor,



<P>after a pause,--"they are certainly married.&nbsp; And your mother



<P>has brought on herself a most appropriate punishment.



<P>The independence she settled on Robert, through resentment



<P>against you, has put it in his power to make his own choice;



<P>and she has actually been bribing one son with a thousand



<P>a-year, to do the very deed which she disinherited the



<P>other for intending to do.&nbsp; She will hardly be less hurt,



<P>I suppose, by Robert's marrying Lucy, than she would have



<P>been by your marrying her."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "She will be more hurt by it, for Robert always



<P>was her favourite.--She will be more hurt by it,



<P>and on the same principle will forgive him much sooner."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In what state the affair stood at present between

them,



<P>Edward knew not, for no communication with any of his family



<P>had yet been attempted by him.&nbsp; He had quitted Oxford



<P>within four and twenty hours after Lucy's letter arrived,



<P>and with only one object before him, the nearest road



<P>to Barton, had had no leisure to form any scheme of conduct,



<P>with which that road did not hold the most intimate connection.



<P>He could do nothing till he were assured of his fate with



<P>Miss Dashwood; and by his rapidity in seeking THAT fate,



<P>it is to be supposed, in spite of the jealousy with



<P>which he had once thought of Colonel Brandon, in spite



<P>of the modesty with which he rated his own deserts,



<P>and the politeness with which he talked of his doubts,



<P>he did not, upon the whole, expect a very cruel reception.



<P>It was his business, however, to say that he DID, and he



<P>said it very prettily.&nbsp; What he might say on the subject



<P>a twelvemonth after, must be referred to the imagination



<P>of husbands and wives.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; That Lucy had certainly meant to deceive, to

go off



<P>with a flourish of malice against him in her message



<P>by Thomas, was perfectly clear to Elinor; and Edward himself,



<P>now thoroughly enlightened on her character, had no



<P>scruple in believing her capable of the utmost meanness



<P>of wanton ill-nature. Though his eyes had been long opened,



<P>even before his acquaintance with Elinor began, to her



<P>ignorance and a want of liberality in some of her opinions--



<P>they had been equally imputed, by him, to her want



<P>of education; and till her last letter reached him,



<P>he had always believed her to be a well-disposed,



<P>good-hearted girl, and thoroughly attached to himself.



<P>Nothing but such a persuasion could have prevented



<P>his putting an end to an engagement, which, long before



<P>the discovery of it laid him open to his mother's anger,



<P>had been a continual source of disquiet and regret to him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I thought it my duty," said he, "independent

of my feelings,



<P>to give her the option of continuing the engagement or not,



<P>when I was renounced by my mother, and stood to all



<P>appearance without a friend in the world to assist me.



<P>In such a situation as that, where there seemed nothing



<P>to tempt the avarice or the vanity of any living creature,



<P>how could I suppose, when she so earnestly, so warmly insisted



<P>on sharing my fate, whatever it might be, that any thing



<P>but the most disinterested affection was her inducement?



<P>And even now, I cannot comprehend on what motive she acted,



<P>or what fancied advantage it could be to her, to be



<P>fettered to a man for whom she had not the smallest regard,



<P>and who had only two thousand pounds in the world.



<P>She could not foresee that Colonel Brandon would give me a living."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No; but she might suppose that something would

occur



<P>in your favour; that your own family might in time relent.



<P>And at any rate, she lost nothing by continuing the engagement,



<P>for she has proved that it fettered neither her inclination



<P>nor her actions.&nbsp; The connection was certainly a



<P>respectable one, and probably gained her consideration among



<P>her friends; and, if nothing more advantageous occurred,



<P>it would be better for her to marry YOU than be single."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Edward was, of course, immediately convinced

that



<P>nothing could have been more natural than Lucy's conduct,



<P>nor more self-evident than the motive of it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor scolded him, harshly as ladies always

scold



<P>the imprudence which compliments themselves, for having



<P>spent so much time with them at Norland, when he must



<P>have felt his own inconstancy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Your behaviour was certainly very wrong,"

said she;



<P>"because--to say nothing of my own conviction, our relations



<P>were all led away by it to fancy and expect WHAT, as you



<P>were THEN situated, could never be."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He could only plead an ignorance of his own

heart,



<P>and a mistaken confidence in the force of his engagement.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I was simple enough to think, that because

my FAITH



<P>was plighted to another, there could be no danger in my being



<P>with you; and that the consciousness of my engagement was



<P>to keep my heart as safe and sacred as my honour.&nbsp; I felt



<P>that I admired you, but I told myself it was only friendship;



<P>and till I began to make comparisons between yourself



<P>and Lucy, I did not know how far I was got.&nbsp; After that,



<P>I suppose, I WAS wrong in remaining so much in Sussex,



<P>and the arguments with which I reconciled myself to the



<P>expediency of it, were no better than these:--The danger



<P>is my own; I am doing no injury to anybody but myself."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor smiled, and shook her head.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Edward heard with pleasure of Colonel Brandon's



<P>being expected at the Cottage, as he really wished



<P>not only to be better acquainted with him, but to have an



<P>opportunity of convincing him that he no longer resented



<P>his giving him the living of Delaford--"Which, at present,"



<P>said he, "after thanks so ungraciously delivered as mine



<P>were on the occasion, he must think I have never forgiven



<P>him for offering."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; NOW he felt astonished himself that he had

never yet



<P>been to the place.&nbsp; But so little interest had be taken



<P>in the matter, that he owed all his knowledge of the house,



<P>garden, and glebe, extent of the parish, condition of



<P>the land, and rate of the tithes, to Elinor herself,



<P>who had heard so much of it from Colonel Brandon, and heard



<P>it with so much attention, as to be entirely mistress of the subject.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; One question after this only remained undecided,



<P>between them, one difficulty only was to be overcome.



<P>They were brought together by mutual affection,



<P>with the warmest approbation of their real friends;



<P>their intimate knowledge of each other seemed to make



<P>their happiness certain--and they only wanted something



<P>to live upon.&nbsp; Edward had two thousand pounds, and Elinor



<P>one, which, with Delaford living, was all that they could



<P>call their own; for it was impossible that Mrs. Dashwood



<P>should advance anything; and they were neither of them



<P>quite enough in love to think that three hundred and fifty



<P>pounds a-year would supply them with the comforts of life.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Edward was not entirely without hopes of some



<P>favourable change in his mother towards him; and on THAT



<P>he rested for the residue of their income.&nbsp; But Elinor



<P>had no such dependence; for since Edward would still



<P>be unable to marry Miss Morton, and his chusing herself



<P>had been spoken of in Mrs. Ferrars's flattering language



<P>as only a lesser evil than his chusing Lucy Steele,



<P>she feared that Robert's offence would serve no other



<P>purpose than to enrich Fanny.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; About four days after Edward's arrival Colonel



<P>Brandon appeared, to complete Mrs. Dashwood's satisfaction,



<P>and to give her the dignity of having, for the first time



<P>since her living at Barton, more company with her than



<P>her house would hold.&nbsp; Edward was allowed to retain the



<P>privilege of first comer, and Colonel Brandon therefore



<P>walked every night to his old quarters at the Park;



<P>from whence he usually returned in the morning, early enough



<P>to interrupt the lovers' first tete-a-tete before breakfast.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; A three weeks' residence at Delaford, where,



<P>in his evening hours at least, he had little to do



<P>but to calculate the disproportion between thirty-six



<P>and seventeen, brought him to Barton in a temper of mind



<P>which needed all the improvement in Marianne's looks,



<P>all the kindness of her welcome, and all the encouragement



<P>of her mother's language, to make it cheerful.



<P>Among such friends, however, and such flattery, he did revive.



<P>No rumour of Lucy's marriage had yet reached him:--he knew



<P>nothing of what had passed; and the first hours of his



<P>visit were consequently spent in hearing and in wondering.



<P>Every thing was explained to him by Mrs. Dashwood,



<P>and he found fresh reason to rejoice in what he had done



<P>for Mr. Ferrars, since eventually it promoted the interest of Elinor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; It would be needless to say, that the gentlemen

advanced



<P>in the good opinion of each other, as they advanced in each



<P>other's acquaintance, for it could not be otherwise.



<P>Their resemblance in good principles and good sense,



<P>in disposition and manner of thinking, would probably



<P>have been sufficient to unite them in friendship,



<P>without any other attraction; but their being in love



<P>with two sisters, and two sisters fond of each other,



<P>made that mutual regard inevitable and immediate,



<P>which might otherwise have waited the effect of time



<P>and judgment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The letters from town, which a few days before

would



<P>have made every nerve in Elinor's body thrill with transport,



<P>now arrived to be read with less emotion that mirth.



<P>Mrs. Jennings wrote to tell the wonderful tale, to vent her



<P>honest indignation against the jilting girl, and pour forth



<P>her compassion towards poor Mr. Edward, who, she was sure,



<P>had quite doted upon the worthless hussy, and was now,



<P>by all accounts, almost broken-hearted, at Oxford.--



<P>"I do think," she continued, "nothing was ever carried



<P>on so sly; for it was but two days before Lucy called



<P>and sat a couple of hours with me.&nbsp; Not a soul suspected



<P>anything of the matter, not even Nancy, who, poor soul!



<P>came crying to me the day after, in a great fright



<P>for fear of Mrs. Ferrars, as well as not knowing how to



<P>get to Plymouth; for Lucy it seems borrowed all her



<P>money before she went off to be married, on purpose



<P>we suppose to make a show with, and poor Nancy had not



<P>seven shillings in the world;--so I was very glad to give



<P>her five guineas to take her down to Exeter, where she



<P>thinks of staying three or four weeks with Mrs. Burgess,



<P>in hopes, as I tell her, to fall in with the Doctor again.



<P>And I must say that Lucy's crossness not to take them



<P>along with them in the chaise is worse than all.



<P>Poor Mr. Edward! I cannot get him out of my head, but you



<P>must send for him to Barton, and Miss Marianne must try to



<P>comfort him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mr. Dashwood's strains were more solemn.



<P>Mrs. Ferrars was the most unfortunate of women--poor



<P>Fanny had suffered agonies of sensibility--and he



<P>considered the existence of each, under such a blow,



<P>with grateful wonder.&nbsp; Robert's offence was unpardonable,



<P>but Lucy's was infinitely worse.&nbsp; Neither of them were



<P>ever again to be mentioned to Mrs. Ferrars; and even,



<P>if she might hereafter be induced to forgive her son,



<P>his wife should never be acknowledged as her daughter,



<P>nor be permitted to appear in her presence.&nbsp; The secrecy



<P>with which everything had been carried on between them,



<P>was rationally treated as enormously heightening



<P>the crime, because, had any suspicion of it occurred



<P>to the others, proper measures would have been taken



<P>to prevent the marriage; and he called on Elinor to join



<P>with him in regretting that Lucy's engagement with Edward



<P>had not rather been fulfilled, than that she should thus



<P>be the means of spreading misery farther in the family.--



<P>He thus continued:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Mrs. Ferrars has never yet mentioned Edward's

name,



<P>which does not surprise us; but, to our great astonishment,



<P>not a line has been received from him on the occasion.



<P>Perhaps, however, he is kept silent by his fear of offending,



<P>and I shall, therefore, give him a hint, by a line



<P>to Oxford, that his sister and I both think a letter



<P>of proper submission from him, addressed perhaps to Fanny,



<P>and by her shewn to her mother, might not be taken amiss;



<P>for we all know the tenderness of Mrs. Ferrars's heart,



<P>and that she wishes for nothing so much as to be on good terms



<P>with her children."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This paragraph was of some importance to the



<P>prospects and conduct of Edward.&nbsp; It determined him



<P>to attempt a reconciliation, though not exactly



<P>in the manner pointed out by their brother and sister.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "A letter of proper submission!" repeated he;



<P>"would they have me beg my mother's pardon for Robert's



<P>ingratitude to HER, and breach of honour to ME?--I can



<P>make no submission--I am grown neither humble nor



<P>penitent by what has passed.--I am grown very happy;



<P>but that would not interest.--I know of no submission



<P>that IS proper for me to make."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You may certainly ask to be forgiven," said

Elinor,



<P>"because you have offended;--and I should think you



<P>might NOW venture so far as to profess some concern



<P>for having ever formed the engagement which drew on you



<P>your mother's anger."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He agreed that he might.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And when she has forgiven you, perhaps a little

humility



<P>may be convenient while acknowledging a second engagement,



<P>almost as imprudent in HER eyes as the first."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He had nothing to urge against it, but still



<P>resisted the idea of a letter of proper submission;



<P>and therefore, to make it easier to him, as he declared



<P>a much greater willingness to make mean concessions



<P>by word of mouth than on paper, it was resolved that,



<P>instead of writing to Fanny, he should go to London,



<P>and personally intreat her good offices in his favour.--



<P>"And if they really DO interest themselves," said Marianne,



<P>in her new character of candour, "in bringing about



<P>a reconciliation, I shall think that even John and Fanny



<P>are not entirely without merit."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; After a visit on Colonel Brandon's side of

only three



<P>or four days, the two gentlemen quitted Barton together.--



<P>They were to go immediately to Delaford, that Edward



<P>might have some personal knowledge of his future home,



<P>and assist his patron and friend in deciding on what



<P>improvements were needed to it; and from thence,



<P>after staying there a couple of nights, he was to proceed



<P>on his journey to town.



<P>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">
