</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 48</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor now found the difference between the

expectation



<P>of an unpleasant event, however certain the mind may be told



<P>to consider it, and certainty itself.&nbsp; She now found, that



<P>in spite of herself, she had always admitted a hope,



<P>while Edward remained single, that something would occur



<P>to prevent his marrying Lucy; that some resolution of



<P>his own, some mediation of friends, or some more eligible



<P>opportunity of establishment for the lady, would arise



<P>to assist the happiness of all.&nbsp; But he was now married;



<P>and she condemned her heart for the lurking flattery,



<P>which so much heightened the pain of the intelligence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; That he should be married soon, before (as

she imagined)



<P>he could be in orders, and consequently before he could



<P>be in possession of the living, surprised her a little



<P>at first.&nbsp; But she soon saw how likely it was that Lucy,



<P>in her self-provident care, in her haste to secure him,



<P>should overlook every thing but the risk of delay.



<P>They were married, married in town, and now hastening



<P>down to her uncle's.&nbsp; What had Edward felt on being within



<P>four miles from Barton, on seeing her mother's servant,



<P>on hearing Lucy's message!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They would soon, she supposed, be settled at



<P>Delaford.--Delaford,--that place in which so much



<P>conspired to give her an interest; which she wished



<P>to be acquainted with, and yet desired to avoid.



<P>She saw them in an instant in their parsonage-house; saw



<P>in Lucy, the active, contriving manager, uniting at once



<P>a desire of smart appearance with the utmost frugality,



<P>and ashamed to be suspected of half her economical practices;--



<P>pursuing her own interest in every thought, courting the



<P>favour of Colonel Brandon, of Mrs. Jennings, and of every



<P>wealthy friend.&nbsp; In Edward--she knew not what she saw,



<P>nor what she wished to see;--happy or unhappy,--nothing



<P>pleased her; she turned away her head from every sketch of him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor flattered herself that some one of their



<P>connections in London would write to them to announce



<P>the event, and give farther particulars,--but day after



<P>day passed off, and brought no letter, no tidings.



<P>Though uncertain that any one were to blame, she found



<P>fault with every absent friend.&nbsp; They were all thoughtless



<P>or indolent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "When do you write to Colonel Brandon, ma'am?"



<P>was an inquiry which sprung from the impatience



<P>of her mind to have something going on.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I wrote to him, my love, last week, and rather



<P>expect to see, than to hear from him again.&nbsp; I earnestly



<P>pressed his coming to us, and should not be surprised



<P>to see him walk in today or tomorrow, or any day."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This was gaining something, something to look

forward to.



<P>Colonel Brandon must have some information to give.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Scarcely had she so determined it, when the

figure



<P>of a man on horseback drew her eyes to the window.



<P>He stopt at their gate.&nbsp; It was a gentleman, it



<P>was Colonel Brandon himself.&nbsp; Now she could hear more;



<P>and she trembled in expectation of it.&nbsp; But--it was



<P>NOT Colonel Brandon--neither his air--nor his height.



<P>Were it possible, she must say it must be Edward.



<P>She looked again.&nbsp; He had just dismounted;--she could not be



<P>mistaken,--it WAS Edward.&nbsp; She moved away and sat down.



<P>"He comes from Mr. Pratt's purposely to see us.&nbsp; I WILL be



<P>calm; I WILL be mistress of myself."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In a moment she perceived that the others were

likewise



<P>aware of the mistake.&nbsp; She saw her mother and Marianne



<P>change colour; saw them look at herself, and whisper



<P>a few sentences to each other.&nbsp; She would have given



<P>the world to be able to speak--and to make them understand



<P>that she hoped no coolness, no slight, would appear



<P>in their behaviour to him;--but she had no utterance,



<P>and was obliged to leave all to their own discretion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Not a syllable passed aloud.&nbsp; They all

waited



<P>in silence for the appearance of their visitor.



<P>His footsteps were heard along the gravel path; in a moment



<P>he was in the passage, and in another he was before them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; His countenance, as he entered the room, was

not



<P>too happy, even for Elinor.&nbsp; His complexion was white



<P>with agitation, and he looked as if fearful of his



<P>reception, and conscious that he merited no kind one.



<P>Mrs. Dashwood, however, conforming, as she trusted,



<P>to the wishes of that daughter, by whom she then meant



<P>in the warmth of her heart to be guided in every thing,



<P>met with a look of forced complacency, gave him her hand,



<P>and wished him joy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He coloured, and stammered out an unintelligible

reply.



<P>Elinor's lips had moved with her mother's, and, when the



<P>moment of action was over, she wished that she had shaken



<P>hands with him too.&nbsp; But it was then too late, and with a



<P>countenance meaning to be open, she sat down again



<P>and talked of the weather.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne had retreated as much as possible



<P>out of sight, to conceal her distress; and Margaret,



<P>understanding some part, but not the whole of the case,



<P>thought it incumbent on her to be dignified, and therefore



<P>took a seat as far from him as she could, and maintained



<P>a strict silence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When Elinor had ceased to rejoice in the dryness



<P>of the season, a very awful pause took place.&nbsp; It was put



<P>an end to by Mrs. Dashwood, who felt obliged to hope that he



<P>had left Mrs. Ferrars very well.&nbsp; In a hurried manner,



<P>he replied in the affirmative.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Another pause.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor resolving to exert herself, though fearing



<P>the sound of her own voice, now said,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Is Mrs. Ferrars at Longstaple?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "At Longstaple!" he replied, with an air of

surprise.--



<P>"No, my mother is in town."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I meant," said Elinor, taking up some work

from



<P>the table, "to inquire for Mrs. EDWARD Ferrars."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She dared not look up;--but her mother and

Marianne both



<P>turned their eyes on him.&nbsp; He coloured, seemed perplexed,



<P>looked doubtingly, and, after some hesitation, said,--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Perhaps you mean--my brother--you mean Mrs.--Mrs.



<P>ROBERT Ferrars."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Mrs. Robert Ferrars!"--was repeated by Marianne

and her



<P>mother in an accent of the utmost amazement;--and though



<P>Elinor could not speak, even HER eyes were fixed on him



<P>with the same impatient wonder.&nbsp; He rose from his seat,



<P>and walked to the window, apparently from not knowing



<P>what to do; took up a pair of scissors that lay there,



<P>and while spoiling both them and their sheath by cutting



<P>the latter to pieces as he spoke, said, in a hurried voice,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Perhaps you do not know--you may not have

heard



<P>that my brother is lately married to--to the youngest--to



<P>Miss Lucy Steele."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; His words were echoed with unspeakable astonishment



<P>by all but Elinor, who sat with her head leaning over



<P>her work, in a state of such agitation as made her hardly



<P>know where she was.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes," said he, "they were married last week,



<P>and are now at Dawlish."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor could sit it no longer.&nbsp; She almost

ran



<P>out of the room, and as soon as the door was closed,



<P>burst into tears of joy, which at first she thought would



<P>never cease.&nbsp; Edward, who had till then looked any where,



<P>rather than at her, saw her hurry away, and perhaps saw--



<P>or even heard, her emotion; for immediately afterwards



<P>he fell into a reverie, which no remarks, no inquiries,



<P>no affectionate address of Mrs. Dashwood could penetrate,



<P>and at last, without saying a word, quitted the room,



<P>and walked out towards the village--leaving the others



<P>in the greatest astonishment and perplexity on a change



<P>in his situation, so wonderful and so sudden;--a perplexity



<P>which they had no means of lessening but by their



<P>own conjectures.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter13</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 13</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Their intended excursion to Whitwell turned

out



<P>very different from what Elinor had expected.&nbsp; She was



<P>prepared to be wet through, fatigued, and frightened;



<P>but the event was still more unfortunate, for they did



<P>not go at all.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; By ten o'clock the whole party was assembled

at



<P>the park, where they were to breakfast.&nbsp; The morning



<P>was rather favourable, though it had rained all night,



<P>as the clouds were then dispersing across the sky,



<P>and the sun frequently appeared.&nbsp; They were all in high



<P>spirits and good humour, eager to be happy, and determined



<P>to submit to the greatest inconveniences and hardships



<P>rather than be otherwise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; While they were at breakfast the letters were

brought in.



<P>Among the rest there was one for Colonel Brandon;--he



<P>took it, looked at the direction, changed colour,



<P>and immediately left the room.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What is the matter with Brandon?" said Sir

John.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Nobody could tell.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I hope he has had no bad news," said Lady

Middleton.



<P>"It must be something extraordinary that could make Colonel



<P>Brandon leave my breakfast table so suddenly."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In about five minutes he returned.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No bad news, Colonel, I hope;" said Mrs. Jennings,



<P>as soon as he entered the room.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "None at all, ma'am, I thank you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Was it from Avignon?&nbsp; I hope it is not

to say



<P>that your sister is worse."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, ma'am. It came from town, and is merely



<P>a letter of business."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But how came the hand to discompose you so

much,



<P>if it was only a letter of business?&nbsp; Come, come,



<P>this won't do, Colonel; so let us hear the truth of it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "My dear madam," said Lady Middleton, "recollect

what



<P>you are saying."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Perhaps it is to tell you that your cousin

Fanny



<P>is married?" said Mrs. Jennings, without attending



<P>to her daughter's reproof.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, indeed, it is not."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, then, I know who it is from, Colonel.&nbsp;

And I



<P>hope she is well."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Whom do you mean, ma'am?" said he, colouring

a little.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh! you know who I mean."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am particularly sorry, ma'am," said he,



<P>addressing Lady Middleton, "that I should receive this



<P>letter today, for it is on business which requires



<P>my immediate attendance in town."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "In town!" cried Mrs. Jennings.&nbsp; "What

can you



<P>have to do in town at this time of year?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "My own loss is great," be continued, "in being

obliged



<P>to leave so agreeable a party; but I am the more concerned,



<P>as I fear my presence is necessary to gain your admittance



<P>at Whitwell."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; What a blow upon them all was this!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But if you write a note to the housekeeper,

Mr. Brandon,"



<P>said Marianne, eagerly, "will it not be sufficient?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He shook his head.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "We must go," said Sir John.--"It shall not

be put



<P>off when we are so near it.&nbsp; You cannot go to town till



<P>tomorrow, Brandon, that is all."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I wish it could be so easily settled.&nbsp;

But it



<P>is not in my power to delay my journey for one day!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "If you would but let us know what your business

is,"



<P>said Mrs. Jennings, "we might see whether it could be put



<P>off or not."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You would not be six hours later," said Willoughby,



<P>"if you were to defer your journey till our return."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I cannot afford to lose ONE hour."--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor then heard Willoughby say, in a low

voice to Marianne,



<P>"There are some people who cannot bear a party of pleasure.



<P>Brandon is one of them.&nbsp; He was afraid of catching cold



<P>I dare say, and invented this trick for getting out of it.



<P>I would lay fifty guineas the letter was of his own writing."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have no doubt of it," replied Marianne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "There is no persuading you to change your

mind,



<P>Brandon, I know of old," said Sir John, "when once you



<P>are determined on anything.&nbsp; But, however, I hope you



<P>will think better of it.&nbsp; Consider, here are the two Miss



<P>Careys come over from Newton, the three Miss Dashwoods



<P>walked up from the cottage, and Mr. Willoughby got up



<P>two hours before his usual time, on purpose to go to Whitwell."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Colonel Brandon again repeated his sorrow at

being



<P>the cause of disappointing the party; but at the same



<P>time declared it to be unavoidable.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, then, when will you come back again?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I hope we shall see you at Barton," added

her ladyship,



<P>"as soon as you can conveniently leave town; and we must



<P>put off the party to Whitwell till you return."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You are very obliging.&nbsp; But it is so

uncertain,



<P>when I may have it in my power to return, that I dare



<P>not engage for it at all."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh! he must and shall come back," cried Sir

John.



<P>"If he is not here by the end of the week, I shall go



<P>after him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Ay, so do, Sir John," cried Mrs. Jennings,

"and then



<P>perhaps you may find out what his business is."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I do not want to pry into other men's concerns.



<P>I suppose it is something he is ashamed of."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Colonel Brandon's horses were announced.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You do not go to town on horseback, do you?"



<P>added Sir John.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No. Only to Honiton.&nbsp; I shall then go

post."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, as you are resolved to go, I wish you



<P>a good journey.&nbsp; But you had better change your mind."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I assure you it is not in my power."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He then took leave of the whole party.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Is there no chance of my seeing you and your

sisters



<P>in town this winter, Miss Dashwood?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am afraid, none at all."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Then I must bid you farewell for a longer

time



<P>than I should wish to do."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; To Marianne, he merely bowed and said nothing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Come Colonel," said Mrs. Jennings, "before

you go,



<P>do let us know what you are going about."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He wished her a good morning, and, attended

by Sir John,



<P>left the room.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The complaints and lamentations which politeness



<P>had hitherto restrained, now burst forth universally;



<P>and they all agreed again and again how provoking it was



<P>to be so disappointed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I can guess what his business is, however,"



<P>said Mrs. Jennings exultingly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Can you, ma'am?" said almost every body.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes; it is about Miss Williams, I am sure."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And who is Miss Williams?" asked Marianne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What! do not you know who Miss Williams is?

I am



<P>sure you must have heard of her before.&nbsp; She is a relation



<P>of the Colonel's, my dear; a very near relation.&nbsp; We will



<P>not say how near, for fear of shocking the young ladies."



<P>Then, lowering her voice a little, she said to Elinor,



<P>"She is his natural daughter."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Indeed!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, yes; and as like him as she can stare.



<P>I dare say the Colonel will leave her all his fortune."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When Sir John returned, he joined most heartily



<P>in the general regret on so unfortunate an event;



<P>concluding however by observing, that as they were



<P>all got together, they must do something by way of



<P>being happy; and after some consultation it was agreed,



<P>that although happiness could only be enjoyed at Whitwell,



<P>they might procure a tolerable composure of mind by driving



<P>about the country.&nbsp; The carriages were then ordered;



<P>Willoughby's was first, and Marianne never looked



<P>happier than when she got into it.&nbsp; He drove through



<P>the park very fast, and they were soon out of sight;



<P>and nothing more of them was seen till their return,



<P>which did not happen till after the return of all the rest.



<P>They both seemed delighted with their drive; but said



<P>only in general terms that they had kept in the lanes,



<P>while the others went on the downs.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; It was settled that there should be a dance

in the evening,



<P>and that every body should be extremely merry all day long.



<P>Some more of the Careys came to dinner, and they had the



<P>pleasure of sitting down nearly twenty to table, which Sir



<P>John observed with great contentment.&nbsp; Willoughby took



<P>his usual place between the two elder Miss Dashwoods.



<P>Mrs. Jennings sat on Elinor's right hand; and they had not



<P>been long seated, before she leant behind her and Willoughby,



<P>and said to Marianne, loud enough for them both to hear,



<P>"I have found you out in spite of all your tricks.



<P>I know where you spent the morning."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne coloured, and replied very hastily,



<P>"Where, pray?"--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Did not you know," said Willoughby, "that

we had



<P>been out in my curricle?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes, yes, Mr. Impudence, I know that very

well,



<P>and I was determined to find out WHERE you had been to.--



<P>I hope you like your house, Miss Marianne.&nbsp; It is a very



<P>large one, I know; and when I come to see you, I hope you



<P>will have new-furnished it, for it wanted it very much



<P>when I was there six years ago."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne turned away in great confusion.



<P>Mrs. Jennings laughed heartily; and Elinor found that in her



<P>resolution to know where they had been, she had actually



<P>made her own woman enquire of Mr. Willoughby's groom;



<P>and that she had by that method been informed that they



<P>had gone to Allenham, and spent a considerable time there



<P>in walking about the garden and going all over the house.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor could hardly believe this to be true,



<P>as it seemed very unlikely that Willoughby should propose,



<P>or Marianne consent, to enter the house while Mrs. Smith was



<P>in it, with whom Marianne had not the smallest acquaintance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As soon as they left the dining-room, Elinor

enquired



<P>of her about it; and great was her surprise when she



<P>found that every circumstance related by Mrs. Jennings



<P>was perfectly true.&nbsp; Marianne was quite angry with her



<P>for doubting it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Why should you imagine, Elinor, that we did

not



<P>go there, or that we did not see the house?&nbsp; Is not it



<P>what you have often wished to do yourself?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes, Marianne, but I would not go while Mrs.

Smith



<P>was there, and with no other companion than Mr. Willoughby."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Mr. Willoughby however is the only person

who can



<P>have a right to shew that house; and as he went in an open



<P>carriage, it was impossible to have any other companion.



<P>I never spent a pleasanter morning in my life."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am afraid," replied Elinor, "that the pleasantness



<P>of an employment does not always evince its propriety."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "On the contrary, nothing can be a stronger

proof



<P>of it, Elinor; for if there had been any real impropriety



<P>in what I did, I should have been sensible of it at



<P>the time, for we always know when we are acting wrong,



<P>and with such a conviction I could have had no pleasure."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But, my dear Marianne, as it has already exposed

you



<P>to some very impertinent remarks, do you not now begin



<P>to doubt the discretion of your own conduct?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "If the impertinent remarks of Mrs. Jennings

are



<P>to be the proof of impropriety in conduct, we are all



<P>offending every moment of our lives.&nbsp; I value not her



<P>censure any more than I should do her commendation.



<P>I am not sensible of having done anything wrong in walking



<P>over Mrs. Smith's grounds, or in seeing her house.



<P>They will one day be Mr. Willoughby's, and--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "If they were one day to be your own, Marianne,



<P>you would not be justified in what you have done."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She blushed at this hint; but it was even visibly



<P>gratifying to her; and after a ten minutes' interval of



<P>earnest thought, she came to her sister again, and said



<P>with great good humour, "Perhaps, Elinor, it WAS rather



<P>ill-judged in me to go to Allenham; but Mr. Willoughby wanted



<P>particularly to shew me the place; and it is a charming house,



<P>I assure you.--There is one remarkably pretty sitting room



<P>up stairs; of a nice comfortable size for constant use,



<P>and with modern furniture it would be delightful.



<P>It is a corner room, and has windows on two sides.



<P>On one side you look across the bowling-green, behind



<P>the house, to a beautiful hanging wood, and on the other you



<P>have a view of the church and village, and, beyond them,



<P>of those fine bold hills that we have so often admired.



<P>I did not see it to advantage, for nothing could be



<P>more forlorn than the furniture,--but if it were newly



<P>fitted up--a couple of hundred pounds, Willoughby says,



<P>would make it one of the pleasantest summer-rooms



<P>in England."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Could Elinor have listened to her without interruption



<P>from the others, she would have described every room



<P>in the house with equal delight.



<P>

</BODY>

<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 7</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Barton Park was about half a mile from the

cottage.



<P>The ladies had passed near it in their way along the valley,



<P>but it was screened from their view at home by the



<P>projection of a hill.&nbsp; The house was large and handsome;



<P>and the Middletons lived in a style of equal hospitality



<P>and elegance.&nbsp; The former was for Sir John's gratification,



<P>the latter for that of his lady.&nbsp; They were scarcely



<P>ever without some friends staying with them in the house,



<P>and they kept more company of every kind than any other



<P>family in the neighbourhood.&nbsp; It was necessary to the



<P>happiness of both; for however dissimilar in temper



<P>and outward behaviour, they strongly resembled each other



<P>in that total want of talent and taste which confined



<P>their employments, unconnected with such as society produced,



<P>within a very narrow compass.&nbsp; Sir John was a sportsman,



<P>Lady Middleton a mother.&nbsp; He hunted and shot, and she



<P>humoured her children; and these were their only resources.



<P>Lady Middleton had the advantage of being able to spoil her



<P>children all the year round, while Sir John's independent



<P>employments were in existence only half the time.



<P>Continual engagements at home and abroad, however,



<P>supplied all the deficiencies of nature and education;



<P>supported the good spirits of Sir John, and gave exercise



<P>to the good breeding of his wife.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lady Middleton piqued herself upon the elegance



<P>of her table, and of all her domestic arrangements;



<P>and from this kind of vanity was her greatest enjoyment



<P>in any of their parties.&nbsp; But Sir John's satisfaction



<P>in society was much more real; he delighted in collecting



<P>about him more young people than his house would hold,



<P>and the noisier they were the better was he pleased.



<P>He was a blessing to all the juvenile part of the neighbourhood,



<P>for in summer he was for ever forming parties to eat cold



<P>ham and chicken out of doors, and in winter his private



<P>balls were numerous enough for any young lady who was not



<P>suffering under the unsatiable appetite of fifteen.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The arrival of a new family in the country

was always



<P>a matter of joy to him, and in every point of view he was



<P>charmed with the inhabitants he had now procured for his



<P>cottage at Barton.&nbsp; The Miss Dashwoods were young, pretty,



<P>and unaffected.&nbsp; It was enough to secure his good opinion;



<P>for to be unaffected was all that a pretty girl could



<P>want to make her mind as captivating as her person.



<P>The friendliness of his disposition made him happy in



<P>accommodating those, whose situation might be considered,



<P>in comparison with the past, as unfortunate.&nbsp; In showing



<P>kindness to his cousins therefore he had the real satisfaction



<P>of a good heart; and in settling a family of females only



<P>in his cottage, he had all the satisfaction of a sportsman;



<P>for a sportsman, though he esteems only those of his sex who



<P>are sportsmen likewise, is not often desirous of encouraging



<P>their taste by admitting them to a residence within his own manor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood and her daughters were met at

the door



<P>of the house by Sir John, who welcomed them to Barton



<P>Park with unaffected sincerity; and as he attended them



<P>to the drawing room repeated to the young ladies the concern



<P>which the same subject had drawn from him the day before,



<P>at being unable to get any smart young men to meet them.



<P>They would see, he said, only one gentleman there



<P>besides himself; a particular friend who was staying at



<P>the park, but who was neither very young nor very gay.



<P>He hoped they would all excuse the smallness of the party,



<P>and could assure them it should never happen so again.



<P>He had been to several families that morning in hopes



<P>of procuring some addition to their number, but it



<P>was moonlight and every body was full of engagements.



<P>Luckily Lady Middleton's mother had arrived at Barton



<P>within the last hour, and as she was a very cheerful



<P>agreeable woman, he hoped the young ladies would not find



<P>it so very dull as they might imagine.&nbsp; The young ladies,



<P>as well as their mother, were perfectly satisfied with



<P>having two entire strangers of the party, and wished for



<P>no more.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings, Lady Middleton's mother, was

a



<P>good-humoured, merry, fat, elderly woman, who talked a



<P>great deal, seemed very happy, and rather vulgar.&nbsp; She was full



<P>of jokes and laughter, and before dinner was over had said



<P>many witty things on the subject of lovers and husbands;



<P>hoped they had not left their hearts behind them in Sussex,



<P>and pretended to see them blush whether they did or not.



<P>Marianne was vexed at it for her sister's sake, and turned



<P>her eyes towards Elinor to see how she bore these attacks,



<P>with an earnestness which gave Elinor far more pain than



<P>could arise from such common-place raillery as Mrs. Jennings's.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Colonel Brandon, the friend of Sir John, seemed

no



<P>more adapted by resemblance of manner to be his friend,



<P>than Lady Middleton was to be his wife, or Mrs. Jennings



<P>to be Lady Middleton's mother.&nbsp; He was silent and grave.



<P>His appearance however was not unpleasing, in spite



<P>of his being in the opinion of Marianne and Margaret



<P>an absolute old bachelor, for he was on the wrong side



<P>of five and thirty; but though his face was not handsome,



<P>his countenance was sensible, and his address was



<P>particularly gentlemanlike.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; There was nothing in any of the party which

could



<P>recommend them as companions to the Dashwoods; but the cold



<P>insipidity of Lady Middleton was so particularly repulsive,



<P>that in comparison of it the gravity of Colonel Brandon,



<P>and even the boisterous mirth of Sir John and his



<P>mother-in-law was interesting.&nbsp; Lady Middleton seemed



<P>to be roused to enjoyment only by the entrance of her



<P>four noisy children after dinner, who pulled her about,



<P>tore her clothes, and put an end to every kind of discourse



<P>except what related to themselves.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In the evening, as Marianne was discovered

to be musical,



<P>she was invited to play.&nbsp; The instrument was unlocked,



<P>every body prepared to be charmed, and Marianne,



<P>who sang very well, at their request went through the



<P>chief of the songs which Lady Middleton had brought into



<P>the family on her marriage, and which perhaps had lain



<P>ever since in the same position on the pianoforte,



<P>for her ladyship had celebrated that event by giving



<P>up music, although by her mother's account, she had



<P>played extremely well, and by her own was very fond of it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne's performance was highly applauded.



<P>Sir John was loud in his admiration at the end of every song,



<P>and as loud in his conversation with the others while every



<P>song lasted.&nbsp; Lady Middleton frequently called him to order,



<P>wondered how any one's attention could be diverted from music



<P>for a moment, and asked Marianne to sing a particular song



<P>which Marianne had just finished.&nbsp; Colonel Brandon alone,



<P>of all the party, heard her without being in raptures.



<P>He paid her only the compliment of attention; and she felt



<P>a respect for him on the occasion, which the others had



<P>reasonably forfeited by their shameless want of taste.



<P>His pleasure in music, though it amounted not to that



<P>ecstatic delight which alone could sympathize with her own,



<P>was estimable when contrasted against the horrible



<P>insensibility of the others; and she was reasonable enough



<P>to allow that a man of five and thirty might well have



<P>outlived all acuteness of feeling and every exquisite



<P>power of enjoyment.&nbsp; She was perfectly disposed to make



<P>every allowance for the colonel's advanced state of life



<P>which humanity required.



<P>

</BODY>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 6</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The first part of their journey was performed

in too



<P>melancholy a disposition to be otherwise than tedious



<P>and unpleasant.&nbsp; But as they drew towards the end of it,



<P>their interest in the appearance of a country which they



<P>were to inhabit overcame their dejection, and a view of



<P>Barton Valley as they entered it gave them cheerfulness.



<P>It was a pleasant fertile spot, well wooded, and rich



<P>in pasture.&nbsp; After winding along it for more than a mile,



<P>they reached their own house.&nbsp; A small green court was



<P>the whole of its demesne in front; and a neat wicket gate



<P>admitted them into it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As a house, Barton Cottage, though small, was

comfortable



<P>and compact; but as a cottage it was defective, for the



<P>building was regular, the roof was tiled, the window



<P>shutters were not painted green, nor were the walls covered



<P>with honeysuckles.&nbsp; A narrow passage led directly through



<P>the house into the garden behind.&nbsp; On each side of the



<P>entrance was a sitting room, about sixteen feet square;



<P>and beyond them were the offices and the stairs.



<P>Four bed-rooms and two garrets formed the rest of the house.



<P>It had not been built many years and was in good repair.



<P>In comparison of Norland, it was poor and small indeed!--but



<P>the tears which recollection called forth as they entered



<P>the house were soon dried away.&nbsp; They were cheered



<P>by the joy of the servants on their arrival, and each



<P>for the sake of the others resolved to appear happy.



<P>It was very early in September; the season was fine,



<P>and from first seeing the place under the advantage



<P>of good weather, they received an impression in its



<P>favour which was of material service in recommending



<P>it to their lasting approbation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The situation of the house was good.&nbsp;

High hills rose



<P>immediately behind, and at no great distance on each side;



<P>some of which were open downs, the others cultivated and woody.



<P>The village of Barton was chiefly on one of these hills,



<P>and formed a pleasant view from the cottage windows.



<P>The prospect in front was more extensive; it commanded the



<P>whole of the valley, and reached into the country beyond.



<P>The hills which surrounded the cottage terminated



<P>the valley in that direction; under another name,



<P>and in another course, it branched out again between two



<P>of the steepest of them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; With the size and furniture of the house Mrs.

Dashwood



<P>was upon the whole well satisfied; for though her former



<P>style of life rendered many additions to the latter



<P>indispensable, yet to add and improve was a delight to her;



<P>and she had at this time ready money enough to supply all



<P>that was wanted of greater elegance to the apartments.



<P>"As for the house itself, to be sure," said she, "it is



<P>too small for our family, but we will make ourselves



<P>tolerably comfortable for the present, as it is too late



<P>in the year for improvements.&nbsp; Perhaps in the spring,



<P>if I have plenty of money, as I dare say I shall, we may



<P>think about building.&nbsp; These parlors are both too small



<P>for such parties of our friends as I hope to see often



<P>collected here; and I have some thoughts of throwing the



<P>passage into one of them with perhaps a part of the other,



<P>and so leave the remainder of that other for an entrance;



<P>this, with a new drawing room which may be easily added,



<P>and a bed-chamber and garret above, will make it a very snug



<P>little cottage.&nbsp; I could wish the stairs were handsome.



<P>But one must not expect every thing; though I suppose it



<P>would be no difficult matter to widen them.&nbsp; I shall see



<P>how much I am before-hand with the world in the spring,



<P>and we will plan our improvements accordingly."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In the mean time, till all these alterations

could



<P>be made from the savings of an income of five hundred



<P>a-year by a woman who never saved in her life, they were



<P>wise enough to be contented with the house as it was;



<P>and each of them was busy in arranging their particular



<P>concerns, and endeavoring, by placing around them books



<P>and other possessions, to form themselves a home.



<P>Marianne's pianoforte was unpacked and properly disposed of;



<P>and Elinor's drawings were affixed to the walls of their



<P>sitting room.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In such employments as these they were interrupted



<P>soon after breakfast the next day by the entrance of



<P>their landlord, who called to welcome them to Barton,



<P>and to offer them every accommodation from his own house



<P>and garden in which theirs might at present be deficient.



<P>Sir John Middleton was a good looking man about forty.



<P>He had formerly visited at Stanhill, but it was too long



<P>for his young cousins to remember him.&nbsp; His countenance



<P>was thoroughly good-humoured; and his manners were



<P>as friendly as the style of his letter.&nbsp; Their arrival



<P>seemed to afford him real satisfaction, and their comfort



<P>to be an object of real solicitude to him.&nbsp; He said much



<P>of his earnest desire of their living in the most sociable



<P>terms with his family, and pressed them so cordially



<P>to dine at Barton Park every day till they were better



<P>settled at home, that, though his entreaties were carried



<P>to a point of perseverance beyond civility, they could



<P>not give offence.&nbsp; His kindness was not confined to words;



<P>for within an hour after he left them, a large basket



<P>full of garden stuff and fruit arrived from the park,



<P>which was followed before the end of the day by a present



<P>of game.&nbsp; He insisted, moreover, on conveying all their



<P>letters to and from the post for them, and would not be



<P>denied the satisfaction of sending them his newspaper



<P>every day.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lady Middleton had sent a very civil message

by him,



<P>denoting her intention of waiting on Mrs. Dashwood as soon as



<P>she could be assured that her visit would be no inconvenience;



<P>and as this message was answered by an invitation



<P>equally polite, her ladyship was introduced to them the next day.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They were, of course, very anxious to see a

person on



<P>whom so much of their comfort at Barton must depend; and the



<P>elegance of her appearance was favourable to their wishes.



<P>Lady Middleton was not more than six or seven and twenty;



<P>her face was handsome, her figure tall and striking,



<P>and her address graceful.&nbsp; Her manners had all the elegance



<P>which her husband's wanted.&nbsp; But they would have been



<P>improved by some share of his frankness and warmth;



<P>and her visit was long enough to detract something from



<P>their first admiration, by shewing that, though perfectly



<P>well-bred, she was reserved, cold, and had nothing to say



<P>for herself beyond the most common-place inquiry or remark.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Conversation however was not wanted, for Sir

John



<P>was very chatty, and Lady Middleton had taken the wise



<P>precaution of bringing with her their eldest child, a fine



<P>little boy about six years old, by which means there was



<P>one subject always to be recurred to by the ladies in case



<P>of extremity, for they had to enquire his name and age,



<P>admire his beauty, and ask him questions which his mother



<P>answered for him, while he hung about her and held



<P>down his head, to the great surprise of her ladyship,



<P>who wondered at his being so shy before company, as he



<P>could make noise enough at home.&nbsp; On every formal visit



<P>a child ought to be of the party, by way of provision



<P>for discourse.&nbsp; In the present case it took up ten minutes



<P>to determine whether the boy were most like his father



<P>or mother, and in what particular he resembled either,



<P>for of course every body differed, and every body was



<P>astonished at the opinion of the others.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; An opportunity was soon to be given to the

Dashwoods



<P>of debating on the rest of the children, as Sir John



<P>would not leave the house without securing their promise



<P>of dining at the park the next day.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <TITLE>chapter12</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 12</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As Elinor and Marianne were walking together

the



<P>next morning the latter communicated a piece of news



<P>to her sister, which in spite of all that she knew



<P>before of Marianne's imprudence and want of thought,



<P>surprised her by its extravagant testimony of both.



<P>Marianne told her, with the greatest delight, that



<P>Willoughby had given her a horse, one that he had bred



<P>himself on his estate in Somersetshire, and which was



<P>exactly calculated to carry a woman.&nbsp; Without considering



<P>that it was not in her mother's plan to keep any horse,



<P>that if she were to alter her resolution in favour of



<P>this gift, she must buy another for the servant, and



<P>keep a servant to ride it, and after all, build a stable



<P>to receive them, she had accepted the present without



<P>hesitation, and told her sister of it in raptures.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "He intends to send his groom into Somersetshire



<P>immediately for it," she added, "and when it arrives we



<P>will ride every day.&nbsp; You shall share its use with me.



<P>Imagine to yourself, my dear Elinor, the delight of a gallop



<P>on some of these downs."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Most unwilling was she to awaken from such

a dream of



<P>felicity to comprehend all the unhappy truths which attended



<P>the affair; and for some time she refused to submit to them.



<P>As to an additional servant, the expense would be a trifle;



<P>Mamma she was sure would never object to it; and any horse



<P>would do for HIM; he might always get one at the park;



<P>as to a stable, the merest shed would be sufficient.



<P>Elinor then ventured to doubt the propriety of her receiving



<P>such a present from a man so little, or at least so lately



<P>known to her.&nbsp; This was too much.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You are mistaken, Elinor," said she warmly,



<P>"in supposing I know very little of Willoughby.



<P>I have not known him long indeed, but I am much better



<P>acquainted with him, than I am with any other creature



<P>in the world, except yourself and mama.&nbsp; It is not



<P>time or opportunity that is to determine intimacy;--



<P>it is disposition alone.&nbsp; Seven years would be insufficient



<P>to make some people acquainted with each other, and seven



<P>days are more than enough for others.&nbsp; I should hold



<P>myself guilty of greater impropriety in accepting a horse



<P>from my brother, than from Willoughby.&nbsp; Of John I know



<P>very little, though we have lived together for years;



<P>but of Willoughby my judgment has long been formed."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor thought it wisest to touch that point

no more.



<P>She knew her sister's temper.&nbsp; Opposition on so tender a



<P>subject would only attach her the more to her own opinion.



<P>But by an appeal to her affection for her mother,



<P>by representing the inconveniences which that indulgent



<P>mother must draw on herself, if (as would probably be



<P>the case) she consented to this increase of establishment,



<P>Marianne was shortly subdued; and she promised not to



<P>tempt her mother to such imprudent kindness by mentioning



<P>the offer, and to tell Willoughby when she saw him next,



<P>that it must be declined.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She was faithful to her word; and when Willoughby



<P>called at the cottage, the same day, Elinor heard her



<P>express her disappointment to him in a low voice, on



<P>being obliged to forego the acceptance of his present.



<P>The reasons for this alteration were at the same time related,



<P>and they were such as to make further entreaty on his



<P>side impossible.&nbsp; His concern however was very apparent;



<P>and after expressing it with earnestness, he added,



<P>in the same low voice,--"But, Marianne, the horse is



<P>still yours, though you cannot use it now.&nbsp; I shall keep



<P>it only till you can claim it.&nbsp; When you leave Barton



<P>to form your own establishment in a more lasting home,



<P>Queen Mab shall receive you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This was all overheard by Miss Dashwood; and

in the



<P>whole of the sentence, in his manner of pronouncing it,



<P>and in his addressing her sister by her christian name alone,



<P>she instantly saw an intimacy so decided, a meaning



<P>so direct, as marked a perfect agreement between them.



<P>>From that moment she doubted not of their being engaged



<P>to each other; and the belief of it created no other surprise



<P>than that she, or any of their friends, should be left



<P>by tempers so frank, to discover it by accident.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Margaret related something to her the next

day,



<P>which placed this matter in a still clearer light.



<P>Willoughby had spent the preceding evening with them,



<P>and Margaret, by being left some time in the parlour



<P>with only him and Marianne, had had opportunity



<P>for observations, which, with a most important face,



<P>she communicated to her eldest sister, when they were



<P>next by themselves.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, Elinor!" she cried, "I have such a secret

to



<P>tell you about Marianne.&nbsp; I am sure she will be married



<P>to Mr. Willoughby very soon."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You have said so," replied Elinor, "almost

every



<P>day since they first met on High-church Down; and they



<P>had not known each other a week, I believe, before you



<P>were certain that Marianne wore his picture round her neck;



<P>but it turned out to be only the miniature of our great uncle."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But indeed this is quite another thing.&nbsp;

I am sure



<P>they will be married very soon, for he has got a lock



<P>of her hair."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Take care, Margaret.&nbsp; It may be only

the hair



<P>of some great uncle of HIS."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But, indeed, Elinor, it is Marianne's. I am

almost



<P>sure it is, for I saw him cut it off.&nbsp; Last night



<P>after tea, when you and mama went out of the room,



<P>they were whispering and talking together as fast as



<P>could be, and he seemed to be begging something of her,



<P>and presently he took up her scissors and cut off a long



<P>lock of her hair, for it was all tumbled down her back;



<P>and he kissed it, and folded it up in a piece of white paper;



<P>and put it into his pocket-book."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; For such particulars, stated on such authority,



<P>Elinor could not withhold her credit; nor was she disposed



<P>to it, for the circumstance was in perfect unison with



<P>what she had heard and seen herself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Margaret's sagacity was not always displayed

in a



<P>way so satisfactory to her sister.&nbsp; When Mrs. Jennings



<P>attacked her one evening at the park, to give the name



<P>of the young man who was Elinor's particular favourite,



<P>which had been long a matter of great curiosity to her,



<P>Margaret answered by looking at her sister, and saying,



<P>"I must not tell, may I, Elinor?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This of course made every body laugh; and Elinor



<P>tried to laugh too.&nbsp; But the effort was painful.



<P>She was convinced that Margaret had fixed on a person



<P>whose name she could not bear with composure to become



<P>a standing joke with Mrs. Jennings.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne felt for her most sincerely; but she

did



<P>more harm than good to the cause, by turning very red



<P>and saying in an angry manner to Margaret,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Remember that whatever your conjectures may

be,



<P>you have no right to repeat them."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I never had any conjectures about it," replied

Margaret;



<P>"it was you who told me of it yourself."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This increased the mirth of the company, and

Margaret



<P>was eagerly pressed to say something more.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh! pray, Miss Margaret, let us know all about

it,"



<P>said Mrs. Jennings.&nbsp; "What is the gentleman's name?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I must not tell, ma'am. But I know very well

what it is;



<P>and I know where he is too."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes, yes, we can guess where he is; at his

own house



<P>at Norland to be sure.&nbsp; He is the curate of the parish



<P>I dare say."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, THAT he is not.&nbsp; He is of no profession

at all."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Margaret," said Marianne with great warmth,



<P>"you know that all this is an invention of your own,



<P>and that there is no such person in existence."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, then, he is lately dead, Marianne, for

I



<P>am sure there was such a man once, and his name begins



<P>with an F."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Most grateful did Elinor feel to Lady Middleton



<P>for observing, at this moment, "that it rained very hard,"



<P>though she believed the interruption to proceed less from



<P>any attention to her, than from her ladyship's great dislike



<P>of all such inelegant subjects of raillery as delighted



<P>her husband and mother.&nbsp; The idea however started by her,



<P>was immediately pursued by Colonel Brandon, who was



<P>on every occasion mindful of the feelings of others;



<P>and much was said on the subject of rain by both of them.



<P>Willoughby opened the piano-forte, and asked Marianne



<P>to sit down to it; and thus amidst the various endeavours



<P>of different people to quit the topic, it fell to the ground.



<P>But not so easily did Elinor recover from the alarm into



<P>which it had thrown her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; A party was formed this evening for going on

the



<P>following day to see a very fine place about twelve miles



<P>from Barton, belonging to a brother-in-law of Colonel Brandon,



<P>without whose interest it could not be seen, as the proprietor,



<P>who was then abroad, had left strict orders on that head.



<P>The grounds were declared to be highly beautiful,



<P>and Sir John, who was particularly warm in their praise,



<P>might be allowed to be a tolerable judge, for he had



<P>formed parties to visit them, at least, twice every summer



<P>for the last ten years.&nbsp; They contained a noble piece



<P>of water; a sail on which was to a form a great part of



<P>the morning's amusement; cold provisions were to be taken,



<P>open carriages only to be employed, and every thing



<P>conducted in the usual style of a complete party of pleasure.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; To some few of the company it appeared rather



<P>a bold undertaking, considering the time of year,



<P>and that it had rained every day for the last fortnight;--



<P>and Mrs. Dashwood, who had already a cold, was persuaded



<P>by Elinor to stay at home.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter04</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 4</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What a pity it is, Elinor," said Marianne,



<P>"that Edward should have no taste for drawing."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No taste for drawing!" replied Elinor, "why

should



<P>you think so? He does not draw himself, indeed, but he has



<P>great pleasure in seeing the performances of other people,



<P>and I assure you he is by no means deficient in natural taste,



<P>though he has not had opportunities of improving it.



<P>Had he ever been in the way of learning, I think he would



<P>have drawn very well.&nbsp; He distrusts his own judgment



<P>in such matters so much, that he is always unwilling



<P>to give his opinion on any picture; but he has an innate



<P>propriety and simplicity of taste, which in general



<P>direct him perfectly right."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne was afraid of offending, and said

no more



<P>on the subject; but the kind of approbation which Elinor



<P>described as excited in him by the drawings of other



<P>people, was very far from that rapturous delight, which,



<P>in her opinion, could alone be called taste.&nbsp; Yet, though



<P>smiling within herself at the mistake, she honoured



<P>her sister for that blind partiality to Edward which produced it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I hope, Marianne," continued Elinor, "you

do not



<P>consider him as deficient in general taste.&nbsp; Indeed, I think



<P>I may say that you cannot, for your behaviour to him



<P>is perfectly cordial, and if THAT were your opinion,



<P>I am sure you could never be civil to him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne hardly knew what to say.&nbsp; She

would



<P>not wound the feelings of her sister on any account,



<P>and yet to say what she did not believe was impossible.



<P>At length she replied:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Do not be offended, Elinor, if my praise of

him



<P>is not in every thing equal to your sense of his merits.



<P>I have not had so many opportunities of estimating the minuter



<P>propensities of his mind, his inclinations and tastes,



<P>as you have; but I have the highest opinion in the world



<P>of his goodness and sense.&nbsp; I think him every thing that is



<P>worthy and amiable."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am sure," replied Elinor, with a smile,



<P>"that his dearest friends could not be dissatisfied



<P>with such commendation as that.&nbsp; I do not perceive



<P>how you could express yourself more warmly."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne was rejoiced to find her sister so

easily pleased.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Of his sense and his goodness," continued

Elinor,



<P>"no one can, I think, be in doubt, who has seen him



<P>often enough to engage him in unreserved conversation.



<P>The excellence of his understanding and his principles



<P>can be concealed only by that shyness which too often



<P>keeps him silent.&nbsp; You know enough of him to do justice



<P>to his solid worth.&nbsp; But of his minuter propensities,



<P>as you call them you have from peculiar circumstances



<P>been kept more ignorant than myself.&nbsp; He and I have



<P>been at times thrown a good deal together, while you



<P>have been wholly engrossed on the most affectionate



<P>principle by my mother.&nbsp; I have seen a great deal of him,



<P>have studied his sentiments and heard his opinion on



<P>subjects of literature and taste; and, upon the whole,



<P>I venture to pronounce that his mind is well-informed,



<P>enjoyment of books exceedingly great, his imagination lively,



<P>his observation just and correct, and his taste delicate



<P>and pure.&nbsp; His abilities in every respect improve



<P>as much upon acquaintance as his manners and person.



<P>At first sight, his address is certainly not striking;



<P>and his person can hardly be called handsome, till the



<P>expression of his eyes, which are uncommonly good,



<P>and the general sweetness of his countenance, is perceived.



<P>At present, I know him so well, that I think him



<P>really handsome; or at least, almost so.&nbsp; What say you,



<P>Marianne?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I shall very soon think him handsome, Elinor,

if I



<P>do not now.&nbsp; When you tell me to love him as a brother,



<P>I shall no more see imperfection in his face, than I now do



<P>in his heart."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor started at this declaration, and was

sorry for



<P>the warmth she had been betrayed into, in speaking of him.



<P>She felt that Edward stood very high in her opinion.



<P>She believed the regard to be mutual; but she required



<P>greater certainty of it to make Marianne's conviction



<P>of their attachment agreeable to her.&nbsp; She knew that



<P>what Marianne and her mother conjectured one moment,



<P>they believed the next--that with them, to wish was to hope,



<P>and to hope was to expect.&nbsp; She tried to explain the real



<P>state of the case to her sister.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I do not attempt to deny," said she, "that

I think



<P>very highly of him--that I greatly esteem, that I like him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne here burst forth with indignation--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Esteem him! Like him! Cold-hearted Elinor!

Oh!



<P>worse than cold-hearted! Ashamed of being otherwise.



<P>Use those words again, and I will leave the room this moment."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor could not help laughing.&nbsp; "Excuse

me,"



<P>said she; "and be assured that I meant no offence to you,



<P>by speaking, in so quiet a way, of my own feelings.



<P>Believe them to be stronger than I have declared;



<P>believe them, in short, to be such as his merit, and the



<P>suspicion--the hope of his affection for me may warrant,



<P>without imprudence or folly.&nbsp; But farther than this you must



<P>not believe.&nbsp; I am by no means assured of his regard for me.



<P>There are moments when the extent of it seems doubtful;



<P>and till his sentiments are fully known, you cannot wonder



<P>at my wishing to avoid any encouragement of my own partiality,



<P>by believing or calling it more than it is.&nbsp; In my heart



<P>I feel little--scarcely any doubt of his preference.



<P>But there are other points to be considered besides



<P>his inclination.&nbsp; He is very far from being independent.



<P>What his mother really is we cannot know; but, from Fanny's



<P>occasional mention of her conduct and opinions, we have



<P>never been disposed to think her amiable; and I am very



<P>much mistaken if Edward is not himself aware that there



<P>would be many difficulties in his way, if he were to wish



<P>to marry a woman who had not either a great fortune or



<P>high rank."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne was astonished to find how much the

imagination



<P>of her mother and herself had outstripped the truth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And you really are not engaged to him!" said

she.



<P>"Yet it certainly soon will happen.&nbsp; But two advantages



<P>will proceed from this delay.&nbsp; I shall not lose you so soon,



<P>and Edward will have greater opportunity of improving



<P>that natural taste for your favourite pursuit which must



<P>be so indispensably necessary to your future felicity.



<P>Oh! if he should be so far stimulated by your genius as to



<P>learn to draw himself, how delightful it would be!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor had given her real opinion to her sister.



<P>She could not consider her partiality for Edward



<P>in so prosperous a state as Marianne had believed it.



<P>There was, at times, a want of spirits about him which,



<P>if it did not denote indifference, spoke a something almost



<P>as unpromising.&nbsp; A doubt of her regard, supposing him



<P>to feel it, need not give him more than inquietude.



<P>It would not be likely to produce that dejection of mind



<P>which frequently attended him.&nbsp; A more reasonable cause



<P>might be found in the dependent situation which forbad



<P>the indulgence of his affection.&nbsp; She knew that his mother



<P>neither behaved to him so as to make his home comfortable



<P>at present, nor to give him any assurance that he might form



<P>a home for himself, without strictly attending to her views



<P>for his aggrandizement.&nbsp; With such a knowledge as this,



<P>it was impossible for Elinor to feel easy on the subject.



<P>She was far from depending on that result of his preference



<P>of her, which her mother and sister still considered



<P>as certain.&nbsp; Nay, the longer they were together the more



<P>doubtful seemed the nature of his regard; and sometimes,



<P>for a few painful minutes, she believed it to be no more



<P>than friendship.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; But, whatever might really be its limits, it

was enough,



<P>when perceived by his sister, to make her uneasy,



<P>and at the same time, (which was still more common,)



<P>to make her uncivil.&nbsp; She took the first opportunity of



<P>affronting her mother-in-law on the occasion, talking to



<P>her so expressively of her brother's great expectations,



<P>of Mrs. Ferrars's resolution that both her sons should



<P>marry well, and of the danger attending any young woman



<P>who attempted to DRAW HIM IN; that Mrs. Dashwood could



<P>neither pretend to be unconscious, nor endeavor to be calm.



<P>She gave her an answer which marked her contempt,



<P>and instantly left the room, resolving that, whatever might



<P>be the inconvenience or expense of so sudden a removal,



<P>her beloved Elinor should not be exposed another week



<P>to such insinuations.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In this state of her spirits, a letter was

delivered



<P>to her from the post, which contained a proposal



<P>particularly well timed.&nbsp; It was the offer of a small house,



<P>on very easy terms, belonging to a relation of her own,



<P>a gentleman of consequence and property in Devonshire.



<P>The letter was from this gentleman himself, and written



<P>in the true spirit of friendly accommodation.



<P>He understood that she was in need of a dwelling;



<P>and though the house he now offered her was merely a cottage,



<P>he assured her that everything should be done to it which



<P>she might think necessary, if the situation pleased her.



<P>He earnestly pressed her, after giving the particulars



<P>of the house and garden, to come with her daughters to



<P>Barton Park, the place of his own residence, from whence



<P>she might judge, herself, whether Barton Cottage, for the



<P>houses were in the same parish, could, by any alteration,



<P>be made comfortable to her.&nbsp; He seemed really anxious to



<P>accommodate them and the whole of his letter was written



<P>in so friendly a style as could not fail of giving pleasure



<P>to his cousin; more especially at a moment when she was



<P>suffering under the cold and unfeeling behaviour of her



<P>nearer connections.&nbsp; She needed no time for deliberation



<P>or inquiry.&nbsp; Her resolution was formed as she read.



<P>The situation of Barton, in a county so far distant from



<P>Sussex as Devonshire, which, but a few hours before,



<P>would have been a sufficient objection to outweigh every



<P>possible advantage belonging to the place, was now its



<P>first recommendation.&nbsp; To quit the neighbourhood of Norland



<P>was no longer an evil; it was an object of desire;



<P>it was a blessing, in comparison of the misery of continuing



<P>her daughter-in-law's guest; and to remove for ever



<P>from that beloved place would be less painful than to



<P>inhabit or visit it while such a woman was its mistress.



<P>She instantly wrote Sir John Middleton her acknowledgment



<P>of his kindness, and her acceptance of his proposal;



<P>and then hastened to shew both letters to her daughters,



<P>that she might be secure of their approbation before her



<P>answer were sent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor had always thought it would be more

prudent



<P>for them to settle at some distance from Norland,



<P>than immediately amongst their present acquaintance.



<P>On THAT head, therefore, it was not for her to oppose



<P>her mother's intention of removing into Devonshire.



<P>The house, too, as described by Sir John, was on so



<P>simple a scale, and the rent so uncommonly moderate,



<P>as to leave her no right of objection on either point;



<P>and, therefore, though it was not a plan which brought



<P>any charm to her fancy, though it was a removal from



<P>the vicinity of Norland beyond her wishes, she made



<P>no attempt to dissuade her mother from sending a letter



<P>of acquiescence.



<P>
