<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter10</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 10</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne's preserver, as Margaret, with more

elegance



<P>than precision, styled Willoughby, called at the cottage



<P>early the next morning to make his personal enquiries.



<P>He was received by Mrs. Dashwood with more than politeness;



<P>with a kindness which Sir John's account of him and her own



<P>gratitude prompted; and every thing that passed during



<P>the visit tended to assure him of the sense, elegance,



<P>mutual affection, and domestic comfort of the family



<P>to whom accident had now introduced him.&nbsp; Of their



<P>personal charms he had not required a second interview



<P>to be convinced.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Miss Dashwood had a delicate complexion,



<P>regular features, and a remarkably pretty figure.



<P>Marianne was still handsomer.&nbsp; Her form, though not so



<P>correct as her sister's, in having the advantage of height,



<P>was more striking; and her face was so lovely, that when



<P>in the common cant of praise, she was called a beautiful girl,



<P>truth was less violently outraged than usually happens.



<P>Her skin was very brown, but, from its transparency,



<P>her complexion was uncommonly brilliant; her features



<P>were all good; her smile was sweet and attractive;



<P>and in her eyes, which were very dark, there was a life,



<P>a spirit, an eagerness, which could hardily be seen



<P>without delight.&nbsp; From Willoughby their expression was at



<P>first held back, by the embarrassment which the remembrance



<P>of his assistance created.&nbsp; But when this passed away,



<P>when her spirits became collected, when she saw that to the



<P>perfect good-breeding of the gentleman, he united frankness



<P>and vivacity, and above all, when she heard him declare,



<P>that of music and dancing he was passionately fond,



<P>she gave him such a look of approbation as secured the



<P>largest share of his discourse to herself for the rest



<P>of his stay.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; It was only necessary to mention any favourite



<P>amusement to engage her to talk.&nbsp; She could not be



<P>silent when such points were introduced, and she



<P>had neither shyness nor reserve in their discussion.



<P>They speedily discovered that their enjoyment of dancing



<P>and music was mutual, and that it arose from a general



<P>conformity of judgment in all that related to either.



<P>Encouraged by this to a further examination of his opinions,



<P>she proceeded to question him on the subject of books;



<P>her favourite authors were brought forward and dwelt



<P>upon with so rapturous a delight, that any young man of



<P>five and twenty must have been insensible indeed, not to



<P>become an immediate convert to the excellence of such works,



<P>however disregarded before.&nbsp; Their taste was strikingly alike.



<P>The same books, the same passages were idolized by each--



<P>or if any difference appeared, any objection arose,



<P>it lasted no longer than till the force of her arguments



<P>and the brightness of her eyes could be displayed.



<P>He acquiesced in all her decisions, caught all her enthusiasm;



<P>and long before his visit concluded, they conversed



<P>with the familiarity of a long-established acquaintance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, Marianne," said Elinor, as soon as he

had left them,



<P>"for ONE morning I think you have done pretty well.



<P>You have already ascertained Mr. Willoughby's opinion in



<P>almost every matter of importance.&nbsp; You know what he thinks



<P>of Cowper and Scott; you are certain of his estimating



<P>their beauties as he ought, and you have received every



<P>assurance of his admiring Pope no more than is proper.



<P>But how is your acquaintance to be long supported, under such



<P>extraordinary despatch of every subject for discourse?



<P>You will soon have exhausted each favourite topic.



<P>Another meeting will suffice to explain his sentiments



<P>on picturesque beauty, and second marriages, and then



<P>you can have nothing farther to ask."--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Elinor," cried Marianne, "is this fair? is

this



<P>just? are my ideas so scanty?&nbsp; But I see what you mean.



<P>I have been too much at my ease, too happy, too frank.



<P>I have erred against every common-place notion of decorum;



<P>I have been open and sincere where I ought to have



<P>been reserved, spiritless, dull, and deceitful--had



<P>I talked only of the weather and the roads, and had I



<P>spoken only once in ten minutes, this reproach would have



<P>been spared."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "My love," said her mother, "you must not be

offended



<P>with Elinor--she was only in jest.&nbsp; I should scold



<P>her myself, if she were capable of wishing to check



<P>the delight of your conversation with our new friend."--



<P>Marianne was softened in a moment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Willoughby, on his side, gave every proof of

his



<P>pleasure in their acquaintance, which an evident wish



<P>of improving it could offer.&nbsp; He came to them every day.



<P>To enquire after Marianne was at first his excuse; but the



<P>encouragement of his reception, to which every day gave



<P>greater kindness, made such an excuse unnecessary before it



<P>had ceased to be possible, by Marianne's perfect recovery.



<P>She was confined for some days to the house; but never had



<P>any confinement been less irksome.&nbsp; Willoughby was a young



<P>man of good abilities, quick imagination, lively spirits,



<P>and open, affectionate manners.&nbsp; He was exactly formed



<P>to engage Marianne's heart, for with all this, he joined



<P>not only a captivating person, but a natural ardour



<P>of mind which was now roused and increased by the example



<P>of her own, and which recommended him to her affection



<P>beyond every thing else.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; His society became gradually her most exquisite

enjoyment.



<P>They read, they talked, they sang together; his musical



<P>talents were considerable; and he read with all the



<P>sensibility and spirit which Edward had unfortunately wanted.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In Mrs. Dashwood's estimation he was as faultless



<P>as in Marianne's; and Elinor saw nothing to censure in him



<P>but a propensity, in which he strongly resembled and peculiarly



<P>delighted her sister, of saying too much what he thought on



<P>every occasion, without attention to persons or circumstances.



<P>In hastily forming and giving his opinion of other people,



<P>in sacrificing general politeness to the enjoyment



<P>of undivided attention where his heart was engaged,



<P>and in slighting too easily the forms of worldly propriety,



<P>he displayed a want of caution which Elinor could not approve,



<P>in spite of all that he and Marianne could say in its support.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne began now to perceive that the desperation



<P>which had seized her at sixteen and a half, of ever



<P>seeing a man who could satisfy her ideas of perfection,



<P>had been rash and unjustifiable.&nbsp; Willoughby was all



<P>that her fancy had delineated in that unhappy hour



<P>and in every brighter period, as capable of attaching her;



<P>and his behaviour declared his wishes to be in that respect



<P>as earnest, as his abilities were strong.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Her mother too, in whose mind not one speculative



<P>thought of their marriage had been raised, by his prospect



<P>of riches, was led before the end of a week to hope and



<P>expect it; and secretly to congratulate herself on having



<P>gained two such sons-in-law as Edward and Willoughby.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Colonel Brandon's partiality for Marianne,

which had



<P>so early been discovered by his friends, now first became



<P>perceptible to Elinor, when it ceased to be noticed



<P>by them.&nbsp; Their attention and wit were drawn off to his



<P>more fortunate rival; and the raillery which the other



<P>had incurred before any partiality arose, was removed



<P>when his feelings began really to call for the ridicule



<P>so justly annexed to sensibility.&nbsp; Elinor was obliged,



<P>though unwillingly, to believe that the sentiments which



<P>Mrs. Jennings had assigned him for her own satisfaction,



<P>were now actually excited by her sister; and that however



<P>a general resemblance of disposition between the parties



<P>might forward the affection of Mr. Willoughby, an equally



<P>striking opposition of character was no hindrance to the



<P>regard of Colonel Brandon.&nbsp; She saw it with concern;



<P>for what could a silent man of five and thirty hope,



<P>when opposed to a very lively one of five and twenty? and as



<P>she could not even wish him successful, she heartily wished



<P>him indifferent.&nbsp; She liked him--in spite of his gravity



<P>and reserve, she beheld in him an object of interest.



<P>His manners, though serious, were mild; and his reserve



<P>appeared rather the result of some oppression of spirits



<P>than of any natural gloominess of temper.&nbsp; Sir John



<P>had dropped hints of past injuries and disappointments,



<P>which justified her belief of his being an unfortunate man,



<P>and she regarded him with respect and compassion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Perhaps she pitied and esteemed him the more



<P>because he was slighted by Willoughby and Marianne,



<P>who, prejudiced against him for being neither lively



<P>nor young, seemed resolved to undervalue his merits.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Brandon is just the kind of man," said Willoughby



<P>one day, when they were talking of him together,



<P>"whom every body speaks well of, and nobody cares about;



<P>whom all are delighted to see, and nobody remembers



<P>to talk to."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "That is exactly what I think of him," cried

Marianne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Do not boast of it, however," said Elinor,

"for it



<P>is injustice in both of you.&nbsp; He is highly esteemed



<P>by all the family at the park, and I never see him myself



<P>without taking pains to converse with him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "That he is patronised by YOU," replied Willoughby,



<P>"is certainly in his favour; but as for the esteem



<P>of the others, it is a reproach in itself.&nbsp; Who would



<P>submit to the indignity of being approved by such a woman



<P>as Lady Middleton and Mrs. Jennings, that could command



<P>the indifference of any body else?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But perhaps the abuse of such people as yourself



<P>and Marianne will make amends for the regard of Lady



<P>Middleton and her mother.&nbsp; If their praise is censure,



<P>your censure may be praise, for they are not more undiscerning,



<P>than you are prejudiced and unjust."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "In defence of your protege you can even be

saucy."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "My protege, as you call him, is a sensible

man;



<P>and sense will always have attractions for me.



<P>Yes, Marianne, even in a man between thirty and forty.



<P>He has seen a great deal of the world; has been abroad,



<P>has read, and has a thinking mind.&nbsp; I have found him



<P>capable of giving me much information on various subjects;



<P>and he has always answered my inquiries with readiness of



<P>good-breeding and good nature."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "That is to say," cried Marianne contemptuously,



<P>"he has told you, that in the East Indies the climate is hot,



<P>and the mosquitoes are troublesome."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "He WOULD have told me so, I doubt not, had

I made



<P>any such inquiries, but they happened to be points



<P>on which I had been previously informed."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Perhaps," said Willoughby, "his observations

may



<P>have extended to the existence of nabobs, gold mohrs,



<P>and palanquins."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I may venture to say that HIS observations



<P>have stretched much further than your candour.



<P>But why should you dislike him?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I do not dislike him.&nbsp; I consider him,

on the contrary,



<P>as a very respectable man, who has every body's good word,



<P>and nobody's notice; who, has more money than he can spend,



<P>more time than he knows how to employ, and two new coats



<P>every year."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Add to which," cried Marianne, "that he has



<P>neither genius, taste, nor spirit.&nbsp; That his understanding



<P>has no brilliancy, his feelings no ardour, and his voice



<P>no expression."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You decide on his imperfections so much in

the mass,"



<P>replied Elinor, "and so much on the strength of your



<P>own imagination, that the commendation I am able to give



<P>of him is comparatively cold and insipid.&nbsp; I can only



<P>pronounce him to be a sensible man, well-bred, well-informed,



<P>of gentle address, and, I believe, possessing an amiable heart."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Miss Dashwood," cried Willoughby, "you are

now using



<P>me unkindly.&nbsp; You are endeavouring to disarm me by reason,



<P>and to convince me against my will.&nbsp; But it will not do.



<P>You shall find me as stubborn as you can be artful.&nbsp; I have



<P>three unanswerable reasons for disliking Colonel Brandon;



<P>he threatened me with rain when I wanted it to be fine;



<P>he has found fault with the hanging of my curricle,



<P>and I cannot persuade him to buy my brown mare.&nbsp; If it



<P>will be any satisfaction to you, however, to be told,



<P>that I believe his character to be in other respects



<P>irreproachable, I am ready to confess it.&nbsp; And in return



<P>for an acknowledgment, which must give me some pain,



<P>you cannot deny me the privilege of disliking him as much



<P>as ever."



<P>

<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 38</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings was very warm in her praise of

Edward's



<P>conduct, but only Elinor and Marianne understood its



<P>true merit.&nbsp; THEY only knew how little he had had to tempt



<P>him to be disobedient, and how small was the consolation,



<P>beyond the consciousness of doing right, that could



<P>remain to him in the loss of friends and fortune.



<P>Elinor gloried in his integrity; and Marianne forgave all



<P>his offences in compassion for his punishment.&nbsp; But though



<P>confidence between them was, by this public discovery,



<P>restored to its proper state, it was not a subject on



<P>which either of them were fond of dwelling when alone.



<P>Elinor avoided it upon principle, as tending to fix still



<P>more upon her thoughts, by the too warm, too positive



<P>assurances of Marianne, that belief of Edward's continued



<P>affection for herself which she rather wished to do away;



<P>and Marianne's courage soon failed her, in trying



<P>to converse upon a topic which always left her more



<P>dissatisfied with herself than ever, by the comparison



<P>it necessarily produced between Elinor's conduct and her own.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She felt all the force of that comparison;

but not



<P>as her sister had hoped, to urge her to exertion now;



<P>she felt it with all the pain of continual self-reproach,



<P>regretted most bitterly that she had never exerted



<P>herself before; but it brought only the torture of penitence,



<P>without the hope of amendment.&nbsp; Her mind was so much weakened



<P>that she still fancied present exertion impossible,



<P>and therefore it only dispirited her more.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Nothing new was heard by them, for a day or

two afterwards,



<P>of affairs in Harley Street, or Bartlett's Buildings.



<P>But though so much of the matter was known to them already,



<P>that Mrs. Jennings might have had enough to do in spreading



<P>that knowledge farther, without seeking after more,



<P>she had resolved from the first to pay a visit of comfort



<P>and inquiry to her cousins as soon as she could;



<P>and nothing but the hindrance of more visitors than usual,



<P>had prevented her going to them within that time.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The third day succeeding their knowledge of

the



<P>particulars, was so fine, so beautiful a Sunday as to draw



<P>many to Kensington Gardens, though it was only the second



<P>week in March.&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings and Elinor were of the number;



<P>but Marianne, who knew that the Willoughbys were again



<P>in town, and had a constant dread of meeting them,



<P>chose rather to stay at home, than venture into so public



<P>a place.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; An intimate acquaintance of Mrs. Jennings joined



<P>them soon after they entered the Gardens, and Elinor was



<P>not sorry that by her continuing with them, and engaging



<P>all Mrs. Jennings's conversation, she was herself left



<P>to quiet reflection.&nbsp; She saw nothing of the Willoughbys,



<P>nothing of Edward, and for some time nothing of anybody



<P>who could by any chance whether grave or gay, be interesting



<P>to her.&nbsp; But at last she found herself with some surprise,



<P>accosted by Miss Steele, who, though looking rather shy,



<P>expressed great satisfaction in meeting them, and on receiving



<P>encouragement from the particular kindness of Mrs. Jennings,



<P>left her own party for a short time, to join their's.



<P>Mrs. Jennings immediately whispered to Elinor,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Get it all out of her, my dear.&nbsp; She

will tell you



<P>any thing if you ask.&nbsp; You see I cannot leave Mrs. Clarke."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; It was lucky, however, for Mrs. Jennings's

curiosity



<P>and Elinor's too, that she would tell any thing WITHOUT



<P>being asked; for nothing would otherwise have been learnt.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am so glad to meet you;" said Miss Steele,



<P>taking her familiarly by the arm--"for I wanted to see you



<P>of all things in the world."&nbsp; And then lowering her voice,



<P>"I suppose Mrs. Jennings has heard all about it.



<P>Is she angry?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Not at all, I believe, with you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "That is a good thing.&nbsp; And Lady Middleton,

is SHE angry?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I cannot suppose it possible that she should."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am monstrous glad of it.&nbsp; Good gracious!&nbsp;

I have



<P>had such a time of it!&nbsp; I never saw Lucy in such a rage



<P>in my life.&nbsp; She vowed at first she would never trim me



<P>up a new bonnet, nor do any thing else for me again,



<P>so long as she lived; but now she is quite come to,



<P>and we are as good friends as ever.&nbsp; Look, she made me



<P>this bow to my hat, and put in the feather last night.



<P>There now, YOU are going to laugh at me too.&nbsp; But why



<P>should not I wear pink ribbons?&nbsp; I do not care if it IS



<P>the Doctor's favourite colour.&nbsp; I am sure, for my part,



<P>I should never have known he DID like it better than



<P>any other colour, if he had not happened to say so.



<P>My cousins have been so plaguing me!&nbsp; I declare sometimes



<P>I do not know which way to look before them."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She had wandered away to a subject on which

Elinor



<P>had nothing to say, and therefore soon judged it expedient



<P>to find her way back again to the first.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, but Miss Dashwood," speaking triumphantly,



<P>"people may say what they chuse about Mr. Ferrars's



<P>declaring he would not have Lucy, for it is no such thing



<P>I can tell you; and it is quite a shame for such ill-natured



<P>reports to be spread abroad.&nbsp; Whatever Lucy might think



<P>about it herself, you know, it was no business of other



<P>people to set it down for certain."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I never heard any thing of the kind hinted

at before,



<P>I assure you," said Elinor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, did not you?&nbsp; But it WAS said, I

know, very well,



<P>and by more than one; for Miss Godby told Miss Sparks,



<P>that nobody in their senses could expect Mr. Ferrars



<P>to give up a woman like Miss Morton, with thirty thousand



<P>pounds to her fortune, for Lucy Steele that had



<P>nothing at all; and I had it from Miss Sparks myself.



<P>And besides that, my cousin Richard said himself,



<P>that when it came to the point he was afraid Mr. Ferrars



<P>would be off; and when Edward did not come near us



<P>for three days, I could not tell what to think myself;



<P>and I believe in my heart Lucy gave it up all for lost;



<P>for we came away from your brother's Wednesday,



<P>and we saw nothing of him not all Thursday, Friday,



<P>and Saturday, and did not know what was become of him.



<P>Once Lucy thought to write to him, but then her spirits



<P>rose against that.&nbsp; However this morning he came just



<P>as we came home from church; and then it all came out,



<P>how he had been sent for Wednesday to Harley Street,



<P>and been talked to by his mother and all of them,



<P>and how he had declared before them all that he loved



<P>nobody but Lucy, and nobody but Lucy would he have.



<P>And how he had been so worried by what passed,



<P>that as soon as he had went away from his mother's house,



<P>he had got upon his horse, and rid into the country,



<P>some where or other; and how he had stayed about at an inn



<P>all Thursday and Friday, on purpose to get the better



<P>of it.&nbsp; And after thinking it all over and over again,



<P>he said, it seemed to him as if, now he had no fortune,



<P>and no nothing at all, it would be quite unkind to keep



<P>her on to the engagement, because it must be for her loss,



<P>for he had nothing but two thousand pounds, and no hope



<P>of any thing else; and if he was to go into orders,



<P>as he had some thoughts, he could get nothing but a curacy,



<P>and how was they to live upon that?--He could not bear



<P>to think of her doing no better, and so he begged,



<P>if she had the least mind for it, to put an end to the



<P>matter directly, and leave him shift for himself.



<P>I heard him say all this as plain as could possibly be.



<P>And it was entirely for HER sake, and upon HER account,



<P>that he said a word about being off, and not upon his own.



<P>I will take my oath he never dropt a syllable of being



<P>tired of her, or of wishing to marry Miss Morton, or any



<P>thing like it.&nbsp; But, to be sure, Lucy would not give



<P>ear to such kind of talking; so she told him directly



<P>(with a great deal about sweet and love, you know,



<P>and all that--Oh, la! one can't repeat such kind of things



<P>you know)--she told him directly, she had not the least



<P>mind in the world to be off, for she could live with him



<P>upon a trifle, and how little so ever he might have,



<P>she should be very glad to have it all, you know,



<P>or something of the kind.&nbsp; So then he was monstrous happy,



<P>and talked on some time about what they should do,



<P>and they agreed he should take orders directly,



<P>and they must wait to be married till he got a living.



<P>And just then I could not hear any more, for my cousin



<P>called from below to tell me Mrs. Richardson was come in



<P>her coach, and would take one of us to Kensington Gardens;



<P>so I was forced to go into the room and interrupt them,



<P>to ask Lucy if she would like to go, but she did not



<P>care to leave Edward; so I just run up stairs and put



<P>on a pair of silk stockings and came off with the



<P>Richardsons."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I do not understand what you mean by interrupting

them,"



<P>said Elinor; "you were all in the same room together,



<P>were not you?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, indeed, not us.&nbsp; La! Miss Dashwood,

do you



<P>think people make love when any body else is by?&nbsp; Oh,



<P>for shame!--To be sure you must know better than that.



<P>(Laughing affectedly.)--No, no; they were shut up in the



<P>drawing-room together, and all I heard was only by listening



<P>at the door."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How!" cried Elinor; "have you been repeating

to me



<P>what you only learnt yourself by listening at the door?



<P>I am sorry I did not know it before; for I certainly



<P>would not have suffered you to give me particulars of a



<P>conversation which you ought not to have known yourself.



<P>How could you behave so unfairly by your sister?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, la! there is nothing in THAT.&nbsp; I

only stood at



<P>the door, and heard what I could.&nbsp; And I am sure Lucy would



<P>have done just the same by me; for a year or two back,



<P>when Martha Sharpe and I had so many secrets together,



<P>she never made any bones of hiding in a closet, or behind



<P>a chimney-board, on purpose to hear what we said."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor tried to talk of something else; but

Miss



<P>Steele could not be kept beyond a couple of minutes,



<P>from what was uppermost in her mind.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Edward talks of going to Oxford soon," said

she;



<P>"but now he is lodging at No. --, Pall Mall.&nbsp; What an



<P>ill-natured woman his monther is, an't she? And your



<P>brother and sister were not very kind! However,



<P>I shan't say anything against them to YOU; and to be sure



<P>they did send us home in their own chariot, which



<P>was more than I looked for.&nbsp; And for my part, I was all



<P>in a fright for fear your sister should ask us for the



<P>huswifes she had gave us a day or two before; but, however,



<P>nothing was said about them, and I took care to keep mine



<P>out of sight.&nbsp; Edward have got some business at Oxford,



<P>he says; so he must go there for a time; and after THAT,



<P>as soon as he can light upon a Bishop, he will be ordained.



<P>I wonder what curacy he will get!--Good gracious!



<P>(giggling as she spoke) I'd lay my life I know what



<P>my cousins will say, when they hear of it.&nbsp; They will



<P>tell me I should write to the Doctor, to get Edward



<P>the curacy of his new living.&nbsp; I know they will; but I am



<P>sure I would not do such a thing for all the world.--



<P>'La!' I shall say directly, 'I wonder how you could think



<P>of such a thing?&nbsp; I write to the Doctor, indeed!'"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well," said Elinor, "it is a comfort to be

prepared



<P>against the worst.&nbsp; You have got your answer ready."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Miss Steele was going to reply on the same

subject,



<P>but the approach of her own party made another more necessary.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh, la! here come the Richardsons.&nbsp; I

had a vast deal



<P>more to say to you, but I must not stay away from them not



<P>any longer.&nbsp; I assure you they are very genteel people.



<P>He makes a monstrous deal of money, and they keep their



<P>own coach.&nbsp; I have not time to speak to Mrs. Jennings about



<P>it myself, but pray tell her I am quite happy to hear she



<P>is not in anger against us, and Lady Middleton the same;



<P>and if anything should happen to take you and your



<P>sister away, and Mrs. Jennings should want company,



<P>I am sure we should be very glad to come and stay with her



<P>for as long a time as she likes.&nbsp; I suppose Lady Middleton



<P>won't ask us any more this bout.&nbsp; Good-by; I am sorry



<P>Miss Marianne was not here.&nbsp; Remember me kindly to her.



<P>La! if you have not got your spotted muslin on!--I wonder



<P>you was not afraid of its being torn."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Such was her parting concern; for after this,

she had



<P>time only to pay her farewell compliments to Mrs. Jennings,



<P>before her company was claimed by Mrs. Richardson;



<P>and Elinor was left in possession of knowledge which



<P>might feed her powers of reflection some time, though she



<P>had learnt very little more than what had been already



<P>foreseen and foreplanned in her own mind.&nbsp; Edward's marriage



<P>with Lucy was as firmly determined on, and the time



<P>of its taking place remained as absolutely uncertain,



<P>as she had concluded it would be;--every thing depended,



<P>exactly after her expectation, on his getting that preferment,



<P>of which, at present, there seemed not the smallest chance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As soon as they returned to the carriage,



<P>Mrs. Jennings was eager for information; but as Elinor



<P>wished to spread as little as possible intelligence



<P>that had in the first place been so unfairly obtained,



<P>she confined herself to the brief repetition of such



<P>simple particulars, as she felt assured that Lucy,



<P>for the sake of her own consequence, would choose



<P>to have known.&nbsp; The continuance of their engagement,



<P>and the means that were able to be taken for promoting



<P>its end, was all her communication; and this produced



<P>from Mrs. Jennings the following natural remark.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Wait for his having a living!--ay, we all

know how



<P>THAT will end:--they will wait a twelvemonth, and finding



<P>no good comes of it, will set down upon a curacy of fifty



<P>pounds a-year, with the interest of his two thousand pounds,



<P>and what little matter Mr. Steele and Mr. Pratt can



<P>give her.--Then they will have a child every year! and



<P>Lord help 'em! how poor they will be!--I must see



<P>what I can give them towards furnishing their house.



<P>Two maids and two men, indeed!--as I talked of t'other



<P>day.--No, no, they must get a stout girl of all works.--



<P>Betty's sister would never do for them NOW."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The next morning brought Elinor a letter by

the



<P>two-penny post from Lucy herself.&nbsp; It was as follows:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

"Bartlett's Building, March.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I hope my dear

Miss Dashwood will excuse the



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; liberty I take of writing to her; but I know

your



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; friendship for me will make you pleased to

hear such



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; a good account of myself and my dear Edward,

after



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; all the troubles we have went through lately,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; therefore will make no more apologies, but

proceed



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; to say that, thank God! though we have suffered



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; dreadfully, we are both quite well now, and

as happy



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; as we must always be in one another's love.&nbsp;

We have



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; had great trials, and great persecutions, but



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; however, at the same time, gratefully acknowledge



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; many friends, yourself not the least among

them,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; whose great kindness I shall always thankfully



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; remember, as will Edward too, who I have told

of



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; it.&nbsp; I am sure you will be glad to hear,

as likewise



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; dear Mrs. Jennings, I spent two happy hours

with



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; him yesterday afternoon, he would not hear

of our



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; parting, though earnestly did I, as I thought

my



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; duty required, urge him to it for prudence

sake,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; and would have parted for ever on the spot,

would



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; he consent to it; but he said it should never

be,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; he did not regard his mother's anger, while

he could



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; have my affections; our prospects are not very



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; bright, to be sure, but we must wait, and hope

for



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; the best; he will be ordained shortly; and

should



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; it ever be in your power to recommend him to

any



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; body that has a living to bestow, am very sure

you



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; will not forget us, and dear Mrs. Jennings

too,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; trust she will speak a good word for us to

Sir John,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; or Mr. Palmer, or any friend that may be able

to



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; assist us.--Poor Anne was much to blame for

what



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; she did, but she did it for the best, so I

say



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; nothing; hope Mrs. Jennings won't think it

too much



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; trouble to give us a call, should she come

this way



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; any morning, 'twould be a great kindness, and

my



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; cousins would be proud to know her.--My paper

reminds



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; me to conclude; and begging to be most gratefully



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; and respectfully remembered to her, and to

Sir John,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; and Lady Middleton, and the dear children,

when you



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; chance to see them, and love to Miss Marianne,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

"I am, &amp;c."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As soon as Elinor had finished it, she performed



<P>what she concluded to be its writer's real design,



<P>by placing it in the hands of Mrs. Jennings, who read it



<P>aloud with many comments of satisfaction and praise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Very well indeed!--how prettily she writes!--aye,



<P>that was quite proper to let him be off if he would.



<P>That was just like Lucy.--Poor soul! I wish I COULD get



<P>him a living, with all my heart.--She calls me dear



<P>Mrs. Jennings, you see.&nbsp; She is a good-hearted girl



<P>as ever lived.--Very well upon my word.&nbsp; That sentence



<P>is very prettily turned.&nbsp; Yes, yes, I will go and see her,



<P>sure enough.&nbsp; How attentive she is, to think of every



<P>body!--Thank you, my dear, for shewing it me.&nbsp; It is



<P>as pretty a letter as ever I saw, and does Lucy's head



<P>and heart great credit."



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter39</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 39</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The Miss Dashwoods had now been rather more

than



<P>two months in town, and Marianne's impatience to be gone



<P>increased every day.&nbsp; She sighed for the air, the liberty,



<P>the quiet of the country; and fancied that if any place



<P>could give her ease, Barton must do it.&nbsp; Elinor was hardly



<P>less anxious than herself for their removal, and only so much



<P>less bent on its being effected immediately, as that she



<P>was conscious of the difficulties of so long a journey,



<P>which Marianne could not be brought to acknowledge.



<P>She began, however, seriously to turn her thoughts towards



<P>its accomplishment, and had already mentioned their wishes



<P>to their kind hostess, who resisted them with all the



<P>eloquence of her good-will, when a plan was suggested,



<P>which, though detaining them from home yet a few weeks



<P>longer, appeared to Elinor altogether much more eligible



<P>than any other. The Palmers were to remove to Cleveland



<P>about the end of March, for the Easter holidays;



<P>and Mrs. Jennings, with both her friends, received a very



<P>warm invitation from Charlotte to go with them.&nbsp; This would



<P>not, in itself, have been sufficient for the delicacy of



<P>Miss Dashwood;--but it was inforced with so much real



<P>politeness by Mr. Palmer himself, as, joined to the very



<P>great amendment of his manners towards them since her



<P>sister had been known to be unhappy, induced her to accept



<P>it with pleasure.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When she told Marianne what she had done, however,



<P>her first reply was not very auspicious.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Cleveland!"--she cried, with great agitation.



<P>"No, I cannot go to Cleveland."--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You forget," said Elinor gently, "that its

situation



<P>is not...that it is not in the neighbourhood of..."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But it is in Somersetshire.--I cannot go



<P>into Somersetshire.--There, where I looked forward



<P>to going...No, Elinor, you cannot expect me to go there."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor would not argue upon the propriety of

overcoming



<P>such feelings;--she only endeavoured to counteract them by



<P>working on others;--represented it, therefore, as a measure



<P>which would fix the time of her returning to that dear mother,



<P>whom she so much wished to see, in a more eligible,



<P>more comfortable manner, than any other plan could do,



<P>and perhaps without any greater delay.&nbsp; From Cleveland,



<P>which was within a few miles of Bristol, the distance to



<P>Barton was not beyond one day, though a long day's journey;



<P>and their mother's servant might easily come there to attend



<P>them down; and as there could be no occasion of their



<P>staying above a week at Cleveland, they might now be at



<P>home in little more than three weeks' time.&nbsp; As Marianne's



<P>affection for her mother was sincere, it must triumph



<P>with little difficulty, over the imaginary evils she had started.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings was so far from being weary of

her guest,



<P>that she pressed them very earnestly to return with her again



<P>from Cleveland.&nbsp; Elinor was grateful for the attention,



<P>but it could not alter her design; and their mother's



<P>concurrence being readily gained, every thing relative



<P>to their return was arranged as far as it could be;--



<P>and Marianne found some relief in drawing up a statement



<P>of the hours that were yet to divide her from Barton.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Ah! Colonel, I do not know what you and I

shall



<P>do without the Miss Dashwoods;"--was Mrs. Jennings's



<P>address to him when he first called on her, after their



<P>leaving her was settled--"for they are quite resolved



<P>upon going home from the Palmers;--and how forlorn we



<P>shall be, when I come back!--Lord! we shall sit and gape



<P>at one another as dull as two cats."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Perhaps Mrs. Jennings was in hopes, by this

vigorous



<P>sketch of their future ennui, to provoke him to make



<P>that offer, which might give himself an escape from it;--



<P>and if so, she had soon afterwards good reason to think



<P>her object gained; for, on Elinor's moving to the window



<P>to take more expeditiously the dimensions of a print,



<P>which she was going to copy for her friend, he followed



<P>her to it with a look of particular meaning, and conversed



<P>with her there for several minutes.&nbsp; The effect of his



<P>discourse on the lady too, could not escape her observation,



<P>for though she was too honorable to listen, and had even



<P>changed her seat, on purpose that she might NOT hear,



<P>to one close by the piano forte on which Marianne



<P>was playing, she could not keep herself from seeing



<P>that Elinor changed colour, attended with agitation,



<P>and was too intent on what he said to pursue her employment.--



<P>Still farther in confirmation of her hopes, in the interval



<P>of Marianne's turning from one lesson to another,



<P>some words of the Colonel's inevitably reached her ear,



<P>in which he seemed to be apologising for the badness



<P>of his house.&nbsp; This set the matter beyond a doubt.



<P>She wondered, indeed, at his thinking it necessary



<P>to do so; but supposed it to be the proper etiquette.



<P>What Elinor said in reply she could not distinguish,



<P>but judged from the motion of her lips, that she did



<P>not think THAT any material objection;--and Mrs. Jennings



<P>commended her in her heart for being so honest.



<P>They then talked on for a few minutes longer without her



<P>catching a syllable, when another lucky stop in Marianne's



<P>performance brought her these words in the Colonel's calm



<P>voice,--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am afraid it cannot take place very soon."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Astonished and shocked at so unlover-like a

speech,



<P>she was almost ready to cry out, "Lord! what should



<P>hinder it?"--but checking her desire, confined herself



<P>to this silent ejaculation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "This is very strange!--sure he need not wait



<P>to be older."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This delay on the Colonel's side, however,

did not



<P>seem to offend or mortify his fair companion in the least,



<P>for on their breaking up the conference soon afterwards,



<P>and moving different ways, Mrs. Jennings very plainly heard



<P>Elinor say, and with a voice which shewed her to feel what she said,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I shall always think myself very much obliged

to you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings was delighted with her gratitude,



<P>and only wondered that after hearing such a sentence,



<P>the Colonel should be able to take leave of them, as he



<P>immediately did, with the utmost sang-froid, and go away



<P>without making her any reply!--She had not thought her old



<P>friend could have made so indifferent a suitor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; What had really passed between them was to

this effect.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have heard," said he, with great compassion,



<P>"of the injustice your friend Mr. Ferrars has suffered



<P>from his family; for if I understand the matter right,



<P>he has been entirely cast off by them for persevering



<P>in his engagement with a very deserving young woman.--



<P>Have I been rightly informed?--Is it so?--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor told him that it was.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "The cruelty, the impolitic cruelty,"--he replied,



<P>with great feeling,--"of dividing, or attempting to divide,



<P>two young people long attached to each other, is terrible.--



<P>Mrs. Ferrars does not know what she may be doing--what



<P>she may drive her son to.&nbsp; I have seen Mr. Ferrars two



<P>or three times in Harley Street, and am much pleased



<P>with him.&nbsp; He is not a young man with whom one can



<P>be intimately acquainted in a short time, but I have



<P>seen enough of him to wish him well for his own sake,



<P>and as a friend of yours, I wish it still more.



<P>I understand that he intends to take orders.&nbsp; Will you



<P>be so good as to tell him that the living of Delaford,



<P>now just vacant, as I am informed by this day's post,



<P>is his, if he think it worth his acceptance--but THAT,



<P>perhaps, so unfortunately circumstanced as he is now,



<P>it may be&nbsp; nonsense to appear to doubt; I only wish it



<P>were more&nbsp; valuable.-- It is a rectory, but a small one;



<P>the late incumbent, I believe, did not make more than



<P>200 L per annum, and though it is certainly capable



<P>of improvement, I fear, not to such an amount as



<P>to afford him a very comfortable income.&nbsp; Such as it is,



<P>however, my pleasure in presenting him to it,



<P>will be very great.&nbsp; Pray assure him of it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor's astonishment at this commission could



<P>hardly have been greater, had the Colonel been really



<P>making her an offer of his hand.&nbsp; The preferment,



<P>which only two days before she had considered as hopeless



<P>for Edward, was already provided to enable him to marry;--



<P>and SHE, of all people in the world, was fixed on to



<P>bestow it!--Her emotion was such as Mrs. Jennings had



<P>attributed to a very different cause;--but whatever minor



<P>feelings less pure, less pleasing, might have a share



<P>in that emotion, her esteem for the general benevolence,



<P>and her gratitude for the particular friendship,



<P>which together prompted Colonel Brandon to this act,



<P>were strongly felt, and warmly expressed.&nbsp; She thanked him



<P>for it with all her heart, spoke of Edward's principles and



<P>disposition with that praise which she knew them to deserve;



<P>and promised to undertake the commission with pleasure,



<P>if it were really his wish to put off so agreeable an office



<P>to another.&nbsp; But at the same time, she could not help



<P>thinking that no one could so well perform it as himself.



<P>It was an office in short, from which, unwilling to give



<P>Edward the pain of receiving an obligation from HER,



<P>she would have been very glad to be spared herself;--



<P>but Colonel Brandon, on motives of equal delicacy,



<P>declining it likewise, still seemed so desirous of its being



<P>given through her means, that she would not on any account



<P>make farther opposition.&nbsp; Edward, she believed, was still in town,



<P>and fortunately she had heard his address from Miss Steele.



<P>She could undertake therefore to inform him of it,



<P>in the course of the day.&nbsp; After this had been settled,



<P>Colonel Brandon began to talk of his own advantage



<P>in securing so respectable and agreeable a neighbour,



<P>and THEN it was that he mentioned with regret, that the



<P>house was small and indifferent;--an evil which Elinor,



<P>as Mrs. Jennings had supposed her to do, made very light of,



<P>at least as far as regarded its size.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "The smallness of the house," said she,



<P>"I cannot imagine any inconvenience to them,



<P>for it will be in proportion to their family and income."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; By which the Colonel was surprised to find

that SHE



<P>was considering Mr. Ferrars's marriage as the certain



<P>consequence of the presentation; for he did not suppose it



<P>possible that Delaford living could supply such an income,



<P>as anybody in his style of life would venture to settle on--



<P>and he said so.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "This little rectory CAN do no more than make

Mr. Ferrars



<P>comfortable as a bachelor; it cannot enable him to marry.



<P>I am sorry to say that my patronage ends with this;



<P>and my interest is hardly more extensive.&nbsp; If, however,



<P>by an unforeseen chance it should be in my power to serve



<P>him farther, I must think very differently of him



<P>from what I now do, if I am not as ready to be useful



<P>to him then as I sincerely wish I could be at present.



<P>What I am now doing indeed, seems nothing at all,



<P>since it can advance him so little towards what must



<P>be his principal, his only object of happiness.



<P>His marriage must still be a distant good;--at least,



<P>I am afraid it cannot take place very soon.--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Such was the sentence which, when misunderstood,



<P>so justly offended the delicate feelings of Mrs. Jennings;



<P>but after this narration of what really passed between



<P>Colonel Brandon and Elinor, while they stood at the window,



<P>the gratitude expressed by the latter on their parting, may



<P>perhaps appear in general, not less reasonably excited,



<P>nor less properly worded than if it had arisen from



<P>an offer of marriage.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter11</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 11</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Little had Mrs. Dashwood or her daughters imagined



<P>when they first came into Devonshire, that so many



<P>engagements would arise to occupy their time as shortly



<P>presented themselves, or that they should have such frequent



<P>invitations and such constant visitors as to leave them little



<P>leisure for serious employment.&nbsp; Yet such was the case.



<P>When Marianne was recovered, the schemes of amusement at home



<P>and abroad, which Sir John had been previously forming,



<P>were put into execution.&nbsp; The private balls at the park



<P>then began; and parties on the water were made and



<P>accomplished as often as a showery October would allow.



<P>In every meeting of the kind Willoughby was included;



<P>and the ease and familiarity which naturally attended



<P>these parties were exactly calculated to give increasing



<P>intimacy to his acquaintance with the Dashwoods, to afford



<P>him opportunity of witnessing the excellencies of Marianne,



<P>of marking his animated admiration of her, and of receiving,



<P>in her behaviour to himself, the most pointed assurance



<P>of her affection.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor could not be surprised at their attachment.



<P>She only wished that it were less openly shewn; and once



<P>or twice did venture to suggest the propriety of some



<P>self-command to Marianne.&nbsp; But Marianne abhorred all



<P>concealment where no real disgrace could attend unreserve;



<P>and to aim at the restraint of sentiments which were not



<P>in themselves illaudable, appeared to her not merely



<P>an unnecessary effort, but a disgraceful subjection



<P>of reason to common-place and mistaken notions.



<P>Willoughby thought the same; and their behaviour at



<P>all times, was an illustration of their opinions.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When he was present she had no eyes for any

one else.



<P>Every thing he did, was right.&nbsp; Every thing he said, was clever.



<P>If their evenings at the park were concluded with cards,



<P>he cheated himself and all the rest of the party to get



<P>her a good hand.&nbsp; If dancing formed the amusement



<P>of the night, they were partners for half the time;



<P>and when obliged to separate for a couple of dances,



<P>were careful to stand together and scarcely spoke a word



<P>to any body else.&nbsp; Such conduct made them of course



<P>most exceedingly laughed at; but ridicule could not shame,



<P>and seemed hardly to provoke them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood entered into all their feelings

with



<P>a warmth which left her no inclination for checking this



<P>excessive display of them.&nbsp; To her it was but the natural



<P>consequence of a strong affection in a young and ardent mind.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This was the season of happiness to Marianne.



<P>Her heart was devoted to Willoughby, and the fond attachment



<P>to Norland, which she brought with her from Sussex,



<P>was more likely to be softened than she had thought it



<P>possible before, by the charms which his society bestowed



<P>on her present home.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor's happiness was not so great.&nbsp;

Her heart was not



<P>so much at ease, nor her satisfaction in their amusements



<P>so pure.&nbsp; They afforded her no companion that could make



<P>amends for what she had left behind, nor that could teach



<P>her to think of Norland with less regret than ever.



<P>Neither Lady Middleton nor Mrs. Jennings could supply



<P>to her the conversation she missed; although the latter



<P>was an everlasting talker, and from the first had regarded



<P>her with a kindness which ensured her a large share of



<P>her discourse.&nbsp; She had already repeated her own history



<P>to Elinor three or four times; and had Elinor's memory been



<P>equal to her means of improvement, she might have known



<P>very early in their acquaintance all the particulars of



<P>Mr. Jenning's last illness, and what he said to his wife



<P>a few minutes before he died.&nbsp; Lady Middleton was more



<P>agreeable than her mother only in being more silent.



<P>Elinor needed little observation to perceive that her



<P>reserve was a mere calmness of manner with which sense



<P>had nothing to do.&nbsp; Towards her husband and mother she



<P>was the same as to them; and intimacy was therefore



<P>neither to be looked for nor desired.&nbsp; She had nothing



<P>to say one day that she had not said the day before.



<P>Her insipidity was invariable, for even her spirits were



<P>always the same; and though she did not oppose the parties



<P>arranged by her husband, provided every thing were conducted



<P>in style and her two eldest children attended her,



<P>she never appeared to receive more enjoyment from them



<P>than she might have experienced in sitting at home;--



<P>and so little did her presence add to the pleasure



<P>of the others, by any share in their conversation,



<P>that they were sometimes only reminded of her being



<P>amongst them by her solicitude about her troublesome boys.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In Colonel Brandon alone, of all her new acquaintance,



<P>did Elinor find a person who could in any degree claim the



<P>respect of abilities, excite the interest of friendship,



<P>or give pleasure as a companion.&nbsp; Willoughby was out



<P>of the question.&nbsp; Her admiration and regard, even her



<P>sisterly regard, was all his own; but he was a lover;



<P>his attentions were wholly Marianne's, and a far less



<P>agreeable man might have been more generally pleasing.



<P>Colonel Brandon, unfortunately for himself, had no such



<P>encouragement to think only of Marianne, and in conversing



<P>with Elinor he found the greatest consolation for the



<P>indifference of her sister.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor's compassion for him increased, as she

had reason



<P>to suspect that the misery of disappointed love had already



<P>been known to him.&nbsp; This suspicion was given by some words



<P>which accidently dropped from him one evening at the park,



<P>when they were sitting down together by mutual consent,



<P>while the others were dancing.&nbsp; His eyes were fixed



<P>on Marianne, and, after a silence of some minutes,



<P>he said, with a faint smile, "Your sister, I understand,



<P>does not approve of second attachments."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No," replied Elinor, "her opinions are all

romantic."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Or rather, as I believe, she considers them



<P>impossible to exist."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I believe she does.&nbsp; But how she contrives

it



<P>without reflecting on the character of her own father,



<P>who had himself two wives, I know not.&nbsp; A few years



<P>however will settle her opinions on the reasonable basis



<P>of common sense and observation; and then they may be



<P>more easy to define and to justify than they now are,



<P>by any body but herself."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "This will probably be the case," he replied;



<P>"and yet there is something so amiable in the prejudices



<P>of a young mind, that one is sorry to see them give way



<P>to the reception of more general opinions."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I cannot agree with you there," said Elinor.



<P>"There are inconveniences attending such feelings



<P>as Marianne's, which all the charms of enthusiasm and



<P>ignorance of the world cannot atone for.&nbsp; Her systems have



<P>all the unfortunate tendency of setting propriety at nought;



<P>and a better acquaintance with the world is what I look



<P>forward to as her greatest possible advantage."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; After a short pause he resumed the conversation



<P>by saying,--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Does your sister make no distinction in her

objections



<P>against a second attachment? or is it equally criminal



<P>in every body?&nbsp; Are those who have been disappointed



<P>in their first choice, whether from the inconstancy



<P>of its object, or the perverseness of circumstances,



<P>to be equally indifferent during the rest of their lives?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Upon my word, I am not acquainted with the

minutiae



<P>of her principles.&nbsp; I only know that I never yet heard her



<P>admit any instance of a second attachment's being pardonable."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "This," said he, "cannot hold; but a change,



<P>a total change of sentiments--No, no, do not desire it;



<P>for when the romantic refinements of a young mind



<P>are obliged to give way, how frequently are they



<P>succeeded by such opinions as are but too common, and too



<P>dangerous!&nbsp; I speak from experience.&nbsp; I once knew a lady



<P>who in temper and mind greatly resembled your sister,



<P>who thought and judged like her, but who from an inforced



<P>change--from a series of unfortunate circumstances"--



<P>Here he stopt suddenly; appeared to think that he had said



<P>too much, and by his countenance gave rise to conjectures,



<P>which might not otherwise have entered Elinor's head.



<P>The lady would probably have passed without suspicion,



<P>had he not convinced Miss Dashwood that what concerned



<P>her ought not to escape his lips.&nbsp; As it was,



<P>it required but a slight effort of fancy to connect his



<P>emotion with the tender recollection of past regard.



<P>Elinor attempted no more.&nbsp; But Marianne, in her place,



<P>would not have done so little.&nbsp; The whole story would



<P>have been speedily formed under her active imagination;



<P>and every thing established in the most melancholy order



<P>of disastrous love.



<P>

</BODY>

<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">
