   <TITLE>chapter05</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 5</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; No sooner was her answer dispatched, than Mrs.

Dashwood



<P>indulged herself in the pleasure of announcing to her



<P>son-in-law and his wife that she was provided with a house,



<P>and should incommode them no longer than till every thing were



<P>ready for her inhabiting it.&nbsp; They heard her with surprise.



<P>Mrs. John Dashwood said nothing; but her husband civilly



<P>hoped that she would not be settled far from Norland.



<P>She had great satisfaction in replying that she was going



<P>into Devonshire.--Edward turned hastily towards her,



<P>on hearing this, and, in a voice of surprise and concern,



<P>which required no explanation to her, repeated,



<P>"Devonshire! Are you, indeed, going there?&nbsp; So far from hence!



<P>And to what part of it?"&nbsp; She explained the situation.



<P>It was within four miles northward of Exeter.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is but a cottage," she continued, "but

I hope



<P>to see many of my friends in it.&nbsp; A room or two can



<P>easily be added; and if my friends find no difficulty



<P>in travelling so far to see me, I am sure I will find



<P>none in accommodating them."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She concluded with a very kind invitation to



<P>Mr. and Mrs. John Dashwood to visit her at Barton;



<P>and to Edward she gave one with still greater affection.



<P>Though her late conversation with her daughter-in-law had



<P>made her resolve on remaining at Norland no longer than



<P>was unavoidable, it had not produced the smallest effect



<P>on her in that point to which it principally tended.



<P>To separate Edward and Elinor was as far from being her



<P>object as ever; and she wished to show Mrs. John Dashwood,



<P>by this pointed invitation to her brother, how totally she



<P>disregarded her disapprobation of the match.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mr. John Dashwood told his mother again and

again



<P>how exceedingly sorry he was that she had taken a house at



<P>such a distance from Norland as to prevent his being of any



<P>service to her in removing her furniture.&nbsp; He really felt



<P>conscientiously vexed on the occasion; for the very exertion



<P>to which he had limited the performance of his promise to



<P>his father was by this arrangement rendered impracticable.--



<P>The furniture was all sent around by water.&nbsp; It chiefly



<P>consisted of household linen, plate, china, and books,



<P>with a handsome pianoforte of Marianne's.&nbsp; Mrs. John



<P>Dashwood saw the packages depart with a sigh: she could



<P>not help feeling it hard that as Mrs. Dashwood's income



<P>would be so trifling in comparison with their own,



<P>she should have any handsome article of furniture.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood took the house for a twelvemonth;

it was



<P>ready furnished, and she might have immediate possession.



<P>No difficulty arose on either side in the agreement; and she



<P>waited only for the disposal of her effects at Norland,



<P>and to determine her future household, before she set



<P>off for the west; and this, as she was exceedingly rapid



<P>in the performance of everything that interested her,



<P>was soon done.--The horses which were left her by her husband



<P>had been sold soon after his death, and an opportunity



<P>now offering of disposing of her carriage, she agreed



<P>to sell that likewise at the earnest advice of her



<P>eldest daughter.&nbsp; For the comfort of her children, had she



<P>consulted only her own wishes, she would have kept it;



<P>but the discretion of Elinor prevailed.&nbsp; HER wisdom



<P>too limited the number of their servants to three;



<P>two maids and a man, with whom they were speedily provided



<P>from amongst those who had formed their establishment



<P>at Norland.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The man and one of the maids were sent off

immediately



<P>into Devonshire, to prepare the house for their mistress's



<P>arrival; for as Lady Middleton was entirely unknown



<P>to Mrs. Dashwood, she preferred going directly to the



<P>cottage to being a visitor at Barton Park; and she relied



<P>so undoubtingly on Sir John's description of the house,



<P>as to feel no curiosity to examine it herself till she



<P>entered it as her own.&nbsp; Her eagerness to be gone from Norland



<P>was preserved from diminution by the evident satisfaction



<P>of her daughter-in-law in the prospect of her removal;



<P>a satisfaction which was but feebly attempted to be concealed



<P>under a cold invitation to her to defer her departure.



<P>Now was the time when her son-in-law's promise to his



<P>father might with particular propriety be fulfilled.



<P>Since he had neglected to do it on first coming to



<P>the estate, their quitting his house might be looked



<P>on as the most suitable period for its accomplishment.



<P>But Mrs. Dashwood began shortly to give over every



<P>hope of the kind, and to be convinced, from the general



<P>drift of his discourse, that his assistance extended no



<P>farther than their maintenance for six months at Norland.



<P>He so frequently talked of the increasing expenses



<P>of housekeeping, and of the perpetual demands upon his purse,



<P>which a man of any consequence in the world was beyond



<P>calculation exposed to, that he seemed rather to stand



<P>in need of more money himself than to have any design of



<P>giving money away.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In a very few weeks from the day which brought

Sir



<P>John Middleton's first letter to Norland, every thing was



<P>so far settled in their future abode as to enable



<P>Mrs. Dashwood and her daughters to begin their journey.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Many were the tears shed by them in their last



<P>adieus to a place so much beloved.&nbsp; "Dear, dear Norland!"



<P>said Marianne, as she wandered alone before the house,



<P>on the last evening of their being there; "when shall I cease



<P>to regret you!--when learn to feel a home elsewhere!--Oh!



<P>happy house, could you know what I suffer in now viewing



<P>you from this spot, from whence perhaps I may view



<P>you no more!--And you, ye well-known trees!--but you



<P>will continue the same.--No leaf will decay because we



<P>are removed, nor any branch become motionless although we



<P>can observe you no longer!--No; you will continue the same;



<P>unconscious of the pleasure or the regret you occasion,



<P>and insensible of any change in those who walk under your



<P>shade!--But who will remain to enjoy you?"



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter29</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 29</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Before the house-maid had lit their fire the

next day,



<P>or the sun gained any power over a cold, gloomy morning



<P>in January, Marianne, only half dressed, was kneeling



<P>against one of the window-seats for the sake of all



<P>the little light she could command from it, and writing



<P>as fast as a continual flow of tears would permit her.



<P>In this situation, Elinor, roused from sleep by her agitation



<P>and sobs, first perceived her; and after observing her



<P>for a few moments with silent anxiety, said, in a tone



<P>of the most considerate gentleness,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Marianne, may I ask-?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, Elinor," she replied, "ask nothing; you

will



<P>soon know all."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The sort of desperate calmness with which this

was said,



<P>lasted no longer than while she spoke, and was immediately



<P>followed by a return of the same excessive affliction.



<P>It was some minutes before she could go on with her letter,



<P>and the frequent bursts of grief which still obliged her,



<P>at intervals, to withhold her pen, were proofs enough of her



<P>feeling how more than probable it was that she was writing



<P>for the last time to Willoughby.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor paid her every quiet and unobtrusive

attention



<P>in her power; and she would have tried to sooth and



<P>tranquilize her still more, had not Marianne entreated her,



<P>with all the eagerness of the most nervous irritability,



<P>not to speak to her for the world.&nbsp; In such circumstances,



<P>it was better for both that they should not be long together;



<P>and the restless state of Marianne's mind not only prevented



<P>her from remaining in the room a moment after she was dressed,



<P>but requiring at once solitude and continual change of place,



<P>made her wander about the house till breakfast time, avoiding



<P>the sight of every body.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; At breakfast she neither ate, nor attempted

to eat



<P>any thing; and Elinor's attention was then all employed,



<P>not in urging her, not in pitying her, nor in appearing



<P>to regard her, but in endeavouring to engage Mrs. Jenning's



<P>notice entirely to herself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As this was a favourite meal with Mrs. Jennings,



<P>it lasted a considerable time, and they were just setting



<P>themselves, after it, round the common working table, when a



<P>letter was delivered to Marianne, which she eagerly caught



<P>from the servant, and, turning of a death-like paleness,



<P>instantly ran out of the room.&nbsp; Elinor, who saw as plainly



<P>by this, as if she had seen the direction, that it must



<P>come from Willoughby, felt immediately such a sickness



<P>at heart as made her hardly able to hold up her head,



<P>and sat in such a general tremour as made her fear it



<P>impossible to escape Mrs. Jenning's notice.&nbsp; That good lady,



<P>however, saw only that Marianne had received a letter



<P>from Willoughby, which appeared to her a very good joke,



<P>and which she treated accordingly, by hoping, with a laugh,



<P>that she would find it to her liking.&nbsp; Of Elinor's distress,



<P>she was too busily employed in measuring lengths of worsted



<P>for her rug, to see any thing at all; and calmly continuing



<P>her talk, as soon as Marianne disappeared, she said,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Upon my word, I never saw a young woman so



<P>desperately in love in my life!&nbsp; MY girls were nothing



<P>to her, and yet they used to be foolish enough; but as



<P>for Miss Marianne, she is quite an altered creature.



<P>I hope, from the bottom of my heart, he won't keep her



<P>waiting much longer, for it is quite grievous to see her



<P>look so ill and forlorn.&nbsp; Pray, when are they to be married?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor, though never less disposed to speak

than at



<P>that moment, obliged herself to answer such an attack



<P>as this, and, therefore, trying to smile, replied, "And have



<P>you really, Ma'am, talked yourself into a persuasion



<P>of my sister's being engaged to Mr. Willoughby?&nbsp; I thought



<P>it had been only a joke, but so serious a question seems



<P>to imply more; and I must beg, therefore, that you will not



<P>deceive yourself any longer.&nbsp; I do assure you that nothing



<P>would surprise me more than to hear of their being going



<P>to be married."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "For shame, for shame, Miss Dashwood! how can

you



<P>talk so?&nbsp; Don't we all know that it must be a match, that



<P>they were over head and ears in love with each other from



<P>the first moment they met?&nbsp; Did not I see them together



<P>in Devonshire every day, and all day long; and did not I



<P>know that your sister came to town with me on purpose



<P>to buy wedding clothes?&nbsp; Come, come, this won't do.



<P>Because you are so sly about it yourself, you think nobody



<P>else has any senses; but it is no such thing, I can tell you,



<P>for it has been known all over town this ever so long.



<P>I tell every body of it and so does Charlotte."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Indeed, Ma'am," said Elinor, very seriously,



<P>"you are mistaken.&nbsp; Indeed, you are doing a very unkind thing



<P>in spreading the report, and you will find that you have



<P>though you will not believe me now."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Jennings laughed again, but Elinor had

not



<P>spirits to say more, and eager at all events to know



<P>what Willoughby had written, hurried away to their room,



<P>where, on opening the door, she saw Marianne stretched on



<P>the bed, almost choked by grief, one letter in her hand,



<P>and two or three others laying by her.&nbsp; Elinor drew near,



<P>but without saying a word; and seating herself on the bed,



<P>took her hand, kissed her affectionately several times,



<P>and then gave way to a burst of tears, which at first



<P>was scarcely less violent than Marianne's. The latter,



<P>though unable to speak, seemed to feel all the tenderness



<P>of this behaviour, and after some time thus spent in



<P>joint affliction, she put all the letters into Elinor's hands;



<P>and then covering her face with her handkerchief,



<P>almost screamed with agony.&nbsp; Elinor, who knew that such grief,



<P>shocking as it was to witness it, must have its course,



<P>watched by her till this excess of suffering had somewhat



<P>spent itself, and then turning eagerly to Willoughby's letter,



<P>read as follows:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

"Bond Street, January.



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "MY DEAR MADAM,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have just

had the honour of receiving your



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; letter, for which I beg to return my sincere



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; acknowledgments.&nbsp; I am much concerned

to find there



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; was anything in my behaviour last night that

did



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; not meet your approbation; and though I am

quite at



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; a loss to discover in what point I could be

so



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; unfortunate as to offend you, I entreat your



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; forgiveness of what I can assure you to have

been



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; perfectly unintentional.&nbsp; I shall never

reflect on



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; my former acquaintance with your family in

Devonshire



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; without the most grateful pleasure, and flatter



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; myself it will not be broken by any mistake

or



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; misapprehension of my actions.&nbsp; My esteem

for your



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; whole family is very sincere; but if I have

been so



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; unfortunate as to give rise to a belief of

more than



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; I felt, or meant to express, I shall reproach

myself



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; for not having been more guarded in my professions



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; of that esteem.&nbsp; That I should ever have

meant more



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; you will allow to be impossible, when you understand



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; that my affections have been long engaged elsewhere,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; and it will not be many weeks, I believe, before



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; this engagement is fulfilled.&nbsp; It is with

great



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; regret that I obey your commands in returning

the



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; letters with which I have been honoured from

you,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; and the lock of hair, which you so obligingly

bestowed



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; on me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am, dear Madam,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

"Your most obedient



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

"humble servant,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

"JOHN WILLOUGHBY."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; With what indignation such a letter as this

must



<P>be read by Miss Dashwood, may be imagined.&nbsp; Though aware,



<P>before she began it, that it must bring a confession



<P>of his inconstancy, and confirm their separation for ever,



<P>she was not aware that such language could be suffered



<P>to announce it; nor could she have supposed Willoughby



<P>capable of departing so far from the appearance of every



<P>honourable and delicate feeling--so far from the common



<P>decorum of a gentleman, as to send a letter so impudently



<P>cruel: a letter which, instead of bringing with his desire



<P>of a release any professions of regret, acknowledged no



<P>breach of faith, denied all peculiar affection whatever--



<P>a letter of which every line was an insult, and which



<P>proclaimed its writer to be deep in hardened villainy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She paused over it for some time with indignant



<P>astonishment; then read it again and again; but every



<P>perusal only served to increase her abhorrence of the man,



<P>and so bitter were her feelings against him, that she



<P>dared not trust herself to speak, lest she might wound



<P>Marianne still deeper by treating their disengagement,



<P>not as a loss to her of any possible good but as an



<P>escape from the worst and most irremediable of all



<P>evils, a connection, for life, with an unprincipled man,



<P>as a deliverance the most real, a blessing the most important.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In her earnest meditations on the contents

of the letter,



<P>on the depravity of that mind which could dictate it,



<P>and probably, on the very different mind of a very different



<P>person, who had no other connection whatever with the affair



<P>than what her heart gave him with every thing that passed,



<P>Elinor forgot the immediate distress of her sister,



<P>forgot that she had three letters on her lap yet unread,



<P>and so entirely forgot how long she had been in the room,



<P>that when on hearing a carriage drive up to the door,



<P>she went to the window to see who could be coming so



<P>unreasonably early, she was all astonishment to perceive



<P>Mrs. Jennings's chariot, which she knew had not been



<P>ordered till one.&nbsp; Determined not to quit Marianne,



<P>though hopeless of contributing, at present, to her ease,



<P>she hurried away to excuse herself from attending



<P>Mrs. Jennings, on account of her sister being indisposed.



<P>Mrs. Jennings, with a thoroughly good-humoured concern



<P>for its cause, admitted the excuse most readily, and Elinor,



<P>after seeing her safe off, returned to Marianne, whom she



<P>found attempting to rise from the bed, and whom she reached



<P>just in time to prevent her from falling on the floor,



<P>faint and giddy from a long want of proper rest and food;



<P>for it was many days since she had any appetite, and many



<P>nights since she had really slept; and now, when her



<P>mind was no longer supported by the fever of suspense,



<P>the consequence of all this was felt in an aching head,



<P>a weakened stomach, and a general nervous faintness.



<P>A glass of wine, which Elinor procured for her directly,



<P>made her more comfortable, and she was at last able to express



<P>some sense of her kindness, by saying,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Poor Elinor! how unhappy I make you!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I only wish," replied her sister, "there were



<P>any thing I COULD do, which might be of comfort to you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This, as every thing else would have been,



<P>was too much for Marianne, who could only exclaim,



<P>in the anguish of her heart, "Oh! Elinor, I am miserable,



<P>indeed," before her voice was entirely lost in sobs.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor could no longer witness this torrent



<P>of unresisted grief in silence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Exert yourself, dear Marianne," she cried,



<P>"if you would not kill yourself and all who love you.



<P>Think of your mother; think of her misery while YOU suffer:



<P>for her sake you must exert yourself."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I cannot, I cannot," cried Marianne; "leave

me,



<P>leave me, if I distress you; leave me, hate me, forget me!



<P>but do not torture me so.&nbsp; Oh! how easy for those,



<P>who have no sorrow of their own to talk of exertion!&nbsp; Happy,



<P>happy Elinor, YOU cannot have an idea of what I suffer."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Do you call ME happy, Marianne?&nbsp; Ah!

if you knew!--And



<P>can you believe me to be so, while I see you so wretched!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Forgive me, forgive me," throwing her arms

round



<P>her sister's neck; "I know you feel for me; I know what



<P>a heart you have; but yet you are--you must be happy;



<P>Edward loves you--what, oh what, can do away such happiness



<P>as that?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Many, many circumstances," said Elinor, solemnly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, no, no," cried Marianne wildly, "he loves

you,



<P>and only you.&nbsp; You CAN have no grief."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I can have no pleasure while I see you in

this state."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And you will never see me otherwise.&nbsp;

Mine is



<P>a misery which nothing can do away."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You must not talk so, Marianne.&nbsp; Have

you no



<P>comforts? no friends?&nbsp; Is your loss such as leaves



<P>no opening for consolation?&nbsp; Much as you suffer now,



<P>think of what you would have suffered if the discovery



<P>of his character had been delayed to a later period--



<P>if your engagement had been carried on for months and months,



<P>as it might have been, before he chose to put an end to it.



<P>Every additional day of unhappy confidence, on your side,



<P>would have made the blow more dreadful."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Engagement!" cried Marianne, "there has been



<P>no engagement."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No engagement!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, he is not so unworthy as you believe him.



<P>He has broken no faith with me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But he told you that he loved you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes--no--never absolutely.&nbsp; It was every

day implied,



<P>but never professedly declared.&nbsp; Sometimes I thought it



<P>had been--but it never was."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yet you wrote to him?"--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes--could that be wrong after all that had

passed?--



<P>But I cannot talk."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor said no more, and turning again to the

three



<P>letters which now raised a much stronger curiosity



<P>than before, directly ran over the contents of all.



<P>The first, which was what her sister had sent him



<P>on their arrival in town, was to this effect.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

Berkeley Street, January.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How surprised

you will be, Willoughby, on



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; receiving this; and I think you will feel something



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; more than surprise, when you know that I am

in town.



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; An opportunity of coming hither, though with

Mrs.



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Jennings, was a temptation we could not resist.



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; I wish you may receive this in time to come

here



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; to-night, but I will not depend on it.&nbsp;

At any rate



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; I shall expect you to-morrow. For the present,

adieu.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

"M.D."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Her second note, which had been written on

the morning



<P>after the dance at the Middletons', was in these words:--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I cannot express

my disappointment in having



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; missed you the day before yesterday, nor my

astonishment



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; at not having received any answer to a note

which



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; I sent you above a week ago.&nbsp; I have been

expecting



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; to hear from you, and still more to see you,

every



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; hour of the day.&nbsp; Pray call again as soon

as possible,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; and explain the reason of my having expected

this



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; in vain.&nbsp; You had better come earlier

another time,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; because we are generally out by one.&nbsp;

We were last



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; night at Lady Middleton's, where there was

a dance.



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; I have been told that you were asked to be

of the



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; party.&nbsp; But could it be so?&nbsp; You

must be very much



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; altered indeed since we parted, if that could

be



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; the case, and you not there.&nbsp; But I will

not suppose



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; this possible, and I hope very soon to receive

your



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; personal assurance of its being otherwise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

"M.D."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The contents of her last note to him were these:--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What am I to

imagine, Willoughby, by your



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; behaviour last night?&nbsp; Again I demand

an explanation



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; of it.&nbsp; I was prepared to meet you with

the pleasure



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; which our separation naturally produced, with

the



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; familiarity which our intimacy at Barton appeared



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; to me to justify.&nbsp; I was repulsed indeed!

I have



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; passed a wretched night in endeavouring to

excuse



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; a conduct which can scarcely be called less

than



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; insulting; but though I have not yet been able

to



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; form any reasonable apology for your behaviour,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; I am perfectly ready to hear your justification

of



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; it.&nbsp; You have perhaps been misinformed,

or purposely



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; deceived, in something concerning me, which

may have



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; lowered me in your opinion.&nbsp; Tell me what

it is,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; explain the grounds on which you acted, and

I shall



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; be satisfied, in being able to satisfy you.&nbsp;

It



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; would grieve me indeed to be obliged to think

ill



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; of you; but if I am to do it, if I am to learn

that



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; you are not what we have hitherto believed

you, that



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; your regard for us all was insincere, that

your



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; behaviour to me was intended only to deceive,

let



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; it be told as soon as possible.&nbsp; My feelings

are at



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; present in a state of dreadful indecision;

I wish



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; to acquit you, but certainty on either side

will be



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; ease to what I now suffer.&nbsp; If your sentiments

are



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; no longer what they were, you will return my

notes,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; and the lock of my hair which is in your possession.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

"M.D."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; That such letters, so full of affection and

confidence,



<P>could have been so answered, Elinor, for Willoughby's sake,



<P>would have been unwilling to believe.&nbsp; But her condemnation



<P>of him did not blind her to the impropriety of their



<P>having been written at all; and she was silently grieving



<P>over the imprudence which had hazarded such unsolicited



<P>proofs of tenderness, not warranted by anything preceding,



<P>and most severely condemned by the event, when Marianne,



<P>perceiving that she had finished the letters, observed to



<P>her that they contained nothing but what any one would



<P>have written in the same situation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I felt myself," she added, "to be as solemnly



<P>engaged to him, as if the strictest legal covenant



<P>had bound us to each other."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I can believe it," said Elinor; "but unfortunately



<P>he did not feel the same."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "He DID feel the same, Elinor--for weeks and

weeks he



<P>felt it.&nbsp; I know he did.&nbsp; Whatever may have changed him now,

(and



<P>nothing but the blackest art employed against me can have done



<P>it), I was once as dear to him as my own soul could wish.



<P>This lock of hair, which now he can so readily give up,



<P>was begged of me with the most earnest supplication.



<P>Had you seen his look, his manner, had you heard his voice



<P>at that moment!&nbsp; Have you forgot the last evening of our



<P>being together at Barton?&nbsp; The morning that we parted



<P>too!&nbsp; When he told me that it might be many weeks before



<P>we met again--his distress--can I ever forget his distress?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; For a moment or two she could say no more;



<P>but when this emotion had passed away, she added,



<P>in a firmer tone,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Elinor, I have been cruelly used; but not

by Willoughby."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Dearest Marianne, who but himself?&nbsp; By

whom can he



<P>have been instigated?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "By all the world, rather than by his own heart.



<P>I could rather believe every creature of my acquaintance



<P>leagued together to ruin me in his opinion, than believe



<P>his nature capable of such cruelty.&nbsp; This woman of whom he



<P>writes--whoever she be--or any one, in short, but your own



<P>dear self, mama, and Edward, may have been so barbarous



<P>to bely me.&nbsp; Beyond you three, is there a creature



<P>in the world whom I would not rather suspect of evil



<P>than Willoughby, whose heart I know so well?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor would not contend, and only replied,



<P>"Whoever may have been so detestably your enemy, let them



<P>be cheated of their malignant triumph, my dear sister,



<P>by seeing how nobly the consciousness of your own



<P>innocence and good intentions supports your spirits.



<P>It is a reasonable and laudable pride which resists



<P>such malevolence."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, no," cried Marianne, "misery such as mine

has



<P>no pride.&nbsp; I care not who knows that I am wretched.



<P>The triumph of seeing me so may be open to all the world.



<P>Elinor, Elinor, they who suffer little may be proud and



<P>independent as they like--may resist insult, or return



<P>mortification--but I cannot.&nbsp; I must feel--I must be



<P>wretched--and they are welcome to enjoy the consciousness



<P>of it that can."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But for my mother's sake and mine--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I would do more than for my own.&nbsp; But

to appear



<P>happy when I am so miserable--Oh! who can require it?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Again they were both silent.&nbsp; Elinor was

employed



<P>in walking thoughtfully from the fire to the window,



<P>from the window to the fire, without knowing that she



<P>received warmth from one, or discerning objects through



<P>the other; and Marianne, seated at the foot of the bed,



<P>with her head leaning against one of its posts,



<P>again took up Willoughby's letter, and, after shuddering



<P>over every sentence, exclaimed--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is too much! Oh, Willoughby, Willoughby,

could this



<P>be yours!&nbsp; Cruel, cruel--nothing can acquit you.&nbsp; Elinor,



<P>nothing can.&nbsp; Whatever he might have heard against me--



<P>ought he not to have suspended his belief? ought he not to



<P>have told me of it, to have given me the power of clearing



<P>myself? 'The lock of hair, (repeating it from the letter,)



<P>which you so obligingly bestowed on me'--That is unpardonable.



<P>Willoughby, where was your heart when you wrote those words?



<P>Oh, barbarously insolent!--Elinor, can he be justified?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, Marianne, in no possible way."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And yet this woman--who knows what her art

may



<P>have been?--how long it may have been premeditated,



<P>and how deeply contrived by her!--Who is she?--Who can



<P>she be?--Whom did I ever hear him talk of as young and



<P>attractive among his female acquaintance?--Oh! no one,



<P>no one--he talked to me only of myself."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Another pause ensued; Marianne was greatly

agitated,



<P>and it ended thus.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Elinor, I must go home.&nbsp; I must go and

comfort mama.



<P>Can not we be gone to-morrow?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "To-morrow, Marianne!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes, why should I stay here? I came only for



<P>Willoughby's sake--and now who cares for me? Who regards me?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It would be impossible to go to-morrow. We

owe



<P>Mrs. Jennings much more than civility; and civility of



<P>the commonest kind must prevent such a hasty removal as that."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well then, another day or two, perhaps; but

I cannot



<P>stay here long, I cannot stay to endure the questions



<P>and remarks of all these people.&nbsp; The Middletons and



<P>Palmers--how am I to bear their pity?&nbsp; The pity of such



<P>a woman as Lady Middleton!&nbsp; Oh, what would HE say to that!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor advised her to lie down again, and for

a



<P>moment she did so; but no attitude could give her ease;



<P>and in restless pain of mind and body she moved from one



<P>posture to another, till growing more and more hysterical,



<P>her sister could with difficulty keep her on the bed at all,



<P>and for some time was fearful of being constrained to call



<P>for assistance.&nbsp; Some lavender drops, however, which she



<P>was at length persuaded to take, were of use; and from



<P>that time till Mrs. Jennings returned, she continued



<P>on the bed quiet and motionless.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter01</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

<B><I><FONT SIZE=+3>SENSE AND SENSIBILITY</FONT></I></B>

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

<FONT SIZE=+2>by Jane Austen</FONT>

<BR><FONT SIZE=+2></FONT>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 1</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The family of Dashwood had long been settled

in Sussex.



<P>Their estate was large, and their residence was at Norland Park,



<P>in the centre of their property, where, for many generations,



<P>they had lived in so respectable a manner as to engage



<P>the general good opinion of their surrounding acquaintance.



<P>The late owner of this estate was a single man, who lived



<P>to a very advanced age, and who for many years of his life,



<P>had a constant companion and housekeeper in his sister.



<P>But her death, which happened ten years before his own,



<P>produced a great alteration in his home; for to supply



<P>her loss, he invited and received into his house the family



<P>of his nephew Mr. Henry Dashwood, the legal inheritor



<P>of the Norland estate, and the person to whom he intended



<P>to bequeath it.&nbsp; In the society of his nephew and niece,



<P>and their children, the old Gentleman's days were



<P>comfortably spent.&nbsp; His attachment to them all increased.



<P>The constant attention of Mr. and Mrs. Henry Dashwood



<P>to his wishes, which proceeded not merely from interest,



<P>but from goodness of heart, gave him every degree of solid



<P>comfort which his age could receive; and the cheerfulness



<P>of the children added a relish to his existence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; By a former marriage, Mr. Henry Dashwood had

one



<P>son: by his present lady, three daughters.&nbsp; The son,



<P>a steady respectable young man, was amply provided



<P>for by the fortune of his mother, which had been large,



<P>and half of which devolved on him on his coming of age.



<P>By his own marriage, likewise, which happened soon afterwards,



<P>he added to his wealth.&nbsp; To him therefore the succession



<P>to the Norland estate was not so really important as to



<P>his sisters; for their fortune, independent of what might



<P>arise to them from their father's inheriting that property,



<P>could be but small.&nbsp; Their mother had nothing, and their



<P>father only seven thousand pounds in his own disposal;



<P>for the remaining moiety of his first wife's fortune was



<P>also secured to her child, and he had only a life-interest



<P>in it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The old gentleman died: his will was read,

and



<P>like almost every other will, gave as much disappointment



<P>as pleasure.&nbsp; He was neither so unjust, nor so ungrateful,



<P>as to leave his estate from his nephew;--but he left it to him



<P>on such terms as destroyed half the value of the bequest.



<P>Mr. Dashwood had wished for it more for the sake of his



<P>wife and daughters than for himself or his son;--but to



<P>his son, and his son's son, a child of four years old,



<P>it was secured, in such a way, as to leave to himself



<P>no power of providing for those who were most dear



<P>to him, and who most needed a provision by any charge



<P>on the estate, or by any sale of its valuable woods.



<P>The whole was tied up for the benefit of this child, who,



<P>in occasional visits with his father and mother at Norland,



<P>had so far gained on the affections of his uncle,



<P>by such attractions as are by no means unusual in children



<P>of two or three years old; an imperfect articulation,



<P>an earnest desire of having his own way, many cunning tricks,



<P>and a great deal of noise, as to outweigh all the value



<P>of all the attention which, for years, he had received



<P>from his niece and her daughters.&nbsp; He meant not to



<P>be unkind, however, and, as a mark of his affection



<P>for the three girls, he left them a thousand pounds a-piece.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mr. Dashwood's disappointment was, at first,

severe;



<P>but his temper was cheerful and sanguine; and he might



<P>reasonably hope to live many years, and by living economically,



<P>lay by a considerable sum from the produce of an estate



<P>already large, and capable of almost immediate improvement.



<P>But the fortune, which had been so tardy in coming, was his



<P>only one twelvemonth.&nbsp; He survived his uncle no longer;



<P>and ten thousand pounds, including the late legacies,



<P>was all that remained for his widow and daughters.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; His son was sent for as soon as his danger

was known,



<P>and to him Mr. Dashwood recommended, with all the strength



<P>and urgency which illness could command, the interest



<P>of his mother-in-law and sisters.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mr. John Dashwood had not the strong feelings

of the



<P>rest of the family; but he was affected by a recommendation



<P>of such a nature at such a time, and he promised to do



<P>every thing in his power to make them comfortable.



<P>His father was rendered easy by such an assurance,



<P>and Mr. John Dashwood had then leisure to consider how



<P>much there might prudently be in his power to do for them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He was not an ill-disposed young man, unless

to



<P>be rather cold hearted and rather selfish is to be



<P>ill-disposed: but he was, in general, well respected;



<P>for he conducted himself with propriety in the discharge



<P>of his ordinary duties.&nbsp; Had he married a more amiable woman,



<P>he might have been made still more respectable than he



<P>was:--he might even have been made amiable himself; for he



<P>was very young when he married, and very fond of his wife.



<P>But Mrs. John Dashwood was a strong caricature of himself;--



<P>more narrow-minded and selfish.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When he gave his promise to his father, he

meditated



<P>within himself to increase the fortunes of his sisters



<P>by the present of a thousand pounds a-piece.&nbsp; He then



<P>really thought himself equal to it.&nbsp; The prospect of four



<P>thousand a-year, in addition to his present income,



<P>besides the remaining half of his own mother's fortune,



<P>warmed his heart, and made him feel capable of generosity.--



<P>"Yes, he would give them three thousand pounds: it would



<P>be liberal and handsome! It would be enough to make



<P>them completely easy.&nbsp; Three thousand pounds! he could



<P>spare so considerable a sum with little inconvenience."--



<P>He thought of it all day long, and for many days successively,



<P>and he did not repent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; No sooner was his father's funeral over, than

Mrs. John



<P>Dashwood, without sending any notice of her intention to her



<P>mother-in-law, arrived with her child and their attendants.



<P>No one could dispute her right to come; the house was



<P>her husband's from the moment of his father's decease;



<P>but the indelicacy of her conduct was so much the greater,



<P>and to a woman in Mrs. Dashwood's situation, with only



<P>common feelings, must have been highly unpleasing;--



<P>but in HER mind there was a sense of honor so keen,



<P>a generosity so romantic, that any offence of the kind,



<P>by whomsoever given or received, was to her a source



<P>of immoveable disgust.&nbsp; Mrs. John Dashwood had never



<P>been a favourite with any of her husband's family;



<P>but she had had no opportunity, till the present,



<P>of shewing them with how little attention to the comfort



<P>of other people she could act when occasion required it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; So acutely did Mrs. Dashwood feel this ungracious



<P>behaviour, and so earnestly did she despise her



<P>daughter-in-law for it, that, on the arrival of the latter,



<P>she would have quitted the house for ever, had not the



<P>entreaty of her eldest girl induced her first to reflect



<P>on the propriety of going, and her own tender love for all



<P>her three children determined her afterwards to stay,



<P>and for their sakes avoid a breach with their brother.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor, this eldest daughter, whose advice

was



<P>so effectual, possessed a strength of understanding,



<P>and coolness of judgment, which qualified her,



<P>though only nineteen, to be the counsellor of her mother,



<P>and enabled her frequently to counteract, to the advantage



<P>of them all, that eagerness of mind in Mrs. Dashwood



<P>which must generally have led to imprudence.&nbsp; She had



<P>an excellent heart;--her disposition was affectionate,



<P>and her feelings were strong; but she knew how to govern



<P>them: it was a knowledge which her mother had yet to learn;



<P>and which one of her sisters had resolved never to be taught.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne's abilities were, in many respects,



<P>quite equal to Elinor's. She was sensible and clever;



<P>but eager in everything: her sorrows, her joys, could have



<P>no moderation.&nbsp; She was generous, amiable, interesting: she



<P>was everything but prudent.&nbsp; The resemblance between



<P>her and her mother was strikingly great.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor saw, with concern, the excess of her



<P>sister's sensibility; but by Mrs. Dashwood it was valued



<P>and cherished.&nbsp; They encouraged each other now in the



<P>violence of their affliction.&nbsp; The agony of grief



<P>which overpowered them at first, was voluntarily renewed,



<P>was sought for, was created again and again.&nbsp; They gave



<P>themselves up wholly to their sorrow, seeking increase



<P>of wretchedness in every reflection that could afford it,



<P>and resolved against ever admitting consolation



<P>in future.&nbsp; Elinor, too, was deeply afflicted; but still



<P>she could struggle, she could exert herself.&nbsp; She could



<P>consult with her brother, could receive her sister-in-law



<P>on her arrival, and treat her with proper attention;



<P>and could strive to rouse her mother to similar exertion,



<P>and encourage her to similar forbearance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Margaret, the other sister, was a good-humored,



<P>well-disposed girl; but as she had already imbibed



<P>a good deal of Marianne's romance, without having



<P>much of her sense, she did not, at thirteen, bid fair



<P>to equal her sisters at a more advanced period of life.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter15</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 15</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood's visit to Lady Middleton took

place



<P>the next day, and two of her daughters went with her;



<P>but Marianne excused herself from being of the party,



<P>under some trifling pretext of employment; and her mother,



<P>who concluded that a promise had been made by Willoughby



<P>the night before of calling on her while they were absent,



<P>was perfectly satisfied with her remaining at home.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; On their return from the park they found Willoughby's



<P>curricle and servant in waiting at the cottage,



<P>and Mrs. Dashwood was convinced that her conjecture



<P>had been just.&nbsp; So far it was all as she had foreseen;



<P>but on entering the house she beheld what no foresight



<P>had taught her to expect.&nbsp; They were no sooner in the



<P>passage than Marianne came hastily out of the parlour



<P>apparently in violent affliction, with her handkerchief



<P>at her eyes; and without noticing them ran up stairs.



<P>Surprised and alarmed they proceeded directly into the room



<P>she had just quitted, where they found only Willoughby,



<P>who was leaning against the mantel-piece with his back



<P>towards them.&nbsp; He turned round on their coming in,



<P>and his countenance shewed that he strongly partook



<P>of the emotion which over-powered Marianne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Is anything the matter with her?" cried Mrs.

Dashwood



<P>as she entered--"is she ill?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I hope not," he replied, trying to look cheerful;



<P>and with a forced smile presently added, "It is I who may



<P>rather expect to be ill--for I am now suffering under a



<P>very heavy disappointment!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Disappointment?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes, for I am unable to keep my engagement

with you.



<P>Mrs. Smith has this morning exercised the privilege



<P>of riches upon a poor dependent cousin, by sending me on



<P>business to London.&nbsp; I have just received my dispatches,



<P>and taken my farewell of Allenham; and by way of exhilaration



<P>I am now come to take my farewell of you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "To London!--and are you going this morning?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Almost this moment."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "This is very unfortunate.&nbsp; But Mrs. Smith

must



<P>be obliged;--and her business will not detain you from



<P>us long I hope."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He coloured as he replied, "You are very kind,

but I



<P>have no idea of returning into Devonshire immediately.



<P>My visits to Mrs. Smith are never repeated within



<P>the twelvemonth."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And is Mrs. Smith your only friend?&nbsp;

Is Allenham the only



<P>house in the neighbourhood to which you will be welcome?



<P>For shame, Willoughby, can you wait for an invitation here?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; His colour increased; and with his eyes fixed



<P>on the ground he only replied, "You are too good."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood looked at Elinor with surprise.



<P>Elinor felt equal amazement.&nbsp; For a few moments every one



<P>was silent.&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood first spoke.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have only to add, my dear Willoughby, that

at



<P>Barton cottage you will always be welcome; for I will not



<P>press you to return here immediately, because you only



<P>can judge how far THAT might be pleasing to Mrs. Smith;



<P>and on this head I shall be no more disposed to question



<P>your judgment than to doubt your inclination."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "My engagements at present," replied Willoughby,



<P>confusedly, "are of such a nature--that--I dare not flatter myself"--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He stopt.&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood was too much

astonished



<P>to speak, and another pause succeeded.&nbsp; This was broken



<P>by Willoughby, who said with a faint smile, "It is folly



<P>to linger in this manner.&nbsp; I will not torment myself



<P>any longer by remaining among friends whose society



<P>it is impossible for me now to enjoy."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He then hastily took leave of them all and

left



<P>the room.&nbsp; They saw him step into his carriage,



<P>and in a minute it was out of sight.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood felt too much for speech, and

instantly



<P>quitted the parlour to give way in solitude to the concern



<P>and alarm which this sudden departure occasioned.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor's uneasiness was at least equal to her

mother's.



<P>She thought of what had just passed with anxiety and distrust.



<P>Willoughby's behaviour in taking leave of them, his embarrassment,



<P>and affectation of cheerfulness, and, above all, his unwillingness



<P>to accept her mother's invitation, a backwardness so unlike a lover,



<P>so unlike himself, greatly disturbed her.&nbsp; One moment she feared



<P>that no serious design had ever been formed on his side; and the



<P>next that some unfortunate quarrel had taken place between him and



<P>her sister;--the distress in which Marianne had quitted the room



<P>was such as a serious quarrel could most reasonably account for,



<P>though when she considered what Marianne's love for him was,



<P>a quarrel seemed almost impossible.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; But whatever might be the particulars of their

separation,



<P>her sister's affliction was indubitable; and she thought



<P>with the tenderest compassion of that violent sorrow



<P>which Marianne was in all probability not merely giving



<P>way to as a relief, but feeding and encouraging as a duty.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In about half an hour her mother returned,

and though



<P>her eyes were red, her countenance was not uncheerful.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Our dear Willoughby is now some miles from

Barton, Elinor,"



<P>said she, as she sat down to work, "and with how heavy a heart



<P>does he travel?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is all very strange.&nbsp; So suddenly

to be gone! It



<P>seems but the work of a moment.&nbsp; And last night he was



<P>with us so happy, so cheerful, so affectionate?&nbsp; And now,



<P>after only ten minutes notice--Gone too without intending



<P>to return!--Something more than what be owned to us must



<P>have happened.&nbsp; He did not speak, he did not behave



<P>like himself.&nbsp; YOU must have seen the difference as well as I.



<P>What can it be?&nbsp; Can they have quarrelled?&nbsp; Why else should

he



<P>have shewn such unwillingness to accept your invitation here?"--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It was not inclination that he wanted, Elinor;

I could



<P>plainly see THAT.&nbsp; He had not the power of accepting it.



<P>I have thought it all over I assure you, and I can



<P>perfectly account for every thing that at first seemed



<P>strange to me as well as to you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Can you, indeed!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes. I have explained it to myself in the

most



<P>satisfactory way;--but you, Elinor, who love to doubt



<P>where you can--it will not satisfy YOU, I know; but you



<P>shall not talk ME out of my trust in it.&nbsp; I am persuaded



<P>that Mrs. Smith suspects his regard for Marianne,



<P>disapproves of it, (perhaps because she has other views



<P>for him,) and on that account is eager to get him away;--



<P>and that the business which she sends him off to transact



<P>is invented as an excuse to dismiss him.&nbsp; This is what I



<P>believe to have happened.&nbsp; He is, moreover, aware that she



<P>DOES disapprove the connection, he dares not therefore



<P>at present confess to her his engagement with Marianne,



<P>and he feels himself obliged, from his dependent situation,



<P>to give into her schemes, and absent himself from



<P>Devonshire for a while.&nbsp; You will tell me, I know,



<P>that this may or may NOT have happened; but I will listen



<P>to no cavil, unless you can point out any other method



<P>of understanding the affair as satisfactory at this.



<P>And now, Elinor, what have you to say?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Nothing, for you have anticipated my answer."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Then you would have told me, that it might

or might not



<P>have happened.&nbsp; Oh, Elinor, how incomprehensible are your



<P>feelings!&nbsp; You had rather take evil upon credit than good.



<P>You had rather look out for misery for Marianne, and guilt



<P>for poor Willoughby, than an apology for the latter.



<P>You are resolved to think him blameable, because he took



<P>leave of us with less affection than his usual behaviour



<P>has shewn.&nbsp; And is no allowance to be made for inadvertence,



<P>or for spirits depressed by recent disappointment?&nbsp; Are



<P>no probabilities to be accepted, merely because they



<P>are not certainties?&nbsp; Is nothing due to the man whom we



<P>have all such reason to love, and no reason in the world



<P>to think ill of?&nbsp; To the possibility of motives unanswerable



<P>in themselves, though unavoidably secret for a while?&nbsp; And,



<P>after all, what is it you suspect him of?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I can hardly tell myself.&nbsp; But suspicion

of



<P>something unpleasant is the inevitable consequence



<P>of such an alteration as we just witnessed in him.



<P>There is great truth, however, in what you have now urged



<P>of the allowances which ought to be made for him, and it



<P>is my wish to be candid in my judgment of every body.



<P>Willoughby may undoubtedly have very sufficient



<P>reasons for his conduct, and I will hope that he has.



<P>But it would have been more like Willoughby to acknowledge



<P>them at once.&nbsp; Secrecy may be advisable; but still I



<P>cannot help wondering at its being practiced by him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Do not blame him, however, for departing from



<P>his character, where the deviation is necessary.



<P>But you really do admit the justice of what I have said



<P>in his defence?--I am happy--and he is acquitted."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Not entirely.&nbsp; It may be proper to conceal

their



<P>engagement (if they ARE engaged) from Mrs. Smith--



<P>and if that is the case, it must be highly expedient



<P>for Willoughby to be but little in Devonshire at present.



<P>But this is no excuse for their concealing it from us."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Concealing it from us! my dear child, do you

accuse



<P>Willoughby and Marianne of concealment? This is strange



<P>indeed, when your eyes have been reproaching them every day



<P>for incautiousness."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I want no proof of their affection," said

Elinor;



<P>"but of their engagement I do."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am perfectly satisfied of both."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yet not a syllable has been said to you on

the



<P>subject, by either of them."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have not wanted syllables where actions

have



<P>spoken so plainly.&nbsp; Has not his behaviour to Marianne



<P>and to all of us, for at least the last fortnight,



<P>declared that he loved and considered her as his future wife,



<P>and that he felt for us the attachment of the nearest



<P>relation?&nbsp; Have we not perfectly understood each other?



<P>Has not my consent been daily asked by his looks, his manner,



<P>his attentive and affectionate respect?&nbsp; My Elinor,



<P>is it possible to doubt their engagement?&nbsp; How could



<P>such a thought occur to you?&nbsp; How is it to be supposed



<P>that Willoughby, persuaded as he must be of your



<P>sister's love, should leave her, and leave her perhaps



<P>for months, without telling her of his affection;--that



<P>they should part without a mutual exchange of confidence?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I confess," replied Elinor, "that every circumstance



<P>except ONE is in favour of their engagement;



<P>but that ONE is the total silence of both on the subject,



<P>and with me it almost outweighs every other."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How strange this is!&nbsp; You must think

wretchedly indeed



<P>of Willoughby, if, after all that has openly passed between them,



<P>you can doubt the nature of the terms on which they are together.



<P>Has he been acting a part in his behaviour to your sister



<P>all this time?&nbsp; Do you suppose him really indifferent to her?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, I cannot think that.&nbsp; He must and

does love her



<P>I am sure."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But with a strange kind of tenderness, if

he can



<P>leave her with such indifference, such carelessness



<P>of the future, as you attribute to him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You must remember, my dear mother, that I

have never



<P>considered this matter as certain.&nbsp; I have had my doubts,



<P>I confess; but they are fainter than they were, and they



<P>may soon be entirely done away.&nbsp; If we find they correspond,



<P>every fear of mine will be removed."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "A mighty concession indeed!&nbsp; If you were

to see



<P>them at the altar, you would suppose they were going to



<P>be married.&nbsp; Ungracious girl!&nbsp; But I require no such proof.



<P>Nothing in my opinion has ever passed to justify doubt;



<P>no secrecy has been attempted; all has been uniformly open



<P>and unreserved.&nbsp; You cannot doubt your sister's wishes.



<P>It must be Willoughby therefore whom you suspect.&nbsp; But why?



<P>Is he not a man of honour and feeling? Has there been any



<P>inconsistency on his side to create alarm? can he be deceitful?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I hope not, I believe not," cried Elinor.



<P>"I love Willoughby, sincerely love him; and suspicion of his



<P>integrity cannot be more painful to yourself than to me.



<P>It has been involuntary, and I will not encourage it.



<P>I was startled, I confess, by the alteration in his



<P>manners this morning;--he did not speak like himself,



<P>and did not return your kindness with any cordiality.



<P>But all this may be explained by such a situation of his



<P>affairs as you have supposed.&nbsp; He had just parted from



<P>my sister, had seen her leave him in the greatest affliction;



<P>and if he felt obliged, from a fear of offending Mrs. Smith,



<P>to resist the temptation of returning here soon, and yet



<P>aware that by declining your invitation, by saying



<P>that he was going away for some time, he should seem



<P>to act an ungenerous, a suspicious part by our family,



<P>be might well be embarrassed and disturbed.&nbsp; In such a case,



<P>a plain and open avowal of his difficulties would have been



<P>more to his honour I think, as well as more consistent



<P>with his general character;--but I will not raise objections



<P>against any one's conduct on so illiberal a foundation,



<P>as a difference in judgment from myself, or a deviation from



<P>what I may think right and consistent."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You speak very properly.&nbsp; Willoughby

certainly does



<P>not deserve to be suspected.&nbsp; Though WE have not known



<P>him long, he is no stranger in this part of the world;



<P>and who has ever spoken to his disadvantage?&nbsp; Had he been



<P>in a situation to act independently and marry immediately,



<P>it might have been odd that he should leave us without



<P>acknowledging everything to me at once: but this is not the case.



<P>It is an engagement in some respects not prosperously begun,



<P>for their marriage must be at a very uncertain distance;



<P>and even secrecy, as far as it can be observed, may now



<P>be very advisable."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They were interrupted by the entrance of Margaret;



<P>and Elinor was then at liberty to think over the representations



<P>of her mother, to acknowledge the probability of many,



<P>and hope for the justice of all.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They saw nothing of Marianne till dinner time,



<P>when she entered the room and took her place at the table



<P>without saying a word.&nbsp; Her eyes were red and swollen;



<P>and it seemed as if her tears were even then restrained



<P>with difficulty.&nbsp; She avoided the looks of them all,



<P>could neither eat nor speak, and after some time, on her



<P>mother's silently pressing her hand with tender compassion,



<P>her small degree of fortitude was quite overcome, she burst



<P>into tears and left the room.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This violent oppression of spirits continued

the



<P>whole evening.&nbsp; She was without any power, because she



<P>was without any desire of command over herself.



<P>The slightest mention of anything relative to Willoughby



<P>overpowered her in an instant; and though her family



<P>were most anxiously attentive to her comfort, it was



<P>impossible for them, if they spoke at all, to keep clear



<P>of every subject which her feelings connected with him.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 14</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The sudden termination of Colonel Brandon's

visit



<P>at the park, with his steadiness in concealing its cause,



<P>filled the mind, and raised the wonder of Mrs. Jennings



<P>for two or three days; she was a great wonderer, as every



<P>one must be who takes a very lively interest in all the



<P>comings and goings of all their acquaintance.&nbsp; She wondered,



<P>with little intermission what could be the reason of it;



<P>was sure there must be some bad news, and thought over



<P>every kind of distress that could have befallen him,



<P>with a fixed determination that he should not escape



<P>them all.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Something very melancholy must be the matter,



<P>I am sure," said she.&nbsp; "I could see it in his face.



<P>Poor man!&nbsp; I am afraid his circumstances may be bad.



<P>The estate at Delaford was never reckoned more than two thousand



<P>a year, and his brother left everything sadly involved.



<P>I do think he must have been sent for about money matters,



<P>for what else can it be?&nbsp; I wonder whether it is so.



<P>I would give anything to know the truth of it.&nbsp; Perhaps it



<P>is about Miss Williams and, by the bye, I dare say it is,



<P>because he looked so conscious when I mentioned her.



<P>May be she is ill in town; nothing in the world more likely,



<P>for I have a notion she is always rather sickly.



<P>I would lay any wager it is about Miss Williams.



<P>It is not so very likely he should be distressed in



<P>his circumstances NOW, for he is a very prudent man,



<P>and to be sure must have cleared the estate by this time.



<P>I wonder what it can be!&nbsp; May be his sister is worse



<P>at Avignon, and has sent for him over.&nbsp; His setting off



<P>in such a hurry seems very like it.&nbsp; Well, I wish him out



<P>of all his trouble with all my heart, and a good wife into



<P>the bargain."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; So wondered, so talked Mrs. Jennings.&nbsp;

Her opinion



<P>varying with every fresh conjecture, and all seeming



<P>equally probable as they arose.&nbsp; Elinor, though she felt



<P>really interested in the welfare of Colonel Brandon,



<P>could not bestow all the wonder on his going so suddenly



<P>away, which Mrs. Jennings was desirous of her feeling;



<P>for besides that the circumstance did not in her opinion



<P>justify such lasting amazement or variety of speculation,



<P>her wonder was otherwise disposed of.&nbsp; It was engossed



<P>by the extraordinary silence of her sister and Willoughby



<P>on the subject, which they must know to be peculiarly



<P>interesting to them all.&nbsp; As this silence continued,



<P>every day made it appear more strange and more incompatible



<P>with the disposition of both.&nbsp; Why they should not openly



<P>acknowledge to her mother and herself, what their constant



<P>behaviour to each other declared to have taken place,



<P>Elinor could not imagine.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She could easily conceive that marriage might

not



<P>be immediately in their power; for though Willoughby



<P>was independent, there was no reason to believe him rich.



<P>His estate had been rated by Sir John at about six or seven



<P>hundred a year; but he lived at an expense to which that income



<P>could hardly be equal, and he had himself often complained



<P>of his poverty.&nbsp; But for this strange kind of secrecy



<P>maintained by them relative to their engagement, which



<P>in fact concealed nothing at all, she could not account;



<P>and it was so wholly contradictory to their general



<P>opinions and practice, that a doubt sometimes entered



<P>her mind of their being really engaged, and this doubt



<P>was enough to prevent her making any inquiry of Marianne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Nothing could be more expressive of attachment



<P>to them all, than Willoughby's behaviour.&nbsp; To Marianne



<P>it had all the distinguishing tenderness which a lover's



<P>heart could give, and to the rest of the family it was the



<P>affectionate attention of a son and a brother.&nbsp; The cottage



<P>seemed to be considered and loved by him as his home;



<P>many more of his hours were spent there than at Allenham;



<P>and if no general engagement collected them at the park,



<P>the exercise which called him out in the morning was



<P>almost certain of ending there, where the rest of the day



<P>was spent by himself at the side of Marianne, and by his



<P>favourite pointer at her feet.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; One evening in particular, about a week after



<P>Colonel Brandon left the country, his heart seemed



<P>more than usually open to every feeling of attachment



<P>to the objects around him; and on Mrs. Dashwood's



<P>happening to mention her design of improving the cottage



<P>in the spring, he warmly opposed every alteration



<P>of a place which affection had established as perfect with him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What!" he exclaimed--"Improve this dear cottage!



<P>No. THAT I will never consent to.&nbsp; Not a stone must



<P>be added to its walls, not an inch to its size,



<P>if my feelings are regarded."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Do not be alarmed," said Miss Dashwood,



<P>"nothing of the kind will be done; for my mother



<P>will never have money enough to attempt it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am heartily glad of it", he cried.&nbsp;

"May she



<P>always be poor, if she can employ her riches no better."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Thank you, Willoughby.&nbsp; But you may be

assured that I



<P>would not sacrifice one sentiment of local attachment



<P>of yours, or of any one whom I loved, for all the improvements



<P>in the world.&nbsp; Depend upon it that whatever unemployed



<P>sum may remain, when I make up my accounts in the spring,



<P>I would even rather lay it uselessly by than dispose



<P>of it in a manner so painful to you.&nbsp; But are you really



<P>so attached to this place as to see no defect in it?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I am," said he.&nbsp; "To me it is faultless.&nbsp;

Nay, more,



<P>I consider it as the only form of building in which happiness



<P>is attainable, and were I rich enough I would instantly pull



<P>Combe down, and build it up again in the exact plan of this cottage."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "With dark narrow stairs and a kitchen that

smokes,



<P>I suppose," said Elinor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes," cried he in the same eager tone, "with

all



<P>and every thing belonging to it;--in no one convenience



<P>or INconvenience about it, should the least variation



<P>be perceptible.&nbsp; Then, and then only, under such a roof, I



<P>might perhaps be as happy at Combe as I have been at Barton."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I flatter myself," replied Elinor, "that even

under



<P>the disadvantage of better rooms and a broader staircase,



<P>you will hereafter find your own house as faultless as you



<P>now do this."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "There certainly are circumstances," said Willoughby,



<P>"which might greatly endear it to me; but this place will



<P>always have one claim of my affection, which no other can



<P>possibly share."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood looked with pleasure at Marianne,



<P>whose fine eyes were fixed so expressively on Willoughby,



<P>as plainly denoted how well she understood him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How often did I wish," added he, "when I was

at



<P>Allenham this time twelvemonth, that Barton cottage were



<P>inhabited!&nbsp; I never passed within view of it without admiring



<P>its situation, and grieving that no one should live in it.



<P>How little did I then think that the very first news



<P>I should hear from Mrs. Smith, when I next came into



<P>the country, would be that Barton cottage was taken: and I



<P>felt an immediate satisfaction and interest in the event,



<P>which nothing but a kind of prescience of what happiness I



<P>should experience from it, can account for.&nbsp; Must it not have



<P>been so, Marianne?" speaking to her in a lowered voice.



<P>Then continuing his former tone, he said, "And yet this



<P>house you would spoil, Mrs. Dashwood?&nbsp; You would rob it



<P>of its simplicity by imaginary improvement! and this dear



<P>parlour in which our acquaintance first began, and in which



<P>so many happy hours have been since spent by us together,



<P>you would degrade to the condition of a common entrance,



<P>and every body would be eager to pass through the room



<P>which has hitherto contained within itself more real



<P>accommodation and comfort than any other apartment of



<P>the handsomest dimensions in the world could possibly afford."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood again assured him that no alteration



<P>of the kind should be attempted.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You are a good woman," he warmly replied.



<P>"Your promise makes me easy.&nbsp; Extend it a little farther,



<P>and it will make me happy.&nbsp; Tell me that not only your



<P>house will remain the same, but that I shall ever find



<P>you and yours as unchanged as your dwelling; and that you



<P>will always consider me with the kindness which has made



<P>everything belonging to you so dear to me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The promise was readily given, and Willoughby's



<P>behaviour during the whole of the evening declared



<P>at once his affection and happiness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Shall we see you tomorrow to dinner?" said

Mrs. Dashwood,



<P>when he was leaving them.&nbsp; "I do not ask you to come in



<P>the morning, for we must walk to the park, to call on Lady Middleton."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He engaged to be with them by four o'clock.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">
