<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 28</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Nothing occurred during the next three or four

days,



<P>to make Elinor regret what she had done, in applying



<P>to her mother; for Willoughby neither came nor wrote.



<P>They were engaged about the end of that time to attend



<P>Lady Middleton to a party, from which Mrs. Jennings was



<P>kept away by the indisposition of her youngest daughter;



<P>and for this party, Marianne, wholly dispirited,



<P>careless of her appearance, and seeming equally indifferent



<P>whether she went or staid, prepared, without one look



<P>of hope or one expression of pleasure.&nbsp; She sat by the



<P>drawing-room fire after tea, till the moment of Lady



<P>Middleton's arrival, without once stirring from her seat,



<P>or altering her attitude, lost in her own thoughts,



<P>and insensible of her sister's presence; and when at



<P>last they were told that Lady Middleton waited for them



<P>at the door, she started as if she had forgotten that



<P>any one was expected.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They arrived in due time at the place of destination,



<P>and as soon as the string of carriages before them



<P>would allow, alighted, ascended the stairs, heard their



<P>names announced from one landing-place to another in an



<P>audible voice, and entered a room splendidly lit up,



<P>quite full of company, and insufferably hot.&nbsp; When they had



<P>paid their tribute of politeness by curtsying to the lady



<P>of the house, they were permitted to mingle in the crowd,



<P>and take their share of the heat and inconvenience, to



<P>which their arrival must necessarily add.&nbsp; After some time



<P>spent in saying little or doing less, Lady Middleton sat



<P>down to Cassino, and as Marianne was not in spirits for



<P>moving about, she and Elinor luckily succeeding to chairs,



<P>placed themselves at no great distance from the table.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They had not remained in this manner long,

before Elinor



<P>perceived Willoughby, standing within a few yards



<P>of them, in earnest conversation with a very fashionable



<P>looking young woman.&nbsp; She soon caught his eye, and he



<P>immediately bowed, but without attempting to speak to her,



<P>or to approach Marianne, though he could not but see her;



<P>and then continued his discourse with the same lady.



<P>Elinor turned involuntarily to Marianne, to see whether



<P>it could be unobserved by her.&nbsp; At that moment she first



<P>perceived him, and her whole countenance glowing with



<P>sudden delight, she would have moved towards him instantly,



<P>had not her sister caught hold of her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Good heavens!" she exclaimed, "he is there--he



<P>is there--Oh! why does he not look at me? why cannot



<P>I speak to him?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Pray, pray be composed," cried Elinor, "and

do



<P>not betray what you feel to every body present.



<P>Perhaps he has not observed you yet."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This however was more than she could believe

herself;



<P>and to be composed at such a moment was not only beyond



<P>the reach of Marianne, it was beyond her wish.&nbsp; She sat



<P>in an agony of impatience which affected every feature.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; At last he turned round again, and regarded

them both;



<P>she started up, and pronouncing his name in a tone



<P>of affection, held out her hand to him.&nbsp; He approached,



<P>and addressing himself rather to Elinor than Marianne,



<P>as if wishing to avoid her eye, and determined not to



<P>observe her attitude, inquired in a hurried manner after



<P>Mrs. Dashwood, and asked how long they had been in town.



<P>Elinor was robbed of all presence of mind by such an address,



<P>and was unable to say a word.&nbsp; But the feelings of her sister



<P>were instantly expressed.&nbsp; Her face was crimsoned over,



<P>and she exclaimed, in a voice of the greatest emotion,



<P>"Good God! Willoughby, what is the meaning of this?



<P>Have you not received my letters?&nbsp; Will you not shake



<P>hands with me?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He could not then avoid it, but her touch seemed



<P>painful to him, and he held her hand only for a moment.



<P>During all this time he was evidently struggling for composure.



<P>Elinor watched his countenance and saw its expression



<P>becoming more tranquil.&nbsp; After a moment's pause, he spoke



<P>with calmness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I did myself the honour of calling in Berkeley



<P>Street last Tuesday, and very much regretted that I was



<P>not fortunate enough to find yourselves and Mrs. Jennings



<P>at home.&nbsp; My card was not lost, I hope."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But have you not received my notes?" cried

Marianne



<P>in the wildest anxiety.&nbsp; "Here is some mistake I am



<P>sure--some dreadful mistake.&nbsp; What can be the meaning



<P>of it?&nbsp; Tell me, Willoughby; for heaven's sake tell me,



<P>what is the matter?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He made no reply; his complexion changed and

all his



<P>embarrassment returned; but as if, on catching the eye



<P>of the young lady with whom he had been previously talking,



<P>he felt the necessity of instant exertion, he recovered



<P>himself again, and after saying, "Yes, I had the pleasure



<P>of receiving the information of your arrival in town,



<P>which you were so good as to send me," turned hastily away



<P>with a slight bow and joined his friend.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne, now looking dreadfully white, and

unable



<P>to stand, sunk into her chair, and Elinor, expecting every



<P>moment to see her faint, tried to screen her from the



<P>observation of others, while reviving her with lavender water.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Go to him, Elinor," she cried, as soon as

she



<P>could speak, "and force him to come to me.&nbsp; Tell him



<P>I must see him again--must speak to him instantly.--



<P>I cannot rest--I shall not have a moment's peace till this



<P>is explained--some dreadful misapprehension or other.--



<P>Oh go to him this moment."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How can that be done? No, my dearest Marianne,



<P>you must wait.&nbsp; This is not the place for explanations.



<P>Wait only till tomorrow."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; With difficulty however could she prevent her



<P>from following him herself; and to persuade her to check



<P>her agitation, to wait, at least, with the appearance



<P>of composure, till she might speak to him with more privacy



<P>and more effect, was impossible; for Marianne continued



<P>incessantly to give way in a low voice to the misery



<P>of her feelings, by exclamations of wretchedness.



<P>In a short time Elinor saw Willoughby quit the room by the



<P>door towards the staircase, and telling Marianne that he



<P>was gone, urged the impossibility of speaking to him again



<P>that evening, as a fresh argument for her to be calm.



<P>She instantly begged her sister would entreat Lady



<P>Middleton to take them home, as she was too miserable



<P>to stay a minute longer.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lady Middleton, though in the middle of a rubber,



<P>on being informed that Marianne was unwell, was too



<P>polite to object for a moment to her wish of going away,



<P>and making over her cards to a friend, they departed



<P>as soon the carriage could be found.&nbsp; Scarcely a word



<P>was spoken during their return to Berkeley Street.



<P>Marianne was in a silent agony, too much oppressed even



<P>for tears; but as Mrs. Jennings was luckily not come home,



<P>they could go directly to their own room, where hartshorn



<P>restored her a little to herself.&nbsp; She was soon undressed



<P>and in bed, and as she seemed desirous of being alone,



<P>her sister then left her, and while she waited the return



<P>of Mrs. Jennings, had leisure enough for thinking over



<P>the past.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; That some kind of engagement had subsisted



<P>between Willoughby and Marianne she could not doubt,



<P>and that Willoughby was weary of it, seemed equally clear;



<P>for however Marianne might still feed her own wishes,



<P>SHE could not attribute such behaviour to mistake



<P>or misapprehension of any kind.&nbsp; Nothing but a thorough



<P>change of sentiment could account for it.&nbsp; Her indignation



<P>would have been still stronger than it was, had she



<P>not witnessed that embarrassment which seemed to speak



<P>a consciousness of his own misconduct, and prevented



<P>her from believing him so unprincipled as to have been



<P>sporting with the affections of her sister from the first,



<P>without any design that would bear investigation.



<P>Absence might have weakened his regard, and convenience



<P>might have determined him to overcome it, but that such



<P>a regard had formerly existed she could not bring herself



<P>to doubt.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As for Marianne, on the pangs which so unhappy

a meeting



<P>must already have given her, and on those still more



<P>severe which might await her in its probable consequence,



<P>she could not reflect without the deepest concern.



<P>Her own situation gained in the comparison; for while she



<P>could ESTEEM Edward as much as ever, however they might be



<P>divided in future, her mind might be always supported.



<P>But every circumstance that could embitter such an evil



<P>seemed uniting to heighten the misery of Marianne



<P>in a final separation from Willoughby--in an immediate



<P>and irreconcilable rupture with him.



<P>

<HTML>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 16</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne would have thought herself very inexcusable



<P>had she been able to sleep at all the first night



<P>after parting from Willoughby.&nbsp; She would have been



<P>ashamed to look her family in the face the next morning,



<P>had she not risen from her bed in more need of repose



<P>than when she lay down in it.&nbsp; But the feelings which



<P>made such composure a disgrace, left her in no danger



<P>of incurring it.&nbsp; She was awake the whole night, and she



<P>wept the greatest part of it.&nbsp; She got up with a headache,



<P>was unable to talk, and unwilling to take any nourishment;



<P>giving pain every moment to her mother and sisters,



<P>and forbidding all attempt at consolation from either.



<P>Her sensibility was potent enough!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When breakfast was over she walked out by herself,



<P>and wandered about the village of Allenham, indulging the



<P>recollection of past enjoyment and crying over the present



<P>reverse for the chief of the morning.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The evening passed off in the equal indulgence

of feeling.



<P>She played over every favourite song that she had been used



<P>to play to Willoughby, every air in which their voices



<P>had been oftenest joined, and sat at the instrument gazing



<P>on every line of music that he had written out for her,



<P>till her heart was so heavy that no farther sadness



<P>could be gained; and this nourishment of grief was every



<P>day applied.&nbsp; She spent whole hours at the pianoforte



<P>alternately singing and crying; her voice often totally



<P>suspended by her tears.&nbsp; In books too, as well as in music,



<P>she courted the misery which a contrast between the past



<P>and present was certain of giving.&nbsp; She read nothing



<P>but what they had been used to read together.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Such violence of affliction indeed could not

be supported



<P>for ever; it sunk within a few days into a calmer melancholy;



<P>but these employments, to which she daily recurred,



<P>her solitary walks and silent meditations, still produced



<P>occasional effusions of sorrow as lively as ever.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; No letter from Willoughby came; and none seemed

expected



<P>by Marianne.&nbsp; Her mother was surprised, and Elinor again



<P>became uneasy.&nbsp; But Mrs. Dashwood could find explanations



<P>whenever she wanted them, which at least satisfied herself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Remember, Elinor," said she, "how very often

Sir John



<P>fetches our letters himself from the post, and carries them



<P>to it.&nbsp; We have already agreed that secrecy may be necessary,



<P>and we must acknowledge that it could not be maintained if



<P>their correspondence were to pass through Sir John's hands."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor could not deny the truth of this, and

she tried



<P>to find in it a motive sufficient for their silence.



<P>But there was one method so direct, so simple, and in



<P>her opinion so eligible of knowing the real state



<P>of the affair, and of instantly removing all mystery,



<P>that she could not help suggesting it to her mother.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Why do you not ask Marianne at once," said

she,



<P>"whether she is or she is not engaged to Willoughby?&nbsp; From you,



<P>her mother, and so kind, so indulgent a mother, the question



<P>could not give offence.&nbsp; It would be the natural result



<P>of your affection for her.&nbsp; She used to be all unreserve,



<P>and to you more especially."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I would not ask such a question for the world.



<P>Supposing it possible that they are not engaged,



<P>what distress would not such an enquiry inflict!&nbsp; At any



<P>rate it would be most ungenerous.&nbsp; I should never deserve



<P>her confidence again, after forcing from her a confession



<P>of what is meant at present to be unacknowledged to any one.



<P>I know Marianne's heart: I know that she dearly loves me,



<P>and that I shall not be the last to whom the affair is made known,



<P>when circumstances make the revealment of it eligible.



<P>I would not attempt to force the confidence of any one;



<P>of a child much less; because a sense of duty would prevent



<P>the denial which her wishes might direct."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor thought this generosity overstrained,



<P>considering her sister's youth, and urged the matter farther,



<P>but in vain; common sense, common care, common prudence,



<P>were all sunk in Mrs. Dashwood's romantic delicacy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; It was several days before Willoughby's name



<P>was mentioned before Marianne by any of her family;



<P>Sir John and Mrs. Jennings, indeed, were not so nice;



<P>their witticisms added pain to many a painful hour;--



<P>but one evening, Mrs. Dashwood, accidentally taking up a



<P>volume of Shakespeare, exclaimed,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "We have never finished Hamlet, Marianne; our

dear



<P>Willoughby went away before we could get through it.



<P>We will put it by, that when he comes again...But it may



<P>be months, perhaps, before THAT happens."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Months!" cried Marianne, with strong surprise.



<P>"No--nor many weeks."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood was sorry for what she had said;



<P>but it gave Elinor pleasure, as it produced a reply



<P>from Marianne so expressive of confidence in Willoughby



<P>and knowledge of his intentions.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; One morning, about a week after his leaving

the country,



<P>Marianne was prevailed on to join her sisters in their



<P>usual walk, instead of wandering away by herself.



<P>Hitherto she had carefully avoided every companion in



<P>her rambles.&nbsp; If her sisters intended to walk on the downs,



<P>she directly stole away towards the lanes; if they talked



<P>of the valley, she was as speedy in climbing the hills,



<P>and could never be found when the others set off.



<P>But at length she was secured by the exertions of Elinor,



<P>who greatly disapproved such continual seclusion.&nbsp; They walked



<P>along the road through the valley, and chiefly in silence,



<P>for Marianne's MIND could not be controlled, and Elinor,



<P>satisfied with gaining one point, would not then attempt more.



<P>Beyond the entrance of the valley, where the country,



<P>though still rich, was less wild and more open, a long



<P>stretch of the road which they had travelled on first coming



<P>to Barton, lay before them; and on reaching that point,



<P>they stopped to look around them, and examine a prospect



<P>which formed the distance of their view from the cottage,



<P>from a spot which they had never happened to reach in any



<P>of their walks before.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Amongst the objects in the scene, they soon

discovered



<P>an animated one; it was a man on horseback riding towards them.



<P>In a few minutes they could distinguish him to be a gentleman;



<P>and in a moment afterwards Marianne rapturously exclaimed,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is he; it is indeed;--I know it is!"--and

was



<P>hastening to meet him, when Elinor cried out,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Indeed, Marianne, I think you are mistaken.&nbsp;

It is



<P>not Willoughby.&nbsp; The person is not tall enough for him,



<P>and has not his air."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "He has, he has," cried Marianne, "I am sure

he has.



<P>His air, his coat, his horse.&nbsp; I knew how soon he would come."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; She walked eagerly on as she spoke; and Elinor,



<P>to screen Marianne from particularity, as she felt almost



<P>certain of its not being Willoughby, quickened her



<P>pace and kept up with her.&nbsp; They were soon within



<P>thirty yards of the gentleman.&nbsp; Marianne looked again;



<P>her heart sunk within her; and abruptly turning round,



<P>she was hurrying back, when the voices of both her sisters



<P>were raised to detain her; a third, almost as well known



<P>as Willoughby's, joined them in begging her to stop,



<P>and she turned round with surprise to see and welcome



<P>Edward Ferrars.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He was the only person in the world who could



<P>at that moment be forgiven for not being Willoughby;



<P>the only one who could have gained a smile from her;



<P>but she dispersed her tears to smile on HIM, and in her



<P>sister's happiness forgot for a time her own disappointment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He dismounted, and giving his horse to his

servant,



<P>walked back with them to Barton, whither he was purposely



<P>coming to visit them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He was welcomed by them all with great cordiality,



<P>but especially by Marianne, who showed more warmth of



<P>regard in her reception of him than even Elinor herself.



<P>To Marianne, indeed, the meeting between Edward and her sister



<P>was but a continuation of that unaccountable coldness which she



<P>had often observed at Norland in their mutual behaviour.



<P>On Edward's side, more particularly, there was a deficiency



<P>of all that a lover ought to look and say on such an occasion.



<P>He was confused, seemed scarcely sensible of pleasure



<P>in seeing them, looked neither rapturous nor gay,



<P>said little but what was forced from him by questions,



<P>and distinguished Elinor by no mark of affection.



<P>Marianne saw and listened with increasing surprise.



<P>She began almost to feel a dislike of Edward; and it ended,



<P>as every feeling must end with her, by carrying back her



<P>thoughts to Willoughby, whose manners formed a contrast



<P>sufficiently striking to those of his brother elect.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; After a short silence which succeeded the first



<P>surprise and enquiries of meeting, Marianne asked



<P>Edward if he came directly from London.&nbsp; No, he had



<P>been in Devonshire a fortnight.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "A fortnight!" she repeated, surprised at his

being



<P>so long in the same county with Elinor without seeing



<P>her before.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; He looked rather distressed as he added, that

he



<P>had been staying with some friends near Plymouth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Have you been lately in Sussex?" said Elinor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I was at Norland about a month ago."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And how does dear, dear Norland look?" cried

Marianne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Dear, dear Norland," said Elinor, "probably

looks



<P>much as it always does at this time of the year.



<P>The woods and walks thickly covered with dead leaves."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh," cried Marianne, "with what transporting

sensation



<P>have I formerly seen them fall!&nbsp; How have I delighted,



<P>as I walked, to see them driven in showers about me



<P>by the wind!&nbsp; What feelings have they, the season, the air



<P>altogether inspired!&nbsp; Now there is no one to regard them.



<P>They are seen only as a nuisance, swept hastily off,



<P>and driven as much as possible from the sight."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is not every one," said Elinor, "who has

your



<P>passion for dead leaves."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No; my feelings are not often shared, not

often



<P>understood.&nbsp; But SOMETIMES they are."--As she said this,



<P>she sunk into a reverie for a few moments;--but rousing



<P>herself again, "Now, Edward," said she, calling his attention



<P>to the prospect, "here is Barton valley.&nbsp; Look up to it,



<P>and be tranquil if you can.&nbsp; Look at those hills!



<P>Did you ever see their equals?&nbsp; To the left is Barton park,



<P>amongst those woods and plantations.&nbsp; You may see the end



<P>of the house.&nbsp; And there, beneath that farthest hill,



<P>which rises with such grandeur, is our cottage."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is a beautiful country," he replied; "but

these



<P>bottoms must be dirty in winter."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How can you think of dirt, with such objects

before you?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Because," replied he, smiling, "among the

rest of the



<P>objects before me, I see a very dirty lane."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "How strange!" said Marianne to herself as

she walked on.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Have you an agreeable neighbourhood here?&nbsp;

Are the



<P>Middletons pleasant people?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, not all," answered Marianne; "we could

not



<P>be more unfortunately situated."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Marianne," cried her sister, "how can you

say so? How can



<P>you be so unjust?&nbsp; They are a very respectable family, Mr. Ferrars;



<P>and towards us have behaved in the friendliest manner.&nbsp; Have you



<P>forgot, Marianne, how many pleasant days we have owed to them?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No," said Marianne, in a low voice, "nor how

many



<P>painful moments."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor took no notice of this; and directing



<P>her attention to their visitor, endeavoured to support



<P>something like discourse with him, by talking of their



<P>present residence, its conveniences, &amp;c. extorting from him



<P>occasional questions and remarks.&nbsp; His coldness and reserve



<P>mortified her severely; she was vexed and half angry;



<P>but resolving to regulate her behaviour to him by the past



<P>rather than the present, she avoided every appearance



<P>of resentment or displeasure, and treated him as she



<P>thought he ought to be treated from the family connection.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter02</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 2</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. John Dashwood now installed herself mistress



<P>of Norland; and her mother and sisters-in-law were degraded



<P>to the condition of visitors.&nbsp; As such, however, they were



<P>treated by her with quiet civility; and by her husband



<P>with as much kindness as he could feel towards anybody



<P>beyond himself, his wife, and their child.&nbsp; He really



<P>pressed them, with some earnestness, to consider Norland



<P>as their home; and, as no plan appeared so eligible



<P>to Mrs. Dashwood as remaining there till she could



<P>accommodate herself with a house in the neighbourhood,



<P>his invitation was accepted.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; A continuance in a place where everything reminded



<P>her of former delight, was exactly what suited her mind.



<P>In seasons of cheerfulness, no temper could be more cheerful



<P>than hers, or possess, in a greater degree, that sanguine



<P>expectation of happiness which is happiness itself.



<P>But in sorrow she must be equally carried away by her fancy,



<P>and as far beyond consolation as in pleasure she was



<P>beyond alloy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. John Dashwood did not at all approve of

what her



<P>husband intended to do for his sisters.&nbsp; To take three



<P>thousand pounds from the fortune of their dear little boy



<P>would be impoverishing him to the most dreadful degree.



<P>She begged him to think again on the subject.&nbsp; How could



<P>he answer it to himself to rob his child, and his only



<P>child too, of so large a sum?&nbsp; And what possible claim



<P>could the Miss Dashwoods, who were related to him only by



<P>half blood, which she considered as no relationship at all,



<P>have on his generosity to so large an amount.&nbsp; It was very



<P>well known that no affection was ever supposed to exist



<P>between the children of any man by different marriages;



<P>and why was he to ruin himself, and their poor little Harry,



<P>by giving away all his money to his half sisters?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It was my father's last request to me," replied



<P>her husband, "that I should assist his widow and daughters."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "He did not know what he was talking of, I

dare say;



<P>ten to one but he was light-headed at the time.



<P>Had he been in his right senses, he could not have thought



<P>of such a thing as begging you to give away half your



<P>fortune from your own child."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "He did not stipulate for any particular sum,



<P>my dear Fanny; he only requested me, in general terms,



<P>to assist them, and make their situation more comfortable



<P>than it was in his power to do.&nbsp; Perhaps it would



<P>have been as well if he had left it wholly to myself.



<P>He could hardly suppose I should neglect them.



<P>But as he required the promise, I could not do less



<P>than give it; at least I thought so at the time.



<P>The promise, therefore, was given, and must be performed.



<P>Something must be done for them whenever they leave Norland



<P>and settle in a new home."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Well, then, LET something be done for them;



<P>but THAT something need not be three thousand pounds.



<P>Consider," she added, "that when the money is once



<P>parted with, it never can return.&nbsp; Your sisters will marry,



<P>and it will be gone for ever.&nbsp; If, indeed, it could



<P>be restored to our poor little boy--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Why, to be sure," said her husband, very gravely,



<P>"that would make great difference.&nbsp; The time may come when



<P>Harry will regret that so large a sum was parted with.



<P>If he should have a numerous family, for instance, it would



<P>be a very convenient addition."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "To be sure it would."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Perhaps, then, it would be better for all

parties,



<P>if the sum were diminished one half.--Five hundred pounds



<P>would be a prodigious increase to their fortunes!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh! beyond anything great!&nbsp; What brother

on earth



<P>would do half so much for his sisters, even if REALLY



<P>his sisters!&nbsp; And as it is--only half blood!--But you



<P>have such a generous spirit!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I would not wish to do any thing mean," he

replied.



<P>"One had rather, on such occasions, do too much than



<P>too little.&nbsp; No one, at least, can think I have not



<P>done enough for them: even themselves, they can hardly



<P>expect more."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "There is no knowing what THEY may expect,"



<P>said the lady, "but we are not to think of their



<P>expectations: the question is, what you can afford to do."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Certainly--and I think I may afford to give

them five



<P>hundred pounds a-piece.&nbsp; As it is, without any addition



<P>of mine, they will each have about three thousand pounds



<P>on their mother's death--a very comfortable fortune



<P>for any young woman."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "To be sure it is; and, indeed, it strikes

me that



<P>they can want no addition at all.&nbsp; They will have ten



<P>thousand pounds divided amongst them.&nbsp; If they marry,



<P>they will be sure of doing well, and if they do not,



<P>they may all live very comfortably together on the interest



<P>of ten thousand pounds."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "That is very true, and, therefore, I do not

know whether,



<P>upon the whole, it would not be more advisable to do



<P>something for their mother while she lives, rather than



<P>for them--something of the annuity kind I mean.--My sisters



<P>would feel the good effects of it as well as herself.



<P>A hundred a year would make them all perfectly comfortable."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; His wife hesitated a little, however, in giving



<P>her consent to this plan.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "To be sure," said she, "it is better than

parting with



<P>fifteen hundred pounds at once.&nbsp; But, then, if Mrs. Dashwood



<P>should live fifteen years we shall be completely taken in."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Fifteen years! my dear Fanny; her life cannot



<P>be worth half that purchase."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Certainly not; but if you observe, people

always



<P>live for ever when there is an annuity to be paid them;



<P>and she is very stout and healthy, and hardly forty.



<P>An annuity is a very serious business; it comes over



<P>and over every year, and there is no getting rid



<P>of it.&nbsp; You are not aware of what you are doing.



<P>I have known a great deal of the trouble of annuities;



<P>for my mother was clogged with the payment of three



<P>to old superannuated servants by my father's will,



<P>and it is amazing how disagreeable she found it.



<P>Twice every year these annuities were to be paid; and then



<P>there was the trouble of getting it to them; and then one



<P>of them was said to have died, and afterwards it turned



<P>out to be no such thing.&nbsp; My mother was quite sick of it.



<P>Her income was not her own, she said, with such perpetual



<P>claims on it; and it was the more unkind in my father,



<P>because, otherwise, the money would have been entirely at



<P>my mother's disposal, without any restriction whatever.



<P>It has given me such an abhorrence of annuities, that I am



<P>sure I would not pin myself down to the payment of one for



<P>all the world."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is certainly an unpleasant thing," replied

Mr. Dashwood,



<P>"to have those kind of yearly drains on one's income.



<P>One's fortune, as your mother justly says, is NOT one's own.



<P>To be tied down to the regular payment of such a sum,



<P>on every rent day, is by no means desirable: it takes away



<P>one's independence."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Undoubtedly; and after all you have no thanks

for it.



<P>They think themselves secure, you do no more than what



<P>is expected, and it raises no gratitude at all.&nbsp; If I were you,



<P>whatever I did should be done at my own discretion entirely.



<P>I would not bind myself to allow them any thing yearly.



<P>It may be very inconvenient some years to spare a hundred,



<P>or even fifty pounds from our own expenses."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I believe you are right, my love; it will

be better



<P>that there should by no annuity in the case; whatever I



<P>may give them occasionally will be of far greater assistance



<P>than a yearly allowance, because they would only enlarge



<P>their style of living if they felt sure of a larger income,



<P>and would not be sixpence the richer for it at the end



<P>of the year.&nbsp; It will certainly be much the best way.



<P>A present of fifty pounds, now and then, will prevent



<P>their ever being distressed for money, and will, I think,



<P>be amply discharging my promise to my father."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "To be sure it will.&nbsp; Indeed, to say the

truth,



<P>I am convinced within myself that your father had no idea



<P>of your giving them any money at all.&nbsp; The assistance



<P>he thought of, I dare say, was only such as might be



<P>reasonably expected of you; for instance, such as looking



<P>out for a comfortable small house for them, helping them



<P>to move their things, and sending them presents of fish



<P>and game, and so forth, whenever they are in season.



<P>I'll lay my life that he meant nothing farther; indeed,



<P>it would be very strange and unreasonable if he did.



<P>Do but consider, my dear Mr. Dashwood, how excessively



<P>comfortable your mother-in-law and her daughters may live



<P>on the interest of seven thousand pounds, besides the



<P>thousand pounds belonging to each of the girls, which brings



<P>them in fifty pounds a year a-piece, and, of course,



<P>they will pay their mother for their board out of it.



<P>Altogether, they will have five hundred a-year amongst them,



<P>and what on earth can four women want for more than



<P>that?--They will live so cheap! Their housekeeping will



<P>be nothing at all.&nbsp; They will have no carriage, no horses,



<P>and hardly any servants; they will keep no company,



<P>and can have no expenses of any kind!&nbsp; Only conceive



<P>how comfortable they will be!&nbsp; Five hundred a year! I am



<P>sure I cannot imagine how they will spend half of it;



<P>and as to your giving them more, it is quite absurd to think



<P>of it.&nbsp; They will be much more able to give YOU something."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Upon my word," said Mr. Dashwood, "I believe

you



<P>are perfectly right.&nbsp; My father certainly could mean



<P>nothing more by his request to me than what you say.



<P>I clearly understand it now, and I will strictly fulfil



<P>my engagement by such acts of assistance and kindness



<P>to them as you have described.&nbsp; When my mother removes



<P>into another house my services shall be readily given



<P>to accommodate her as far as I can.&nbsp; Some little present



<P>of furniture too may be acceptable then."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Certainly," returned Mrs. John Dashwood.&nbsp;

"But, however,



<P>ONE thing must be considered.&nbsp; When your father and mother



<P>moved to Norland, though the furniture of Stanhill



<P>was sold, all the china, plate, and linen was saved,



<P>and is now left to your mother.&nbsp; Her house will therefore



<P>be almost completely fitted up as soon as she takes it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "That is a material consideration undoubtedly.



<P>A valuable legacy indeed! And yet some of the plate would



<P>have been a very pleasant addition to our own stock here."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes; and the set of breakfast china is twice



<P>as handsome as what belongs to this house.&nbsp; A great



<P>deal too handsome, in my opinion, for any place THEY



<P>can ever afford to live in.&nbsp; But, however, so it is.



<P>Your father thought only of THEM.&nbsp; And I must say this:



<P>that you owe no particular gratitude to him, nor attention



<P>to his wishes; for we very well know that if he could,



<P>he would have left almost everything in the world to THEM."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This argument was irresistible.&nbsp; It gave

to his



<P>intentions whatever of decision was wanting before; and he



<P>finally resolved, that it would be absolutely unnecessary,



<P>if not highly indecorous, to do more for the widow



<P>and children of his father, than such kind of neighbourly



<P>acts as his own wife pointed out.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <TITLE>chapter03</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 3</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood remained at Norland several months;



<P>not from any disinclination to move when the sight of every



<P>well known spot ceased to raise the violent emotion which it



<P>produced for a while; for when her spirits began to revive,



<P>and her mind became capable of some other exertion than that



<P>of heightening its affliction by melancholy remembrances,



<P>she was impatient to be gone, and indefatigable in her inquiries



<P>for a suitable dwelling in the neighbourhood of Norland;



<P>for to remove far from that beloved spot was impossible.



<P>But she could hear of no situation that at once answered



<P>her notions of comfort and ease, and suited the prudence



<P>of her eldest daughter, whose steadier judgment rejected



<P>several houses as too large for their income, which her



<P>mother would have approved.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood had been informed by her husband

of the



<P>solemn promise on the part of his son in their favour,



<P>which gave comfort to his last earthly reflections.



<P>She doubted the sincerity of this assurance no more than he



<P>had doubted it himself, and she thought of it for her daughters'



<P>sake with satisfaction, though as for herself she was



<P>persuaded that a much smaller provision than 7000L would



<P>support her in affluence.&nbsp; For their brother's sake, too,



<P>for the sake of his own heart, she rejoiced; and she



<P>reproached herself for being unjust to his merit before,



<P>in believing him incapable of generosity.&nbsp; His attentive



<P>behaviour to herself and his sisters convinced her that



<P>their welfare was dear to him, and, for a long time,



<P>she firmly relied on the liberality of his intentions.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The contempt which she had, very early in their

acquaintance,



<P>felt for her daughter-in-law, was very much increased



<P>by the farther knowledge of her character, which half



<P>a year's residence in her family afforded; and perhaps



<P>in spite of every consideration of politeness or maternal



<P>affection on the side of the former, the two ladies might



<P>have found it impossible to have lived together so long,



<P>had not a particular circumstance occurred to give



<P>still greater eligibility, according to the opinions



<P>of Mrs. Dashwood, to her daughters' continuance at Norland.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; This circumstance was a growing attachment

between



<P>her eldest girl and the brother of Mrs. John Dashwood,



<P>a gentleman-like and pleasing young man, who was introduced



<P>to their acquaintance soon after his sister's establishment



<P>at Norland, and who had since spent the greatest part



<P>of his time there.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Some mothers might have encouraged the intimacy

from



<P>motives of interest, for Edward Ferrars was the eldest son



<P>of a man who had died very rich; and some might have repressed



<P>it from motives of prudence, for, except a trifling sum,



<P>the whole of his fortune depended on the will of his mother.



<P>But Mrs. Dashwood was alike uninfluenced by either consideration.



<P>It was enough for her that he appeared to be amiable,



<P>that he loved her daughter, and that Elinor returned



<P>the partiality.&nbsp; It was contrary to every doctrine of



<P>her's that difference of fortune should keep any couple



<P>asunder who were attracted by resemblance of disposition;



<P>and that Elinor's merit should not be acknowledged



<P>by every one who knew her, was to her comprehension impossible.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Edward Ferrars was not recommended to their

good



<P>opinion by any peculiar graces of person or address.



<P>He was not handsome, and his manners required intimacy



<P>to make them pleasing.&nbsp; He was too diffident to do justice



<P>to himself; but when his natural shyness was overcome,



<P>his behaviour gave every indication of an open,



<P>affectionate heart.&nbsp; His understanding was good,



<P>and his education had given it solid improvement.



<P>But he was neither fitted by abilities nor disposition



<P>to answer the wishes of his mother and sister, who longed



<P>to see him distinguished--as--they hardly knew what.



<P>They wanted him to make a fine figure in the world in some



<P>manner or other.&nbsp; His mother wished to interest him in



<P>political concerns, to get him into parliament, or to see



<P>him connected with some of the great men of the day.



<P>Mrs. John Dashwood wished it likewise; but in the mean while,



<P>till one of these superior blessings could be attained, it would



<P>have quieted her ambition to see him driving a barouche.



<P>But Edward had no turn for great men or barouches.



<P>All his wishes centered in domestic comfort and the quiet



<P>of private life.&nbsp; Fortunately he had a younger brother



<P>who was more promising.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Edward had been staying several weeks in the

house



<P>before he engaged much of Mrs. Dashwood's attention;



<P>for she was, at that time, in such affliction as rendered



<P>her careless of surrounding objects.&nbsp; She saw only that he



<P>was quiet and unobtrusive, and she liked him for it.



<P>He did not disturb the wretchedness of her mind by



<P>ill-timed conversation.&nbsp; She was first called to observe



<P>and approve him farther, by a reflection which Elinor



<P>chanced one day to make on the difference between him



<P>and his sister.&nbsp; It was a contrast which recommended him



<P>most forcibly to her mother.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "It is enough," said she; "to say that he is

unlike



<P>Fanny is enough.&nbsp; It implies everything amiable.



<P>I love him already."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I think you will like him," said Elinor, "when

you



<P>know more of him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Like him!" replied her mother with a smile.



<P>"I feel no sentiment of approbation inferior to love."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You may esteem him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have never yet known what it was to separate



<P>esteem and love."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood now took pains to get acquainted

with him.



<P>Her manners were attaching, and soon banished his reserve.



<P>She speedily comprehended all his merits; the persuasion



<P>of his regard for Elinor perhaps assisted her penetration;



<P>but she really felt assured of his worth: and even that



<P>quietness of manner, which militated against all her



<P>established ideas of what a young man's address ought to be,



<P>was no longer uninteresting when she knew his heart to be



<P>warm and his temper affectionate.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; No sooner did she perceive any symptom of love



<P>in his behaviour to Elinor, than she considered their



<P>serious attachment as certain, and looked forward



<P>to their marriage as rapidly approaching.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "In a few months, my dear Marianne." said she,



<P>"Elinor will, in all probability be settled for life.



<P>We shall miss her; but SHE will be happy."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh! Mamma, how shall we do without her?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "My love, it will be scarcely a separation.



<P>We shall live within a few miles of each other, and shall



<P>meet every day of our lives.&nbsp; You will gain a brother,



<P>a real, affectionate brother.&nbsp; I have the highest opinion



<P>in the world of Edward's heart.&nbsp; But you look grave,



<P>Marianne; do you disapprove your sister's choice?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Perhaps," said Marianne, "I may consider it



<P>with some surprise.&nbsp; Edward is very amiable, and I love



<P>him tenderly.&nbsp; But yet--he is not the kind of young



<P>man--there is something wanting--his figure is not striking;



<P>it has none of that grace which I should expect



<P>in the man who could seriously attach my sister.



<P>His eyes want all that spirit, that fire, which at once



<P>announce virtue and intelligence.&nbsp; And besides all this,



<P>I am afraid, Mamma, he has no real taste.&nbsp; Music seems



<P>scarcely to attract him, and though he admires Elinor's



<P>drawings very much, it is not the admiration of a person



<P>who can understand their worth.&nbsp; It is evident, in spite of



<P>his frequent attention to her while she draws, that in fact



<P>he knows nothing of the matter.&nbsp; He admires as a lover,



<P>not as a connoisseur.&nbsp; To satisfy me, those characters



<P>must be united.&nbsp; I could not be happy with a man whose



<P>taste did not in every point coincide with my own.



<P>He must enter into all my feelings; the same books,



<P>the same music must charm us both.&nbsp; Oh! mama, how spiritless,



<P>how tame was Edward's manner in reading to us last night!



<P>I felt for my sister most severely.&nbsp; Yet she bore it



<P>with so much composure, she seemed scarcely to notice it.



<P>I could hardly keep my seat.&nbsp; To hear those beautiful lines



<P>which have frequently almost driven me wild, pronounced



<P>with such impenetrable calmness, such dreadful indifference!"--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "He would certainly have done more justice

to



<P>simple and elegant prose.&nbsp; I thought so at the time;



<P>but you WOULD give him Cowper."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Nay, Mamma, if he is not to be animated by

Cowper!--



<P>but we must allow for difference of taste.&nbsp; Elinor has



<P>not my feelings, and therefore she may overlook it, and



<P>be happy with him.&nbsp; But it would have broke MY heart,



<P>had I loved him, to hear him read with so little sensibility.



<P>Mama, the more I know of the world, the more am I convinced



<P>that I shall never see a man whom I can really love.



<P>I require so much!&nbsp; He must have all Edward's virtues,



<P>and his person and manners must ornament his goodness



<P>with every possible charm."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Remember, my love, that you are not seventeen.



<P>It is yet too early in life to despair of such a happiness.



<P>Why should you be less fortunate than your mother?&nbsp; In



<P>one circumstance only, my Marianne, may your destiny be



<P>different from her's!"



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter17</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER 17</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mrs. Dashwood was surprised only for a moment

at



<P>seeing him; for his coming to Barton was, in her opinion,



<P>of all things the most natural.&nbsp; Her joy and expression



<P>of regard long outlived her wonder.&nbsp; He received the kindest



<P>welcome from her; and shyness, coldness, reserve could not



<P>stand against such a reception.&nbsp; They had begun to fail him



<P>before he entered the house, and they were quite overcome



<P>by the captivating manners of Mrs. Dashwood.&nbsp; Indeed a man



<P>could not very well be in love with either of her daughters,



<P>without extending the passion to her; and Elinor had the



<P>satisfaction of seeing him soon become more like himself.



<P>His affections seemed to reanimate towards them all,



<P>and his interest in their welfare again became perceptible.



<P>He was not in spirits, however; he praised their house,



<P>admired its prospect, was attentive, and kind; but still



<P>he was not in spirits.&nbsp; The whole family perceived it,



<P>and Mrs. Dashwood, attributing it to some want of liberality



<P>in his mother, sat down to table indignant against all



<P>selfish parents.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What are Mrs. Ferrars's views for you at present,

Edward?"



<P>said she, when dinner was over and they had drawn round



<P>the fire; "are you still to be a great orator in spite of yourself?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No. I hope my mother is now convinced that

I have



<P>no more talents than inclination for a public life!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But how is your fame to be established? for

famous you



<P>must be to satisfy all your family; and with no inclination



<P>for expense, no affection for strangers, no profession,



<P>and no assurance, you may find it a difficult matter."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I shall not attempt it.&nbsp; I have no wish

to be



<P>distinguished; and have every reason to hope I never shall.



<P>Thank Heaven! I cannot be forced into genius and eloquence."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You have no ambition, I well know.&nbsp; Your

wishes



<P>are all moderate."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "As moderate as those of the rest of the world,



<P>I believe.&nbsp; I wish as well as every body else to be



<P>perfectly happy; but, like every body else it must be



<P>in my own way.&nbsp; Greatness will not make me so."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Strange that it would!" cried Marianne.&nbsp;

"What have



<P>wealth or grandeur to do with happiness?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Grandeur has but little," said Elinor, "but

wealth



<P>has much to do with it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Elinor, for shame!" said Marianne, "money

can only



<P>give happiness where there is nothing else to give it.



<P>Beyond a competence, it can afford no real satisfaction,



<P>as far as mere self is concerned."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Perhaps," said Elinor, smiling, "we may come



<P>to the same point.&nbsp; YOUR competence and MY wealth



<P>are very much alike, I dare say; and without them,



<P>as the world goes now, we shall both agree that every



<P>kind of external comfort must be wanting.&nbsp; Your ideas



<P>are only more noble than mine.&nbsp; Come, what is your competence?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "About eighteen hundred or two thousand a year;



<P>not more than THAT."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor laughed.&nbsp; "TWO thousand a year!

ONE is my



<P>wealth! I guessed how it would end."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And yet two thousand a-year is a very moderate

income,"



<P>said Marianne.&nbsp; "A family cannot well be maintained on



<P>a smaller.&nbsp; I am sure I am not extravagant in my demands.



<P>A proper establishment of servants, a carriage, perhaps two,



<P>and hunters, cannot be supported on less."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor smiled again, to hear her sister describing



<P>so accurately their future expenses at Combe Magna.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Hunters!" repeated Edward--"but why must you

have



<P>hunters?&nbsp; Every body does not hunt."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne coloured as she replied, "But most

people do."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I wish," said Margaret, striking out a novel

thought,



<P>"that somebody would give us all a large fortune apiece!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh that they would!" cried Marianne, her eyes



<P>sparkling with animation, and her cheeks glowing



<P>with the delight of such imaginary happiness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "We are all unanimous in that wish, I suppose,"



<P>said Elinor, "in spite of the insufficiency of wealth."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Oh dear!" cried Margaret, "how happy I should

be!



<P>I wonder what I should do with it!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Marianne looked as if she had no doubt on that

point.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I should be puzzled to spend so large a fortune

myself,"



<P>said Mrs. Dashwood, "if my children were all to be rich



<P>my help."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You must begin your improvements on this house,"



<P>observed Elinor, "and your difficulties will soon vanish."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "What magnificent orders would travel from

this family



<P>to London," said Edward, "in such an event!&nbsp; What a happy



<P>day for booksellers, music-sellers, and print-shops!&nbsp; You,



<P>Miss Dashwood, would give a general commission for every



<P>new print of merit to be sent you--and as for Marianne,



<P>I know her greatness of soul, there would not be music enough



<P>in London to content her.&nbsp; And books!--Thomson, Cowper,



<P>Scott--she would buy them all over and over again: she



<P>would buy up every copy, I believe, to prevent their



<P>falling into unworthy hands; and she would have every



<P>book that tells her how to admire an old twisted tree.



<P>Should not you, Marianne?&nbsp; Forgive me, if I am very saucy.



<P>But I was willing to shew you that I had not forgot our



<P>old disputes."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I love to be reminded of the past, Edward--whether

it



<P>be melancholy or gay, I love to recall it--and you



<P>will never offend me by talking of former times.



<P>You are very right in supposing how my money would be



<P>spent--some of it, at least--my loose cash would certainly



<P>be employed in improving my collection of music and books."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "And the bulk of your fortune would be laid

out



<P>in annuities on the authors or their heirs."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, Edward, I should have something else to

do



<P>with it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Perhaps, then, you would bestow it as a reward

on that



<P>person who wrote the ablest defence of your favourite maxim,



<P>that no one can ever be in love more than once in their



<P>life--your opinion on that point is unchanged, I presume?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Undoubtedly. At my time of life opinions are

tolerably fixed.



<P>It is not likely that I should now see or hear any thing to change them."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Marianne is as steadfast as ever, you see,"

said Elinor,



<P>"she is not at all altered."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "She is only grown a little more grave than

she was."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Nay, Edward," said Marianne, "you need not

reproach me.



<P>You are not very gay yourself."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Why should you think so!" replied he, with

a sigh.



<P>"But gaiety never was a part of MY character."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Nor do I think it a part of Marianne's," said

Elinor;



<P>"I should hardly call her a lively girl--she is very earnest,



<P>very eager in all she does--sometimes talks a great deal



<P>and always with animation--but she is not often really merry."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I believe you are right," he replied, "and

yet I



<P>have always set her down as a lively girl."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I have frequently detected myself in such

kind of mistakes,"



<P>said Elinor, "in a total misapprehension of character in some



<P>point or other: fancying people so much more gay or grave,



<P>or ingenious or stupid than they really are, and I can



<P>hardly tell why or in what the deception originated.



<P>Sometimes one is guided by what they say of themselves,



<P>and very frequently by what other people say of them,



<P>without giving oneself time to deliberate and judge."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But I thought it was right, Elinor," said

Marianne,



<P>"to be guided wholly by the opinion of other people.



<P>I thought our judgments were given us merely to be subservient



<P>to those of neighbours.&nbsp; This has always been your doctrine,



<P>I am sure."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "No, Marianne, never.&nbsp; My doctrine has

never aimed



<P>at the subjection of the understanding.&nbsp; All I have



<P>ever attempted to influence has been the behaviour.



<P>You must not confound my meaning.&nbsp; I am guilty, I confess,



<P>of having often wished you to treat our acquaintance



<P>in general with greater attention; but when have I advised



<P>you to adopt their sentiments or to conform to their



<P>judgment in serious matters?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "You have not been able to bring your sister

over to your



<P>plan of general civility," said Edward to Elinor, "Do you gain



<P>no ground?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Quite the contrary," replied Elinor,



<P>looking expressively at Marianne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "My judgment," he returned, "is all on your

side



<P>of the question; but I am afraid my practice is much



<P>more on your sister's.&nbsp; I never wish to offend, but I



<P>am so foolishly shy, that I often seem negligent,



<P>when I am only kept back by my natural awkwardness.



<P>I have frequently thought that I must have been intended



<P>by nature to be fond of low company, I am so little at



<P>my ease among strangers of gentility!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Marianne has not shyness to excuse any inattention



<P>of hers," said Elinor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "She knows her own worth too well for false

shame,"



<P>replied Edward.&nbsp; "Shyness is only the effect of a sense



<P>of inferiority in some way or other.&nbsp; If I could persuade



<P>myself that my manners were perfectly easy and graceful,



<P>I should not be shy."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "But you would still be reserved," said Marianne,



<P>"and that is worse."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Edward started--"Reserved! Am I reserved, Marianne?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Yes, very."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "I do not understand you," replied he, colouring.



<P>"Reserved!--how, in what manner?&nbsp; What am I to tell you?



<P>What can you suppose?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Elinor looked surprised at his emotion; but

trying



<P>to laugh off the subject, she said to him, "Do not you



<P>know my sister well enough to understand what she means?



<P>Do not you know she calls every one reserved who does not



<P>talk as fast, and admire what she admires as rapturously



<P>as herself?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Edward made no answer.&nbsp; His gravity and

thoughtfulness



<P>returned on him in their fullest extent--and he sat



<P>for some time silent and dull.



<P>
